R:DET a -> an
 [explain]:  Always use an (NOT a) before a word beginning with a vowel sound: ‘an egg’ ‘an envelope’.
[wrong sentence]:  I hope you all have a enjoyable stay.


 [explain]:  Use an (NOT a) before an abbreviation that begins with a vowel sound: ‘an MSc’ ‘an MP’.
[wrong sentence]:  My husband is doing a MSc in civil engineering.


 [explain]:  Use an (NOT a) before words beginning with h when the h is not pronounced: ‘an honour’ , ‘an hour’.
[wrong sentence]:  Sometimes it is difficult to live a honest life.


R:DET a -> some
 [explain]:  Advice is an uncountable noun: ‘I could do with some advice.’ ; Compare: ‘She gave me a good piece of advice.’
[wrong sentence]:  She gave me a good advice.


 [explain]:  See Language Note at SCENERY
[wrong sentence]:  He’s gone to buy a fresh bread.


 [explain]:  See Language Note at SCENERY
[wrong sentence]:  On my birthday I had a good fun with my friends.


 [explain]:  Luggage is an uncountable noun: ‘Do you have any more luggage?’
[wrong sentence]:  He had brought a heavy luggage with him.


 [explain]:  News is an uncountable noun: ‘Why do Clive’s letters never contain any news?’ There’s one piece of news that I’m sure will interest you.’
[wrong sentence]:  I’m looking forward to hearing a good news from you.


 [explain]:  Scissors is a plural noun: ‘Do we have any sharp scissors?’ ‘I could do with a new pair of scissors.’
[wrong sentence]:  First you cut along the dotted line with a scissors.


 [explain]:  Toast is an uncountable-noun: ‘There’s some toast for you in the kitchen.’ ‘How many pieces/slices of toast would you like?’
[wrong sentence]:  I usually have a cup of coffee and a toast for breakfast.


R:DET a -> any
 [explain]:  Air (= the mixture of gases that we breathe) is an uncountable noun: ‘Let’s go for a walk and get some air in our lungs.’
[wrong sentence]:  It’s hard to find a fresh air nowadays.


 [explain]:  Clothes is a plural noun: ‘I need some new clothes.’ (NOT ‘a new clothes’)
[wrong sentence]:  Don’t go out and buy a special clothes.


R:DET no -> any
 [explain]:  See Language Note at NOT
[wrong sentence]:  There were hardly no trees left, just bare rocky land.


R:DET some -> any
 [explain]:  See Language Note at NOT
[wrong sentence]:  The refugees don’t have some rights at all.


 [explain]:  Use some, something etc in offers, requests, and questions that expect or hope for a ‘yes’ answer: ‘Who’d like something to eat?’ ‘Could you give me some help, please?’ ‘Aren’t there some letters to be posted?’ In other questions, use any, anything etc: ‘Did you get any letters today?’ ‘Have you seen any good films recently?’
[wrong sentence]:  Do you have some questions?


R:DET an -> a
 [explain]:  Before a word beginning with h, use a if the h is pronounced: ‘a house’, ‘a half’, ‘a horrible day’.
[wrong sentence]:  I had never visited an hospital before.


R:DET any -> a
 [explain]:  Any is usually used with uncountable nouns and plural countables (NOT with singular countable nouns). Compare: ‘Do you have any money?’ (money is an uncountable noun) ‘Do you have any fifty-cent coins?’ (coins is a plural countable noun) ‘Do you have a fifty-cent coin?’ (coin is a singular countable noun)
[wrong sentence]:  Has Atsuko found any job yet?
[wrong sentence]:  If there is any hole in the balloon, the air will escape.


R:DET the -> a
 [explain]:  a degree (NOT the): ‘She is studying for a postgraduate degree.’
[wrong sentence]:  I have the degree in economics.


R:DET any -> each
 [explain]:  Any smoker must remember that the people around him are inhaling the smoke. Every smoker must remember that the people around him are inhaling the smoke.
[wrong sentence]:  Any day was the same.


R:DET any -> all
 [explain]:  To refer to all the people or things in a group or category, use each/every + singular countable noun OR all + plural countable noun (NOT any): ‘Every house in the street had one or two broken windows.’ ‘All students are required to register during the first week.’
[wrong sentence]:  Any parents are thrilled when their first baby arrives.


R:DET the -> his
 [explain]:  in my/them/heir etc hand/s (NOT the): ‘You can’t argue when someone has a gun in their hand.’
[wrong sentence]:  He came out of the bathroom with a towel in the hand.


R:DET its -> it’s
 [explain]:  See IT’S
[wrong sentence]:  Its a nice place to have a holiday.


R:DET the -> that
 [explain]:  When" you are telling a story or reporting what happened, use at that moment ‘At that moment the car skidded on the ice and went off the road.’
[wrong sentence]:  At the moment the girl opened her eyes.


R:DET an -> my
 [explain]:  Own always follows my/her/their/Jill’s etc (NOT an) ‘their own children ‘, ‘her own flat’ , ‘Tina ‘s own radio’.
[wrong sentence]:  I now have enough money to buy an own car.


R:DET these -> this
 [explain]:  To refer back to the points that you have just made, use (all) this (NOT (all) these): ‘In spite of all this, we managed to enjoy ourselves.’ ‘I find all this very confusing.’ officers waiting to see you.’ ; Note that in this pattern there has no meaning. It simply fills the subject position.
[wrong sentence]:  The present government doesn’t care enough about the poorer sections of our society. Businessmen are encouraged to exploit workers and make huge profits. In spite of all these, I believe in the principles of free enterprise.


R:DET which -> who/that
 [explain]:  Which is used to refer to things (NOT people): ‘I like music which/that helps me to relax.’
[wrong sentence]:  Students which fail the exam have to take the course again.


U:DET a -> NONE
 [explain]:  Do not use a/an before an adjective (e.g. ‘deaf’, ‘British’) unless the adjective is followed by a noun: ‘Her husband is disabled.’ Compare: ‘The footbridge cannot be used by the disabled.’ (= all people who are disabled)
[wrong sentence]:  The child had been a deaf since birth.
[wrong sentence]:  One of the girls I share with is a British.


 [explain]:  Behaviour is an uncountable noun.
[wrong sentence]:  Such a behaviour can easily cause offence.


 [explain]:  cancer (uncountable) = a type of serious disease: ‘It is generally believed that diet plays an important role in the prevention of cancer.’ ‘Skin cancer is related to prolonged exposure to sunlight.’ a cancer (countable) = an abnormal growth in someone’s body which is caused by this disease; tumour: ‘Some small cancers may be destroyed by the body’s defence mechanisms.’
[wrong sentence]:  Her husband died 10 years ago of a lung cancer.


 [explain]:  Care is nearly always an uncountable noun: ‘Care of the environment has become a priority in government thinking.’ ‘Would you like me to take care of the plants while you’re away?’
[wrong sentence]:  These children need a special care and attention.
[wrong sentence]:  My host family took a good care of me.


 [explain]:  When company means ‘someone or something that stops you from feeling lonely or bored; companionship’, it is uncountable: ‘Whenever he goes off on a long trip, he takes his radio with him for company.’
[wrong sentence]:  For those who live by themselves, television provides a good company.


 [explain]:  in good/excellent/perfect/bad/terrible … condition (WITHOUT a/an) ‘Most of the CDs were still is the car in?’
[wrong sentence]:  You should try to keep the car in a good condition.


 [explain]:  In its usual meaning, damage is an uncountable noun: ‘The insurance company will pay for any damage.’ ‘The ceiling had suffered a great deal of damage.’ damages (plural noun) = a sum of money that someone is awarded in a court of law: ‘She was awarded $3000 in damages.’ ‘She claimed damages of £2000 for wrongful dismissal.’
[wrong sentence]:  The car crashed into a tree and suffered a severe damage.


 [explain]:  Food is nearly always uncountable: ‘We need to go out and buy some food.’ It is countable only when it refers to a particular kind of food: ‘baby foods’, ‘health foods’.
[wrong sentence]:  It is a disgrace to serve such a poor food.


 [explain]:  See Language Note at SCENERY
[wrong sentence]:  It would be a great fun if we could go together.


 [explain]:  When you mean ‘a single hair’ or ‘several individual hairs’, hair is a countable noun: ‘There was a long black hair in my soup.’ ‘Stand still and I’ll brush the hairs off your jacket.’ When you mean ‘all the hair on a person’s head’, hair is uncountable: ‘Where do you go to have your hair cut?’
[wrong sentence]:  He had a black hair and very clear eyes.


 [explain]:  Health is an uncountable noun: ‘Worry can affect your health.’"Nothing in life is more important than good health.’
[wrong sentence]:  I hope this letter finds you in a good health.


 [explain]:  Help is usually an uncountable noun: ‘Do you want some help?’ ‘He doesn’t like asking for help.’ Note however the phrase be a (great/tremendous) help: ‘Thanks for coming and looking after the children. You’ve been a great help.’ ‘It would be a great help if you could just address all the envelopes.’
[wrong sentence]:  Remember to call me if you need a help.


 [explain]:  leave (= a number of days, weeks or months when someone does not have to do their usual job) is usually an uncountable noun: ‘Government officers get six weeks’ annual leave.’ ‘First she was on sick leave, then she took study leave, and now she’s applied for maternity leave!’
[wrong sentence]:  Every woman is entitled to a maternity leave.


 [explain]:  a lot of (WITH a): ‘I bet she’s got a lot of money.’ lots of (WITHOUT a): ‘I bet she’s got lots of money.’
[wrong sentence]:  She makes us do a lots of homework.


 [explain]:  Luck is an uncountable noun: ‘She’s had a lot of bad luck recently.’ ‘Meeting the right partner is just a matter of luck.’
[wrong sentence]:  I’d just like to say goodbye and wish you a good luck.
[wrong sentence]:  What a rotten luck! There he was again, sitting right beside me.


 [explain]:  Music is an uncountable noun: ‘Her hobbies are gardening and music.’
[wrong sentence]:  Are we allowed to play a pop music?


 [explain]:  Pay is an uncountable noun: ‘They’ve given him a week’s leave without pay.’
[wrong sentence]:  Very few office workers get a good pay.


 [explain]:  Permission is an uncountable noun: ‘If you want to put up a tent, you’ll have to get the farmer’s permission.’ ‘Nobody is allowed to leave early without permission.’
[wrong sentence]:  To get a job in Switzerland, foreigners need a special permission.


 [explain]:  See Language Note at SCENERY
[wrong sentence]:  We have made a great progress in the field of medicine.


 [explain]:  Proof (= evidence) is usually an uncountable noun: ‘Investigators now have proof of his involvement in the arms deal.’
[wrong sentence]:  These bones are a proof that the animals really existed.


 [explain]:  Research is usually an uncountable noun: ‘A team of American scientists is carrying out research into the effects of acid rain.’ Researches (plural) is also used, especially in formal styles of British English and usually refers to a series of related studies by a particular person or team: ‘His researches go back to the 1950s.’ ‘Their more recent researches point to a decline in the mortality rate.’ The more usual word for this meaning, especially in American English, is studies.
[wrong sentence]:  I’m doing a research into the causes of child abuse.


 [explain]:  Scenery is an uncountable noun: ‘Some of the scenery is quite spectacular.’
[wrong sentence]:  What a beautiful scenery!


 [explain]:  Use theta/my etc only when you are talking about a particular school: ‘She goes to a very good school.’ ‘Our two boys go to the same school.’ When you refer to school as a type of place or activity, use leave school, start school, go to school, etc (WITHOUT the/a/my etc): ‘Most children go to primary school at the age of five.’ The same rule applies to kindergarten, college, university, church, prison, jail and (in British
[wrong sentence]:  He still isn’t old enough to go to a school.


 [explain]:  go/be on strike (WITHOUT a): ‘Transport workers have gone on strike for better pay and shorter hours.’ ‘The miners are still on strike.’
[wrong sentence]:  The whole workforce is threatening to go on a strike.


 [explain]:  have good/poor/little/no taste (in sth) , (WITHOUT a/an): ‘She certainly has remarkably good taste in clothes.’
[wrong sentence]:  I buy all my family’s clothes because my husband has such a poor taste.


 [explain]:  Thunder is an uncountable noun: ‘When Mr Cameron got angry , his voice was as loud as thunder.’
[wrong sentence]:  I heard a terribly loud noise, like a thunder.


 [explain]:  Treasure ( = a collection of valuable objects) is an uncountable noun: ‘It’s an adventure story about a search for buried treasure.’
[wrong sentence]:  There is always controversy when a treasure is discovered.


 [explain]:  See Language Note at INFORMATION
[wrong sentence]:  We had a miserable weather while on holiday.


 [explain]:  See Language Note at OCCUPATION
[wrong sentence]:  I’ve come to England to do a research work in civil engineering.
[wrong sentence]:  Without a full-time work, some people think it’s not worth living.


U:DET an -> NONE
 [explain]:  In British English accommodation (= a place to stay or live in) is always uncountable: ‘For the first year we stayed in rented accommodation.’ In American English both accommodation and accommodations (plural) are used.
[wrong sentence]:  Could you help me look for an accommodation?


 [explain]:  When you are talking about the idea or process of doing something, action is uncountable: ‘There is too much talk and not enough action.’ In the phrase take action, action is always uncountable: ‘This illegal trade will continue unless action is taken to stop it.’
[wrong sentence]:  The government should take an action to reduce the birth rate.
[wrong sentence]:  Few people are aware that an urgent action is needed.


 [explain]:  Evidence is an uncountable noun: ‘Medical evidence suggests that men are more likely to have heart attacks than women.’
[wrong sentence]:  This true story provides an evidence of the power of advertising.


 [explain]:  Glasses (= spectacles) is a plural noun: ‘I wear glasses just for reading.’ ‘Have you seen my glasses?’
[wrong sentence]:  He wears an old-fashioned glasses which make him look intelligent.


 [explain]:  a parking space/place = a place in a street, car park etc where a vehicle can be left: ‘My first attempt to back into the parking space was a disaster.’ a car park (AmE parking lot) = a large open area or building where cars can be left: ‘The supermarket has its own car park.’ parking (uncountable noun) = space(s) where vehicles can be left: ‘Parking is available at
[wrong sentence]:  What the town needs is an underground parking.


 [explain]:  be good/excellent/exceptional value (for money)(without a/an): ‘£600 might seem expensive for a two-week holiday, but when you look at all the extras it’s quite good value.’
[wrong sentence]:  In any case, the car gives you an exceptional value for money.


U:DET the -> NONE
 [explain]:  See Note at THE 4
[wrong sentence]:  The country’s economy is based on the agriculture.


 [explain]:  For general reference, put all/most/some etc immediately in front of the noun: ‘In Japan most people use chopsticks.’ For specific reference, use all/most/some + of the/these/their etc + noun: ‘Most of the Americans I met were very friendly.’
[wrong sentence]:  I like all the kinds of music.


 [explain]:  When breakfast/lunch/dinner etc refer to an event (rather than to the meal itself), they are used without at/the/this/my etc: ‘What shall we do after lunch?’ ‘Pam and Simon have invited us to dinner.’ ‘What time do you have breakfast?’ You use at/he/this/my etc when you refer to the meal itself: ‘We enjoyed the evening, even though the dinner was cold.’ ‘I don’t usually have time for a cooked breakfast.’
[wrong sentence]:  After the breakfast, we went shopping.


 [explain]:  See Note at SCHOOL 1
[wrong sentence]:  Not so long ago nearly everybody used to go to the church.


 [explain]:  See Note at SCHOOL 1
[wrong sentence]:  By going to the college or university, you become more mature.


 [explain]:  lose control (of sth), WITHOUT the, his, my, etc: ‘In 1993 Roseberg lost control of the company after a surprise takeover bid.’
[wrong sentence]:  He lost the control of the car and hit a wall.


 [explain]:  American culture, British culture, etc (WITHOUT the): ‘The lecture this afternoon is on the history of French culture and institutions.’
[wrong sentence]:  The year in Boston taught me a lot about the American culture.


 [explain]:  all day (WITHOUT the): ‘What have you been doing all day?’
[wrong sentence]:  Some people watch television all the day.


 [explain]:  See THE 4
[wrong sentence]:  The goal is to provide the free education for every girl and boy.


 [explain]:  See Note at THE 4
[wrong sentence]:  Our lives depend on the electricity.


 [explain]:  Do not use the before half except when talking about a particular half. Compare: ‘He spends half the week playing golf.’ ‘During the second half ,of the concert I could hardly stay awake.’
[wrong sentence]:  He agreed to give her the half of the money.


 [explain]:  See Note at SCHOOL 1
[wrong sentence]:  I’ve been in the hospital for the last five weeks.


 [explain]:  When industry means all industries seen together as one thing. it is an uncountable noun: ‘She can’t decide whether she wants a career in banking or in industry.’
[wrong sentence]:  Another thing that education has changed is the industry.


 [explain]:  See Note at THE 4
[wrong sentence]:  The legal system ensures the justice for everyone.


 [explain]:  When life means ‘the way of life that is connected with a particular type of situation, group or occupation’, it is usually used without the: ‘I found city life too stressful.’ ‘Life in New York is full of excitement.’ ‘What do you think of married life?’
[wrong sentence]:  He didn’t enjoy the life in the army at all.


 [explain]:  When you mean ‘the human race’, use man (WITHOUT the): ‘I can’t believe that man has actually stepped foot on the moon.’
[wrong sentence]:  Every day the man is inventing new machines.


 [explain]:  mankind (= the human race) WITHOUT the: ‘Travelling into space was a great advance for mankind.’
[wrong sentence]:  These bombs could lead to the destruction of the mankind.


 [explain]:  Nature (= the natural world of birds, trees, rivers etc) is never used with the: ‘We must stop destroying nature before it is too late.’
[wrong sentence]:  I have always found the nature fascinating.


 [explain]:  See Language Note at TIME
[wrong sentence]:  I have some spare time this week but the next week I’ll have to start work.


 [explain]:  in recent weeks/months/years: ‘Flight cancellations have increased in recent weeks.’
[wrong sentence]:  Tennis has become popular in Germany in the recent years.


 [explain]:  Use theta/my etc only when you are talking about a particular school: ‘She goes to a very good school.’ ‘Our two boys go to the same school.’ When you refer to school as a type of place or activity, use leave school, start school, go to school, etc (WITHOUT the/a/my etc): ‘Most children go to primary school at the age of five.’ The same rule applies to kindergarten, college, university, church, prison, jail and (in British
[wrong sentence]:  Her daughter wants to leave the school and get married.


 [explain]:  go/head/swim/drift etc out to sea (WITHOUT the): ‘Just ten minutes after getting on board, we were heading out to sea.’
[wrong sentence]:  Every day Kino went out to the sea to look for the pearl.


 [explain]:  When it means ‘the general system which helps people to live together in an organized way’, society is uncountable and is used without the : ‘People who drink and drive are a danger to society.’
[wrong sentence]:  I was asked to give a talk about women and their role in the society.


 [explain]:  When you mean ‘everything beyond the Earth’s atmosphere’, use space (WITHOUT the ): ‘He was the first German astronaut to travel in space.’
[wrong sentence]:  There are hundreds of millions of stars in the space.


 [explain]:  See Note at THE 4
[wrong sentence]:  One of the world’s greatest problems is the terrorism.


 [explain]:  When you say the date, use ‘March the twenty-fifth’ or ‘the twenty-fifth of March’.
[wrong sentence]:  She is arriving on March the 25th.


 [explain]:  speak/learn/know etc + name of a language (WITHOUT the): ‘She speaks fluent German.’ ‘Do you know any Malay?’ ‘I’d like to learn Mandarin.’ Note that the + English/Japanese etc + language may be used when you talk about a language in terms of its history, structure, users etc: ‘The English language has evolved over many centuries.’
[wrong sentence]:  Very few people can speak the English well in Japan.


 [explain]:  Do not use the with the plural form of a countable noun when it is used in a general sense. ; Compare: ‘She likes cats.’ (= cats in general) The cats we saw in Venice looked very hungry.’ (= a particular group of cats)
[wrong sentence]:  I have just seen a new magazine about the computers.


 [explain]:  Do not use the with an uncountable noun when it is used in a general sense: ‘She hates dishonesty.’ ‘Power doesn’t interest him.’
[wrong sentence]:  Nowadays the pollution is a very serious problem.
[wrong sentence]:  A lot of people are afraid of the death.
[wrong sentence]:  My main hobby is the photography.


 [explain]:  Do not use the before the names of airports and railway stations: ‘Charles de Gaulle (Airport)’, ‘Narita (Airport)’, ‘Charing Cross (Station)’
[wrong sentence]:  Our plane arrived at the Gatwick Airport.


 [explain]:  The is not usually used in the names of streets and roads: ‘Oxford Street’, ‘Fifth Avenue’, ‘Fir Tree Avenue’, ‘Blue Pool Road’. ; Note that when someone mentions ‘the Oxford road’ or ‘the London road’, they mean the road that leads to Oxford/London.
[wrong sentence]:  The language school is in the Malibu Street.


 [explain]:  Do not use the with the name of a mountain: ‘Mount Everest’, ‘Mount Fuji’, ‘Mount Olympus’. Note, however, that the is used with the names of groups of mountains: ‘the Alps’, ‘the Andes’, ‘the Himalayas’.
[wrong sentence]:  Climbing the Mount Fuji in winter can be very dangerous.

[wrong sentence]:  They were both found guilty and sent to the prison.


 [explain]:  London/Leeds University (WITMOUT the) the University of London/Leeds (WITH the)
[wrong sentence]:  I am studying sociology at the London University.


 [explain]:  Users of American English would say: ‘After graduating from high school, we all went to college.’
[wrong sentence]:  After leaving school, we all went to the university.


 [explain]:  See Note at THE 4
[wrong sentence]:  Some of these films are full of the violence.


U:DET my -> NONE
 [explain]:  When breakfast/lunch/dinner etc refer to an event (rather than to the meal itself), they are used without at/the/this/my etc: ‘What shall we do after lunch?’ ‘Pam and Simon have invited us to dinner.’ ‘What time do you have breakfast?’ You use at/he/this/my etc when you refer to the meal itself: ‘We enjoyed the evening, even though the dinner was cold.’ ‘I don’t usually have time for a cooked breakfast.’
[wrong sentence]:  Before my breakfast I usually go for a run.


 [explain]:  lose control (of sth), WITHOUT the, his, my, etc: ‘In 1993 Roseberg lost control of the company after a surprise takeover bid.’
[wrong sentence]:  I had lost my control and crashed into a tree.


 [explain]:  come/spring to mind (WITHOUT my, his, their, etc): ‘When I’m planning a camping holiday, the first thing that comes to mind is the weather.’ Having in mind that the roads will be busy, I think that we should make an early start. Bearing in mind that the roads will be busy, I think that we should make an early start. bear in mind (NOT have): ‘Something else you need to bear in mind is the fact that not all countries have public transport.’
[wrong sentence]:  The first thing that comes to my mind when I think about France is wine.


 [explain]:  See Language Note at OCCUPATION
[wrong sentence]:  I got up and got ready to go to my work.


U:DET his -> NONE
 [explain]:  (be/go somewhere) on business: ‘I’m off to Amsterdam tomorrow, on business as usual.’
[wrong sentence]:  My father is now in Paris on his business.


U:DET some -> NONE
 [explain]:  Do not use a determiner (e.g. some, the, their) before certain when it means ‘particular’ (see the note at certain 1 above): ‘Each member of the committee has certain duties to perform.’
[wrong sentence]:  Under some certain circumstances, such as war, food has to be rationed.


U:DET this -> NONE
 [explain]:  See Language Note at TIME Those
[wrong sentence]:  Will you be at home on this Sunday afternoon?


U:DET the education -> education
 [explain]:  See THE 4
[wrong sentence]:  The education has changed my country a lot.


U:DET the industry -> industry
 [explain]:  When industry means all industries seen together as one thing. it is an uncountable noun: ‘She can’t decide whether she wants a career in banking or in industry.’
[wrong sentence]:  The industry as a whole must become more environmentally friendly.


U:DET the last -> last
 [explain]:  See Language Note at TIME
[wrong sentence]:  The last Monday was a terrible day.


U:DET the life -> life
 [explain]:  When life means ‘the way of life that is connected with a particular type of situation, group or occupation’, it is usually used without the: ‘I found city life too stressful.’ ‘Life in New York is full of excitement.’ ‘What do you think of married life?’
[wrong sentence]:  The life in the countryside is quiet and relaxed.


U:DET a part -> part
 [explain]:  It is unusual to use a before part of unless part of has an adjective in front of it. Compare: ‘Lack of money was part of the problem.’ ‘Lack of money was a large part of the problem.’
[wrong sentence]:  A part of the difficulty was caused by her poor English.


R:PREP abandoned -> abolish
 [explain]:  abandon = give up a plan, activity or attempt to do something, without being successful: ‘Bad weather forced them to abandon the search.’ ‘Without government support, the project will have to be abandoned.’ abolish = remove a law, tradition or system, often by introducing a new law; do away with: ‘In which year was slavery abolished in the United States?’ ‘I’d hate to see the monarchy abolished.’
[wrong sentence]:  Since capital punishment was abandoned, the crime rate has increased.


R:PREP of -> in
 [explain]:  ability in a language or subject: ‘Sarah has demonstrated considerable ability in both maths and chemistry.’
[wrong sentence]:  I want to improve my ability of English.


 [explain]:  The phrase that follows the best (+ noun) usually begins with in (NOT of): ‘one of the best restaurants in London’, ‘the best team in the league’, ‘some of the best students in the class’.
[wrong sentence]:  It is one of the best theatres of Spain.
[wrong sentence]:  He is the best surgeon of the hospital.


 [explain]:  Use change of when you mean that someone or something has been replaced: ‘What the country needs is a change of government.’ (= a completely new government) When you mean that someone or something is now different in some way, use change in: ‘The
[wrong sentence]:  We can reduce the unemployment rate with a change of the economy.
[wrong sentence]:  I’m disappointed by all the changes of London.


 [explain]:  a course in/on sth (NOT of): I’ve been attending an evening course in a course in/on sth (NOT of): ‘I’ve been attending an evening course in business studies.’ ‘I’m interested in doing a correspondence course in English and Maths.’ ‘There is also a two-day course on new technology for teachers.’
[wrong sentence]:  I’m taking an intermediate course of English.


 [explain]:  a degree in a particular subject (NOT of): ‘a degree in law’.
[wrong sentence]:  He is studying for a degree of computer science.


 [explain]:  difference in a particular shared feature, practice or quality: ‘Since there was very little difference in price, we bought the large packet.’ ‘Differences in eating habits can cause considerable embarrassment.’
[wrong sentence]:  As soon as I arrived in the USA, I noticed a big difference of social behaviour.


 [explain]:  a fail in an amount, rate, level, standard etc: ‘Sharp falls in house prices have renewed the interest of the first-time buyer.’
[wrong sentence]:  This problem has been caused by a fall of the birth rate.


 [explain]:  the + noun + in a group: ‘The girls in my group all come from Italy.’
[wrong sentence]:  The people of each group have to guess the word.


 [explain]:  increase in sth (NOT of): ‘There’s been a sudden increase in political activity.’ ‘We can expect further increases in the cost of living.’
[wrong sentence]:  There has been an increase of the number of cars.


 [explain]:  To refer to the country/region/area etc where something is or takes place, use in (NOT of): ‘the longest river in Brazil’, ‘the second largest city in Spain’, ‘one of the most picturesque spots in the whole of Tuscany’
[wrong sentence]:  We stayed in one of the most beautiful villages of Kent.


 [explain]:  superlative + noun + in the world: ‘At that time China was the richest and most powerful country in the world.’
[wrong sentence]:  The sewing machine is one of the most widely used products of the world.


R:PREP among -> in
 [explain]:  Do not use among when the preceding verb/noun/adjective requires a different preposition: e.g. arouse anger in someone. Among these problems, the most serious is the excessive growth of the world’s population. The most serious of these problems is the excessive growth of the world’s population. When a phrase beginning with among comes at the start of a sentence, it is usually followed by the verb be (NOT by the subject of the sentence): ‘Among her reasons for resigning is the fact that she wants to move back to her home town.’ ‘Among those attending the ceremony were the Sultan of Pahang and his wife.’ Note also that this structure is not common and is used only in formal styles,
[wrong sentence]:  Try to avoid arousing anger and fear among the children.


R:PREP on -> in

R:PREP as -> like
 [explain]:  In comparisons, the word that is usually used in front of a noun or noun phrase is like (NOT as): ‘James is very tall, just like his father.’ ‘Their car is like ours - old and full of rust.’ As is used in the patterns (not) as ... as, not so ... as, and the same (...) as: ‘James is as tall as his father.’ ‘Their car is the same colour as ours.’ The book is concerned with important social issues as child abuse and women’s rights. The book is concerned with important social issues such as child abuse and women’s rights. The book is concerned with important social issues like child abuse and women’s rights. Examples of a class or category are introduced by such as or like (NOT as): ‘Serious diseases such as AIDS and cancer can cause a great deal of suffering.’
[wrong sentence]:  His skin is not as the skin of a young man.
[wrong sentence]:  It looked very fragile and so I handled it as china.

 [explain]:  When you are talking about direction, use towards (NOT to): ‘If you walk along the river bank towards Skipton, you come to a bridge.’ ‘Keep going towards Manchester until you see the sign for the airport.’
[wrong sentence]:  At that moment he noticed a man running to him.


R:PREP behind -> under
 [explain]:  behind = at the back of something: ‘Lizzie ran and hid behind a tree.’ under (or beneath/underneath) = directly below something, and sometimes touching or covered by it: ‘Come and stand under my umbrella.’ ‘I eventually found the letter under a pile of old newspapers.’
[wrong sentence]:  At night I keep my wallet behind my pillow.


R:PREP below -> under
 [explain]:  below =at or to a lower level than something: ‘My room is on the third floor, and John’s is on the floor below.’ ‘Our helicopter hovered just below the summit so that we could film the rescue.’ under (or beneath/underneath) = directly below something, and sometimes touching or covered by it: ‘Come and stand under my umbrella.’ ‘I eventually found the letter under a pile of old newspapers.’
[wrong sentence]:  Below the table there were some empty wine bottles.


R:PREP in -> under
 [explain]:  under guarantee (NOT in): ‘The watch was still under guarantee so they repaired it free of charge.’
[wrong sentence]:  May I remind you that the cassette recorder is still in guarantee.


R:PREP beside -> besides
 [explain]:  beside = next to: ‘She walked over and sat down beside me.’ besides = in addition to; also: ‘Who did you Invite besides Tom and Mary?’ ‘Besides tennis, hat other games do you play?’ ‘I’m too old to apply for the job. Besides, it would mean moving house.’
[wrong sentence]:  We need to discover another source of income, beside oil.

[wrong sentence]:  Except smoke and traffic fumes, there are several other types of pollution.

[wrong sentence]:  We went swimming in the river besides my house.


R:PREP between -> among
 [explain]:  Between is used when there are people or things on either side of someone or something: ‘The ball went straight between the goalkeeper’s legs.’ ‘The teacher walked up and down between the rows of desks.’ ‘Give me a number between 4 and 14.’ Among (or amongst) is used when (at least three) people or things are considered as a group or mass: ‘I eventually found the photograph among a pile of old letters.’
[wrong sentence]:  He wandered silently between the passengers on the boat.
[wrong sentence]:  Between all the magazines on the shelves, only one was of any interest.


R:PREP between -> from
 [explain]:  Use between ... and or from ... to (NOT between ... to).
[wrong sentence]:  Between June 1987 to March 1990 I was in France.


R:PREP by -> from
 [explain]:  You get information from a source: ‘You can learn a great deal from a good dictionary.’ ‘I obtained their new number from directory enquiries.’
[wrong sentence]:  We can gain a lot of knowledge by these books.

 [explain]:  choose between two (or a few) possibilities: ‘You have to choose between a beginner’s course and a more advanced course.’ ‘If I had to choose between staying here and living abroad, I’d stay here.’ choose from a large number of possibilities: ‘When it comes to wallpaper, there are hundreds of different patterns to choose from.’
[wrong sentence]:  There is also a library where you can choose among a wide range of books.

 [explain]:  from ... to ... : ‘from Monday to Wednesday’, ‘from 7 a.m. to 3 p.m.’, ‘from 1947 to 1966’
[wrong sentence]:  I stayed at your hotel for three nights, since 23rd November to 26th November.


R:PREP by -> near
 [explain]:  by = next to or very close to something: ‘I’ve been sitting by the phone all morning waiting for her to ring.’ ‘I’ll meet you on the corner, by the bank.’ When talking about the position of towns, cities, countries etc, use near: ‘I spent the first month in a little Village called Farchant, near GarmenPartenkirchen.’
[wrong sentence]:  I am staying in a small town by Oxford.

R:PREP by -> after
 [explain]:  day after day = repeatedly or continuously, especially in a boring or unpleasant way: ‘I get fed up with listening to their complaints day after day.’ ‘I’m not prepared to sit here day after day doing nothing.’ day by day = gradually: ‘Day by day the weather is getting warmer.’ ‘Their love grew day by day.’

 [explain]:  name a child after sb: ‘We’ve decided to name her Sarah, after her grandmother.’

R:PREP if -> whether
 [explain]:  it depends on whether (NOT if): ‘We don’t know yet. It all depends on whether the car is fixed in time.’
[wrong sentence]:  It depends on if you’ve got enough money.


R:PREP that -> whether
 [explain]:  When doubt is used to express certainty or near certainty, it is usually followed by a that-clause: There’s no doubt that he’s innocent.’ ‘I’ve no doubt that he’s innocent.’ ‘I’m in little doubt that he’s innocent.’ ‘I don’t doubt that he’s innocent.’ In this meaning, doubt is used with a negative word, e.g. not/no/little/not much. When doubt means ‘think that something is unlikely’, it is usually followed by if/whether: ‘I doubt whether he’s innocent.’ (= I think that he is probably guilty) ‘She doubts whether she’ll be able to come on Sunday.’ Note that some people may also say ‘I doubt that he’s innocent’, but careful users regard this as incorrect.
[wrong sentence]:  I doubt that she is telling the truth.


R:PREP despite -> although
 [explain]:  Despite and in spite of are prepositions (NOT conjunctions). Unlike although (a conjunction), they cannot introduce a clause that has a finite verb (‘was’). Compare: ‘In spite of/Despite owning two cars, he can’t drive.’ ‘Although he owns two cars, he can’t drive.’
[wrong sentence]:  Despite the train was empty, he came and sat in front of me.

 [explain]:  discrimination against a group of people (NOT on/about/for etc): ‘Feminist groups see this practice as a form of discrimination against women.’
[wrong sentence]:  It is time that sexual discrimination on women was eliminated.


R:PREP during -> while
 [explain]:  See Note at WHILE 1
[wrong sentence]:  This was my biggest problem during I was living in the United States.
[wrong sentence]:  During waiting for the train, I met an old friend of mine.


R:PREP except -> unless
 [explain]:  Except (a preposition) cannot be followed by a clause that has a finite verb (‘came’). Use unless (a conjunction) or except when, except while, except if, except that: ‘Americans are just like any other people in the world, except that they are more tolerant.’ ‘He tends to ignore me except when he needs something.’
[wrong sentence]:  The old lady never spoke to anyone except someone came to visit her.


R:PREP for -> over
 [explain]:  Use for to say ‘how long’: ‘He was with the company for forty years.’ ‘He hasn’t eaten anything for the last two days.’ Use over/during/in to say ‘when’, especially when this is a long period of time: ‘She’s been a great help to me in recent months.’ ‘During the next ten years he worked his way up from office boy to general manager.’
[wrong sentence]:  Unemployment has become a serious problem for the last few years.


R:PREP for -> because
 [explain]:  Do not use for to mean ‘because’. This usage has almost disappeared and is found only in very formal styles.
[wrong sentence]:  I do badly in tests for I am lazy.


R:PREP into -> upon
 [explain]:  intrude (on/upon sb/sth): ‘I’m not intruding, am I?’ ‘Governments should not intrude on the rights of the individual.’
[wrong sentence]:  Reporters have no right to intrude into people’s private lives.


R:PREP like -> as if
 [explain]:  The use of like to mean ‘as if’ is very informal and is usually regarded as non-standard.
[wrong sentence]:  She spoke very loudly like I was deaf.


R:PREP because -> that
 [explain]:  the/sb’s reason ... is that (NOT is because): ‘Her reason for going all the way to the Bahamas is that she wants some winter sunshine.’
[wrong sentence]:  My reason for not buying the car was because it was too expensive.


R:PREP so -> that
 [explain]:  so + adjective/adverb + that clause: ‘She was so clever that all the universities wanted her.’ ‘The pianist played so badly that the audience walked out.’
[wrong sentence]:  My English is so poor so my wife has to translate everything.


R:PREP with -> as
 [explain]:  the same ... as (NOT with/like/than): ‘She goes to the same school as my sister.’ ‘Women have the same abilities
[wrong sentence]:  Paris has the same level of pollution with Los Angeles.


R:PREP like -> as
 [explain]:  the same ... as (NOT with/like/than): ‘She goes to the same school as my sister.’ ‘Women have the same abilities
[wrong sentence]:  The belt is made of the same material like the coat.


R:PREP than -> as
 [explain]:  the same ... as (NOT with/like/than): ‘She goes to the same school as my sister.’ ‘Women have the same abilities
[wrong sentence]:  There were thousands of girls in the same situation than me.


R:PREP that -> as
 [explain]:  When making a comparison, use as/so ... as (NOT as/so ... that): ‘It’s as hard to get into university today as it was ten years ago.’
[wrong sentence]:  Children are not as easy to please nowadays that they were in the past.


R:PREP under -> below
 [explain]:  See Note at BELOW 1
[wrong sentence]:  Prices are not allowed to fall under this level.


R:PREP until -> up to
 [explain]:  up to a particular age (NOT until): The competition is open to all children up to the age of eleven.’
[wrong sentence]:  The school caters for children until the age of eleven.


R:PREP usually -> usual
 [explain]:  as usual (NOT as usually): ‘John’s late as usual.’ ‘As usual, everyone was out in the garden when I arrived.’ ; the same as usual (NOT ... as usually): ‘Apart from his hair, he looked the same as usual.’
[wrong sentence]:  As usually, he arrived five minutes late.


R:PREP while -> during
 [explain]:  While (conjunction) introduces a clause: ‘While we were on holiday, we did a lot of walking.’ During (preposition) introduces a noun phrase: ‘During the holiday we did a lot of walking.’
[wrong sentence]:  The Japanese have a tendency to keep silent while meetings.


R:OTHER abandoned -> remote
 [explain]:  abandoned = left for ever by the owners or occupiers: ‘The field was littered with abandoned cars.’ ‘Some of these old abandoned coal mines could be dangerous.’ remote = far away and difficult to reach: ‘The tribe lives in a small remote mountain village.’ ‘We have now managed to bring famine relief to people in even the most remote areas.’
[wrong sentence]:  It is difficult to reach abandoned places such as small country villages.


R:OTHER are not able to -> can’t
 [explain]:  For actions that are controlled by laws or rules, use can, can’t, etc, or be (not) allowed to: ‘Now that they are both sixteen, they can get married.’ ‘The goalkeeper can touch the ball, but nobody else can.’
[wrong sentence]:  In some countries you are not able to drink until you are 21.


R:OTHER about -> with regard to
 [explain]:  About may be used to introduce a topic (or change of topic), but only in informal styles: ‘About that book you asked for - I’ll get you a copy when I go to London.’ In other styles, use with regard to, regarding, as for, as regards or as far as ... is concerned: ‘With regard to the matter of unemployment, I would like to add a few remarks to those of the previous speaker.’ ‘I regret to Information that Language and Culture is out of print. As regards your second question, I suggest that you write to the British Museum.’
[wrong sentence]:  I am always delighted when I receive a letter from you. About the party on December 26th, I shall be very pleased to attend.


R:OTHER concerning -> with regard to
 [explain]:  To introduce a new topic, use with regard to, regarding, as regards, as far as ... is concerned (NOT concerning): ‘As far as food is concerned, the college has its own canteen.’ Compare: ‘He was then asked several questions concerning his banking activities.’ ‘We got into an interesting discussion concerning the need for censorship.’
[wrong sentence]:  Concerning your accommodation, there are several possibilities.


R:OTHER about -> as for
 [explain]:  About may be used to introduce a topic (or change of topic), but only in informal styles: ‘About that book you asked for - I’ll get you a copy when I go to London.’ In other styles, use with regard to, regarding, as for, as regards or as far as ... is concerned: ‘With regard to the matter of unemployment, I would like to add a few remarks to those of the previous speaker.’ ‘I regret to Information that Language and Culture is out of print. As regards your second question, I suggest that you write to the British Museum.’
[wrong sentence]:  People usually sleep with the doors and windows closed. About public transport, the bus and train services are excellent.


R:OTHER above -> over
 [explain]:  Do not use above with numbers (unless referring to points on a scale): ‘He is over eighty years of age.’ ‘I receive over twenty letters a day.’ Compare ‘Don’t let the temperature get above thirty degrees.’
[wrong sentence]:  There were above a hundred people in the crowd.


R:OTHER the above -> my/this
 [explain]:  Above is used in formal writing to refer to something that has been mentioned earlier: ‘From the above arguments It can be seen that supporters of the dam project fall into two camps.’ In informal styles, this use of above is inappropriate. Taking all the above into account, one could say that tourism does more harm than good. Taking all the above arguments into account, one could say that tourism does more harm than good. Instead of using the above as a loose reference to something mentioned earlier, make the reference more precise by using the above + noun (or the + noun + above): ‘the above reasons’, ‘the statement above’.
[wrong sentence]:  What do you think of the above suggestion?


R:OTHER were absent from -> miss
 [explain]:  be absent = not be present at something that you are officially supposed to attend: ‘Her teacher wanted to know why she’d been absent.’ miss = not be present at something: ‘She’s missed a lot of classes this term because of illness.’ ‘I wouldn’t miss Sandro’s party for the world!’ (= I really want to go to it). be at = be present at something: ‘John won’t be at the meeting. He’s had to fly to Romeon business.’
[wrong sentence]:  It’s a pity that you were absent from the training session.


R:OTHER lost -> miss
 [explain]:  If something is not in the place where you left it and you cannot find it anywhere, it is missing (NOT lost): ‘Police are still searching for the missing diamonds.’ ‘She was making her way towards immigration when she discovered that her passport was missing.’
[wrong sentence]:  This morning I noticed that my purse was lost.


R:OTHER were absent from -> weren’t at
 [explain]:  be absent = not be present at something that you are officially supposed to attend: ‘Her teacher wanted to know why she’d been absent.’ miss = not be present at something: ‘She’s missed a lot of classes this term because of illness.’ ‘I wouldn’t miss Sandro’s party for the world!’ (= I really want to go to it). be at = be present at something: ‘John won’t be at the meeting. He’s had to fly to Romeon business.’
[wrong sentence]:  It’s a pity that you were absent from the training session.


R:OTHER actually -> in fact
 [explain]:  When actually means ‘in fact’, it is usually used to correct a misunderstanding: ‘People think we’ve got lots of money, but actually we’re quite poor.’ ‘I’m sorry to have kept you waiting.’ ‘Well actually I’ve only just arrived.’ ; When you simply want to develop a previous statement, use in fact or as a matter of fact: ‘The winter of 1940 was extremely bad. In fact most people say it was the worst winter in living memory.’ ‘The company is doing very well. As a matter of fact, our sales have doubled.’ See also ACTUAL
[wrong sentence]:  I never get bored by this city. Actually, each time I return I find something new to interest me.


R:OTHER actually -> at present
 [explain]:  When you mean ‘at the present time’, use at present, at the moment or currently (NOT actually) : ‘At present the company is very short of staff.’ ‘At the moment I’m working part-time in a travel agency.’
[wrong sentence]:  We need to produce and export more than we do actually.


R:OTHER admiring -> looking at
 [explain]:  Do not use admire when you just mean ‘see’ or ‘look at’. Admire means ‘look at someone or something with a strong feeling of pleasure’. This meaning of admire is found mainly in novels and tourist brochures: ‘Come and admire the magnificence of the Niagara Falls.’ ‘Rupert was sitting outside on the verandah, admiring the many jewels in the night sky.’
[wrong sentence]:  I enjoyed admiring all the old buildings.


R:OTHER of -> to/in
 [explain]:  the advantage/s of (doing/having) sth: ‘He described the advantages of renewing the contract.’ ‘The advantages of independence soon became clear.’ there are (several/many etc) advantages to/in (doing/having) sth: ‘There are advantages to working in a supermarket.’ ‘There are clear advantages to such an approach.’ ‘Are there any advantages in appointing coordinators?’
[wrong sentence]:  There are many advantages of having your own computer.


R:OTHER affect -> effect
 [explain]:  To affect something (verb) is to have an effect on it (noun): ‘Smoking affects your health.’ (= smoking has an effect on your health)
[wrong sentence]:  The programme is about computers and their affect on our lives.


R:OTHER after -> later
 [explain]:  When you mention a time in the past that is measured from an earlier time in the past, use a month later, three months later, etc: ‘Six months later they got married.’
[wrong sentence]:  I promised to meet Hitomi at the exhibition a week after.


R:OTHER after -> afterwards
 [explain]:  After is used instead of afterwards only in informal styles, usually in phrases such as ‘soon after’, ‘not long after’ or ‘just after’. Careful users generally prefer afterwards, especially at the beginning of a sentence: ‘Shortly afterwards it was announced that the bank had collapsed.’ In American English after is often used instead of afterwards.
[wrong sentence]:  My first aim is to get a master’s degree. After I would like to go and work in Canada.
[wrong sentence]:  A police car arrived within minutes and soon after an ambulance came.


 [explain]:  In informal styles, after is used in phrases such as ‘soon after’, ‘not long after’ and ‘just after’. Careful users prefer afterwards, especially in formal styles: ‘Shortly afterwards it was announced that the bank had collapsed.’
[wrong sentence]:  A police car arrived within minutes and soon after an ambulance came.


R:OTHER i’ve -> i
 [explain]:  With references to past time such as yesterday, last week, a year ago, use a past tense (NOT the present perfect): ‘I came to England exactly six months ago.’ (NOT ‘have come’)
[wrong sentence]:  I’m writing in reply to your letter that I’ve received two days ago.


R:OTHER i’ll -> i
 [explain]:  See Language Note at WILL
[wrong sentence]:  When I’ll return home, I’ll look for a better job.


R:OTHER ago -> before
 [explain]:  See Note at BEFORE 1
[wrong sentence]:  The train left at exactly 3 o’clock. Just five minutes ago I had been stuck in a traffic jam.


R:OTHER alarmed -> alert
 [explain]:  alarm = make someone feel worried about a possible danger: ‘We don’t wish to alarm people unnecessarily, but it would be wise to avoid drinking the tap water here.’ alert = inform someone of possible danger so that they can be ready to deal with it: ‘When the pilot realized that one engine had failed, he alerted air traffic control.’
[wrong sentence]:  Finally we got really worried and alarmed the local police.


R:OTHER alive -> live
 [explain]:  alive = not dead: ‘He was very seriously ill and is lucky to be alive.’ living = (1) alive now: ‘He has no living relatives.’ (2) used to refer to all creatures and things that live and die: ‘Yeast is a living organism and too much heat or cold can kill it.’ ‘The ants will eat any living thing that comes into their path.’
[wrong sentence]:  Every alive creature in the sea is affected by pollution.


R:OTHER being alive -> live
 [explain]:  When you mean ‘continue to be alive’, use the verb live: ‘Her grandmother lived to a great age.’ ‘The baby was four months premature and was not expected to live.’
[wrong sentence]:  My reason for being alive had disappeared.


R:OTHER life -> live
 [explain]:  live = heard or seen while it is actually being played or performed: ‘Tonight’s live concert comes from the Royal Opera House, Covent Garden.’
[wrong sentence]:  On Saturday nights there is usually life music at the club.


R:OTHER all -> everything
 [explain]:  Do not use all to mean ‘everything’ unless it is immediately followed by a relative clause: ‘Is that all she wanted to know?’
[wrong sentence]:  People envy her because she is good at all.
[wrong sentence]:  Nobody understands all.


R:OTHER alone -> on their own
 [explain]:  on your own = without anyone’s help or supervision; independently: ‘He built the car all on his own.’
[wrong sentence]:  Children learn a lot by doing things alone.


R:OTHER i -> i’ve
 [explain]:  Already is usually used with the present perfect tense (NOT the past tense): ‘If he’s already seen the film, he won’t want to see it again.’
[wrong sentence]:  Next July I’m going back to Hong Kong. I booked the tickets already.


R:OTHER already -> yet
 [explain]:  Instead of using not + already, use not + yet: ‘We haven’t been paid yet.’ ‘The plane hasn’t landed yet.’
[wrong sentence]:  The new shop has not been opened already.
[wrong sentence]:  The war is not over already.


R:OTHER also -> either
 [explain]:  See Note at TOO 2
[wrong sentence]:  I don’t like your climate and I don’t like English food also.


R:OTHER too -> either
 [explain]:  In a negative clause use either (NOT too/also/as well): ‘The cheese cake was disappointing and the coffee wasn’t very special either.’ Compare: ‘The food was excellent and the service was good too.’
[wrong sentence]:  For some reason they didn’t receive the fax and your letter hasn’t arrived too.


R:OTHER anger -> angry
 [explain]:  Anger is a noun and a verb: ‘The workers who lost their jobs expressed anger and resentment.’ ‘The government’s handling of the affair has angered local residents.’ The adjective is angry: ‘Some of the women felt angry about the way they were treated.’
[wrong sentence]:  I was surprised and anger that he did not apologize.


R:OTHER another -> other
 [explain]:  another + singular: ‘another child’ other + plural: ‘other children’
[wrong sentence]:  Rio has another important sights such as the famous football stadium.


R:OTHER else -> other
 [explain]:  other than = apart from; except for (NOT else than): ‘The two of us had nothing that was worth stealing other than my watch.’
[wrong sentence]:  She said that she hadn’t told anybody else than her husband.


R:OTHER another -> some more
 [explain]:  another + singular countable noun: ‘We need another chair.’ (some) more + uncountable noun: ‘We need (some) more furniture.’
[wrong sentence]:  He gave the class another homework.


R:OTHER another -> something else
 [explain]:  When you mean ‘a different person/thing/place’, use someone else, something else, somewhere else: ‘I got fed up with the job and decided to do something else.’
[wrong sentence]:  I hate horror films. Let’s watch another.


R:OTHER another man -> someone else
 [explain]:  When you mean ‘a different person/thing/place’, use someone else, something else, somewhere else: ‘I got fed up with the job and decided to do something else.’
[wrong sentence]:  If Henry is busy, get another man to help you.


R:OTHER another’s -> someone else’s
 [explain]:  When you mean ‘a different person/thing/place’, use someone else, something else, somewhere else: ‘I got fed up with the job and decided to do something else.’
[wrong sentence]:  Can’t you use another’s computer?


R:OTHER a -> one
 [explain]:  from one ... to another (NOT from a/an ... to another): ‘The job involves travelling from one country to another.’
[wrong sentence]:  Without a car, it takes a long time to get from a place to another.


R:OTHER he -> one
 [explain]:  In British English (unlike American English) it is not possible to change from one to he/his/her etc. Note, however, that most speakers find the repetition of one awkward and try to avoid it: ‘One cannot succeed without working hard.’ ‘Success calls for a lot of hard work.’ See also Language Note at HE
[wrong sentence]:  One cannot succeed unless he works hard.


R:OTHER anxious -> worry
 [explain]:  worried = unhappy because you have (or expect that you are going to have) a problem: ‘The business is losing money and the boss is very worried.’
[wrong sentence]:  She is anxious that the hotel rates will be too expensive.


R:OTHER anybody -> any
 [explain]:  Before an of phrase, use any (NOT anybody/anyone).
[wrong sentence]:  Please ask your staff if anybody of them has seen my purse.


R:OTHER anyone -> any
 [explain]:  Before an of phrase, use any (NOT anybody/anyone).
[wrong sentence]:  He doesn’t know anyone of them, but he doesn’t care.


R:OTHER anyway -> on the other hand
 [explain]:  Anyhow and anyway are used to connect sentences only in informal styles (NOT in essays, written reports, etc). Their main uses are: (1) to show that you are about to return to the main topic or story line: ‘Anyway, as soon as the plane landed he was rushed off to hospital and that was the last I saw of him.’ (2) to show that your next point is just as important or relevant as your last one; ‘In any case, I’m too busy to play tennis this afternoon. Anyhow, it looks like it’s going to rain.’ For reasons of style and logic, anyhow and anyway cannot be used in place of however, on the other hand or nevertheless.
[wrong sentence]:  Unfortunately, the beach is not white and sandy. Anyway, it is seldom crowded and we can enjoy the sea.


R:OTHER anyway -> nevertheless
 [explain]:  Anyhow and anyway are used to connect sentences only in informal styles (NOT in essays, written reports, etc). Their main uses are: (1) to show that you are about to return to the main topic or story line: ‘Anyway, as soon as the plane landed he was rushed off to hospital and that was the last I saw of him.’ (2) to show that your next point is just as important or relevant as your last one; ‘In any case, I’m too busy to play tennis this afternoon. Anyhow, it looks like it’s going to rain.’ For reasons of style and logic, anyhow and anyway cannot be used in place of however, on the other hand or nevertheless.
[wrong sentence]:  I’m sorry that I won’t be able to attend your wedding. Anyway, I would like to give you something for your new home.


R:OTHER anywhere -> everywhere
 [explain]:  When you mean ‘in/to all places’, use everywhere (NOT anywhere).
[wrong sentence]:  This is a serious problem for teachers anywhere.


R:OTHER that i can’t -> for not being able to
 [explain]:  apologize (to sb) for (doing) sth: ‘I’d like to apologize for causing you so much trouble.’
[wrong sentence]:  I apologize that I can’t come to the wedding.


R:OTHER appreciate -> like
 [explain]:  appreciate = like or enjoy something because you have studied it and understand the value of it: ‘I’ve never appreciated modern art.’ ‘It was during my years in Paris that I learned to appreciate good wine.’
[wrong sentence]:  I don’t appreciate magazines or newspapers that have been censored.


R:OTHER to be -> like
 [explain]:  When you are making a comparison, use seem like: ‘Suddenly she seemed like a complete stranger.’ ‘The last few days had seemed like a dream.’
[wrong sentence]:  This kind of diet seems to be a punishment.


R:OTHER approximately -> around
 [explain]:  Approximately is used mainly in formal written styles: ‘It is estimated that, during the period in question, approximately 47,000 cars were stolen in the London area alone.’
[wrong sentence]:  The party should end at approximately midnight.


R:OTHER argue -> have a discussion
 [explain]:  When people argue (or quarrel), they disagree strongly about something and are often angry or upset: ‘Parents should try not to argue in front of their children.’ ‘We were always arguing about silly things, like whose turn it was to do the shopping.’ When people consider a subject from several points of view, they have a discussion or have a debate (= a formal discussion in which two or more groups take it in turns to present their opinions).
[wrong sentence]:  In the first class after lunch we usually argue.


R:OTHER arithmetics -> arithmetic
 [explain]:  mathematics (WITH s) but arithmetic (WITHOUT s): ‘Arithmetic isn’t difficult if it’s taught properly.’ See also MATHEMATICS
[wrong sentence]:  She comes to see me three times a week for help with her arithmetics.


R:OTHER won’t -> don’t
 [explain]:  See Language Note at WILL as soon as
[wrong sentence]:  I’ll go with you as long as I won’t have to sing.


R:OTHER aspect -> point of view
 [explain]:  aspect = one of the parts or features of a situation, idea, problem, etc; ‘Modern technology affects all aspects of our daily lives: ‘The book concentrates on the cruel aspects of war.’ point of view = a particular way of looking at a situation, event, problem, etc: ‘From a vegetarian’s point of view, these new controls on meat preparation are of little interest: ‘What does the process of getting old mean from a medical point of view?’ ‘I wish you’d try and see things from my point of view for a change.’ Another function of newspapers is to shape public opinion and in this aspect the
[wrong sentence]:  From a biological aspect, the two plants are very similar.


R:OTHER assassins -> murderers
 [explain]:  See Language Note at KILL
[wrong sentence]:  The prison is full of crooks, thieves and assassins.


R:OTHER assist to -> attend
 [explain]:  assist = (formal) help: ‘Should you have difficulty in finding a book, the library staff will be pleased to assist you.’ attend = (formal) (1) be present at a meeting or event; go to: ‘He’s had to cancel the trip in order to attend his mother’s funeral.’ (2) be a member of something that has regular meetings, such as a school or class; go to: ‘The couples who attended the prenatal classes found childbirth much easier, especially the wives.’ See also ATIEND 1
[wrong sentence]:  All members are required to assist to the meeting.


R:OTHER to -> in their
 [explain]:  assist (sb) in/with sth: ‘A Swiss rescue team is being flown in to assist in the search for survivors.’ ‘I’ve been asked to assist with the wedding arrangements.’ assist (sb) in doing sth (NOT to do): ‘Our main job is to assist foreign governments in developing their economies.’
[wrong sentence]:  One of the prison guards assisted them to escape.


R:OTHER a bad thing -> unfortunate
 [explain]:  The phrase bad thing/s is generally over-used and often sounds unnatural. See also THING
[wrong sentence]:  It’s a bad thing that the population is increasing so quickly.


R:OTHER battle -> fight
 [explain]:  battle = struggle to do or get something: ‘Doctors are still battling to save the child’s life.’ fight = use violence to hurt or kill people: ‘My grandfather fought in the Spanish Civil War.’
[wrong sentence]:  Many young Americans refused to battle in Vietnam.


R:OTHER bear -> have
 [explain]:  Bear (sb) a child is used only in formal styles and refers to the physical process of giving birth: ‘She bore her husband two daughters and one son.’ The subject of bear is always a woman. When you are talking about beginning a family or the number of children in a family, use have (NOT bear): ‘We’d like 10 have children while we’re still young.’
[wrong sentence]:  I want to get married and bear children.


R:OTHER beautiful -> interesting/newsy
 [explain]:  See Language Note at KILL
[wrong sentence]:  Thank you for your beautiful letter.


R:OTHER left my bed -> got up
 [explain]:  get up = get out of bed and start your day: ‘I’ve got to get up early in the morning to catch the
[wrong sentence]:  I left my bed at 8 a.m. and had a shower.


R:OTHER before -> ago
 [explain]:  ago = before ‘now’, the moment of speaking: ‘Her plane landed ten minutes ago. In few, here she comes now.’ ‘I saw him just five minutes ago." (=five minutes before now) before = before then, a time in the past: ‘l went to the airport last Monday to meet Sue, I hadn’t been to the airport before.’ (= before last Monday). ‘I saw him last Friday in London and two days before in Leeds.’
[wrong sentence]:  Not so long before we used animals for transportation.


R:OTHER besides -> apart from
 [explain]:  When you want to say that someone or something is not included in your main statement, use apart from (NOT beside/s): ‘Apart from Peter, everyone had a good time.’
[wrong sentence]:  Besides the goalkeeper, who was a disaster, the team played very well.


R:OTHER you -> you’d
 [explain]:  had better (not): ‘If the phone rings again, you’d better answer it.’ ‘If it’s a secret, you’d better not tell me.’
[wrong sentence]:  You better make sure you’re not late again.


R:OTHER had better -> should
 [explain]:  Had better is used in informal styles when you give someone strong advice about what to do in a particular situation. The situation usually exists at the moment of speaking and so there is usually a sense of urgency in the advice: ‘You’d better hurry or you’ll miss the bus.’ ‘You’d better ring your parents -just in case they’re worrying about you.’ To give advice on a general situation or to say that one course of action is better than another one, use should, ought to or it would be better to: ‘Parents should teach their children to be kind to animals.’ ‘Rather than complain and risk upsetting her, it would be better to say nothing.’
[wrong sentence]:  If people want to be healthy, they had better be more careful about what they eat.


R:OTHER to -> and
 [explain]:  Use between ... and or from ... to (NOT between ... to).
[wrong sentence]:  It will take them between six to eight weeks to finish it.
[wrong sentence]:  Children between three to five go to kindergarten.


R:OTHER riding a bicycle -> cycle
 [explain]:  cycling = riding a bicycle as a sport or leisure activity: ‘In the summer I do a lot of cycling.’ Compare: ‘He learned to ride a bicycle at the age of four
[wrong sentence]:  I enjoy riding a bicycle and playing badminton.


R:OTHER ride our bicycles -> go cycling
 [explain]:  cycling = riding a bicycle as a sport or leisure activity: ‘In the summer I do a lot of cycling.’ Compare: ‘He learned to ride a bicycle at the age of four
[wrong sentence]:  In the evenings we usually ride our bicycles.


R:OTHER a little bit -> slightly
 [explain]:  A bit and a little bit are used mainly in informal styles. In other styles, it is better to use a little, slightly, rather, quite or somewhat: ‘His brother was somewhat older than we had expected.’
[wrong sentence]:  My diet is a little bit different nowadays.


 [explain]:  Use a bit/little too or slightly/rather/somewhat too (NOT a little bit too): ‘She is still a bit too young.’ ‘The pears were a little too hard.’ ‘The basic salary is rather too low.’
[wrong sentence]:  He is a little bit too short to be a policeman.
[wrong sentence]:  The speech was a little bit too formal.


R:OTHER bit -> little
 [explain]:  A bit and a little bit are used mainly in informal styles. In other styles, it is better to use a little, slightly, rather, quite or somewhat: ‘His brother was somewhat older than we had expected.’
[wrong sentence]:  Thank you very much for giving us a bit of your free time.


R:OTHER little bit -> bit/little
 [explain]:  Use a bit/little too or slightly/rather/somewhat too (NOT a little bit too): ‘She is still a bit too young.’ ‘The pears were a little too hard.’ ‘The basic salary is rather too low.’
[wrong sentence]:  He is a little bit too short to be a policeman.
[wrong sentence]:  The speech was a little bit too formal.


R:OTHER a -> (my
 [explain]:  have (your) breakfast/lunch/dinner etc (NOT a): ‘Have you had (your) breakfast yet?’ make/prepare (the/your) breakfast/lunch/dinner etc (NOT a): ‘Whose turn is it to make the dinner tonight?’
[wrong sentence]:  Before I have a breakfast, I usually make my bed.


R:OTHER a -> (the
 [explain]:  have (your) breakfast/lunch/dinner etc (NOT a): ‘Have you had (your) breakfast yet?’ make/prepare (the/your) breakfast/lunch/dinner etc (NOT a): ‘Whose turn is it to make the dinner tonight?’
[wrong sentence]:  Every morning my first job is to prepare a breakfast.


 [explain]:  flu or the flu (NOT a flu): ‘They are both off work with flu.’ ‘Even our doctor is down with the flu.’
[wrong sentence]:  I think I have caught a flu.


R:OTHER breath -> breathe
 [explain]:  Breath is a noun. Breathe is a verb.’ The verb is longer than the noun both in its spelling and in its vowel sound.
[wrong sentence]:  She had a very bad cold and couldn’t breath properly.


R:OTHER broken -> not working
 [explain]:  broken = physically damaged: ‘a broken windscreen’ not working = not functioning properly: ‘I can’t ring him because his phone’s not working.’
[wrong sentence]:  The car could not stop because its brakes were broken.


R:OTHER phone call -> call/ring
 [explain]:  The verb is call/ring/phone/telephone (NOT phone call).
[wrong sentence]:  If you receive this note, please phone call me.


R:OTHER phone call me -> give me a call/ring
 [explain]:  The verb is call/ring/phone/telephone (NOT phone call).
[wrong sentence]:  If you receive this note, please phone call me.


R:OTHER with number -> on
 [explain]:  call sb on a particular number (British English) call sb at a particular number (American English)
[wrong sentence]:  Please call me with number 0248 312689.


R:OTHER when -> on
 [explain]:  To show that two things happen at the same time or that one thing happens immediately after the other, use on/upon doing sth (NOT when): ‘On examining the suitcase, he noticed that the locks had been tampered with.’
[wrong sentence]:  When hearing that the child had been found, she burst into tears.


R:OTHER calm -> quiet
 [explain]:  Calm is usually used to describe situations where there has recently been violence or noisy activity: ‘After yesterday’s fighting, the streets of Jerusalem are reported to be calm again this morning.’
[wrong sentence]:  Go and find somewhere calm and get some rest.


R:OTHER calm -> quiet and relaxing
 [explain]:  Calm is usually used to describe situations where there has recently been violence or noisy activity: ‘After yesterday’s fighting, the streets of Jerusalem are reported to be calm again this morning.’
[wrong sentence]:  What you need is a calm holiday somewhere.


R:OTHER calm -> peace
 [explain]:  Peace and quiet is a fixed phrase: ‘It’s impossible to find peace and quiet in a house full of children.’
[wrong sentence]:  You need calm and quiet to digest your lunch properly.


R:OTHER can -> will be able to
 [explain]:  Use can to talk about someone’s present ability: ‘All our children can swim.’
[wrong sentence]:  The next generation can fly to the moon for their holidays.


R:OTHER in case -> if
 [explain]:  In British English in case is used only when you talk about something that is done as a precaution: ‘Let’s wait for another five minutes, just in case he shows up.’
[wrong sentence]:  In case a woman goes out to work, she shouldn’t have to do all the housework.


R:OTHER cause -> reason
 [explain]:  cause = an action, event, situation etc that makes something happen: The cause of the fire is still being investigated.’ These outbreaks of violence will continue to occur until the causes have been eliminated.’ reason = something that provides an explanation: ‘I’m sure that they must have good reasons for wanting to live abroad.’ ‘The reason why there is only one applicant is that the job wasn’t advertised.’
[wrong sentence]:  For this cause the journey took a long time.


R:OTHER most important -> major
 [explain]:  a major/chief/primary cause (NOT important)
[wrong sentence]:  Smoking is one of the most important causes of lung cancer.


R:OTHER a certain -> some
 [explain]:  Use certain + noun when you continue (or could continue) by giving details: ‘There are certain advantages to living in the countryside, the most important being the fresh air.’ ‘I’m not allowed to eat certain types of seafood, especially squid and octopus.’ Use some + noun (+ or other) when you cannot or do not wish to give details: ‘In the end, he sold it to some second-hand car dealer.’ ‘If the factory is shut down for some reason, what will happen to all the workers?’ The phrase some + noun + or other is often used in informal styles to suggest that the person or thing is completely unknown to you and not worth thinking about: ‘Apparently, their daughter has got engaged to some shop assistant or other.’
[wrong sentence]:  Every creature must have a certain way of protecting itself.
[wrong sentence]:  Just suppose, for a certain reason, that there was suddenly a shortage of oil.


R:OTHER cheap  -> reasonable
 [explain]:  See Language Note at KILL
[wrong sentence]:  The train fare is very cheap .


R:OTHER between -> from
 [explain]:  choose between two (or a few) possibilities: ‘You have to choose between a beginner’s course and a more advanced course.’ ‘If I had to choose between staying here and living abroad, I’d stay here.’ choose from a large number of possibilities: ‘When it comes to wallpaper, there are hundreds of different patterns to choose from.’
[wrong sentence]:  There are over forty different courses to choose between.


R:OTHER comprehensive -> understand
 [explain]:  comprehensive = including everything or almost everything: ‘The witness provided a comprehensive account of the accident.’ understanding = feeling sympathy for someone: ‘As people grow older, they tend to be a bit more understanding and easier to live with.’
[wrong sentence]:  The teachers are very kind and comprehensive.


R:OTHER to come to the conclusion -> to conclude
 [explain]:  To introduce a concluding statement, use in conclusion; ‘by way of conclusion, or to conclude: ‘By way of conclusion, I’d just like to add that the answers to the questions I have raised would still appear to be a long way off .’
[wrong sentence]:  To come to the conclusion, I would like to say that everyone should read the book.


R:OTHER confident of myself -> self-confident
 [explain]:  If you have confidence in your own abilities, you feel self-confident: ‘Even as a child he was surprisingly self-confident and didn’t mind being left with strangers.’
[wrong sentence]:  I began to feel more confident of myself.


R:OTHER contrary -> other hand
 [explain]:  Use on the contrary to show that you strongly disagree with a previous statement and believe that the opposite is true: ‘Your parents didn’t want you to go abroad, did they?’ ‘On the contrary, they were all for it.’ To introduce a statement that is in sharp contrast with a previous statement, use on the other hand: ‘These new XJlOO computers are amazingly fast. On the other hand, they’re very expensive.’
[wrong sentence]:  I’ve never been able to cook. My sister on the contrary can bake wonderful biscuits and cakes.


R:OTHER lost -> gone out of
 [explain]:  People lose control of vehicles, machines etc: ‘The pilot lost control of the aircraft and it plummeted to the ground.’ Vehicles and machines are/go out of control: ‘The aircraft went out of control and plummeted to the ground.’
[wrong sentence]:  My boat had lost control.


R:OTHER criminal -> crime
 [explain]:  ‘criminal activity’, ‘a criminal offence’, ‘a criminal record’, ‘criminal law’, BUT ‘crime prevention’, ‘the crime rate’, ‘crime statistics’, ‘a life of crime’
[wrong sentence]:  The criminal rate among juveniles is still increasing.

 [explain]:  ‘criminal activity’, ‘a criminal offence’, ‘a criminal record’, ‘criminal law’, BUT ‘crime prevention’, ‘the crime rate’, ‘crime statistics’, ‘a life of crime’
