R:OTHER cure -> heal
 [explain]:  Cuts, wounds, injuries, etc heal: ‘Cuts generally take longer to heal in humid climates.’
[wrong sentence]:  The wound took several weeks to cure.


R:OTHER cure -> recover
 [explain]:  When people who have been ill or injured return to their normal state of health, they recover or get better: ‘Some of the flood victims are still recovering in hospital.’ ‘It takes a long time to recover from glandular fever.’ ‘Within a month of the heart attack, he had fully recovered.’
[wrong sentence]:  Those who are seriously ill take a long time to cure.


R:OTHER damaged -> hurt
 [explain]:  During the protests, some students were killed and others were seriously damaged. During the protests, some students were killed and others were seriously injured/wounded. Damaged is used in connection with things or parts of your body (NOT people): ‘The engine was too badly damaged to be repaired.’ ‘The cause of the oil leak was a damaged pipeline.’ People are hurt or injured (badly hurt) in an accident, earthquake, hurricane etc: ‘The scaffolding collapsed, killing one of the construction workers and injuring two passers-by.’ Someone who is injured by a weapon. such as a gun or knife. is wounded: ‘He is accused of wounding a fellow prisoner.’ ‘The wounded soldiers were sent home for medical treatment.’
[wrong sentence]:  The driver was very lucky and was only slightly damaged.


R:OTHER dares -> is prepared
 [explain]:  Dare (= have enough courage) is used mainly in negative sentences and questions: ‘I wouldn’t dare to take the car without permission.’ ‘She stood at the edge of the cliff, not daring to look down.’ In affirmative sentences, use be ready/prepared/willing: ‘Some people are prepared to do anything for money.’ ‘Despite the threat on his life, he’s ready to testify.’
[wrong sentence]:  He dares to die rather than break his promise.


R:OTHER dared -> was ready
 [explain]:  Dare (= have enough courage) is used mainly in negative sentences and questions: ‘I wouldn’t dare to take the car without permission.’ ‘She stood at the edge of the cliff, not daring to look down.’ In affirmative sentences, use be ready/prepared/willing: ‘Some people are prepared to do anything for money.’ ‘Despite the threat on his life, he’s ready to testify.’
[wrong sentence]:  I didn’t enjoy the drink but I dared to try it again.


R:OTHER 6 days -> 6-day
 [explain]:  day after day = repeatedly or continuously, especially in a boring or unpleasant way: ‘I get fed up with listening to their complaints day after day.’ ‘I’m not prepared to sit here day after day doing nothing.’ day by day = gradually: ‘Day by day the weather is getting warmer.’ ‘Their love grew day by day.’
[wrong sentence]:  My travel agent had arranged a 6 days coach tour.


R:OTHER in our days -> nowadays
 [explain]:  The phrase ‘in our days’ does not exist. For this meaning, use nowadays, these days, or today: ‘I get the feeling that some people nowadays cannot live without television.’
[wrong sentence]:  In our days we know more about the causes of pollution.


R:OTHER is dead -> die
 [explain]:  Dead is an adjective and describes a state: ‘I can’t tell whether that plant is dead or alive.’ ‘Some of the fish were dead and were floating on the surface.’ Died is the past tense and past participle of die: ‘I think she died from a heart attack.’ ‘He died on the way to hospital.’ See also note at DIED The purpose of this ceremony is to honour the dead people
[wrong sentence]:  My father is dead when I was still a baby.


R:OTHER dead -> die
 [explain]:  Dead is an adjective and describes a state: ‘I can’t tell whether that plant is dead or alive.’ ‘Some of the fish were dead and were floating on the surface.’ Died is the past tense and past participle of die: ‘I think she died from a heart attack.’ ‘He died on the way to hospital.’ See also note at DIED The purpose of this ceremony is to honour the dead people
[wrong sentence]:  The doctor said that she dead because the wound became infected.


R:OTHER a great deal of -> many
 [explain]:  a great/good deal of + uncountable noun: ‘a great deal of money/time/pleasure’ a large number of + plural count noun: ‘a large number of coins/cars/tourists’ a great/good many + plural count noun: ‘This operation has already saved the lives of a great many people.’ When there is no need to emphasize the size of the number, use many or (especially in informal styles) a lot of: ‘Many people have stopped smoking.’
[wrong sentence]:  A great deal of countries have already signed the agreement.


R:OTHER a lot of -> many
 [explain]:  many + weeks/months/years (NOT a lot of): ‘Many years have passed since we were at school together.’
[wrong sentence]:  The problem is that for a lot of years smoking was not considered to be anti-social.
[wrong sentence]:  My husband and I met each other a lot of years ago.


R:OTHER denied -> said he hadn’t
 [explain]:  You deny an accusation or claim (NOT a question): ‘The accused denied both charges.’ ‘He denied being anywhere near the scene of the crime.’
[wrong sentence]:  She asked him if he had seen a little boy but he denied.

 [explain]:  In Argentina, Christmas celebrations are completely different as the ones in

R:OTHER discussing -> talk

[wrong sentence]:  Some people were discussing; some listening to the radio.

 [explain]:  There is no doubt that most parents are willing to spend a lot of money on their child’s education .

 [explain]:  there is no doubt that (NOT it is ... ): There is no doubt that the number of casualties would have escalated had it not been for UN intervention.’
[wrong sentence]:  It is no doubt that the rich have a great advantage.


 [explain]:  there is no need to do sth (NOT it is ... ): ‘There’s no need to start getting upset.’
[wrong sentence]:  It is no need to tell the police about the accident.


R:OTHER in that place -> there
 [explain]:  there =in/to that place: ‘It’s a long way to New Zealand but I’d love to go there.’
[wrong sentence]:  I left the coat in my room but it may not be in that place any longer.


R:OTHER without -> no
 [explain]:  without doubt = ‘I firmly believe this to be true’: ‘He is without doubt one of the greatest composers the world has ever known.’ no doubt = ‘I expect’ or ‘I suppose’: ‘No doubt you could do with a drink.’ They will no doubt be writing to us again.’
[wrong sentence]:  Without doubt you’re tired after your journey.


R:OTHER no -> without
 [explain]:  without doubt = ‘I firmly believe this to be true’: ‘He is without doubt one of the greatest composers the world has ever known.’ no doubt = ‘I expect’ or ‘I suppose’: ‘No doubt you could do with a drink.’ They will no doubt be writing to us again.’
[wrong sentence]:  The recovery of the Mary Rose is, no doubt, a great scientific achievement.

R:OTHER nearly drown -> drown
 [explain]:  drown (verb) = die by being under the water and unable to breathe: ‘Help him! He’s drowning!’ ‘The lifeguard got to him too late. He had already drowned.’ ‘She keeps away from the water because she’s afraid of drowning.’
[wrong sentence]:  The boy was nearly drown.


R:OTHER being drown -> drown
 [explain]:  drown (verb) = die by being under the water and unable to breathe: ‘Help him! He’s drowning!’ ‘The lifeguard got to him too late. He had already drowned.’ ‘She keeps away from the water because she’s afraid of drowning.’
[wrong sentence]:  A man jumped into the river to save her from being drown.


R:OTHER edited -> publish
 [explain]:  edit = examine and make changes and corrections to a manuscript in preparation for printing: ‘The text was still full of mistakes and obviously hadn’t been edited.’ publish = produce (a book, magazine, newspaper, etc) for sale to the public: ‘The book was first published in 1987.’
[wrong sentence]:  I have never seen the magazine before - I think it’s only just been edited.

 [explain]:  See Language Note opposite
[wrong sentence]:  In the end, I would like to wish you all a very interesting and enjoyable stay.


R:OTHER endure -> suffer from
 [explain]:  endure a painful or very unpleasant situation: ‘The people in this country have had to endure almost a decade of economic hardship.’ suffer from/with a disease or medical condition: ‘My youngest son suffers from asthma.’
[wrong sentence]:  More than 160 million people endure malaria.


R:OTHER energetic -> energy
 [explain]:  energetic = active and able to work or play for a long time: ‘Does anyone feel energetic enough to go for another swim?’ ‘My mother was a bustling energetic woman, always in a hurry, always busy.’ energy = the power obtained from oil, coal, the sun, etc: ‘Oil is not only an expensive source of energy, it’s running out.’ ‘There should be more research into solar energy.’
[wrong sentence]:  Our main energetic source is nuclear power.


R:OTHER entered -> got on
 [explain]:  get on/board a train/plane/ship: ‘The ticket inspector wanted to know where I had got on.’ ‘There’s still time for some duty-free shopping before we have to board the plane.’
[wrong sentence]:  I entered the train in Oslo.


R:OTHER enter -> get into
 [explain]:  get into (a) college, university etc (= be admitted to): ‘What qualifications do you need to get into medical school?’
[wrong sentence]:  Unfortunately, some of my classmates didn’t enter university.


R:OTHER equipments -> equipment
 [explain]:  Equipment is an uncountable noun: ‘The school is trying to raise funds to buy some new sports equipment.’
[wrong sentence]:  They were not satisfied with the new equipments.


R:OTHER estimated -> esteem
 [explain]:  estimate = calculate an amount or price without being exact: ‘The police department estimates that the number of crimes will increase this year by about 15%.’ ‘At that point, the public sector deficit was estimated to be around £45 billion.’ ‘The population of Adelaide is estimated at 917,000.’ ; be highly esteemed/thought of = be greatly admired, especially by a large number of people: ‘In the world of fashion, his work is highly thought of.’
[wrong sentence]:  Her master’s thesis was highly estimated and is now being prepared for publication.

[wrong sentence]:  I am pleased to be able to write to you eventually.


R:OTHER eventually -> in due course
 [explain]:  When you mean that something will be done at the appropriate time (remembering that other things have to be done first), use in due course: ‘The 300 new computers have now been received and arrangements for their distribution will be made in due course.’
[wrong sentence]:  You will be informed of the committee’s decision eventually.


R:OTHER do exercise -> practise

[wrong sentence]:  You will never be able to speak fluently unless you do exercise.

 [explain]:  accept/appreciate the fact that ... (NOT agree to/with the fact that ... ): ‘I appreciate the fact that funds are limited.’
[wrong sentence]:  I agree with the fact that politics and ecology will never go hand in hand.

 [explain]:  See Language Note at INDUSTRY
[wrong sentence]:  The brick factory was closed down during the recession.


R:OTHER works -> work
 [explain]:  If the noun after percentage of is plural, the verb is plural: ‘A high percentage of these school leavers have no qualifications.’
[wrong sentence]:  Only a small percentage of the windmills still works.

 [explain]:  Put factory after the thing that is produced: ‘a bottle factory’, ‘a clothing factory’.

R:OTHER far -> a long way
 [explain]:  ‘It’s far to the nearest garage,’ she said. ‘It’s a long way to the nearest garage,’ she said. Far is mainly used in questions and negative sentences: ‘How far is it to the station?’ ‘Oxford isn’t far from London.’ ‘It’s not far.’ In affirmative sentences, people usually say a long way: ‘Their house is a long way from the town centre.’ Far is used in affirmative sentences only when it appears in phrases such as too far, quite far or far away: ‘I suggest you take the bus - it’s too far to walk.’
[wrong sentence]:  Singapore is far from London.


R:OTHER far away -> (away
 [explain]:  Far cannot be used after a unit of distance. Compare: ‘The house is rather far from the school.’ (WITHOUT unit of distance) ‘The house is about two kilometres (away) from the school.’ (WITH unit of distance) Far East See MIDDLE EAST
[wrong sentence]:  My village is about 10 minutes far away from Ayia Napa.


R:OTHER very -> (absolutely
 [explain]:  See Note at VERY 2
[wrong sentence]:  I was very fascinated by the speed with which they worked.


 [explain]:  See Note at VERY 2
[wrong sentence]:  Meno Park in Central Tokyo is very huge.


 [explain]:  See Note at VERY 2
[wrong sentence]:  The traffic jams are very terrible.


 [explain]:  Do not use very or extremely with adjectives which already have a strong meaning, e.g. boiling (= very hot), convinced (= very sure), exhausted (= very tired), huge (= very big), terrible (= very bad). If you want to increase the strength of these words, use absolutely or (depending on the particular adjective) completely, totally, utterly or quite: ‘By the time I got home I was completely exhausted.’ With adjectives which do not have a strong meaning, use very or extremely (NOT absolutely, completely etc): ‘By the time I got back home I was very tired.’
[wrong sentence]:  Meno Park in Central Tokyo is very huge.
[wrong sentence]:  The traffic jams are very terrible.


R:OTHER a little bit too fat -> slightly overweight
 [explain]:  In western society being fat is considered to be unattractive. The word fat is common but it is not polite. To say the same thing in a less direct way, use words such as overweight (weighing more than is normal), plump (having a pleasantly rounded shape), chubby (to describe babies and children), stout (short and rather fat), or large/big: ‘Large people sometimes have difficulty finding fashionable clothes to fit them.’ Another way to avoid fat is to use a phrase such as put on weight or (have) a weight problem: ‘He’s worried about his weight problem.’
[wrong sentence]:  His wife is a little bit too fat.


R:OTHER got very fat -> put on a lot of weight
 [explain]:  In western society being fat is considered to be unattractive. The word fat is common but it is not polite. To say the same thing in a less direct way, use words such as overweight (weighing more than is normal), plump (having a pleasantly rounded shape), chubby (to describe babies and children), stout (short and rather fat), or large/big: ‘Large people sometimes have difficulty finding fashionable clothes to fit them.’ Another way to avoid fat is to use a phrase such as put on weight or (have) a weight problem: ‘He’s worried about his weight problem.’
[wrong sentence]:  He’s got very fat since we last saw each other.

R:OTHER fit for -> suit
 [explain]:  fit = (of clothes, shoes, rings etc) be the correct size and shape for you: ‘These trousers don’t fit me any more.’ ‘The next size up should fit.’ ; suit = be suitable: ‘Try and choose a career that suits you.’ ‘You should buy a dictionary that suits your needs, not just anyone.’
[wrong sentence]:  A school like Summerhill will not fit for everybody.


R:OTHER follow -> come with
 [explain]:  Follow means ‘move in the same direction as someone or something that is moving in front of you’: ‘I have a feeling that the car behind is following us.’
[wrong sentence]:  You can go in your own car or follow me.


R:OTHER i’m -> i’ve been
 [explain]:  For actions and situations which began in the past and continue up to ‘now’ (the moment of speaking), use the present perfect tense: ‘I’ve been working part-time for the last six months.’ ‘We haven’t seen each other for the past eight months.’ Note that in informal American English the past tense is also used for this purpose.
[wrong sentence]:  I’m waiting here for almost an hour.


R:OTHER a good fortune -> fortunate/lucky
 [explain]:  Good fortune is quite rare. It occurs mainly in the phrase have the good fortune to do sth: ‘He had the good fortune to marry a woman who was both kind and understanding.’
[wrong sentence]:  It was a good fortune that the driver was able to stop in time.


R:OTHER friendly -> yours
 [explain]:  To end a letter to a friend, use Yours or With best wishes (NOT friendly). See also YOURS 1
[wrong sentence]:  I look forward to hearing from you. Friendly, Leine.


R:OTHER in front of -> opposite
 [explain]:  in front of = in a position directly before: ‘There was a bus in front of the car and a truck behind it.’ ‘All I could see was the enormous hat of the woman sitting in front of me.’ opposite = on the other side of the street, room, etc facing: ‘The police station is directly opposite the town hall.’ ‘He looked out of his bedroom window at the house opposite.’
[wrong sentence]:  The bus stop is outside the post office, in front of the library.


R:OTHER fun  -> pleasure
 [explain]:  When you are talking about something that provides intellectual or spiritual satisfaction, such as a piano concert or a visit to an art gallery, use enjoyment/enjoyable or pleasure/pleasurable: ‘Reading is her one source of pleasure.’ ‘We spent an enjoyable afternoon at the art gallery.’
[wrong sentence]:  A good novel can give you a lot of fun .


R:OTHER very -> a lot of
 [explain]:  Fun is a noun (NOT an adjective): ‘a lot of fun’, ‘not much fun’, ‘great fun’
[wrong sentence]:  The game we played was very fun.


R:OTHER many -> a lot of
 [explain]:  Many and much are used mainly in questions and negative sentences: ‘Does he have many friends?’ ‘It appears that he doesn’t have many friends.’ In affirmative sentences, phrases such as a lot of and plenty of are used. Note however that many and much are used in affirmative sentences after too, so, and as (‘You ask too many questions.’) and sometimes in formal styles (‘Many accidents arise as a result of negligence’).
[wrong sentence]:  One of the policemen started asking me many questions.
[wrong sentence]:  We are given many tests.


 [explain]:  quite a lot (of) NOT quite many/much: ‘Quite a lot of time has already been wasted.’ I’ve made quite a lot of new friends.’
[wrong sentence]:  There are quite many foreign students in London.


R:OTHER much -> a lot of
 [explain]:  See Note at MANY 1
[wrong sentence]:  As a doctor he earns much money.


R:OTHER very -> much
 [explain]:  Fun is a noun (NOT an adjective): ‘a lot of fun’, ‘not much fun’, ‘great fun’
[wrong sentence]:  College life is not very fun.


R:OTHER funny -> fun
 [explain]:  Someone or something that is funny makes you laugh: ‘I always laugh at Jenny’s jokes - they’re really funny.’ ‘The funniest part was when her father-in-law sat on the wedding cake.’ Someone or something that is fun gives you pleasure or enjoyment: ‘The Disneyland trip sounds like a lot of fun.’
[wrong sentence]:  It will be more funny to go by bus.
[wrong sentence]:  We’ve organized lots of funny activities, including a picnic and a trip to the zoo.


R:OTHER to swim -> swim
 [explain]:  go swimming/sightseeing/shopping etc: ‘They’ve probably gone fishing.’
[wrong sentence]:  We go to swim every day on holiday.


R:OTHER gonna -> going to
 [explain]:  Do not use gonna (= going to) or wanna (= want to) unless you are trying to represent informal conversation.
[wrong sentence]:  The principal is gonna give a talk in the morning.
[wrong sentence]:  Do you know when the plane is gonna land?


R:OTHER visiting -> going to
 [explain]:  go to or (more formal) attend a school, college, class etc (= go there regularly as a student): ‘Her son goes to a small private school near Chingford.’ ‘I’ve started going to evening classes.’
[wrong sentence]:  I’ve been Visiting a language school in Cambridge.


R:OTHER good -> well
 [explain]:  Good is an adjective: ‘She is a very good singer.’
[wrong sentence]:  I don’t speak English very good.


 [explain]:  If you are successful (in a test, interview etc), you do well (in it): ‘I did quite well in maths and chemistry but my physics result was very disappointing.’
[wrong sentence]:  I hope I’ll do good in tomorrow’s test.


R:OTHER there’s -> it’s
 [explain]:  it is no good/use (doing sth) = it is pointless: ‘It’s no good having a car if you can’t drive.’
[wrong sentence]:  There’s no good putting a very young child in a kindergarten.


R:OTHER is -> it’s
 [explain]:  See IT 1
[wrong sentence]:  Is very easy to get lost in a big city.


 [explain]:  When you comment on a situation, the subject of the sentence is usually it: It’s impossible to read in the dark.’ ‘It was good to see you again.’ ‘It’s hot today, isn’t it?’ ‘It was a miracle that nobody was injured.’ Note that in this pattern It has no meaning. It simply fills the subject position. it’s
[wrong sentence]:  Is easy to buy exactly what you want in a big city.


R:OTHER it has passed -> it’s
 [explain]:  We first met each other almost a year ago.
[wrong sentence]:  It has passed almost a year since we first met each other.


R:OTHER grave -> serious
 [explain]:  grave = extremely serious and worrying, especially because someone’s life or happiness is in danger: ‘The situation has become increasingly grave and war now seems inevitable.’ ‘The shortage of food and medical supplies is giving rise to grave concern.’ We went to see the ‘Valley of the Kings’, where the grave of Tut-mah-Amun was found. We went to see the ‘Valley of the Kings’, where the tomb of Tut-mah-Amun was found. grave = a place in the ground where a dead person is put: ‘The coffin was slowly lowered into the grave.’ tomb (pronounced /tuːm/) = a place where a dead person is put. A tomb is usually made of stone and is above the ground: ‘Our first stop was the Tomb of the Unknown Soldier.’
[wrong sentence]:  I don’t have any grave problems.
[wrong sentence]:  Blackmail is a grave crime.


R:OTHER had not -> didn’t have
 [explain]:  When have is-used as a main verb, the negative and question forms are made with do: ‘I didn’t have any breakfast this morning.’ ; Note the alternative: ‘After buying the food, he hadn’t got any money left.’
[wrong sentence]:  After buying the food, he had not any money left.


R:OTHER heard -> listened to
 [explain]:  like hearing tapes and watching English films.
[wrong sentence]:  They all sat down and heard my story.


R:OTHER hit -> knocked over
 [explain]:  knock over = hit something by accident so that it falls from a standing position: ‘The cat jumped up onto the table and knocked over a full glass of red wine.’
[wrong sentence]:  Turning round, I hit a vase which broke into several pieces.


R:OTHER in my -> at
 [explain]:  at home (NOT in my/our etc home): ‘I’m fed up with being at home all the time.’
[wrong sentence]:  I have to stay in my home to look after our baby.


R:OTHER in our -> at
 [explain]:  at home (NOT in my/our etc home): ‘I’m fed up with being at home all the time.’
[wrong sentence]:  We decided to spend the weekend in our home.


R:OTHER outside the home -> out
 [explain]:  When someone leaves their house for a short while, they go out: ‘I’d like to go out but I’m too tired.’
[wrong sentence]:  If they want to go outside the home, they have to ask their husbands.


R:OTHER work outside their home -> go out to work
 [explain]:  See Language Note at OCCUPATION
[wrong sentence]:  Saudi women were not allowed to work outside their home.


R:OTHER honestly -> to be honest
 [explain]:  Use honestly when you want someone to believe that what you are saying is really true: ‘I honestly don’t mind where we go, as long as we go somewhere.’ ‘I was going to give it back to you, honestly.’
[wrong sentence]:  Honestly, we didn’t play very well in the final.


R:OTHER honestly -> to tell you the truth
 [explain]:  Use honestly when you want someone to believe that what you are saying is really true: ‘I honestly don’t mind where we go, as long as we go somewhere.’ ‘I was going to give it back to you, honestly.’
[wrong sentence]:  Honestly, I don’t really like her husband.


R:OTHER twelve hours -> twelve-hour
 [explain]:  See Language Note at HUNDRED
[wrong sentence]:  It was a twelve hours trip.


R:OTHER how -> what
 [explain]:  When you ask for or give a description of someone or something, use what ... like (NOT how): ‘What’s your new English teacher like?’ ‘This drawing gives you an idea of what the new shopping complex will look like.’ ‘What does it feel like to win an Olympic gold medal?’ ‘What do the apples taste like?’
[wrong sentence]:  How do the new shoes feel like?
[wrong sentence]:  Could you describe how the driver looks like?


 [explain]:  When you ask someone for their opinion, use what ... think (NOT how): ‘What do you think of Ann’s new car?’
[wrong sentence]:  How do you think of the hotel?
[wrong sentence]:  Should I tell him the truth? How do you think?


R:OTHER however -> but
 [explain]:  Unlike but, however is an adverb (NOT a conjunction) and is used only in formal styles: ‘I was hoping to deal with this matter quickly. However, the situation is more complicated than I thought.’ ‘The newspapers always carried stories of new advances and glorious victories. In reality, however, the war was not going well.’
[wrong sentence]:  I don’t like shopping however I’II come with you.


R:OTHER i -> me
 [explain]:  In comparisons use than me/him/her/us/them (object pronouns) or than I am/he is/she is/we are/they are: ‘You’re stronger than him.’ ‘You’re stronger than he is.’
[wrong sentence]:  She is one year younger than I.


R:OTHER immigrate -> emigrate
 [explain]:  The verb immigrate (= enter a foreign country and make your home there) is seldom used. Instead people tend to use immigrant and immigration (nouns): ‘When jobs became scarce, the number of new immigrants suddenly decreased.’ ‘Immigration has decreased in recent years.’ emigrate = leave your own country and make your home in another one: ‘His parents emigrated from Scotland to Canada in 1933.’
[wrong sentence]:  Some of those who can’t find a job decide to immigrate.


R:OTHER includes -> consists of
 [explain]:  See Language Note on next page
[wrong sentence]:  Education in Jordan includes three stages.
[wrong sentence]:  The poem includes just two short verses.


R:OTHER a -> (any
 [explain]:  See Language Note at SCENERY
[wrong sentence]:  Let me know if you need a further information.


 [explain]:  Transport is an uncountable noun: ‘How will you get there without transport?’ ‘People should be encouraged to use public transport.’
[wrong sentence]:  Some countries don’t have a public transport.


R:OTHER to -> that he (should
 [explain]:  insist on (doing) sth: ‘He insisted on paying for the meal himself.’ insist that sb (should) do sth: ‘He insisted that he (should) pay for the meal himself.’ in spite of
[wrong sentence]:  The porter insisted to help us with our baggage.


R:OTHER interior -> internal
 [explain]:  interior = on the inside of a building, room, vehicle etc: ‘The interior doors are still sound but the exterior doors need replacing.’ the interior = the part of a country that is a long way from the coast: ‘The convoy set off at dawn for the interior.’ internal = involving what happens within a particular country: ‘Each country has the right to control its own internal affairs.’
[wrong sentence]:  Solutions to the country’s interior problems are still a long way away.


R:OTHER it’s -> its
 [explain]:  It’s (WITH an apostrophe) = ‘it is’ or ‘it has’: ‘It’s still raining.’ ‘It’s been raining again.’ its (WITHOUT an apostrophe) = a possessive form like my, his, their, etc: ‘The dog has hurt its nose again.’
[wrong sentence]:  The cat sat in front of the fire, cleaning it’s whiskers.


R:OTHER half-time -> part-time
 [explain]:  See Language Note at OCCUPATION
[wrong sentence]:  I’m willing to accept any job, even a half-time one.


R:OTHER the last -> recent
 [explain]:  in recent weeks/months etc (NOT last): ‘In recent months this stretch of the road has seen three serious accidents.’
[wrong sentence]:  In the last years many people have stopped smoking.


R:OTHER lastly -> in the end/eventually
 [explain]:  See Language Note at END
[wrong sentence]:  Lastly I got bored with walking round the streets, and went back home.


R:OTHER i play -> i’ve been playing
 [explain]:  Lately is usually used with the present perfect tense: ‘I haven’t seen much of your sister lately. Has she gone away?’
[wrong sentence]:  Lately I play a lot of Miles Davis tapes.


R:OTHER lessened -> reduce
 [explain]:  Lessen is mainly used in connection with pain and feelings: ‘I’ll give you an injection to lessen the pain.’ ‘No amount of comforting could lessen Charles’ disappointment.’ When you mean ‘make something smaller in amount, number, price or size’, use reduce: ‘The workforce has been reduced by 50 percent.’
[wrong sentence]:  Fortunately, my workload has been lessened.


R:OTHER increase -> rise
 [explain]:  See Note at INCREASE 2
[wrong sentence]:  What will we do if the sea level continues to increase?


R:OTHER met in my life -> ever met
 [explain]:  When a superlative (‘most attractive’) is followed by a relative clause, use that ... ever ... (NOT that ... in my/her etc life): ‘It was the best holiday we’d ever had.’ ‘This is the worst film I’ve ever seen.’
[wrong sentence]:  He was the most attractive man that I had met in my life.


R:OTHER put a limit -> place restrictions
 [explain]:  When you are talking about the control of something by laws, rules etc, use restrict/restriction: ‘The government plans to restrict the sale of guns.’ ‘The 1986 law imposed new financial restrictions on private companies.’ ‘
[wrong sentence]:  One solution would be for the government to put a limit on when these programmes may be broadcast.


R:OTHER linens -> linen
 [explain]:  Linen is an uncountable noun: ‘The linen is changed once a week.’
[wrong sentence]:  We complained about the dirty bed linens.


R:OTHER living -> life
 [explain]:  See Note at LIFE 2
[wrong sentence]:  We have different languages, traditions and living styles.


R:OTHER it very much -> it
 [explain]:  love sb (very much): ‘I love him very much.’ love sth (WITHOUT very much): ‘I love tennis.’
[wrong sentence]:  I’m sure that once you see Venice, you’ll love it very much.


R:OTHER luxurious thing -> luxury
 [explain]:  a luxury = something that you do not need: ‘We can hardly afford to buy food, yet alone waste money on luxuries.’
[wrong sentence]:  Many people today do not consider a car as a luxurious thing.


R:OTHER luxury life -> life of luxury
 [explain]:  A life of luxury is a fixed phrase: ‘His dream is to marry a princess and live a life of luxury.’
[wrong sentence]:  Some successful criminals enjoy a luxury life.


R:OTHER the majority of -> most
 [explain]:  The majority of (= more than half) is usually used in formal styles: ‘The majority of the government voted against the bill.’ In other styles most (= nearly all) usually sounds more natural: ‘Most people have never even heard of him.’
[wrong sentence]:  The majority of houses in Germany have fitted carpets.


R:OTHER mankind’s -> the human
 [explain]:  Mankind (or the human race) refers to all human beings considered as a group: ‘The greatest danger to mankind is not science but ignorance.’ ‘These are the most devastating weapons mankind has ever devised.’ ‘What is mankind’s long-term role in space?’ When you mean ‘belonging to or typical of people (as opposed to God animals, machines etc)’, the usual pattern is human + noun: ‘Greed and envy are common human failings.’ Note that phrases such as human nature, human error, human interest and human weakness are very common.
[wrong sentence]:  In art we discover the beauty of mankind’s imagination.


R:OTHER manners -> traditions/customs
 [explain]:  See Language Note below One of the biggest differences I noticed was the American manner of being polite to strangers. One of the biggest differences I noticed was the American custom of being polite to strangers. See Language Note below
[wrong sentence]:  Every society has its own laws and manners.


R:OTHER maybe you’ll -> you may
 [explain]:  Maybe you will have problems breathing.
[wrong sentence]:  Maybe you’ll find the film very boring.


R:OTHER meals  -> dish
 [explain]:  See Language Note at PLATE
[wrong sentence]:  You will be able to try some of the traditional meals .


R:OTHER all -> whatever
 [explain]:  They intend to become famous by all means, even if they have to risk their lives. They intend to become famous by whatever means, even if they have to risk their lives. By all means (= certainly) is used in formal spoken English to show that you are happy to accept someone’s request or suggestion: ‘Would you mind if I stayed a bit longer?’ ‘By all means.’ When you mean ‘by using any method, even if it is unkind or illegal’, use by any means or by whatever means (are available/necessary): ‘They are determined to get their hands on the property by whatever means are necessary.’
[wrong sentence]:  He was determined to get the money by all means.


R:OTHER mend  -> repair
 [explain]:  mend (or repair) a roof, fence, watch, radio, camera, puncture etc: ‘Once you’ve mended the kettle, we can have a cup of tea.’ repair a vehicle or something that is wrong with a vehicle: ‘How will you get to work while your car is being repaired?’
[wrong sentence]:  Old cars are easier to mend .


R:OTHER millions -> million
 [explain]:  See Language Note at HUNDRED
[wrong sentence]:  The paper has a circulation of approximately 3.9 millions.
[wrong sentence]:  Belgium has ten millions inhabitants.


R:OTHER twenty minutes -> twenty-minute
 [explain]:  See Language Note at HUNDRED
[wrong sentence]:  The college is a twenty minutes bus ride from my flat.


R:OTHER mood  -> atmosphere
 [explain]:  The streets were full of people in a holiday mood. mood = the way someone feels at a particular time, such as happy, angry, etc: ‘I’d keep clear of
[wrong sentence]:  The streets were very crowded and had a holiday mood .


R:OTHER you must -> you’ll
 [explain]:  When you inform someone of something, use will be + pleased/interested + to hear/know/learn (NOT must): ‘You will be pleased to know that your old friend Peter has been promoted to
[wrong sentence]:  You must be pleased to hear that I’ve already got the tickets.


R:OTHER narrows -> shrink
 [explain]:  narrow (of roads, rivers etc) = become less wide: ‘Just beyond the bend, the river begins to narrow.’ shrink (especially of cloth) = become smaller as a result of being wet or placed in water: ‘I suggest you buy the larger size just in case it shrinks.’
[wrong sentence]:  When immersed in water, the cloth narrows.


R:OTHER natives -> local residents
 [explain]:  Nowadays the noun native is usually used in formal styles to refer to the place where someone was born: ‘His real name was Harvey Pepper, a native of Montreal.’ or to the country/region where a particular animal or plant has always grown: ‘This useful herb is a native of southern
[wrong sentence]:  The airport extension is strongly opposed by the natives.


R:OTHER which was near -> nearby
 [explain]:  To say that something is only a short distance from a place, use nearby: ‘I stopped at one of the nearby cottages and asked the way.’ ‘It’s very convenient having a supermarket nearby.’
[wrong sentence]:  I ran to the telephone box which was near to call an ambulance.


R:OTHER nearby -> near
 [explain]:  Nearby is used as an adjective or adverb: ‘We flew from a nearby airport.’ ‘There is an airport nearby.’
[wrong sentence]:  Their house is nearby the new airport.


R:OTHER need not to -> needn’t
 [explain]:  needn’t/need not do sth (WITHOUT to): ‘You needn’t wait if you’re in a hurry.’ don’t need to do sth (WITH to): ‘You don’t need to wait if you’re in a hurry.’
[wrong sentence]:  They need not to feel ashamed of themselves.


R:OTHER needn’t -> didn’t need
 [explain]:  needn’t/need not do sth (WITHOUT to): ‘You needn’t wait if you’re in a hurry.’ don’t need to do sth (WITH to): ‘You don’t need to wait if you’re in a hurry.’
[wrong sentence]:  Catherine told her husband he needn’t to worry.


R:OTHER loud -> lot of
 [explain]:  A loud noise (countable) describes what you hear when, for example, somebody slams a door or drops a plate. To refer to continuous noise made during an activity, use noise as an uncountable noun: ‘They don’t know the difference between music and noise.’
[wrong sentence]:  The restaurant was full and there was a loud noise.
[wrong sentence]:  You can talk as long as you don’t make a loud noise.


R:OTHER nothing -> anything
 [explain]:  See Language Note below
[wrong sentence]:  It was so dark that I couldn’t see nothing.


R:OTHER something -> anything
 [explain]:  See Note at SOME 2
[wrong sentence]:  Does your country export something?


R:OTHER take any notice of -> see/notice
 [explain]:  take notice of = pay attention to: ‘Nobody ever takes any notice of what I say.’ notice = become aware of; see: ‘I didn’t suspect anything until I noticed that my chequebook was missing.’
[wrong sentence]:  It was so crowded that at first I didn’t take any notice of him.


R:OTHER nowadays -> today’s
 [explain]:  Nowadays is an adverb (NOT an adjective): ‘Video cameras are very popular nowadays.’
[wrong sentence]:  This is a major problem for nowadays society.


R:OTHER oblige -> force
 [explain]:  If someone makes you do something that you do not want to do, they force/compel you to do it (or make you do it): ‘They forced him to hand over the money by threatening to kill him.’
[wrong sentence]:  You can’t oblige children to study if they don’t want to.


R:OTHER occasion -> a chance
 [explain]:  occasion = the time when an event happens: ‘I’ve been to Rome on several occasions.’ (= several times) opportunity = a time when it is possible to do something that you want to do: ‘The meeting on Tuesday will be a good opportunity for you to make some new contacts.’ ‘She has considerable ability and should be given more opportunity to use it.’ chance = an informal word for ‘opportunity’: ‘If I had the chance, I’d like to be an airline pilot.’ ‘I’ve been so busy this morning I haven’t had a chance to sit down.’ See also OPPORTUNITY
[wrong sentence]:  I never had occasion to take the Proficiency examination.


R:OTHER occur -> take place
 [explain]:  Occur is usually used in connection with unplanned events: ‘Many of the serious accidents that occur are caused by human error.’ ‘Tornadoes occur when a warm weather front meets a body of very cold air.’
[wrong sentence]:  The concert will occur at eight o’clock next Tuesday.


R:OTHER p.m -> (in the evening
 [explain]:  Use EITHER o’clock OR a.m./p.m. (NOT both).
[wrong sentence]:  By seven o’clock p.m. the child had been found.


R:OTHER 9.0 -> 
 [explain]:  Do not use o’clock after 6.00,7.00 etc. Compare: ‘8 a.m.’, ‘8.00’, ‘8.00 a.m.’, ‘8 o’clock’.
[wrong sentence]:  I start work at 9.00 o’clock.


R:OTHER them -> theirs
 [explain]:  When the meaning is possessive, use of + mine/yours/his/hers/ours/theirs. Compare: ‘That’s a photograph of him.’ (= showing him), ‘That’s a photograph of his.’ (= belonging to or taken by him)
[wrong sentence]:  He is a good friend of them.


R:OTHER officers -> office workers
 [explain]:  offer to do sth = express willingness to do something: ‘She’s offered to help me.’ invite sb to (do) sth = ask someone if they would like to come to a party, wedding etc, or join you in a social activity: ‘Have you and Valerie to the party?’
[wrong sentence]:  Most of my friends got jobs as shop assistants or officers.


R:OTHER twenty years old -> twenty-year-old
 [explain]:  Use years old after the verb be: ‘Sarah is eight years old.’
[wrong sentence]:  He is married to a twenty years old American girl.


R:OTHER eight-years-old -> eight-year-old
 [explain]:  Use years old after the verb be: ‘Sarah is eight years old.’
[wrong sentence]:  Eight-years-old Sarah had a few surprises up her sleeve.


R:OTHER these ones -> these
 [explain]:  Avoid ones immediately after these/those, especially in formal styles: ‘Within this group, there are those who are willing to take risks and those who are more cautious.’
[wrong sentence]:  If you can carry those books, I’ll bring these ones.


R:OTHER ones -> one’s
 [explain]:  The possessive form is one ‘s (WITH ‘s): ‘It is difficult to estimate one’s chances of success.’ See also IT’S
[wrong sentence]:  One mustn’t waste ones time when there is so much to do.


R:OTHER opened -> turned/switched on
 [explain]:  turn on/off (or switch on/off) a light/radio/television (NOT open/close): ‘The children are not allowed to turn on the television without permission.’
[wrong sentence]:  I got out of bed and opened the radio to listen to the news.


R:OTHER opened -> open
 [explain]:  Use opened to describe an action and open to describe a state.
[wrong sentence]:  I couldn’t buy a newspaper because the shop wasn’t opened.


R:OTHER according to -> in
 [explain]:  according to sb: ‘According to Peter, deforestation is a very serious problem.’ in sb’s opinion (NOT according to ... opinion): ‘In Peter’s opinion, deforestation is a very serious problem.’
[wrong sentence]:  According to Henry’s opinion, less money should be spent on weapons.


R:OTHER oral -> speak
 [explain]:  The use of oral to mean ‘spoken’ is restricted to certain technical phrases used in education: ‘oral skills’, ‘an oral examination’.
[wrong sentence]:  Her oral English is very fluent and clear.


R:OTHER otherwise -> or
 [explain]:  Unlike or, otherwise is an adverb (NOT a conjunction): ‘I’m glad that you told me about the show being cancelled. Otherwise I’d have travelled all the way to Glasgow for nothing.’
[wrong sentence]:  Remember to get there early otherwise you may not get a seat.


R:OTHER for my own -> to myself
 [explain]:  Own (= belonging to you, or only to be used by you) is used either in front of a noun, or in the phrase of your own: ‘I wish I had my own car.’ ‘I wish I had a car of my own.’ have sb/sth (all) to yourself = be the only person or people in a place , using something, talking to someone, etc: ‘After the children had gone, we had the house all to ourselves.’
[wrong sentence]:  I had the whole beach for my own.


R:OTHER his own -> himself
 [explain]:  do/want sth for yourself (NOT for your own): ‘He’s kept all the money for himself.’
[wrong sentence]:  He’s decided to resign and work for his own.


R:OTHER their own -> themselves
 [explain]:  do/want sth for yourself (NOT for your own): ‘He’s kept all the money for himself.’
[wrong sentence]:  They want everything for their own.


R:OTHER very painful -> a lot of pain
 [explain]:  painful = causing pain: ‘The finger I trapped in the door is still very painful.’ ‘The child wriggled free and gave me a painful kick on the ankle.’
[wrong sentence]:  The operation was successful but I still feel very painful.


R:OTHER i beg your pardon -> i’m sorry
 [explain]:  I’d like to beg your pardon because I was late.
[wrong sentence]:  I beg your pardon, but I was very busy and couldn’t spare the time.


R:OTHER take part in -> go to
 [explain]:  go to a party/wedding etc (NOT take part in): ‘We can’t go to the party if we haven’t been invited.’
[wrong sentence]:  Did you take part in Yolanda’s party on Saturday?


R:OTHER take part in -> join
 [explain]:  join a club/society etc (NOT take part in): ‘Guy is thinking about joining the drama society.’
[wrong sentence]:  I think you ought to take part in a club.


R:OTHER partly -> to some extent
 [explain]:  At the end of a clause use to some extent/to a certain extent or to some degree/to a (certain) degree (NOT partly). Compare: ‘I partly agree with him.’ ‘I agree with him to some extent.’
[wrong sentence]:  Putting fluoride in the water may solve the problem partly.


R:OTHER past -> pass
 [explain]:  The past tense and past participle of the verb pass is passed (NOT past): ‘She’s passed all her exams.’ ‘I passed him in the corridor but he didn’t say anything.’ ‘These remedies have been passed down from one generation to the next.’ Past is (1) an adjective: ‘For the past week he’s been ill in bed.’ (2) a preposition: ‘She walked past me very quickly.’ (3) an adverb: ‘She walked past very quickly.’ (4) a noun: ‘He never speaks about his past.’
[wrong sentence]:  Several taxis past me without stopping.
[wrong sentence]:  I was 8 years old when my father past away.


R:OTHER period of time -> while
 [explain]:  a (long/short) time/while (WITHOUT period): ‘You can stay here for a while, if you like.’ ‘Learning a foreign language can take a very long time.’
[wrong sentence]:  She needs somewhere to live for a period of time.


R:OTHER the -> (his
 [explain]:  (their/his mother’s/the headmaster’s) permission but NOT the permission: ‘I’m sure that they’ll let you use the library but it’s better to have official permission.’
[wrong sentence]:  Eventually he gave me the permission to stay at home.


R:OTHER every person -> everyone
 [explain]:  everyone/anyone etc (NOT every/any person): ‘Not everyone has leadership potential.’ See also
[wrong sentence]:  Very soon every person will have a portable computer.


R:OTHER any person -> anyone
 [explain]:  everyone/anyone etc (NOT every/any person): ‘Not everyone has leadership potential.’ See also
[wrong sentence]:  Any person can vote in the election.


R:OTHER personal -> personnel
 [explain]:  See Note at PERSONNEL
[wrong sentence]:  The meals can be improved if the canteen personal agree to cooperate.


R:OTHER personnel -> personal
 [explain]:  personal /ˈpəːsənəl/ (adjective) = concerning or belonging to one person in particular; individual or private: ‘The novel is based on the author’s own personal experience.’ ‘Our bank manager assured us that she would give the matter her personal attention.’ personnel /pɜrsə’nel/ /(noun) = all the people employed in a company, office etc: ‘In the event of a fire, all personnel must report to the reception area.’ ‘The personnel officer wants you to call and arrange an interview.’
[wrong sentence]:  Her books deal mainly with personnel relationships, especially marital problems.


R:OTHER pitiful -> pity for her
 [explain]:  See PITY 1
[wrong sentence]:  We both felt pitiful when we saw how lonely she was.


R:OTHER to any place -> anywhere
 [explain]:  anywhere = in/to any place: ‘Have you seen my comb anywhere?’
[wrong sentence]:  When I lived at home, my father did not let me go to any place on my own.


R:OTHER a place where -> somewhere
 [explain]:  There was nowhere to park. somewhere/nowhere/anywhere + to-v : ‘She needs somewhere to stay.’
[wrong sentence]:  He was looking for a place where to spend the night.


R:OTHER pure -> fresh/drinking
 [explain]:  Use pure when you mean ‘completely clean’: ‘The water in the lake is so pure that you can drink it.’ The usual word for describing air and water is fresh: ‘Her doctor has prescribed a good holiday and lots of fresh air.’
[wrong sentence]:  I always keep a few bottles of pure water on the boat.


R:OTHER went quickly -> run
 [explain]:  Go quickly usually sounds unnatural. The same meaning can be expressed by verbs such as hurry, rush, run, dash, sprint, etc: ‘As soon as we saw the rain clouds, we hurried back home.’
[wrong sentence]:  I went quickly to the nearest phone booth.


R:OTHER rainy -> rain
 [explain]:  When rainy is used it generally comes in front of words like day, afternoon, Sunday and season (NOT after a verb): ‘The baby was born on a rainy Sunday in June.’ ‘When does the rainy season begin?’ The more usual word is wet: ‘Apart from a couple of wet afternoons, the weather was fine.’ ‘If it’s wet tomorrow, we’ll go on Monday instead.’
[wrong sentence]:  It’s rainy again today.


R:OTHER rare -> unusual
 [explain]:  If something is different from what is usual, it is unusual: ‘The bread had an unusual flavour.’ ‘Kit.
[wrong sentence]:  I think that my name is rare.


R:OTHER rather -> prefer
 [explain]:  When you mean ‘prefer’, use prefer/would rather/would prefer (NOT rather on its own): ‘She says she’d rather speak to you herself about it.’
[wrong sentence]:  They rather violent films to romantic ones.


R:OTHER i -> i’d
 [explain]:  When you mean ‘prefer’, use prefer/would rather/would prefer (NOT rather on its own): ‘She says she’d rather speak to you herself about it.’
[wrong sentence]:  I rather take him to somewhere less crowded.


R:OTHER rather -> prefer to
 [explain]:  When you mean ‘prefer’, use prefer/would rather/would prefer (NOT rather on its own): ‘She says she’d rather speak to you herself about it.’
[wrong sentence]:  I rather take him to somewhere less crowded.


R:OTHER because -> why
 [explain]:  The weather here is always warm and this is the reason because there are so many public swimming pools. The weather here is always warm and this is (the reason) why there are so many public swimming pools. reason why (NOT because/how): ‘Most of you are aware of (the reason) why I’ve called this meeting.’ reason for doing sth: ‘Most of you are aware of my reason for calling this meeting.’
[wrong sentence]:  The main reason because I am here is to study for my Master’s degree.


R:OTHER robbed him -> steal
 [explain]:  steal sth: ‘Someone has stolen her car.’ ‘My watch has been stolen.’ ‘The girl has obviously stolen his heart.’ rob sb (of sth): ‘The two youths had tried to rob him, but he managed to fight them off.’ ‘The old couple were robbed of their life’s savings.’
[wrong sentence]:  While he was asleep, she robbed him his watch.


R:OTHER kitchen’s floor -> floor of the kitchen
 [explain]:  The possessive ‘s/s’ is used mainly with nouns that refer to people and animals. Compare: ‘All her father’s tools were missing.’ (NOT ‘tools of her father’) ‘The handles of the tools were all made of wood’ (NOT tools’ handles’) Note also that some nouns can be used like adjectives (WITHOUT ‘s/s’): ‘the kitchen floor’, ‘a garden shed’, ‘a pencil sharpener’.
[wrong sentence]:  The children were playing marbles on the kitchen’s floor.


R:OTHER safety -> safely
 [explain]:  Safety is a noun (NOT an adjective/adverb): ‘The boy has been missing for six days, and there are fears for his safety.’ ‘The firefighters rescued the children and carried them to safety.’
[wrong sentence]:  Promise that you will drive safety.


R:OTHER as -> the
 [explain]:  the same as or (just) like (NOT as same as): ‘New York was not the same as I’d imagined. It was worse.’
[wrong sentence]:  I’d like to visit Vietnam and Malaysia one day, as same as you.


R:OTHER as same as -> (just) like
 [explain]:  the same as or (just) like (NOT as same as): ‘New York was not the same as I’d imagined. It was worse.’
[wrong sentence]:  She had a face as same as a beauty queen’s.


R:OTHER said to -> tell
 [explain]:  tell sb to do sth (NOT say): ‘I told them to wait for you outside.’
[wrong sentence]:  She said to me to ask you to phone her.


R:OTHER several -> a few
 [explain]:  Several means ‘some but not many’: ‘Chicken will keep for several days in a refrigerator.’ When you are talking about a very small number, use a few: ‘He was here just a few minutes ago so he can’t be far away.’
[wrong sentence]:  Try to forget your work for several hours and relax.
[wrong sentence]:  The phrase ‘the role of women’ appeared just several years ago.


R:OTHER shame -> embarrass
 [explain]:  See ASHAMEO she, s/he See Language Note at HE
[wrong sentence]:  The Chinese feel shame when they see people kissing in public.


R:OTHER slept -> went to bed
 [explain]:  sleep = be asleep: ‘Nurse Burnley works all night and sleeps all day.’ go to bed = get into bed at the end of the day: ‘After dinner I had a hot bath and then went to bed.’
[wrong sentence]:  I slept at ten o’clock last night.


R:OTHER started sleeping again -> went back to sleep
 [explain]:  go to sleep or fall asleep = enter a state of unconsciousness, especially so that your mind and body can rest: ‘As soon as I started to read her a story, she went to sleep.’ go back to sleep or fall asleep again = go to sleep again: ‘The alarm clock rang at 7.45 but I just turned over and went back to sleep.’
[wrong sentence]:  After showing the conductor my ticket, I started sleeping again.


R:OTHER so -> such
 [explain]:  A phrase that ends with a noun (‘weather’, ‘fool’) usually begins with such (NOT so): ‘We hadn’t expected such a warm welcome.’ ‘You’re lucky to have such delightful children.’
[wrong sentence]:  We were not prepared for so cold weather.


R:OTHER a very fast -> top
 [explain]:  at great/high/top/full/breakneck speed: ‘He jumped into the car and drove off at great speed.’
[wrong sentence]:  He always drives at a very fast speed.


R:OTHER strange people -> strangers
 [explain]:  A strange person is someone who behaves in a way that other people find disturbing or difficult to understand : ‘I find Barbara a bit strange. She won’t look at you even when you speak to her.’ A stranger is someone you have never met before: ‘We’ve told our children not to accept sweets from strangers .’
[wrong sentence]:  The children were told not to talk to strange people.


R:OTHER style of life  -> lifestyle
 [explain]:  See Note at LIFE 2
[wrong sentence]:  I’d like to live abroad and have a different style of life .


R:OTHER suit -> go with
 [explain]:  When you are talking about the effect of having two different things together, use go with (NOT suit) : ‘I like the wallpaper, but it doesn’t go with the carpet.’ ‘This jacket will go really well with your grey trousers.’
[wrong sentence]:  Red wine doesn’t suit fish.


R:OTHER had a sun bath -> sunbathe
 [explain]:  The noun sunbath does not exist. Use the verb sunbathe /ˈsʌnbeɪð/ or do some sunbathing/go sunbathing: ‘I found him back at the hotel, sunbathing by the pool.’ ‘The sky had clouded over so we couldn’t do any sunbathing.’
[wrong sentence]:  In the afternoon we had a sun bath on the beach.


R:OTHER support -> put up with
 [explain]:  tolerate, put up with, stand sb/sth (NOT support/suffer) = be willing to accept someone or something, even though the person or situation is unpleasant: ‘I’m surprised that she tolerates his behaviour.’ ‘It’s not a bad job, as long as you can stand the long hours.’
[wrong sentence]:  Are you prepared to support the noise of the traffic?


R:OTHER as -> of being
 [explain]:  suspect sb of (doing/being) sth (NOT as): ‘The police suspect her of receiving stolen goods.’ Their father is suspected of being connected with the local mafia.’
[wrong sentence]:  Army intelligence suspected him as a secret agent.


R:OTHER televisions -> television
 [explain]:  Television (also TV) is usually uncountable: ‘The children watch a lot of television.’ ‘The article explains how television affects family life.’ As a countable noun it means ‘a television set’: ‘Some parents buy two televisions - one for the children to watch and one for themselves.’
[wrong sentence]:  The whole world watched the cup final on televisions.


R:OTHER have a tendency -> tend
 [explain]:  To introduce an opinion, use tend to (NOT tendency): ‘She tends to think that small firms should receive greater government support .’
[wrong sentence]:  I have a tendency to agree with the statement.


R:OTHER i -> i’d like to
 [explain]:  Use thanks (informal), thank you or (especially in formal styles) I would/should like to thank you (NOT I thank you): ‘On behalf of the team, I’d like to thank you for your invaluable advice and support.’
[wrong sentence]:  First I thank you for answering my letter so quickly.


R:OTHER that you give -> for giving
 [explain]:  thank sb for doing sth: Thank you for being so understanding .’
[wrong sentence]:  Thank you that you give me a chance to explain the situation.


R:OTHER that -> when
 [explain]:  When the meaning is ‘at/on/in/during which’ (referring to time), use when (NOT that) : ‘These are the times when Dr Roberts will be able to see you.’ Compare: ‘I shall always remember the two days that I spent in Paris.’
[wrong sentence]:  The weather has been very good, except for two days that it rained.


R:OTHER that -> since/as
 [explain]:  When giving a reason for something, use since or as (NOT that): ‘Instead of cooking, why don’t we get a take-away, especially as it’s so late.’
[wrong sentence]:  It worried me that the letter had not arrived, especially that it had never happened before.


R:OTHER that -> so
 [explain]:  To make a precise reference to a previously mentioned action, use do so (NOT do that): ‘I asked him politely to take his feet off the seat but he refused to do so.’
[wrong sentence]:  If you haven’t sent it yet, I’d be pleased if you would do that as soon as possible.


R:OTHER till -> as far as
 [explain]:  See Note at UNTIL 1
[wrong sentence]:  I managed to drive the car till the nearest hospital.


R:OTHER titled ‘a -> entitle
 [explain]:  To introduce the name of a book, story, film, article, song, painting etc, use be called or, especially in formal styles, (be) entitled: ‘Her latest novel is called Educating Peter.’ Compare: ‘The title of her latest novel is Educating Peter.’
[wrong sentence]:  She sent me an article from Life Magazine titled ‘A Soldier’s Anguish’.


R:OTHER more deeply -> better
 [explain]:  understand sb/sth properly/better; fully/really understand sb/sth (NOT deeply): ‘Make sure that you understand the instructions properly before you begin.’ ‘Nobody fully understands how the drug actually works.’ Note the alternative: ‘Reading helps us to develop our understanding of the world.’
[wrong sentence]:  Reading helps us to understand the world more deeply.


R:OTHER unless -> in case
 [explain]:  When you are talking about something that is done as a precaution, use in case (NOT unless): ‘You should take a book with you in case you have to wait.’
[wrong sentence]:  Bring something to eat unless you get hungry.


R:OTHER until -> (all the way) to
 [explain]:  Until and till are usually used in connection with time: The shops are open until six o’clock on weekdays.’
[wrong sentence]:  The new tunnel means that we can drive until London.


R:OTHER usually -> usual
 [explain]:  as usual (NOT as usually): ‘John’s late as usual.’ ‘As usual, everyone was out in the garden when I arrived.’ ; the same as usual (NOT ... as usually): ‘Apart from his hair, he looked the same as usual.’
[wrong sentence]:  The food wasn’t the same as usually.


R:OTHER in vain -> to no avail
 [explain]:  In vain usually comes after verbs such as try, search, hunt, look, wait, fight etc or after the verb be: ‘Her voice was beginning to rise and she tried in vain to control it.’ ‘A team of surgeons battled in vain to save him.’ ‘I was never in any doubt that my efforts would be in vain.’ In other situations, use without success or to no avail: ‘They did everything they could to protect her, but (all) to no avail.
[wrong sentence]:  She tried to get him to listen but in vain.


R:OTHER watch -> look at
 [explain]:  look at/admire/enjoy the view (NOT see/watch): ‘We asked the coach driver to stop so that we could look at the view.’ ; have/get a (good) view from a particular place (NOT see): ‘If you stand where I am, you get a much better view.’
[wrong sentence]:  Come over here and watch the view.


R:OTHER visited -> went to
 [explain]:  You visit places that are of special interest, especially when you are a tourist or official guest: ‘By the end of the day I was fed up with visiting museums and all the travelling around.’ ‘Later in the day Her Majesty will be visiting the Great Ormond Street Children’s Hospital.’ You go to a restaurant, market, library, someone’s house etc: ‘If it rains, we could always go to the cinema.’ ‘I’ve got to go to the dentist’s.’
[wrong sentence]:  Afterwards we visited a restaurant for dinner.


R:OTHER voluntaries -> voluntary helpers
 [explain]:  Voluntary is an adjective (NOT a noun). It describes someone who agrees to work without being paid, or work that is not paid: ‘On Saturdays she does voluntary work at an old people’s home.’
[wrong sentence]:  The hospital could not operate without voluntaries.


R:OTHER voted -> elect
 [explain]:  vote = show which person or which course of action you prefer, especially by marking a piece of paper or raising your hand: ‘Who will you be voting for at the next election?’ ‘Fifty-three per cent of Danes voted in favour of the Maastricht treaty.’ elect = choose a leader or representative by voting: ‘Roman Catholic cardinals are meeting at the
[wrong sentence]:  Next year a new president will be voted.


R:OTHER wait for -> expect
 [explain]:  wait = stay somewhere until someone or something comes: ‘I’ll wait here until you get back.’ ‘It’s quicker to walk than wait for a bus.’ expect = believe that someone or something is going to come: ‘The train is expected to arrive in the next five minutes.’ ‘I can’t leave the house I’m expecting visitors.’
[wrong sentence]:  Anyway, I’II wait for you next weekend.


R:OTHER wanna -> want to
 [explain]:  See Note at GONNA
[wrong sentence]:  We didn’t wanna miss the train.


R:OTHER four weeks -> four-week
 [explain]:  See Language Note at HUNDRED See Language Note AT TIME
[wrong sentence]:  We knew that a four weeks holiday would bring us closer together.


R:OTHER well -> fine
 [explain]:  Well is usually used as an adverb: ‘The team played well on Saturday.’ As an adjective, well usually means ‘in good health’: ‘George can’t come because he’s not very well.’ ‘He always looks well after a holiday.’
[wrong sentence]:  Everything was well until somebody came and sat in the seat next to mine.


R:OTHER completely wet -> soak
 [explain]:  soaked or soaking wet = extremely wet: ‘Don’t leave the cushions in the garden. If it rains, they’ll get soaking wet.’
[wrong sentence]:  It started pouring with rain and we all got completely wet.


R:OTHER what -> (that
 [explain]:  What is not used as a relative pronoun. After all, everything, anything etc, use that or nothing: ‘You can have anything (that) you like.’ ‘I have everything (that) I need for the time being.’
[wrong sentence]:  A woman can do everything what a man can do.


R:OTHER whom -> (that
 [explain]:  Whom is used only in formal styles: Those applicants whom the selection committee recommends for interview should be contacted without delay.’ The police officer to whom the crime was first reported has been transferred to another unit.’ In non-formal styles, use that/who or nothing: The man (that) she intends to marry comes from Stockport.’ ‘The girl (that) you were speaking to is a friend of mine.’ In non-identifying clauses, however, only who is possible: ‘Both girls - who you may remember from Helen’s party -have got places at Oxford.’
[wrong sentence]:  Most of the people whom I met were very kind.


R:OTHER work -> job
 [explain]:  See Language Note at OCCUPATION
[wrong sentence]:  Without a full-time work, some people think it’s not worth living.


R:OTHER doesn’t -> isn’t
 [explain]:  be worth £20lvery little/a fortune etc: ‘These old computers aren’t worth much nowadays.’
[wrong sentence]:  A holiday doesn’t worth all the effort it takes preparing for it.


R:OTHER to see -> see
 [explain]:  it’s worth doing sth; sth is worth doing (NOT to do): ‘It’s worth remembering that these old cars can be very expensive to run.’
[wrong sentence]:  The museum was certainly worth to see.


R:OTHER yours faithfully.. -> yours/with love/with best wishes..
 [explain]:  Yours faithfully and Yours sincerely are used only in formal letters. At the end of a letter to a friend or relative, use Yours, With love, With best wishes, etc.
[wrong sentence]:  Dear Mary Yours faithfully...


R:OTHER yours sincerely.. -> yours/with love/with best wishes..
 [explain]:  Yours faithfully and Yours sincerely are used only in formal letters. At the end of a letter to a friend or relative, use Yours, With love, With best wishes, etc.
[wrong sentence]:  Dear John, Yours sincerely...


R:VERB leaving -> to leave
 [explain]:  be about to do sth (NOT doing) Compare: ‘I was just leaving when the telephone rang.’
[wrong sentence]:  I was about leaving when the telephone rang.


R:VERB accept -> agree
 [explain]:  You accept someone’s advice, opinion, or suggestion BUT you agree (= say you are willing) to do something. Compare: ‘I accepted her suggestion and agreed to see the doctor that evening.’
[wrong sentence]:  The company will not accept to buy new machines.


R:VERB agree -> agree
 [explain]:  thank sb for doing sth: Thank you for being so understanding .’
[wrong sentence]:  Thank you for agree to listen.


R:VERB accept -> allow
 [explain]:  You allow/permit someone to do something, or let them do it: ‘Many parents do not allow/permit their children to watch violent films.’ ‘Many parents do not let their children watch violent films.’
[wrong sentence]:  We can’t accept a motorway to be built through our town.
[wrong sentence]:  The driver did not accept me to get on the bus.


R:VERB accomplish -> achieve
 [explain]:  When you accomplish something, you manage to do it or complete it, especially something that gives you satisfaction: ‘She felt that she could accomplish more through journalism.’ ‘During his five years as President, he accomplished very little.’ When you achieve something, you manage to do or obtain what you have planned to do or obtain, especially after a lot of effort: ‘The company intends to achieve all these goals within the next five years.’ ‘By the age of twenty, she had already achieved her ambition.’ Note that achieve is also used to mean ‘accomplish’: ‘By the end of the course, you will feel that you’ve really achieved something.’
[wrong sentence]:  To accomplish world unity, we need peace.


R:VERB accomplished -> achieve
 [explain]:  When you accomplish something, you manage to do it or complete it, especially something that gives you satisfaction: ‘She felt that she could accomplish more through journalism.’ ‘During his five years as President, he accomplished very little.’ When you achieve something, you manage to do or obtain what you have planned to do or obtain, especially after a lot of effort: ‘The company intends to achieve all these goals within the next five years.’ ‘By the age of twenty, she had already achieved her ambition.’ Note that achieve is also used to mean ‘accomplish’: ‘By the end of the course, you will feel that you’ve really achieved something.’
[wrong sentence]:  A balanced diet is accomplished by eating many different kinds of food.


R:VERB satisfy -> achieve
 [explain]:  achieve your ambition (NOT satisfy, meet, reach, arrive etc): ‘He has achieved his main ambition - to make a name for himself in politics.’
[wrong sentence]:  Not many people manage to satisfy their ambitions.


R:VERB obtain -> achieve
 [explain]:  When you are talking about something that takes a long time and a great amount of work or effort, use achieve (NOT obtain): ‘By the end of the course you really feel that you have achieved something.’ ‘The company intends to achieve all these goals within the next five years.’ ‘Her only purpose in life was to achieve stardom.’
[wrong sentence]:  With these new policies the government hopes to obtain economic stability.
[wrong sentence]:  It has taken women a long time to obtain equality.


R:VERB act -> behave
 [explain]:  It is time that human beings learned how to act properly, without killing each other. It is time that human beings learned how to behave properly, without killing each other. When you are talking about what someone does on a particular occasion, act and behave are interchangeable: ‘Passengers who left the flight in Rhodes said that they had seen two men acting/behaving very suspiciously.’ When you are talking generally about what someone does or what people do, use behave: ‘You can’t expect all babies to behave the same.’ ‘From the way he behaves, anyone would think that he doesn’t get paid.’
[wrong sentence]:  I am interested in the way people act towards each other.


R:VERB admire -> see
 [explain]:  Do not use admire when you just mean ‘see’ or ‘look at’. Admire means ‘look at someone or something with a strong feeling of pleasure’. This meaning of admire is found mainly in novels and tourist brochures: ‘Come and admire the magnificence of the Niagara Falls.’ ‘Rupert was sitting outside on the verandah, admiring the many jewels in the night sky.’
[wrong sentence]:  One hour is not long enough to admire all the exhibits.


R:VERB look -> see
 [explain]:  see = find out (by checking): ‘I’ll go and see whether they have any rooms available.’ ‘While you’re in the kitchen, can you see if the eggs are cooked?’
[wrong sentence]:  Wait here and I’ll look if I can find him for you.


R:VERB meet -> see
 [explain]:  When you greet someone you know well, or talk about meeting them, use see (NOT meet): ‘I hope you’ll come and see us again soon.’
[wrong sentence]:  I can’t wait to meet you again at Christmas.
[wrong sentence]:  Hello John. It’s good to meet you.


R:VERB notice -> see
 [explain]:  Notice is not used with can/could: ‘They were so busy that they didn’t notice how late it was getting.’
[wrong sentence]:  I could notice that he was not enjoying himself.


R:VERB admired -> enjoy
 [explain]:  Do not use admire when you mean ‘enjoy’. Admire means ‘have a very high opinion of someone’: ‘I’ve always admired people who think for themselves.’ ‘Lewis was probably best known and admired for his work on medieval literature.’
[wrong sentence]:  Everybody admired your talk because it was lively and interesting.
[wrong sentence]:  Although it was a sad film, I admired it very much.


R:VERB adore -> like/enjoy/love
 [explain]:  Adore usually expresses a very strong feeling: ‘She adores her grandchildren and is always buying them presents.’ ‘The one singer I absolutely adore is Whitney Houston.’ If you use adore to mean ‘like/ enjoy/love’, you may sound insincere.
[wrong sentence]:  I adore reading too.
[wrong sentence]:  I adore meeting new people.


R:VERB to live -> live
 [explain]:  advise sb to do sth: ‘I advised her to see a lawyer.’ advise doing sth (NOT to do): ‘I would advise leaving very early. Then you’ll miss all the traffic.’
[wrong sentence]:  Nowadays many doctors advise to live in the countryside.


R:VERB live -> live
 [explain]:  See Note at USE 3
[wrong sentence]:  We soon got used to live in the countryside.


R:VERB would live -> live
 [explain]:  When you are talking about the present or the future and you imagine something that is untrue or unlikely, use the past tense (‘lived’) in the if clause (NOT would): ‘If I knew her address, I’d send her a postcard.’ ‘If I won a lot of money, I’d buy a new car.’
[wrong sentence]:  If I would live in the countryside, I would be much healthier.


R:VERB paying -> to pay
 [explain]:  afford (to do) sth: ‘Not many couples can afford to run two cars nowadays.’
[wrong sentence]:  My father couldn’t afford paying for my education.


R:VERB aid -> help
 [explain]:  Aid is mainly used as a noun: ‘Many countries survive on foreign aid from richer neighbours.’ ‘It is government policy to provide aid to the homeless or the unemployed.’ As a verb, aid is used in formal styles and usually means ‘help something recover, develop, grow, etc’: ‘The country’s economic recovery has been aided by the recent peace agreement.’
[wrong sentence]:  Education can aid us to understand our world.
[wrong sentence]:  We must begin to aid ourselves and not wait for other countries.


R:VERB assist -> help
 [explain]:  Assist is used only in formal styles. See Note at ASSIST 1.
[wrong sentence]:  Ask Susie to assist you.


R:VERB avoid -> help
 [explain]:  can’t help doing sth = cannot stop yourself from doing something: ‘Whenever he tries to sing, I just can’t help laughing.’ I told him that we would be grateful if he would kindly avoid ringing our doorbell after midnight. I told him that we would be grateful if he would kindly refrain from ringing our doorbell after midnight. refrain from doing sth = (formal) stop yourself from doing something because other people do not like it: ‘Hotel guests are asked to refrain from smoking in the restaurants.’
[wrong sentence]:  I couldn’t avoid falling in love with her.
[wrong sentence]:  He is such a nice man that you can’t avoid liking him.


R:VERB have -> have
 [explain]:  amount of (singular) + uncountable noun + singular verb amounts of (plural) + uncountable noun + plural verb
[wrong sentence]:  A tremendous amount of research have been carried out.
[wrong sentence]:  The amount of crime have increased.


 [explain]:  each of + plural noun/pronoun + singular verb: ‘Each of the three children was given a balloon.’
[wrong sentence]:  Each of us have a room on the top floor.


 [explain]:  every + noun + singular verb: ‘Every child was given a red balloon.’
[wrong sentence]:  Every job have its good points.


 [explain]:  Everybody, everyone and everything are used with a singular verb: ‘Everybody was tired of waiting.’ ‘During the first few days, everything was new to me.’
[wrong sentence]:  Everybody have to work.


R:VERB take -> have
 [explain]:  In British English the phrase is have a bath (NOT take): ‘There isn’t enough water to have a bath.’
[wrong sentence]:  I always take a bath before going to bed.


 [explain]:  In British English the phrase is have a meal (NOT take): ‘In the evening we had a three-course meal.’ In American English both take a meal and have a meal are used, although take a meal (more formal) is not very common.
[wrong sentence]:  We always take our meals in the canteen.


R:VERB bear -> have
 [explain]:  Bear (sb) a child is used only in formal styles and refers to the physical process of giving birth: ‘She bore her husband two daughters and one son.’ The subject of bear is always a woman. When you are talking about beginning a family or the number of children in a family, use have (NOT bear): ‘We’d like 10 have children while we’re still young.’
[wrong sentence]:  The government also controls the number of children that a couple can bear.


R:VERB should -> have
 [explain]:  had better (not): ‘If the phone rings again, you’d better answer it.’ ‘If it’s a secret, you’d better not tell me.’
[wrong sentence]:  My friends warned me that I should better be careful.


R:VERB eat -> have
 [explain]:  have (your) breakfast/lunch/dinner etc (NOT eat/take): ‘We had dinner in the hotel restaurant.’ have sth for breakfast/lunch/dinner etc: ‘What did you have for lunch?’ Do not use eat unless you wish to emphasize the activity of eating. Compare: ‘We prefer to have dinner in the evening.’ ‘James always takes a long time to eat his dinner.’
[wrong sentence]:  There wasn’t enough time to eat breakfast.


R:VERB taking -> have
 [explain]:  have (your) breakfast/lunch/dinner etc (NOT eat/take): ‘We had dinner in the hotel restaurant.’ have sth for breakfast/lunch/dinner etc: ‘What did you have for lunch?’ Do not use eat unless you wish to emphasize the activity of eating. Compare: ‘We prefer to have dinner in the evening.’ ‘James always takes a long time to eat his dinner.’
[wrong sentence]:  While I am taking breakfast, I listen to the radio.


 [explain]:  See Language Note at DO
[wrong sentence]:  My mother is taking an operation tomorrow.


R:VERB was -> have
 [explain]:  When you are talking about an event, use die (dying, died, died). When you are talking about a state, use be dead. Compare: ‘He died in an ambulance on the way to hospital.’ ‘By the time the ambulance reached the hospital, he was dead.’
[wrong sentence]:  Her husband was died two years earlier.


 [explain]:  Disappeared (from the verb disappear) is not used like an adjective: ‘His wife has disappeared with the children.’ ‘Suddenly, the pain in my back just disappeared.’ Compare: ‘The next morning, the snowman was gone.’ Unlike disappeared, gone (from the verb go) may be used like an adjective.
[wrong sentence]:  The next morning, the snowman was disappeared.


R:VERB made -> have
 [explain]:  have an experience (NOT make): ‘The streets were full of beggars and we had one or two very unpleasant experiences.’ See Language Note at DO
[wrong sentence]:  While I was going home, I made a bad experience.
[wrong sentence]:  I made my first teaching experience in Scotland.


 [explain]:  have no/little/some/considerable etc experience (NOT make): ‘Most of the school inspectors had at least ten years’ teaching experience.’ See Language Note at DO
[wrong sentence]:  I hadn’t made any experience of changing a car wheel.


 [explain]:  have fun: ‘Everyone needs to have a little fun now and again.’ See Language Note at DO
[wrong sentence]:  While on holiday I made lots of fun.


 [explain]:  See Language Note at DO
[wrong sentence]:  It’s time you made a holiday.


 [explain]:  See Language Note at DO
[wrong sentence]:  I made lots of fun in London.


R:VERB get -> have
 [explain]:  have fun: ‘Everyone needs to have a little fun now and again.’ See Language Note at DO
[wrong sentence]:  I’m sure that you will get a lot of fun here.


 [explain]:  See Language Note at DO
[wrong sentence]:  Mr Barrett is going to get an operation on his back.


R:VERB was having -> have
 [explain]:  See Language Note at CONTAIN
[wrong sentence]:  I stayed at home yesterday because I was having a bad cold.


R:VERB make -> have
 [explain]:  See Language Note at DO
[wrong sentence]:  At Easter a lot of pensioners go to Lugano to make a holiday.


 [explain]:  have/hold/throw a party (NOT make/celebrate): ‘Let’s have a party and invite all our friends.’ celebrate Christmas, the New Year, someone’s birthday, retirement, promotion etc: ‘Next month we’re having a party to celebrate our sixth wedding anniversary.’ A party is held somewhere (NOT made/celebrated): ‘Where is the garden party being held?’ See Language Note at DO
[wrong sentence]:  On Christmas Day we always make a big party.


 [explain]:  See Language Note at DO
[wrong sentence]:  We decided to make a picnic in the field opposite the house.


R:VERB cause -> have
 [explain]:  See Language Note at DO
[wrong sentence]:  I doubt whether it will cause an impact on the readers.


R:VERB doing -> have
 [explain]:  have a lesson (NOT do/follow): ‘I didn’t go into the classroom because the children were having a lesson.’ have/take lessons (= arrange for someone to teach you): ‘If you want to improve your English, you should have/take lessons’
[wrong sentence]:  After lunch we’re doing a history lesson.


R:VERB follow -> have
 [explain]:  have a lesson (NOT do/follow): ‘I didn’t go into the classroom because the children were having a lesson.’ have/take lessons (= arrange for someone to teach you): ‘If you want to improve your English, you should have/take lessons’
[wrong sentence]:  I follow lessons on Thursdays and Fridays.


R:VERB making -> have
 [explain]:  See Language Note at DO
[wrong sentence]:  On Saturday we’re making a party.


R:VERB celebrating -> have
 [explain]:  have/hold/throw a party (NOT make/celebrate): ‘Let’s have a party and invite all our friends.’ celebrate Christmas, the New Year, someone’s birthday, retirement, promotion etc: ‘Next month we’re having a party to celebrate our sixth wedding anniversary.’ A party is held somewhere (NOT made/celebrated): ‘Where is the garden party being held?’ See Language Note at DO
[wrong sentence]:  Next Saturday we’re celebrating a small party at John’s house.


R:VERB to have -> have
 [explain]:  Regret is followed by a to-infinitive in formal letters to introduce a piece of bad news: ‘I regret to inform you that your application for a scholarship has been unsuccessful.’ When you are talking about the past, use regret doing sth or regret that: ‘I have often regretted leaving the police force.’ ‘I wonder if they ever regret not having gone to live abroad?’ ‘Now that it was too late, he regretted that he hadn’t worked harder at school.’
