[wrong sentence]:  I now regret not to have worked harder at school.


R:VERB spent -> have
 [explain]:  have a good/marvellous time (NOT spend): ‘In those days all I cared about was having a good time.’
[wrong sentence]:  We spent a very good time in New York.


R:VERB would have -> have
 [explain]:  When you are talking about the past and you imagine something that is impossible, use the past perfect tense (‘had caught’) in the if clause (NOT would have): ‘If he hadn’t got on the plane, he would still be alive.’
[wrong sentence]:  If you would have caught the earlier train, we could have travelled together.


R:VERB annoys -> irritate
 [explain]:  Something unpleasant that happens repeatedly or continuously over a long period of time tends to irritate or frustrate people, especially because they know that they can do nothing to stop it: ‘I felt so tense that even the ticking of the clock began to irritate me.’ ‘I am constantly frustrated by all the niggling little jobs I have to do.’
[wrong sentence]:  The noise of the traffic outside all day annoys me.


R:VERB annoyed -> disturb
 [explain]:  Someone who is emotionally shocked by something they see or read is disturbed/upset/distressed/offended it, they disturbing/upsetting/distressing/offensive: ‘Viewers are warned that this documentary contains a number of violent scenes which they may find disturbing.’
[wrong sentence]:  Some people are annoyed by these violent films.


R:VERB interrupts -> disturb
 [explain]:  interrupt = to stop someone while they are speaking or doing something, specially by saying something yourself: ‘I’m sorry to interrupt but there’s an urgent phone call for you.’ disturb = make it difficult for someone to continue what they are doing: ‘Will it disturb you if I listen to the radio?’ ‘I think we’re disturbing Martin. Let’s go and talk in your office.’
[wrong sentence]:  The noise of the traffic interrupts the local residents.


R:VERB are -> be
 [explain]:  anybody/anyone + singular verb: ‘If anyone calls, tell them I’ll be back after lunch.’
[wrong sentence]:  Anyone are welcome to join us.


 [explain]:  Mathematics (the subject) is an uncountable noun: ‘Mathematics is compulsory. Even if you don’t like it, you still have to take it.’
[wrong sentence]:  Mathematics are my favourite subject.


 [explain]:  News is an uncountable noun: ‘Why do Clive’s letters never contain any news?’ There’s one piece of news that I’m sure will interest you.’
[wrong sentence]:  The news are never very good nowadays.


 [explain]:  the number of ... + singular verb: ‘The number of people claiming unemployment benefit has risen by 5 per cent in the last three months.’
[wrong sentence]:  The number of heavy smokers are decreasing.


 [explain]:  When each of the nouns joined by or is singular, the verb is usually singular: ‘It is important to understand what one’s son or daughter expects out of life.’
[wrong sentence]:  I think the microphone or the recording mechanism are broken.


R:VERB have been -> be
 [explain]:  Use ‘I was born’, ‘They were born’, etc (NOT ‘I have been born’ or ‘I am born’): ‘I’ll never forget the day when the seven puppies were born.’
[wrong sentence]:  I have been born in a town just outside Paris.


R:VERB has -> be
 [explain]:  When you describe or enquire about the colour of something, use be (NOT have): ‘What colour was the dress she was wearing?’
[wrong sentence]:  The belt has the same colour as the coat.


 [explain]:  be a certain size (NOT have) : ‘If the boots are size 43, they should fit me.’
[wrong sentence]:  I’m afraid that the pullover you sent me has the wrong size.


R:VERB was -> be
 [explain]:  Despite and in spite of are prepositions (NOT conjunctions). Unlike although (a conjunction), they cannot introduce a clause that has a finite verb (‘was’). Compare: ‘In spite of/Despite owning two cars, he can’t drive.’ ‘Although he owns two cars, he can’t drive.’
[wrong sentence]:  Despite the train was empty, he came and sat in front of me.


 [explain]:  a number of ... + plural verb: ‘A number of viewers have complained about the excessive violence in the film.’
[wrong sentence]:  A large number of cars was parked outside the school.


 [explain]:  People is a plural noun and takes a plural verb: ‘People have been very kind to me.’
[wrong sentence]:  There was few people at the funeral.


 [explain]:  Use there is/was when the following noun is singular/uncountable: ‘There was a letter on the mat.’ ‘There was smoke all over the house.’ ; Use there are/were when the following noun is plural: ‘There were babies crying in every room.’ ; ‘There are many times when I would prefer to be alone.’ ; Note that a lot of, plenty of, etc do not affect the number of the verb. ; Compare: ‘There was a lot of traffic on the road.’ ‘There were a lot of cars on the road.’
[wrong sentence]:  There was four people in the car.
[wrong sentence]:  There was a lot of girls watching the game.


 [explain]:  Use there is/was when the following noun is singular/uncountable: ‘There was a letter on the mat.’ ‘There was smoke all over the house.’ ; Use there are/were when the following noun is plural: ‘There were babies crying in every room.’ ‘There are many times when I would prefer to be alone.’ ; Note that a lot of, plenty of, etc do not affect the number of the verb. ; Compare: ‘There was a lot of traffic on the road.’ ‘There were a lot of cars on the road.’
[wrong sentence]:  There was four people in the car.
[wrong sentence]:  There was a lot of girls watching the game.


 [explain]:  In formal styles, when you are talking about an unreal situation, use were (NOT was): ‘If the motorway were extended, farming would be severely disrupted.’
[wrong sentence]:  Everybody looked at him as if he was from another planet.


R:VERB were -> be
 [explain]:  each of + plural noun/pronoun + singular verb: ‘Each of the three children was given a balloon.’
[wrong sentence]:  Each of the nurses were very kind.


 [explain]:  After a phrase beginning with one of , the verb is singular: ‘One of the main disadvantages is the cost of the battery.’ However when one of is followed by a relative clause, the verb in the relative clause is usually plural: ‘He’s one of those people who are always complaining .’
[wrong sentence]:  One of the eggs were bad.


R:VERB gone -> be
 [explain]:  In British English, been is used when someone has visited a place and returned. Gone is used when the person is still in the place they are visiting. Compare: ‘Peter has gone to Paris.’ (= he has not yet returned) ; ‘Peter has been to Paris.’ (= he has visited Paris and returned) In American English gone can be used instead of been.
[wrong sentence]:  Have you ever gone to France? she asked.


R:VERB is -> be
 [explain]:  See Note at THERE 2
[wrong sentence]:  There is a lot of countries in the same situation.


 [explain]:  the majority + singular/plural verb: ‘The majority is/are in favour of abolishing the death penalty.’ the majority of + plural count noun + plural verb: ‘The majority of voters are in favour of abolishing the death penalty.’
[wrong sentence]:  The majority of motorists is opposed to the scheme.


 [explain]:  Use there is/was when the following noun is singular/uncountable: ‘There was a letter on the mat.’ ‘There was smoke all over the house.’ ; Use there are/were when the following noun is plural: ‘There were babies crying in every room.’ ; ‘There are many times when I would prefer to be alone.’ ; Note that a lot of, plenty of, etc do not affect the number of the verb. ; Compare: ‘There was a lot of traffic on the road.’ ‘There were a lot of cars on the road.’
[wrong sentence]:  In Barcelona there is plenty of things for visitors to do.


 [explain]:  Use there is/was when the following noun is singular/uncountable: ‘There was a letter on the mat.’ ‘There was smoke all over the house.’ ; Use there are/were when the following noun is plural: ‘There were babies crying in every room.’ ‘There are many times when I would prefer to be alone.’ ; Note that a lot of, plenty of, etc do not affect the number of the verb. ; Compare: ‘There was a lot of traffic on the road.’ ‘There were a lot of cars on the road.’
[wrong sentence]:  In Barcelona there is plenty of things for visitors to do.


R:VERB tells -> be
 [explain]:  You usually say that a book, story, article etc is about a particular subject: ‘The novel is about the experiences of a young German soldier during the First World War.’ Tell of is used only in literary styles: ‘The poem tells of the deeds of a young Greek soldier during the Trojan War.’ Tell about does not exist.
[wrong sentence]:  The article tells about the history of Rome.


R:VERB will be -> be
 [explain]:  See Language Note at WILL
[wrong sentence]:  Who will look after the children while you will be at work?


R:VERB want -> want
 [explain]:  anybody/anyone + singular verb: ‘If anyone calls, tell them I’ll be back after lunch.’
[wrong sentence]:  If anybody want one, please write your name on the board.


R:VERB desired -> want
 [explain]:  In the meaning ‘want/wish’, desire is usually used as a noun (NOT as a verb): ‘His one desire was to live to see his three grandchildren again.’ ‘I have no desire to go there again. Once is enough.’
[wrong sentence]:  Despite all my money, I desired to live in an ordinary house.


R:VERB to want -> want
 [explain]:  it is no good/use (doing sth) = it is pointless: ‘It’s no good having a car if you can’t drive.’
[wrong sentence]:  It’s no good to want to help the poor if you don’t do anything about it.


R:VERB appears -> occur
 [explain]:  appear = (1) become visible or (suddenly) be seen: ‘Small red patches appeared all over the child’s back.’ ‘A minute later the manager appeared and asked what was wrong.’(2) (of something new) become available or be seen for the first time: ‘The first edition appeared in 1987.’ ‘The new model will not appear in the shops until the end of the year.’ ‘When did dinosaurs first appear?’ occur = (1) (of unplanned events) happen: ‘The crash occurred just minutes after take-off.’ ‘These storms usually occur in the late afternoon.’ (2) exist or be found (in a particular place): ‘The
[wrong sentence]:  Unemployment appears in nearly all developed countries.


R:VERB been arised -> arise
 [explain]:  Arise (arising, arose, arisen) is an intransitive verb, just like arrive, happen etc: ‘When there is a clear written agreement, these misunderstandings do not arise.’ ‘The problem first arose when I tried to get a visa.’ ‘Should the need arise, you could sell the Mercedes and buy something cheaper.’
[wrong sentence]:  These problems have been arised as a result of overpopulation.


R:VERB assassinating -> murder
 [explain]:  See Language Note at KILL
[wrong sentence]:  He was charged with assassinating a taxi-driver.


R:VERB avoid -> prevent
 [explain]:  avoid (doing) sth = be careful not to do something: ‘The best way to lose weight is to avoid eating fatty foods.’ ‘These drugs are dangerous and should be avoided.’ ‘Try to avoid subjects that can cause offence.’ prevent sb/sth (from doing sth) = stop someone or something from doing something; stop something from happening: ‘The wall is supposed to prevent dogs from getting into the garden.’ ‘It is hoped that the new speed limit will help to prevent accidents.’
[wrong sentence]:  These new measures are intended to avoid the spread of the disease.


R:VERB to use -> use
 [explain]:  avoid doing sth (NOT to do): ‘He obviously wants to avoid getting involved in the dispute.’
[wrong sentence]:  I avoid to use a computer for personal letters.


R:VERB use -> use
 [explain]:  When you are talking about a past habit, use used to do sth (with silent d): ‘Before I had the accident I used to cycle to work.’
[wrong sentence]:  In my previous job I use to travel a lot.


 [explain]:  be/get used to (doing) sth (with silent d) = be in or get into the habit of doing/hearing/seeing etc something, so that it no longer seems strange or difficult: ‘Being a city girl, she wasn’t used to sitting on a horse.’ ‘I didn’t like the taste of the water at first, but I’m getting used to it.’
[wrong sentence]:  It took me a long time to get use to the local accent.


R:VERB awoke -> wake
 [explain]:  Awake is used mainly as an adjective: ‘It’s gone ten o’clock and the children are still awake.’ As a verb, awake (WITHOUT up) is used only in literary styles: ‘I awoke to the sound of church bells.’ The usual verb is wake up: ‘She told me that she keeps waking up in the middle of the night.’ ‘I was woken up by a loud whistling noise.’
[wrong sentence]:  The next morning we awoke up at 7 o’clock to catch the train.


R:VERB having -> expect
 [explain]:  have a baby = give birth to a baby: ‘She’s worried about having the baby at home in case there are complications.’ be expecting a baby = be pregnant: ‘Janet is expecting another baby.’ Note that be having a baby can be used to mean ‘be pregnant’ but this should be avoided when there is a danger of confusion.
[wrong sentence]:  Sitting in the next seat was a young woman who was having a baby.


R:VERB waiting -> expect
 [explain]:  wait = stay somewhere until someone or something comes: ‘I’ll wait here until you get back.’ ‘It’s quicker to walk than wait for a bus.’ expect = believe that someone or something is going to come: ‘The train is expected to arrive in the next five minutes.’ ‘I can’t leave the house I’m expecting visitors.’
[wrong sentence]:  I’m waiting a letter from my boyfriend.


R:VERB be -> get
 [explain]:  When talking about a change in state, use get/become/grow + adjective (NOT be): ‘I’ve put a couple of apples in your bag in case you get hungry.’ ‘The children were growing impatient.’
[wrong sentence]:  Meanwhile, Sarah was beginning to be upset.


R:VERB went -> get
 [explain]:  I got out of the car and waited outside the shop.
[wrong sentence]:  I went into the car and turned on the engine.


R:VERB become -> get
 [explain]:  To refer to the time in the evening or at night when the daylight disappears, use get dark or (in formal styles) b: ‘In the winter it gets dark by five o’clock.’ ‘It began to grow dark and so we headed back to the shore.’ To describe what happens when all the lights in a room, building, etc suddenly go out, use go dark or (especially in formal styles) be plunged into darkness: ‘During the last storm the whole town was plunged into darkness.’
[wrong sentence]:  Soon it began to become dark and it was time to go home.


R:VERB go -> get
 [explain]:  When you mean ‘arrive’, use get (to): ‘I got to the classroom five minutes late.’ ‘When does the train get there?’
[wrong sentence]:  How do I go to the Holiday Inn?
[wrong sentence]:  He was lost and didn’t know how to go back home.


R:VERB obtain -> get
 [explain]:  Obtain is mainly used in formal styles: ‘Information about visas and passports can be obtained from your local library.’ The usual word for this meaning is get: ‘How long does it take to get a visa?’
[wrong sentence]:  Where did you obtain the ticket?


R:VERB see -> get
 [explain]:  look at/admire/enjoy the view (NOT see/watch): ‘We asked the coach driver to stop so that we could look at the view.’ ; have/get a (good) view from a particular place (NOT see): ‘If you stand where I am, you get a much better view.’
[wrong sentence]:  From the top of the tower you see a wonderful view of the city.


R:VERB be -> become

R:VERB was -> become
 [explain]:  When talking about a change in state, use become + noun phrase (NOT be): ‘In 1975 she became leader of the Conservative Party.’
[wrong sentence]:  After six months he was the general manager.


R:VERB became -> come
 [explain]:  Become means ‘start to be’ and is followed by an adjective or noun (NOT a verb): ‘We didn’t meet again until after she had become famous.’ ‘Brunei became an independent member of the
[wrong sentence]:  Eventually I became to like Singapore.


R:VERB are coming -> come
 [explain]:  When you mention someone’s country or where something was made or grown, use the present simple tense. Compare: ‘She comes from Germany.’ (= she was born in Germany) ‘She is coming from Germany.’ (= she is travelling from Germany)
[wrong sentence]:  The students who are coming from Japan are hard-working.


R:VERB went -> come
 [explain]:  Come is used for movement towards the speaker/listener: ‘Come in and sit down for a few minutes.’ ‘When can’ come and see you?’
[wrong sentence]:  The next morning she went to see us at the hotel.


R:VERB gets -> come
 [explain]:  come to a halt (NOT get): ‘The car that we’d been following came to a sudden halt outside the bank.’
[wrong sentence]:  Don’t stand up until the bus gets to a halt.


R:VERB originates -> come
 [explain]:  Originate is used in connection with things, ideas, customs etc (NOT people): ‘No one really knows how the solar system originated.’ If you are born in and/or grow up in a particular place, town, country etc, you come from that place: ‘Where does Agneta come from Sweden or Norway?’ ‘Alcohol’ , ‘alcove’ and ‘sofa’ originate in Arabic. ‘Alcohol’, ‘alcove’ and ‘sofa’ come from Arabic. Words that enter a language from other languages come from (or are derived from) those languages: ‘The name ‘terrier’ comes from the Latin word ‘terra’ meaning ‘the earth’.’
[wrong sentence]:  One of our teachers originates from Scotland.


R:VERB become -> come
 [explain]:  come true (NOT become): ‘His dream came true on the day apartheid was abolished.’ ‘If scientists’ predictions come true, the Earth’s temperature will rise by five degrees in the next fifty years.’
[wrong sentence]:  I hope all your dreams become true.


R:VERB became -> begin
 [explain]:  begin/start to do sth (NOT become): ‘My evenings were free and so I began to learn German.’
[wrong sentence]:  When my first child was born, I became to think seriously about my future.


R:VERB becomes -> is becoming
 [explain]:  To refer to something that is still happening or taking place, use the present progressive tense of become: ‘It’s becoming difficult to find somewhere to park.’
[wrong sentence]:  The housing problem in Hong Kong becomes more serious.


R:VERB is belong -> belong
 [explain]:  Belong is a verb (NOT an adjective).
[wrong sentence]:  The Toyota is belong to my wife.
[wrong sentence]:  Patreze is belong to the Williams team.

 [explain]:  See Language Note at DO
[wrong sentence]:  We all made our best to see that they enjoyed themselves.

[wrong sentence]:  Our teacher wanted to know why we hadn’t made our homework.


 [explain]:  do a job (NOT make): ‘There are lots of jobs to do when we get home.’ (= pieces of work) ‘There’s no need to thank me. I was just doing my job.’ (= what I do to earn a living) do a good/great/marvellous etc job: ‘You can always rely on Charlie to do a good job.’ Note however the expression make a good/bad job of sth: ‘She’s made a good job of the decorating.’ (= she has done it well)
[wrong sentence]:  The photographer made quite a good job.


 [explain]:  For actions, the usual verb is do (NOT make): ‘Governments are not doing enough to reduce unemployment.’ ‘I’m so worried I don’t know what to do.’ See Language Note at DO

 [explain]:  do your exercises (NOT make): ‘Always warm up before you start doing these exercises.’ See Language Note at DO

 [explain]:  For actions, the usual verb is do (NOT make): ‘Governments are not doing enough to reduce unemployment.’ ‘I’m so worried I don’t know what to do.’ See Language Note at DO

R:VERB does -> do
 [explain]:  People is a plural noun and takes a plural verb: ‘People have been very kind to me.’
[wrong sentence]:  I think people who does these things should be punished.


R:VERB can -> do
 [explain]:  When remember means ‘bring the memory of a person or thing into your mind (i.e. recall) then can/could is usually optional: ‘I (can) still remember the sad look on her face.’ Use can/could to suggest effort: ‘I just can’t remember how the film ended.’ ‘I wish I could remember their address." [wrong sentence]:  Can you remember me? I used to sit at the back of your class.


R:VERB making -> do
 [explain]:  do/carry out/conduct/be engaged in research: ‘We have received a small grant to conduct research into housing design for older women.’ See Language Note at DO

R:VERB makes -> do
 [explain]:  I do sport (NOT make): ‘Do you do any sport at school?’
[wrong sentence]:  He makes all types of sport.


R:VERB become -> go
 [explain]:  go blind (not become): ‘When I couldn’t see anything, I thought I’d gone blind.’
[wrong sentence]:  Some people become blind with the disease.


R:VERB come -> go
 [explain]:  Come is used for movement towards the place where the speaker is, was, or intends to be, or towards the person being talked about: ‘Come and look at this.’ ‘Why didn’t he come to see me?’ ‘He was just about to go out when his wife came into the office in tears.’ Go is used for movement in other directions: ‘I wish those noisy children would go away.’ ‘Let’s go to London for a few days.’
[wrong sentence]:  He was afraid of his father and didn’t want to come back home.


R:VERB became -> go
 [explain]:  People lose control of vehicles, machines etc: ‘The pilot lost control of the aircraft and it plummeted to the ground.’ Vehicles and machines are/go out of control: ‘The aircraft went out of control and plummeted to the ground.’
[wrong sentence]:  There was a loud bang and my car became out of control.


 [explain]:  To refer to the time in the evening or at night when the daylight disappears, use get dark or (in formal styles) b: ‘In the winter it gets dark by five o’clock.’ ‘It began to grow dark and so we headed back to the shore.’ To describe what happens when all the lights in a room, building, etc suddenly go out, use go dark or (especially in formal styles) be plunged into darkness: ‘During the last storm the whole town was plunged into darkness.’
[wrong sentence]:  The last candle went out and everything became dark.


R:VERB going -> go
 [explain]:  do (anything/nothing/what you like etc) + except + bare infinitive: ‘There was nothing I could do except sit and wait.’
[wrong sentence]:  In their free time they do nothing except going from one cafeteria to another.


 [explain]:  would rather do sth (NOT to do or doing): ‘They’d rather wait and see what happens.’

R:VERB to go -> go
 [explain]:  instead of (doing) sth: ‘Instead of waiting for a bus, we decided that it would be quicker to walk.’

 [explain]:  risk doing sth (NOT to do): ‘I couldn’t risk taking a photograph – there were too many soldiers around.’

 [explain]:  go mad (NOT become): ‘Eventually, rejected by Hamlet, Ophelia goes mad and drowns herself.’

R:VERB keeps -> turn
 [explain]:  turn a blind eye (to sth) = pretend not to notice something: ‘The government prefers to turn a blind eye to these activities.’
[wrong sentence]:  Usually he just keeps a blind eye and lets them carry on.


R:VERB book -> order
 [explain]:  book = reserve a seat on a plane, a room in a hotel, a table at a restaurant, etc: ‘The train was packed and I wished I’d booked a seat.’ order = ask for certain goods to be sent to a shop, especially because a customer wants to buy them: ‘If you want to be sure of getting a daily newspaper, it’s best to order one.’
[wrong sentence]:  I’m afraid that we’re out of stock but I can book one for you.


R:VERB boring -> bore
 [explain]:  lf someone or something is boring/exciting/frightening/interesting (-ING), you feel bored/excited/frightened/interested (-ED): ‘The lecture was terribly boring. Some of us were so bored that we fell asleep.’
[wrong sentence]:  You’ll be boring with nothing to do.


R:VERB borrow -> lend
 [explain]:  When you borrow something (from someone), you are allowed to use it: ‘Can I borrow one of your pencils?’ When you lend something (to someone), you let them use it: ‘He asked me to lend him one of my pencils.’
[wrong sentence]:  I asked my friend to borrow me some money.

 [explain]:  When you borrow something (from someone), you are allowed to use it: ‘Can I borrow one of your pencils?’ When you lend something (to someone), you let them use it: ‘He asked me to lend him one of my pencils.’
[wrong sentence]:  He borrowed me some of his books.

 [explain]:  See Language Note at TAKE

[wrong sentence]:  Ali asked us if we would lead him to the theatre one night.

 [explain]:  See Language Note at TAKE

R:VERB drinking -> take
 [explain]:  take/have your medicine (NOT drink): ‘Have you taken/had your medicine today?’
[wrong sentence]:  After drinking the medicine, I felt sleepy.


R:VERB feel -> take
 [explain]:  take pity on: ‘Eventually a kind motorist took pity on us and offered us a lift.’ feel pity for: ‘Being a widow, she expects you to feel pity for her.’
[wrong sentence]:  She expected me to feel pity on her and help her.


R:VERB send -> take
 [explain]:  The ministry’s policy is for ambulances to send patients to designated hospitals within their zones. The ministry’s policy is for ambulances to take patients to designated hospitals within their zones. See Language Note at TAKE
[wrong sentence]:  Could you send me to the airport on Saturday?


R:VERB have -> take
 [explain]:  be/take a certain size (NOT have): ‘What size are you?’ ‘Before the baby was born, I used to take a size 12.’
[wrong sentence]:  As far as I remember, you both have the same size.


R:VERB make -> take
 [explain]:  take/do a test (NOT make) : ‘Before they start their courses , all the new students take a placement test.’ See Language Note at DO
[wrong sentence]:  On my first day at the school, I had to make a test.


R:VERB bring -> fetch
