[wrong sentence]:  I went back into the house to bring my sunglasses.


R:VERB can -> could
 [explain]:  To make a polite request, use could: ‘Could you pass the butter, please?’
[wrong sentence]:  I’d be grateful if you can confirm whether you are coming.
[wrong sentence]:  Can you possibly send me an application form?


 [explain]:  When you are thinking about the present or the future, use wish (that) +would /could/had etc. (NOT will/can/have etc.) ‘I wish I knew his telephone number.’ ‘I wish I didn’t have to go to school tomorrow.’
[wrong sentence]:  He wishes he can drive a car; taxis are so expensive.


R:VERB couldn’t -> could
 [explain]:  See Language Note at NOT
[wrong sentence]:  When we arrived at the hotel, we couldn’t hardly believe our eyes.


 [explain]:  After neither and neither .. nor ... the verb is affirmative (NOT negative): ‘Neither applicant had the right qualifications.’ ‘Neither the teachers nor the students had been informed.’ See Language Note at NOT
[wrong sentence]:  Neither John’s father nor mine couldn’t understand the problem.


R:VERB cancelled -> postpone
 [explain]:  cancel = arrange for a planned event not to take place after all: ‘Five of their players were either ill or injured, and so the match had to be cancelled.’ postpone = arrange for a planned event to take place at a later time or date: ‘We’ve decided to postpone the wedding until Steve has found a job.’
[wrong sentence]:  The meeting has been cancelled until next Thursday.


R:VERB catch -> understand
 [explain]:  Catch (= hear and/or understand) is used only in connection with what someone has just said: ‘I’m afraid I didn’t quite catch the last point. Could you go over it again?’ ‘Did either of you manage to catch her name?’
[wrong sentence]:  The dialogue in this video is very difficult to catch.
[wrong sentence]:  At that time I couldn’t speak or catch English at all.


R:VERB realize -> understand
 [explain]:  See Language Note below
[wrong sentence]:  I didn’t realize the meaning of ‘junk mail’.
[wrong sentence]:  The criminal mind is not always easy to realize.


R:VERB choose -> prefer
 [explain]:  prefer = like something more than something else: ‘Which do you prefer, black coffee or white coffee?’ ‘Peter prefers classical music to rock.’ ‘I’d prefer to stay here, if you don’t mind.’
[wrong sentence]:  If you choose to see a film, we can go to the cinema instead.
[wrong sentence]:  I choose the first story because it’s more exciting.


R:VERB circulates -> revolve
 [explain]:  circulate = (of news, stories, rumours etc) spread by being passed from one person to another: ‘One of the rumours circulating at the moment is that the company is about to go bankrupt.’ revolve around = (of a novel, film, story etc) be about: ‘His latest film revolves around the difficulties of being a single parent.’
[wrong sentence]:  The story circulates around his career in the army.


R:VERB claiming -> demand
 [explain]:  claim = ask to be given something that belongs to you or that you think you are entitled to: ‘People on a low income are able to claim legal aid.’ demand = ask strongly for something: ‘The laboratory was surrounded by demonstrators demanding an end to animal experiments.’ Note also clamour for = repeatedly ask for something in a noisy or angry way: ‘In response to the increase in domestic violence, people are clamouring for stricter laws that will help to protect wives and children.’
[wrong sentence]:  The public are claiming stricter laws.


R:VERB closed -> lock
 [explain]:  lock/shut sb in a room, house, etc (NOT close): ‘He was picked up by the police and locked in a cell for the night.’
[wrong sentence]:  They closed the man in a room until the police came.


R:VERB concentrated -> concentrate
 [explain]:  concentrate (on sth) NOT be concentrated: ‘How can children concentrate on their homework when they have one eye on the television?’
[wrong sentence]:  I am concentrated on both speaking and writing.


R:VERB be concentrated -> concentrate
 [explain]:  concentrate (on sth) NOT be concentrated: ‘How can children concentrate on their homework when they have one eye on the television?’
[wrong sentence]:  A bus driver has to be concentrated and should not speak to the passengers.


R:VERB confront -> are confronted
 [explain]:  confront sb/sth (WITHOUT with): ‘Sooner or later the management will have to confront these
[wrong sentence]:  Almost every day we confront with some new environmental problem.


R:VERB to visit -> visit
 [explain]:  consider doing sth (= think about something that you might do in the future): ‘To save money, we even considered repairing the roof ourselves.’
[wrong sentence]:  We’re considering to visit Switzerland next year.


 [explain]:  it’s worth doing sth; sth is worth doing (NOT to do): ‘It’s worth remembering that these old cars can be very expensive to run.’
[wrong sentence]:  It’s also worth to visit the north of England if you have time.


R:VERB was containing -> contain
 [explain]:  See Language Note opposite.
[wrong sentence]:  The wallet was containing about $25.


R:VERB including -> contain
 [explain]:  See Language Note on next page
[wrong sentence]:  One of the bottles including the virus had been stolen.


R:VERB include -> contain
 [explain]:  See Language Note on next page
[wrong sentence]:  Classes should include no more than 20 students.


R:VERB includes -> contain
 [explain]:  See Language Note on next page
[wrong sentence]:  Milk also includes vitamins.


R:VERB control -> inspect
 [explain]:  control = make people or machines do what you want them to do: ‘Who will control the aircraft if the pilot has a heart attack?’ ‘The police were unable to control the crowd.’ Inspect =carefully check or examine something: The building is regularly inspected by fire-safety officers.’ ‘I got out of the car to inspect the damage.’
[wrong sentence]:  I was surprised that nobody wanted to control my luggage.


R:VERB controlling -> inspect
 [explain]:  control = make people or machines do what you want them to do: ‘Who will control the aircraft if the pilot has a heart attack?’ ‘The police were unable to control the crowd.’ Inspect =carefully check or examine something: The building is regularly inspected by fire-safety officers.’ ‘I got out of the car to inspect the damage.’
[wrong sentence]:  After controlling his fingerprints, the police arrested him.


R:VERB controlled -> inspect
 [explain]:  control = make people or machines do what you want them to do: ‘Who will control the aircraft if the pilot has a heart attack?’ ‘The police were unable to control the crowd.’ Inspect =carefully check or examine something: The building is regularly inspected by fire-safety officers.’ ‘I got out of the car to inspect the damage.’
[wrong sentence]:  At this stage in the process, every mould is controlled again.


R:VERB convince -> persuade
 [explain]:  convince = make someone feel completely certain that something is true: ‘Somehow the party will have to convince the voters that it is capable of governing the country.’ ‘She failed to convince the jury of her innocence.’ persuade = make someone agree to do something (or believe that something is true): ‘Her parents have persuaded her to stop seeing him.’ ‘Despite our efforts to persuade them, they still haven’t signed the contract.’
[wrong sentence]:  We all tried to convince her to sing.


R:VERB could -> managed to
 [explain]:  When talking about a skill or general ability in the past, use could or be able to: ‘By the time she was four, she could/was able to swim the whole length of the pool.’
[wrong sentence]:  By reading quickly, I could finish the book before the library closed.


R:VERB do -> commit
 [explain]:  commit a crime (NOT do, make, perform): ‘Women commit far fewer crimes than men.’
[wrong sentence]:  We need to understand why people do these crimes.


R:VERB making -> commit
 [explain]:  commit a crime (NOT do, make, perform): ‘Women commit far fewer crimes than men.’
[wrong sentence]:  Somehow we must stop people from making these crimes.


R:VERB doing -> commit
 [explain]:  commit an offence (NOT do): ‘He is accused of committing various minor offences.’
[wrong sentence]:  Public caning would deter other students from doing the offence.


R:VERB did -> make
 [explain]:  make a criticism (of sth): ‘The committee has made four specific criticisms of the government’s transport policy.’
[wrong sentence]:  The writer who did that criticism was unaware of the facts.


R:VERB done -> make
 [explain]:  make an effort (NOT do): ‘The manager would like to see the whole team making more of an effort.’ See Language Note at DO
[wrong sentence]:  Little effort has been done to solve the problem.


 [explain]:  See Language Note at TRIP
[wrong sentence]:  It wasn’t the first journey he had done to Paris.


R:VERB do -> make
 [explain]:  make an effort (NOT do): ‘The manager would like to see the whole team making more of an effort.’ See Language Note at DO
[wrong sentence]:  Sometimes I have to do a great effort not to cry.


 [explain]:  make a mistake (NOT do): ‘It’s easy to make mistakes when you’re tired.’ See Language Note at DO
[wrong sentence]:  It doesn’t matter if you do a slight mistake.


R:VERB to make -> make
 [explain]:  mean to do sth = intend to do something: ‘I’ve been meaning to write to you for ages.’ mean doing sth (or mean + that clause) = involve doing something: ‘The new job will mean getting up an hour earlier in the morning.’ ‘The new job will mean that I have to get up ....’
[wrong sentence]:  Being a good flight attendant means to make your passengers feel relaxed.


R:VERB gained -> make
 [explain]:  make a profit (NOT gain): ‘Derek would never sell you anything without making a good profit.’ ‘The company made a profit of $53 million last year.’
[wrong sentence]:  The company has gained enormous profits in recent years.


 [explain]:  make a profit (NOT gain): ‘Derek would never sell you anything without making a good profit.’ ‘The company made a profit of $53 million last year.’
[wrong sentence]:  The company has gained enormous profits in recent years.


R:VERB cry -> scream
 [explain]:  cry =shout something: Help!" she cried.’ cry out = make a sudden loud noise when you are frightened, shocked, hurt, etc: ‘When they tried to move him, he cried out in pain.’ scream = make a loud, high, continuous noise, especially when you are very frightened, very excited or in great pain: ‘One of the firemen thought he heard someone screaming inside the building.’ ‘The fans didn’t stop screaming until the group had left the stage.’
[wrong sentence]:  The children got very excited and began to cry.


R:VERB cries -> shout
 [explain]:  shout = speak in a very loud voice, especially because you want someone to hear you or because you are angry: ‘There’s no need to shout. I’m not deaf, you know.’ ‘The demonstrators marched through the streets shouting: No more war! No more war!’ When he reached the point in his story when his friends were arrested and tortured, he began to cry. When he reached the point in his story when his friends were arrested and tortured, he began to weep. cry = the opposite of laugh: ‘As the child was running towards me, she fell over and began to cry.’ ‘Babies always cry when they’re hungry.’ weep = cry quietly, usually because of great sadness. This word is mainly used in literary styles: ‘He knelt down b small grave and wept.’
[wrong sentence]:  Even when she is angry, she never cries.


R:VERB cured -> treat
 [explain]:  Doctors and nurses treat an injury, disease, sick person, etc: ‘Serious burns must be treated as soon as possible.’ ‘The usual way of treating malaria is to give the patient large doses of quinine.’ ‘Some hospitals refuse to treat people who don’t have medical insurance.’
[wrong sentence]:  I was the doctor who cured your head injury.


R:VERB made -> did/caused
 [explain]:  do/cause damage (NOT make or produce): ‘According to local farmers, the rabbits do a lot of damage to the crops.’ ‘It’s the gas from fridges that causes most of the damage.’ See Language Note at DO
[wrong sentence]:  The floods made a lot of damage.


R:VERB produced -> cause
 [explain]:  do/cause damage (NOT make or produce): ‘According to local farmers, the rabbits do a lot of damage to the crops.’ ‘It’s the gas from fridges that causes most of the damage.’ See Language Note at DO
[wrong sentence]:  Most of the damage has been produced by acid rain.


R:VERB dare to say -> daresay
 [explain]:  I daresay (also written I dare say) is a fixed phrase: ‘I dare say that we’ll be hearing from them again.’ ‘The team will put up a good performance, I daresay, but I don’t think they’ll win.’ ‘How dare you to come in without knocking!’ he shouted. ‘How dare you come in without knocking!’ he shouted. When dare comes in front of the subject, use an infinitive WITHOUT to: ‘How dare you say such a thing!’ ‘Dare I mention it to her? She’ll be furious.’
[wrong sentence]:  I dare to say that the book is worth reading.


R:VERB demanded -> require
 [explain]:  If you have to do something because of a rule, law etc. you are required to do it: ‘Under the law the President is required to notify Congress when US troops face imminent hostilities.’
[wrong sentence]:  In my new job I am demanded to work overtime.


R:VERB made -> hold
 [explain]:  hold/stage a demonstration (NOT make): ‘In London, students and lecturers staged a mass demonstration against the proposed education cuts.’
[wrong sentence]:  Many demonstrations have been made in recent years in protest against the level of pollution.


 [explain]:  have/hold/throw a party (NOT make/celebrate): ‘Let’s have a party and invite all our friends.’ celebrate Christmas, the New Year, someone’s birthday, retirement, promotion etc: ‘Next month we’re having a party to celebrate our sixth wedding anniversary.’ A party is held somewhere (NOT made/celebrated): ‘Where is the garden party being held?’ See Language Note at DO
[wrong sentence]:  The party was being made at a friend’s house.


R:VERB denied -> refuse
 [explain]:  deny sth = say that it is not true: ‘He has been accused of stealing a car, but he denies it.’ ‘Both companies denied that they had been discharging toxic waste.’ refuse (to do sth) = say that you will not do it: ‘Employers are refusing to discuss a pay settlement until the staff return to work.’ ‘The students were told to leave the building, but they refused.’
[wrong sentence]:  When she denied to wear the uniform, she was dismissed.
[wrong sentence]:  He asked his parents to help him, but they denied.


R:VERB disagreed -> refuse
 [explain]:  disagree = not have the same opinion: ‘He disagreed with nearly everything I said.’ ‘Why did she disagree with you?’ ‘We tended to disagree about politics, but we were still good friends.’ refuse (to do sth) = say that you will not do something that someone has asked you to do: ‘The students were asked to leave the building but they refused.’ ‘If anyone refuses to pay their bill, send for the manager.’
[wrong sentence]:  Those who disagreed to join the army were put in prison.


R:VERB was depend -> depend
 [explain]:  Do not confuse depend on/upon and the more formal phrase be dependent on/upon. Compare: ‘The speed of a car depends on the size of the engine.’ ‘The speed of a car is dependent on the size of the engine.’
[wrong sentence]:  The number of hours he worked was depend on the number of absentees.


R:VERB is depending -> depend
 [explain]:  When depend on/upon means ‘be shaped or determined by’, it is not used in progressive tenses. See Language Note at CONTAIN
[wrong sentence]:  Whether or not she passes is depending upon how hard she works.


R:VERB relies -> depend
 [explain]:  When you mean that one thing is decided or shaped by another thing, use depend on (NOT rely on): ‘The cost of the trip depends on how long you go for and where you stay.’ Compare: ‘Since his wife died, he has had nobody that he could really depend/ rely on.’
[wrong sentence]:  I think it all relies on whether you like being on your own.
[wrong sentence]:  A country’s strength relies on unity.


R:VERB desire -> hope
 [explain]:  In the meaning ‘want/wish’, desire is usually used as a noun (NOT as a verb): ‘His one desire was to live to see his three grandchildren again.’ ‘I have no desire to go there again. Once is enough.’
[wrong sentence]:  I desire that world peace will continue forever.


R:VERB expect -> hope
 [explain]:  Use expect when you have a reason for thinking that something is going to happen: ‘Her husband doesn’t like travelling so I expect she’ll come on her own.’
[wrong sentence]:  I expect that you will be able to come to the party.


R:VERB wish -> hope
 [explain]:  Use wish that (+ past/past perfect tense) for things that cannot happen or will probably not happen: ‘I wish I hadn’t told them my address.’ ‘I wish you could stay here longer.’ (= this will probably not happen) Use hope that (+ present/present perfect tense) for things that may easily happen or may easily have happened: ‘I hope you’ve had a successful trip.’ ‘We hope you all have a very merry Christmas.’ When wish is used for this meaning, the object is a noun phrase (NOT a that clause): ‘I wish you a safe journey.’ ‘We’d like to wish you all a very merry Christmas.’
[wrong sentence]:  I wish you have a wonderful holiday.
[wrong sentence]:  I wish you will enjoy your stay here.


R:VERB destroyed -> spoil
 [explain]:  When you mean ‘remove the pleasure or enjoyment from’, use spoil or ruin (= spoil completely): ‘The trip was spoilt by bad weather.’ ‘I’ve spent weeks planning this surprise party for Dad, and now you’ve ruined it by telling him.’
[wrong sentence]:  This unpleasant man with his endless complaints destroyed my journey.


R:VERB disagree -> don’t agree
 [explain]:  When the subject is a particular person, use not agree to introduce a that clause: ‘I don’t agree that the people there are repressed.’ ‘She cannot agree that farmers should be an exception.’ In more general statements, disagree is usually used with not, nobody etc before a that clause, to give an affirmative meaning; ‘No one can disagree that these crimes must be stopped.’ (=everyone must agree that ...) ‘Few would disagree that she has served her country well.’ (=most would agree that ...)
[wrong sentence]:  I disagree that heart transplants should be stopped.


R:VERB dress -> wear
 [explain]:  See Language Note at WEAR
[wrong sentence]:  At work I have to dress a dark blue suit.


R:VERB earned -> win
 [explain]:  You earn money by doing work for which you are paid: ‘She earns $4,000 a month.’ You win money by being lucky in a competition etc: The last time he played roulette, he won about $50,000.’
[wrong sentence]:  He earned a lot of money on the lottery.


R:VERB effect -> affect
 [explain]:  affect sth (verb) = have an effect on sth (noun) Compare: ‘There is no doubt that smoking affects people’s health.’ ‘There is no doubt that smoking has an effect on people’s health.’ When used as a verb, effect means ‘bring about’: ‘The new president effected several major changes.’
[wrong sentence]:  I thought that the long illness would effect my chances of passing the exam.


R:VERB touched -> affect
 [explain]:  When you mean ‘have an effect on’, use affect (NOT touch): ‘How will these new taxes affect people on low incomes?’ ‘Farms in the south of the country have been seriously affected by the drought.’
[wrong sentence]:  People living in the city were not touched by the famine.


R:VERB elect -> choose
 [explain]:  elect = choose (someone) by voting for them: ‘The committee has elected a new chairman.’ choose = decide which one you want: ‘We chose Greece because we hadn’t been there before.’
[wrong sentence]:  Some people elect marriage partners who are totally unsuitable.


R:VERB endure -> bear
 [explain]:  When you mean ‘strongly dislike’ or ‘be unable to accept’, use can’t bear/stand: ‘He used to like cricket, but now he can’t stand it.’ ‘He couldn’t bear the thought of his wife leaving him.’
[wrong sentence]:  Some people cannot endure the idea of devoting themselves to children.


R:VERB to speak -> speak
 [explain]:  enjoy doing sth: ‘I wouldn’t enjoy sitting at a desk all day.’
[wrong sentence]:  I enjoy to speak foreign languages.


R:VERB talk -> speak
 [explain]:  speak English/French/Thai etc (NOT talk): ‘Do you speak Italian?’ ‘I didn’t know you could speak
[wrong sentence]:  I couldn’t talk English in those days.


R:VERB ensure -> assure
 [explain]:  ensure = make certain (that something will happen or exist): ‘We need to ensure that our prices remain competitive.’ ‘Measures are being taken to ensure public safety.’ assure = tell someone that something will definitely happen or is definitely true, especially to make them feel less worried: ‘I assure you that the report will be on your desk by tomorrow lunchtime at the latest.’ ‘The doctor assured me that there was no need for alarm.’
[wrong sentence]:  We ensure you that we will do our best.


R:VERB entered -> board
 [explain]:  get on/board a train/plane/ship: ‘The ticket inspector wanted to know where I had got on.’ ‘There’s still time for some duty-free shopping before we have to board the plane.’
[wrong sentence]:  I entered the train in Oslo.


R:VERB establish -> open
 [explain]:  You establish a major company or organization that will probably run forever or for a very long time: ‘As soon as Rwanda became independent, it established its own national bank.’ ‘Most of the money is to be used to establish local industries and mobilize the workforce.’ You start or start up a small company or organization: ‘They intend to use the money to start up their own taxi business.’ You open a new shop, restaurant, shopping mall etc: ‘They’re planning to open a small hotel just outside town.’
[wrong sentence]:  I want to establish a cooperative store.


R:VERB estimate -> assess
 [explain]:  assess = consider something carefully and give an opinion about how good or bad it is: ‘Parents should be shown how to assess the quality of a school.’ ‘This test provides a simple means of assessing an applicant’s suitability.’
[wrong sentence]:  Each of us gave a short talk and then we had to estimate each other’s performance.


R:VERB give -> set
 [explain]:  set an example or set a good example = behave correctly so that the people who copy you will behave correctly: ‘The school captain is expected to set a good example.’
[wrong sentence]:  A manager is supposed to give a good example.


R:VERB saying -> to say
 [explain]:  When you are talking about purpose, use except + to-v: ‘I rarely go the market, except to buy vegetables.’
[wrong sentence]:  I had never really spoken to him except saying hello.


R:VERB seeing -> to see
 [explain]:  The choice of verb form after except is sometimes determined by a word that comes earlier in the sentence, e.g. want + to-v, good at + v-ing.
[wrong sentence]:  They want nothing except seeing that the children are happy.


R:VERB to wash -> wash
 [explain]:  The choice of verb form after except is sometimes determined by a word that comes earlier in the sentence, e.g. want + to-v, good at + v-ing.
[wrong sentence]:  My brother is good at everything except to wash the dishes.


R:VERB excuse -> forgive
 [explain]:  Excuse is used only for minor faults and offences: ‘I hope you’ll excuse my untidy handwriting. I’m trying to write this letter on a train.’ Forgive is used for all faults and offences, both minor and major: ‘I don’t think he’ll ever forgive me for calling off the wedding.’ ‘Don’t forget her birthday or she’ll never forgive you.’
[wrong sentence]:  He was sorry that he had lied to her and asked her to excuse him.


R:VERB been existing -> exist
 [explain]:  See Language Note at CONTAIN
[wrong sentence]:  The problem of hunger has been existing for centuries.


R:VERB expand -> increase
 [explain]:  expand = become (or make something become) larger in size, area or activity: ‘Metals expand when they are heated.’ ‘Within three years this small business had expanded into a chain of department stores.’ increase = become (or make something become) larger in number, amount, cost, etc: ‘Sales of new cars increased from 1.2 million in 1993 to 1.8 million in 1994.’ ‘By the year 2020 the government aims to increase the number of secondary schools by 50%.’
[wrong sentence]:  I’m doing the course to expand my job opportunities.


R:VERB expand -> spread
 [explain]:  When you are talking about a disease, fire, war, social problem, feeling, etc, use spread: ‘The army was called in to stop the riots from spreading.’ ‘Dissatisfaction with the present government seems to be spreading.’
[wrong sentence]:  The epidemic began to expand rapidly.


 [explain]:  When you are talking about news, information, ideas, etc, use spread: ‘Within hours the news of the bank’s collapse had spread all over the world.’ ‘I hope you haven’t been spreading rumours.’
[wrong sentence]:  The newspapers are not allowed to expand political ideas.


R:VERB make -> perform
 [explain]:  perform/conduct/carry out/do an experiment (NOT make): ‘Further experiments will have to be conducted before the drug can be tested on humans.’ See Language Note at DO
[wrong sentence]:  They shouldn’t be allowed to make these experiments.


R:VERB explode -> blow up
 [explain]:  People blow up buildings, planes etc. Bombs, fuel tanks etc explode.
[wrong sentence]:  The hijackers threatened to explode the plane.


R:VERB exposed -> exhibit
 [explain]:  expose = let something be seen, especially something that is usually hidden: ‘He undid his shirt to expose a white hairless chest.’ display/exhibit a work of art: ‘The gallery exhibits mainly contemporary sculpture and photography.’
[wrong sentence]:  His paintings have been exposed in art galleries all over the world.


R:VERB is feeling -> feel
 [explain]:  See Language Note at CONTAIN
[wrong sentence]:  He is feeling that they have made a big mistake.


R:VERB fit -> suit
 [explain]:  fit = (of clothes, shoes, rings etc) be the correct size and shape for you: ‘These trousers don’t fit me any more.’ ‘The next size up should fit.’ ; suit = be suitable: ‘Try and choose a career that suits you.’ ‘You should buy a dictionary that suits your needs, not just anyone.’
[wrong sentence]:  You should see a doctor or specialist to find out which type of diet will fit you.


R:VERB fit -> match
 [explain]:  match (or go with) = (of clothes, soft furnishings etc) look good when seen together: ‘We chose a dark green carpet to go with our yellow curtains.’ ‘I can’t wear blue shoes with a black skirt - they don’t match.’
[wrong sentence]:  Of course you will need some old paintings to fit the furniture.


R:VERB forgotten -> leave
 [explain]:  If you forget to take something with you, you leave it somewhere. Do not use forget if you mention a place: ‘If the keys aren’t in your jacket, you must have left them in the restaurant.’ ‘I’ve left all my money at home.’
[wrong sentence]:  Would you please check whether I have forgotten a black handbag in Room 21.


R:VERB gain -> earn
 [explain]:  earn or (especially in informal styles) get/make (a sum of) money by going to work, from investments, etc (NOT gain): ‘She earns $4,000 a month.’
[wrong sentence]:  For two weeks’ work, I gain about 700,000 yen.
[wrong sentence]:  Without a job it’s impossible to gain any money.


R:VERB got -> have (got
 [explain]:  Got is not used to replace have: ‘I have two sisters.’ ‘I’ve got two sisters.’ (NOT ‘I got two sisters.’)
[wrong sentence]:  I got several friends who don’t want children.


R:VERB grow -> bring up
 [explain]:  bring up (or raise) = look after children until they are old enough to leave home, teaching them how to behave and what to think about the world: ‘He was brought up to believe that men and women are equal.’
[wrong sentence]:  Some parents need to be taught how to grow their children.


R:VERB charged -> find
 [explain]:  find sb guilty/innocent: ‘Despite the flimsy evidence, the jury found her guilty.’ ‘The accused was found guilty and sentenced to five years’ imprisonment. ‘
[wrong sentence]:  The jury charged the defendant guilty.


R:VERB judged -> find
 [explain]:  find sb guilty/innocent: ‘Despite the flimsy evidence, the jury found her guilty.’ ‘The accused was found guilty and sentenced to five years’ imprisonment. ‘
[wrong sentence]:  He was tried and judged guilty of murder.


R:VERB taken -> acquire
 [explain]:  Acquire/adopt/develop/get into a habit (NOT take): ‘He’s developed the unfortunate habit of biting his fingernails.’
[wrong sentence]:  Once one has taken the habit, smoking is very difficult to give up.


R:VERB to worry -> worry
 [explain]:  can’t/couldn’t help doing sth: ‘I couldn’t help laughing when I saw what he was doing.’
[wrong sentence]:  The girl’s parents couldn’t help to worry about her.


R:VERB hinder -> deter
 [explain]:  deter sb (from doing sth) = persuade someone not to do something: ‘The threat of imprisonment has failed to deter these young offenders.’
[wrong sentence]:  Capital punishment aims to hinder people from committing such crimes.


R:VERB hiring -> rent
 [explain]:  In British English you hire a suit, meeting hall, bicycle, fishing rod etc. You obtain the use of these things, usually for a short period, by making a single payment: ‘Why buy a wedding dress when you can hire one?’ ‘Herr Platzer showed us where we could hire bicycles.’ You rent a house, shop, apartment, television etc, usually for a longer period, by making a series of payments: ‘There are plenty of offices to rent in the town centre.’ When talking about cars, you can use rent or hire: ‘There’s usually a place at the airport where you can hire/rent a car.’ In American English you always rent something (not hire), regardless of what it is or the length of the arrangement.
[wrong sentence]:  I’m hiring a small house near the university.


R:VERB lend -> rent
 [explain]:  See Note at HIRE
[wrong sentence]:  We usually lend a villa so that we can cook for ourselves.


R:VERB hit -> punch
 [explain]:  punch and kick (NOT hit and kick): ‘The boy on the ground was punching and kicking to defend himself.’
[wrong sentence]:  I was so terrified that I hit and kicked them with all my might.


R:VERB held -> picked up
 [explain]:  hold = have something in your hand/hands/arms etc: ‘I’d been holding the baby for nearly an hour and my right arm was getting tired.’ get/take hold of = put your fingers or hands around something and hold it: ‘Quick! Get hold of the rope! The boat’s drifting away.’ pick up = put your fingers around something and take it: ‘He bent down to pick up the glove that she’d dropped.’
[wrong sentence]:  As soon as she saw the mouse, she held a knife.


R:VERB would -> will
 [explain]:  You hope that someone will do something or that something will happen (NOT would): ‘I hope that you won’t be offended if I don’t come.
[wrong sentence]:  I hope that you would think seriously about this matter.
[wrong sentence]:  I hope that one day things would change.


R:VERB shall -> will
 [explain]:  For future reference, use will with the second and third person: ‘I’m confident you will not be disappointed.’ ‘If we don’t hurry, the match will have started.’ Use will/shall with the first person (I/we): ‘This time next week I will/shall be in Florence.’ ‘I’m sure we will/shall be seeing each other again.’
[wrong sentence]:  The next meeting shall take place in Vienna.


R:VERB imitate -> copy
 [explain]:  imitate = do something in exactly the same way that someone else does it: ‘Have you heard him trying to imitate an Englishman speaking French?’ ‘He walks as if he is trying to imitate Donald
[wrong sentence]:  I think I shall imitate my friend and join the club too.


R:VERB immigrate -> emigrate
 [explain]:  The verb immigrate (= enter a foreign country and make your home there) is seldom used. Instead people tend to use immigrant and immigration (nouns): ‘When jobs became scarce, the number of new immigrants suddenly decreased.’ ‘Immigration has decreased in recent years.’ emigrate = leave your own country and make your home in another one: ‘His parents emigrated from Scotland to Canada in 1933.’
[wrong sentence]:  We’re planning to immigrate to the United States next year.


R:VERB migrate -> emigrate
 [explain]:  migrate = (of birds, animals and people) travel from one place to spend a length of time in another, especially to find food, water, warmer weather, etc: ‘How do birds know when to migrate and how do they find their way back home?’ emigrate = (of people) leave your own country and make your home in another one: ‘There were no jobs back home so we decided to emigrate.’
[wrong sentence]:  A lot of new doctors migrate to America where they can earn more money.


R:VERB including -> enclose
 [explain]:  See Language Note on next page
[wrong sentence]:  I am including a photograph of myself.


R:VERB increase -> improve
 [explain]:  increase = become or make (something) greater in amount, number or degree: ‘The government has increased taxes.’ ‘As their profits increase, the companies expand.’ ‘The number of words in the language is increasing all the time.’ improve = become or make (something) better: ‘Efforts are being made to improve the quality of the medical services.’
[wrong sentence]:  I’d like to increase my English.
[wrong sentence]:  We can increase the economic situation by working harder.


R:VERB increase -> raise
 [explain]:  When you are talking about the level or standard of something, use raise/rise (NOT increase): ‘The standard of living continues to rise.’
[wrong sentence]:  The government is trying to increase the level of education.


R:VERB rising -> raise
 [explain]:  See RAISE 3
[wrong sentence]:  Many firms try to survive by rising productivity.


R:VERB increased -> rise
 [explain]:  When you are talking about the level or standard of something, use raise/rise (NOT increase): ‘The standard of living continues to rise.’
[wrong sentence]:  In Hong Kong the standard of living has increased sharply.


R:VERB raised -> rise
 [explain]:  Raise (raising, raised, raised) is a transitive verb: ‘They wouldn’t dare to raise taxes just before an election.’ Rise (rising, rose, risen) is intransitive: ‘The divorce rate has risen steadily over the last forty years.’ ‘Prices rose again last month.’
[wrong sentence]:  Sales raised rapidly.
[wrong sentence]:  The cost of living has raised by 20 per cent this year.


R:VERB raise -> rise
 [explain]:  Raise (raising, raised, raised) is a transitive verb: ‘They wouldn’t dare to raise taxes just before an election.’ Rise (rising, rose, risen) is intransitive: ‘The divorce rate has risen steadily over the last forty years.’ ‘Prices rose again last month.’
[wrong sentence]:  The rain had caused the water level to raise.


R:VERB injuring -> damage
 [explain]:  Injure is used only in connection with people and animals: ‘Latest reports from the site of the crash indicate that three people are dead and twelve are seriously injured.’
[wrong sentence]:  We must all stop injuring the environment.


R:VERB injured -> wound
 [explain]:  wound = injure a person or animal by using a weapon such as a gun or knife: ‘He is accused of wounding a fellow prisoner.’ ‘The sergeant had been seriously wounded and was losing blood.’
[wrong sentence]:  He shot dead 22 innocent people and injured 23 others.


R:VERB intend -> tend
 [explain]:  intend = plan to do something: ‘I intend to arrive early and make sure that I get a seat.’ tend = be likely or true in most cases: ‘At that age, girls tend to be more mature than boys.’
[wrong sentence]:  Students that are motivated intend to work harder.


R:VERB invent -> discover
 [explain]:  invent = create a machine, instrument, system or process which has never existed before: ‘Who invented the telephone?’ ‘The cotton gin was invented by Eli Whitney in 1793.’ discover = find or find out something for the first time: ‘Penicillin was discovered almost by accident.’ ‘I’ve just discovered a new way of cooking spaghetti.’
[wrong sentence]:  It will not be long before scientists invent a cure for this terrible disease.


R:VERB invented -> coin
 [explain]:  coin a new word or expression: ‘The term ‘black hole’ was coined in 1969 by the American scientist John Wheeler.’
[wrong sentence]:  The word ‘workaholic’ was invented in America.


R:VERB to deal -> deal
 [explain]:  involve (doing) sth: ‘A proper repair would involve stripping all the tiles off the roof.’
[wrong sentence]:  My job involves to deal with customer complaints.


R:VERB saying -> tell
 [explain]:  tell (sb) a joke (NOT say) = tell someone a short funny story to make them laugh: ‘Come on, Peter!
[wrong sentence]:  He kept saying the same joke again and again.


 [explain]:  tell a lie (NOT say or speak): ‘I always know when he’s telling lies.’
[wrong sentence]:  I was sure the old woman was saying lies.


 [explain]:  say sth: ‘I forgot to say goodbye.’ tell sb sth: ‘Eventually he told me the truth.’
[wrong sentence]:  He was tired of people saying him what to do.


 [explain]:  tell the truth (NOT say): ‘I’m still not convinced that he’s telling the truth.’
[wrong sentence]:  We all thought he was saying the truth.


R:VERB said -> tell
 [explain]:  tell sb to do sth (NOT say): ‘I told them to wait for you outside.’
[wrong sentence]:  The policeman said me to go with him to the police station.


 [explain]:  say that: ‘She said that she might be late.’ tell sb that: ‘She told me that she might be late.’
[wrong sentence]:  When he arrived, they said him that his friend had died.


R:VERB talk -> tell
 [explain]:  When there is something that you want someone to know, you tell them about it: ‘She told me about her holiday plans.’ When you have a conversation , you talk (to someone) about something: ‘We talked about where we could go at the weekend .’
[wrong sentence]:  I’d like to talk you about a dream I had last night.


R:VERB judged -> try
 [explain]:  be tried = be judged in a court of law: ‘He is being tried for the murder of his wife and her lover.’
[wrong sentence]:  He was judged and sentenced to life imprisonment.


R:VERB taste -> try
 [explain]:  When you taste something, you put a little into your mouth to see what it is like: ‘The chef stirred the soup, tasted it, and then added a little salt. When you have something to eat or drink for the first time , you try it. ‘I think I’ll try the onion soup. What’ s it like?’
[wrong sentence]:  During your visit you’ll be able to taste some of the local Catalan specialties.


R:VERB knocked -> hit
 [explain]:  When someone tries to hurt another person, they hit them: ‘He still had a bruise from the time she had hit him.’
[wrong sentence]:  He thinks that someone knocked him on the head.


R:VERB know -> find out
 [explain]:  Two police officers visited him to know where he had been on the night of the murder. Two police officers visited him to find out where he had been on the night of the murder, find out = get information about something that you want to know: ‘We need to find out why these cars have stopped selling.’
[wrong sentence]:  I ran downstairs to know what was happening.


R:VERB laying -> lie
 [explain]:  lay (laying, laid, laid) = put something somewhere: ‘She laid the money on the table in front of me.’ lie (lying, lay, lain) = be in a horizontal position (on the ground/on a bed etc): ‘He was lying on his back, staring up at the sky.’ ‘I could lie here all day!’
[wrong sentence]:  We broke down the door and found him laying on the carpet.
[wrong sentence]:  The tourists were laying in the hot sun like corpses.


R:VERB lend -> borrow
 [explain]:  See Note at BORROW
[wrong sentence]:  Could I lend your pen just for a minute?


R:VERB loaned -> borrow
 [explain]:  loan = (1) (noun) a sum of money that you borrow, usually from a bank: They are currently negotiating a $100 million loan.’ (2) (verb) lend a painting, work of art etc to an art gallery or museum: The pictures have been loaned to the National Gallery for the forthcoming exhibition.’ (3) (verb; especially in American English) let someone use something; lend: ‘Why don’t you ask
[wrong sentence]:  The military government loaned millions of dollars from the world bank.


R:VERB lend -> hire/rent
 [explain]:  See Note at HIRE
[wrong sentence]:  To lend a car, you have to have a driving licence.


R:VERB lengthen -> prolong
 [explain]:  lengthen = make something longer; become longer: ‘We can use the old curtains, but they’ll have to be lengthened.’ ‘As evening fell, the shadows lengthened.’ ; prolong = prevent a feeling, activity or life from ending: ‘He asked her another question just to prolong the conversation.’
[wrong sentence]:  The question is whether doctors should lengthen life when there is no hope of recovery.


R:VERB lessen -> reduce
 [explain]:  Lessen is mainly used in connection with pain and feelings: ‘I’ll give you an injection to lessen the pain.’ ‘No amount of comforting could lessen Charles’ disappointment.’ When you mean ‘make something smaller in amount, number, price or size’, use reduce: ‘The workforce has been reduced by 50 percent.’
[wrong sentence]:  The best solution is to lessen the amount of traffic entering the town.


R:VERB do -> have/take
 [explain]:  have a lesson (NOT do/follow): ‘I didn’t go into the classroom because the children were having a lesson.’ have/take lessons (= arrange for someone to teach you): ‘If you want to improve your English, you should have/take lessons’
[wrong sentence]:  I’ve started to do lessons in English and French.


R:VERB let -> rent/hire
 [explain]:  Let = allow someone to live in your house, flat etc in return for money: ‘During the holiday season we let the cottage to tourists.’ rent/hire = See Note at HIRE
[wrong sentence]:  You can travel by train or let a car.


R:VERB lied -> lay
 [explain]:  lie (lying, lied, lied) = say something which is not true in an attempt to deceive: ‘I wonder why he lied about his age?’ lie (lying, lay, lain) down = be in or get into a horizontal position: ‘I lay down on the bed and fell sound asleep.’
[wrong sentence]:  After a while he stopped running and lied down on the ground.


R:VERB lighted -> light
 [explain]:  In British English the usual past tense and past participle form of light is lit. Lighted is mainly used before a noun. Compare: ‘He lit a cigarette.’ ‘The cigarette hadn’t been lit.’ ‘He was holding a lighted cigarette.’
[wrong sentence]:  Suddenly a wonderful smile lighted up her face.
[wrong sentence]:  It was dark inside so I lighted a match.
[wrong sentence]:  The road was poorly lighted and it was difficult to see.


R:VERB likes -> would like
 [explain]:  When you mean ‘want’, use would like.
[wrong sentence]:  What he likes to know is how much it will cost.


R:VERB do -> would
 [explain]:  When you mean ‘want’, use would like.
[wrong sentence]:  Do you like me to show you where to go?


R:VERB should -> would
 [explain]:  When talking about an imaginary situation, use should/would in the main clause after a first person subject: ‘I should/would accept the job if I were you.’ After a second or third person subject, use would in the main clause (NOT should): ‘He would accept the job if the salary were better.’
[wrong sentence]:  I have friends who should love to stay at home, but they have to go to work.


R:VERB will -> would
 [explain]:  A reporting verb in the past tense (e.g. ‘thought’) is usually followed by would/could (NOT will/can). Compare: ‘I think she will accept any job that comes along.’ ‘I thought she would accept any job that came along.’
[wrong sentence]:  When capital punishment was abolished, people thought that murders will become more numerous.


 [explain]:  When you are thinking about the present or the future, use wish (that) +would /could/had etc. (NOT will/can/have etc.) ‘I wish I knew his telephone number.’ ‘I wish I didn’t have to go to school tomorrow.’
[wrong sentence]:  I wish that they will stop killing each other.


R:VERB going -> to go
 [explain]:  would like (sb) to do sth: ‘Would you like me to help you with the packing?’ ‘I’d like you all to listen carefully.’
[wrong sentence]:  Would you like going for a walk with me?


R:VERB lived -> stay
 [explain]:  stay at/in a hotel, youth hostel, etc (NOT live in): ‘In London we usually stay at the Savoy.’
[wrong sentence]:  At the hotel, we lived in room 101.


R:VERB stayed -> stay
 [explain]:  When you use or to join two verbs, both verbs should have the same form. Compare: ‘We used to go to the cinema or watch the television.’ ‘We went to the cinema or watched the television.’
[wrong sentence]:  At night we used to go out with our friends or stayed at home listening to music.


R:VERB to stay -> stay
 [explain]:  Regret is followed by a to-infinitive in formal letters to introduce a piece of bad news: ‘I regret to inform you that your application for a scholarship has been unsuccessful.’ When you are talking about the past, use regret doing sth or regret that: ‘I have often regretted leaving the police force.’ ‘I wonder if they ever regret not having gone to live abroad?’ ‘Now that it was too late, he regretted that he hadn’t worked harder at school.’
[wrong sentence]:  In any case I didn’t regret to stay at home.


R:VERB lose -> miss
 [explain]:  If you do not attend a class, meeting, activity, etc, you miss it: ‘If you don’t hurry up, you’ll miss your appointment.’ ‘I’d hate to miss one of John’s parties.’
[wrong sentence]:  His illness has caused him to lose a lot of lessons.


R:VERB lose -> waste
 [explain]:  Waste your/sb’s time = (cause someone to) spend an amount of time without doing anything useful: ‘I wish they’d stop asking me silly questions and wasting my time.’ ; Compare: ‘The puncture meant that we lost a lot of time.’
[wrong sentence]:  I don’t care if they’re lazy, as long as they don’t lose my time.


R:VERB may -> might
 [explain]:  To refer to something in the past that was possible but did not actually happen, use might have or could have (NOT may have): ‘If you had worked harder, you might have passed the exam.’
[wrong sentence]:  If I hadn’t seen the car coming, I may have been killed.


R:VERB to wait -> wait
 [explain]:  (not) mind doing sth: ‘Would you mind posting this letter for me when you go into town?’
[wrong sentence]:  I don’t mind to wait a bit longer.


R:VERB will arrive -> arrive
 [explain]:  See Language Note at WILL
[wrong sentence]:  I’d like to see him in my office the moment he will arrive.


R:VERB must -> should
 [explain]:  Use must (or have to) when, for example, there is a law or rule and you are not free to choose or decide for yourself: ‘Candidates must answer all the questions in Part A and two questions in Part
[wrong sentence]:  People are not as careful as they must be and drop their litter in the streets.


R:VERB would -> should
 [explain]:  When you are talking about the present or the future and you imagine something that is possible but unlikely, use if ... should (NOT if … would): ‘If anyone should need me, I’ll be back in half an hour.’
[wrong sentence]:  If you would have any more questions, I’ll do my best to answer them.


R:VERB hide -> be hiding
 [explain]:  When you do not actually know where someone is or what they are doing, but certain facts allow you to guess, use must be or must be doing: ‘If she isn’t in her office, she must be in the canteen.’ ‘If she’s in the canteen, she must be having her lunch.’
[wrong sentence]:  If you can’t find her, she must hide somewhere.


R:VERB named -> call
 [explain]:  When you mention the name by which someone or something is known, use call/be called (NOT name/be named): ‘Most people call him Bob but he prefers to be called Robert.’ Compare: ‘They’ve named/called the baby Louise.’ (= the name they have chosen for the baby is
[wrong sentence]:  I stayed in and watched a film named ‘The Clockwork Orange’.
[wrong sentence]:  This beautiful village is named ‘Cadaques’.


R:VERB name -> call
 [explain]:  When you mention the name by which someone or something is known, use call/be called (NOT name/be named): ‘Most people call him Bob but he prefers to be called Robert.’ Compare: ‘They’ve named/called the baby Louise.’ (= the name they have chosen for the baby is
[wrong sentence]:  What we used to name ‘the Cold War’ is now over.


R:VERB titled -> call
 [explain]:  To introduce the name of a book, story, film, article, song, painting etc, use be called or, especially in formal styles, (be) entitled: ‘Her latest novel is called Educating Peter.’ Compare: ‘The title of her latest novel is Educating Peter.’
[wrong sentence]:  He was reading a short story by Saki titled ‘The Open Window’.


R:VERB neglect -> forget
 [explain]:  neglect = (1) fail to look after someone or something properly: ‘The garden has been badly neglected and will require a lot of attention.’ (2) (formal) fail to do something, especially something that you ought to do: ‘The public are demanding to know why the government neglected to warn them of the oil shortage.’ forget = fail to realize something (and be guided by it): ‘Children tend to forget that their parents like to have fun too.’
[wrong sentence]:  Some teachers neglect how much a student can take in during one lesson.


R:VERB nominated -> appoint
 [explain]:  nominate = suggest someone for election or selection (for a job or position): ‘We need to nominate someone to take over from Harry as our new public relations officer.’ appoint = give someone a job or position: ‘Mr H. Wilks has been officially appointed as the society’s new public relations officer.’
[wrong sentence]:  Mr Tong was nominated manager of the company in 1984.


R:VERB could notice -> notice
 [explain]:  Notice is not used with can/could: ‘They were so busy that they didn’t notice how late it was getting.’
[wrong sentence]:  I could notice that he was not enjoying himself.


R:VERB remarked -> notice
 [explain]:  remark = say what you think or have noticed about someone or something: ‘ "There’s a strange smell in here," she remarked.’ ‘Sarah remarked that the fire was going out, but nobody seemed to care.’ ‘I couldn’t help remarking on his new hair style.’ notice = become aware of someone or something: ‘If we keep very quiet, they may not notice us.’ ‘Have you noticed that she has stopped wearing her wedding ring?’
[wrong sentence]:  As soon as I came in I remarked your note on the table.


R:VERB offered -> invite
 [explain]:  offer to do sth = express willingness to do something: ‘She’s offered to help me.’ invite sb to (do) sth = ask someone if they would like to come to a party, wedding etc, or join you in a social activity: ‘Have you invited Mark and Valerie to the party?’
[wrong sentence]:  Her parents have offered me to go on holiday with them.


R:VERB will stop -> stop
 [explain]:  See Language Note at WILL
[wrong sentence]:  Once it will stop raining, we can go out.


R:VERB open -> introduce
 [explain]:  introduce a (new) topic/subject/argument (NOT open): ‘You can’t introduce new material in the last section of a report.’
[wrong sentence]:  If anyone tried to open a new topic, she would immediately interrupt.


R:VERB saying -> express
 [explain]:  express/give your opinion (NOT say): ‘The newspapers express a wide range of political opinions.’
[wrong sentence]:  They are not afraid of saying their opinions.


R:VERB ordered -> book
 [explain]:  See Note at BOOK
[wrong sentence]:  I’ve ordered two seats for tomorrow night’s performance.


R:VERB reserved -> book
 [explain]:  reserve (or book) = arrange for a seat on a train, a seat in a theatre, a table in a restaurant etc, to be kept for you to use at a certain time: ‘I’d like to reserve two seats in the front row for tomorrow night’s performance.’ book = (British English) arrange for a hotel room, holiday accommodation etc, to be kept for you to use at a certain time: ‘We’ve booked a holiday cottage in the Lake District for the first two weeks in June.’ Note that in American English the usual word for this meaning is reserve.
[wrong sentence]:  We’ve reserved the school hall for the evening.


R:VERB reserve -> book
 [explain]:  reserve (or book) = arrange for a seat on a train, a seat in a theatre, a table in a restaurant etc, to be kept for you to use at a certain time: ‘I’d like to reserve two seats in the front row for tomorrow night’s performance.’ book = (British English) arrange for a hotel room, holiday accommodation etc, to be kept for you to use at a certain time: ‘We’ve booked a holiday cottage in the Lake District for the first two weeks in June.’ Note that in American English the usual word for this meaning is reserve.
[wrong sentence]:  I’ve finally managed to reserve a two-room house on the beach.


R:VERB pardoned -> excuse
 [explain]:  pardon = (formal) forgive: ‘I’m sure they will pardon the occasional mistake.’ excuse = give someone permission to stay away from school, work etc, or leave a classroom, meeting etc: ‘Can I be excused from swimming today please? I’ve got a cold.’
[wrong sentence]:  I asked the teacher if I could be pardoned for a few minutes.


R:VERB parking -> park
 [explain]:  Use park when you are talking about what a driver does: ‘You’re not allowed to park (your car) in the city centre.’ ‘Where’ve you parked (your car)?’ To talk about a vehicle that has been left somewhere and is not moving, use parked: ‘That Ford van has been parked there for over a week.’ ‘The street is always full of parked cars.’
[wrong sentence]:  The car was parking outside the flat all night.


R:VERB pass -> spend
 [explain]:  You spend your holidays/a period of time somewhere (NOT pass): ‘We spent a lazy afternoon down by the river.’ When pass is used in connection with time, it is usually intransitive: ‘Two weeks passed and there was still no reply.’
[wrong sentence]:  We like to pass our holidays near the sea.


R:VERB passed -> spend
 [explain]:  You spend your holidays/a period of time somewhere (NOT pass): ‘We spent a lazy afternoon down by the river.’ When pass is used in connection with time, it is usually intransitive: ‘Two weeks passed and there was still no reply.’
[wrong sentence]:  We passed the night in a cheap hotel.
[wrong sentence]:  I passed Christmas in London.


R:VERB stay -> spend
 [explain]:  spend a period of time somewhere (NOT stay): ‘We spent the afternoon at John’s house.’ ‘Where are you going to spend Christmas?’
[wrong sentence]:  He’s invited us to stay a few days with him.


R:VERB performed -> play
 [explain]:  play a particular part or role in a film, play etc (NOT perform): ‘What’s the name of that goodlooking American actor who played Butch Cassidy?’ Compare: ‘She has never performed in front of a live audience before.’ ‘Tonight’s concert will be performed by the Boston Symphony Orchestra.’
[wrong sentence]:  In his last film he performed a middle-aged school teacher.


R:VERB to play -> play
 [explain]:  stop doing sth = cease or discontinue an activity: ‘I stopped reading and turned out the light.’ stop to do sth = halt or pause (in order to do something) : ‘Although I was in a hurry, I stopped to talk to him.’
[wrong sentence]:  I stopped to play the piano at the age of eight.


R:VERB proceeded -> precede
 [explain]:  proceed = (begin and) continue: ‘After a five-minute delay, the lecture proceeded.’ ‘According to latest reports, negotiations are proceeding smoothly.’ precede = come, go, or happen (immediately) before someone or something: ‘Churchill was a much stronger leader than the man who preceded him.’ ‘The snowfall was preceded by a sudden drop in temperature.’
[wrong sentence]:  The main film was proceeded by a short cartoon.


R:VERB proposed -> offer
 [explain]:  offer = (1) tell someone that they can have something if they want it: ‘He offered me a cup of tea.’ ‘She was offered a job on a local newspaper.’(2) tell someone that you are willing to do something: ‘He offered to drive me back to my hotel.’
[wrong sentence]:  A friend of mine has proposed me a job in his restaurant.
[wrong sentence]:  He proposed to put them up for a few days.


R:VERB protest -> complain
 [explain]:  protest = say or do something to show that you strongly disagree with something: ‘The crowds were protesting against the government’s purchase of nuclear weapons.’ ; complain = say that you are annoyed or unhappy about something: ‘He’s always complaining about the weather.’ ‘If you think you’ve been overcharged, you should complain to the manager.’
[wrong sentence]:  She told the shop assistant that she wanted to protest about the cardigan she had bought.


R:VERB raise -> grow
 [explain]:  raise plants, vegetables, animals etc, especially on a farm to sell as food: ‘He grew up in Nebraska where his parents raised chickens.’ grow plants, flowers, vegetables, etc, in a garden for pleasure: ‘This year I thought I’d try growing a few tomatoes.’
[wrong sentence]:  When I was a child, I used to raise my own flowers in a corner of the garden.


R:VERB realize -> know
 [explain]:  See Language Note below
[wrong sentence]:  The school year is divided into three terms, as you realize.


R:VERB realized -> know
 [explain]:  See Language Note below
[wrong sentence]:  Nobody realized whether he was coming.


R:VERB realize -> recognize
 [explain]:  See Language Note below
[wrong sentence]:  I’m sure you will realize the song when you hear it.
[wrong sentence]:  Our own mistakes are sometimes difficult to realize.


R:VERB realize -> practise
 [explain]:  When realize means ‘make something actually happen or become real’, it is usually used with words such as ambition, intention, expectation, hope, dream, fear: ‘I feel confident that the high expectations I have of my new job will be realized.’
[wrong sentence]:  Many people in developing countries do not realize family planning.


R:VERB recognized -> realize
 [explain]:  See Language Note at REALIZE
[wrong sentence]:  On the plane home, I suddenly recognized that I had left my coat at the hotel.


R:VERB to let -> let
 [explain]:  recommend sth: ‘Can you recommend a good hotel?’ recommend that: ‘My accountant recommends that I should open an offshore account.’ ‘We recommend you choose your wedding ring about three months in advance.’ ‘They recommend that 100 be regarded as a minimum number.’ recommend doing sth: ‘I’d never recommend sending a young child to boarding school.’ British English also uses recommend sb to do sth: ‘I wouldn’t recommend you to let your children watch it.’
[wrong sentence]:  I wouldn’t recommend to let your children watch it.


R:VERB refused -> reject
 [explain]:  refuse = say no (when someone wants you to do or accept something): ‘Some of the staff refuse to attend lunchtime meetings.’ ‘We can’t possibly refuse the invitation.’ ‘Simon had to refuse the job offer because it would have meant moving house again’ reject = say that you do not support (an idea, belief, suggestion, plan, proposal, etc): ‘Vegetarians reject the theory that you must eat meat to get all the nutrients you need.’ ‘The belief that a woman’s place is in the home has been widely rejected.’
[wrong sentence]:  He refused their lifestyle and decided to seek a simpler alternative.


 [explain]:  If someone makes you feel that you are no longer loved, needed etc, they reject you (and you feel rejected): ‘It’s terrible to feel rejected by someone you care for.’
[wrong sentence]:  He felt refused by the man he admired most.


R:VERB refuse -> reject
 [explain]:  refuse = say no (when someone wants you to do or accept something): ‘Some of the staff refuse to attend lunchtime meetings.’ ‘We can’t possibly refuse the invitation.’ ‘Simon had to refuse the job offer because it would have meant moving house again’ reject = say that you do not support (an idea, belief, suggestion, plan, proposal, etc): ‘Vegetarians reject the theory that you must eat meat to get all the nutrients you need.’ ‘The belief that a woman’s place is in the home has been widely rejected.’
[wrong sentence]:  I refuse the idea that men and women are psychologically different.


R:VERB regard -> regard
 [explain]:  with regard to (WITHOUT s): ‘With regard to the minutes of our last meeting, may I first draw your attention to Item 3.’ as regards (WITH s): ‘As regards transport, I would suggest that we hire a mini-bus for the days in question.’ See also REFERENCE, REGARDING
[wrong sentence]:  As regard our working environment, the machines make too much noise.


R:VERB remind -> remember
 [explain]:  remind = cause (someone) to remember something: ‘The smell of fresh coffee always reminds me of Salvo’s.’ Compare: ‘Whenever I look at the photograph, I am reminded of the happy days we spent together at college.’
[wrong sentence]:  I remind the happy days we spent together at college.


R:VERB rent -> hire
 [explain]:  See Note at HIRE 1
[wrong sentence]:  If you like riding, there are horses you can rent.
[wrong sentence]:  How much will it cost to rent some skis?


R:VERB resist -> stand
 [explain]:  resist = fight against; oppose: ‘We shall resist any changes that threaten our personal freedom.’ can’t stand/bear (= be unable to tolerate): ‘I’d like to live in Africa but I don’t think I could bear the heat.’
[wrong sentence]:  She couldn’t resist his rudeness any longer and walked out of the room.


R:VERB response -> respond
 [explain]:  Respond is a verb: ‘I’m surprised they responded so quickly.’ Response is a noun: ‘I’m surprised we got such a quick response.’ ‘In response to your last comment, I’m afraid I don’t agree.’
[wrong sentence]:  We’re still waiting for them to response.


R:VERB retired -> resign
 [explain]:  retire = leave your job at the end of your working life, usually because you have reached a particular age: ‘In the UK, men usually retire at the age of 65 and women at 60.’ ‘If you retire early, you won’t get your full pension.’ resign = leave your job because you do not like it, because you have found a better one, etc: ‘If she doesn’t get a salary increase, she’s going to resign.’
[wrong sentence]:  After just two months he retired and went to work for a smaller company.


R:VERB robbed -> steal
 [explain]:  steal sth: ‘Someone has stolen her car.’ ‘My watch has been stolen.’ ‘The girl has obviously stolen his heart.’ rob sb (of sth): ‘The two youths had tried to rob him, but he managed to fight them off.’ ‘The old couple were robbed of their life’s savings.’
[wrong sentence]:  The wicked old man robbed all my money.
[wrong sentence]:  They robbed $6000 from the office safe.


R:VERB running -> move
 [explain]:  Vehicles travel, move or go at a certain speed in a certain direction (NOT run): ‘We’re travelling at just under 70 miles an hour.’ ‘How fast was the train going when the accident occurred?’ ‘s/s’
[wrong sentence]:  The car was running too fast for me to see the number plate.


R:VERB sale -> sell
 [explain]:  Sale /seɪl/ is a noun: ‘This new law makes the sale of pirated video tapes a criminal offence.’ ‘Are you sure the house is for sale?’
[wrong sentence]:  This company prefers to employ girls to sale their products.


R:VERB salute -> welcome
 [explain]:  salute (of members of the armed forces) make a formal sign of respect, especially by raising the right arm: ‘Always salute a superior officer.’ welcome = meet someone when they arrive and show that you are pleased that they have come: ‘The visitors were welcomed at reception and shown where to go.’
[wrong sentence]:  A group of officials were at the airport to salute the visitors.


R:VERB say -> talk
 [explain]:  talk about a particular topic (NOT say about): ‘He refuses to talk about politics.’ ‘She’s always talking about her father’s health.’
[wrong sentence]:  In our next class we’re going to say about pollution.


R:VERB says -> talk
 [explain]:  talk about a particular topic (NOT say about): ‘He refuses to talk about politics.’ ‘She’s always talking about her father’s health.’
[wrong sentence]:  The magazine also says about English football.


R:VERB telling -> talk
 [explain]:  talk about sth (NOT tell about): ‘Once she felt more relaxed, she began to talk about her real reasons for coming to see me.’ Compare tell sb about sth: ‘Did you tell him about the party on Saturday?’ ‘Don’t forget to tell the doctor about your headaches.’
[wrong sentence]:  Ryan was always telling about herself and her problems.


R:VERB search -> look
 [explain]:  Use search for (and seek) only when someone or something is very difficult to find: ‘Investigators are still searching for clues as to the cause of the crash.’ Otherwise, use look for: ‘I’ll stay here with the bags while you go and look for a taxi.’ ‘If you’re looking for some new shoes, you should try Clinkard’s.’
[wrong sentence]:  I decided to search for another hotel further down the road.


R:VERB to look -> look
 [explain]:  spend your time/five minutes/two days etc + doing sth: ‘She spends all her time working on her thesis.’
[wrong sentence]:  Parents should spend more time to look after their children.


 [explain]:  stop doing sth = cease or discontinue an activity: ‘I stopped reading and turned out the light.’ stop to do sth = halt or pause (in order to do something) : ‘Although I was in a hurry, I stopped to talk to him.’
[wrong sentence]:  He loved toys and couldn’t stop to look at them.


R:VERB seat -> sit
 [explain]:  seat /siːt/ is a noun: ‘You should book your seat two weeks in advance.’ sit /sɪt/ is a verb: ‘My legs were aching but there was nowhere to sit.’
[wrong sentence]:  Let’s go and seat out in the garden.


R:VERB seeing -> watch
 [explain]:  see = notice something with your eyes, especially without concentrating or paying attention: ‘Did you see anyone go out?’ Turn the light on if you can’t see.’ watch = look at someone or something and pay careful attention, especially for a long time: ‘She watched the man with interest as he made his way through the crowd.’ ‘After dinner we usually sit down and watch the news.’ I’m sending you the magazine so you can see by yourself how beautiful the country is. I’m sending you the magazine so you can see for yourself how beautiful the country is. see sth for yourself (NOT by) = check that something is really true, correct, as described etc by seeing it with your own eyes: ‘If you think I’m exaggerating, come and see for yourself.’
[wrong sentence]:  He sat there all morning seeing the planes taking off.


R:VERB see -> watch
 [explain]:  see = notice something with your eyes, especially without concentrating or paying attention: ‘Did you see anyone go out?’ Turn the light on if you can’t see.’ watch = look at someone or something and pay careful attention, especially for a long time: ‘She watched the man with interest as he made his way through the crowd.’ ‘After dinner we usually sit down and watch the news.’ I’m sending you the magazine so you can see by yourself how beautiful the country is. I’m sending you the magazine so you can see for yourself how beautiful the country is. see sth for yourself (NOT by) = check that something is really true, correct, as described etc by seeing it with your own eyes: ‘If you think I’m exaggerating, come and see for yourself.’
[wrong sentence]:  The teacher told us to see what he was doing.


R:VERB separated -> divide
 [explain]:  separate= place or keep (people or things) apart from one another: ‘Break an egg into a bowl and separate the white from the yolk.’ divide = cause something to consist of (or be seen as) a number of parts, groups, sections etc: ‘The manufacturing process is divided into three stages.’
[wrong sentence]:  Americans can be separated into a number of ethnic groups.
[wrong sentence]:  England was separated into 650 constituencies.


R:VERB sightsee -> see/visit
 [explain]:  The verb sightsee does not exist.
[wrong sentence]:  You will be able to sightsee the volcanoes.


R:VERB sightseeing -> seeing/visiting
 [explain]:  The verb sightsee does not exist.
[wrong sentence]:  I enjoy sightseeing new places when I’m abroad.


R:VERB am -> have been
 [explain]:  When you talk about an action or situation which began in the past and continues into the present, use a present perfect tense: ‘I’ve been waiting here since 9 o’clock.’
[wrong sentence]:  I am studying law since 1992.


R:VERB haven’t seen -> saw
 [explain]:  it’s ages/a long time /two weeks (etc) since + past tense (NOT present perfect; WITHOUT not) : ‘It’s almost a year since I arrived.’ ‘It must be ages since we last had a meal together.’
[wrong sentence]:  It’s a long time since I haven’t seen you last.


R:VERB sink -> drown
 [explain]:  Sink is used in connection with ships, boats and objects which go down and disappear beneath the surface of water: ‘The ship had been holed in the collision and was beginning to sink.’
[wrong sentence]:  I knew that if I fell into the sea, I would sink.


R:VERB do -> give
 [explain]:  give/make a speech (NOT do): ‘After the meal the bride’s father stood up and made a short speech.’
[wrong sentence]:  I was asked to do a speech welcoming the new students.


R:VERB stolen -> rob
 [explain]:  rob a bank, post office etc (NOT steal) : ‘Apart from the two cashiers , nobody realized that the bank was being robbed.’
[wrong sentence]:  The bank in our town has been stolen twice this year.


R:VERB suffer -> tolerate
 [explain]:  See Note at SUPPORT
[wrong sentence]:  The woman was so unpleasant that none of us could suffer her.


R:VERB support -> tolerate
 [explain]:  tolerate, put up with, stand sb/sth (NOT support/suffer) = be willing to accept someone or something, even though the person or situation is unpleasant: ‘I’m surprised that she tolerates his behaviour.’ ‘It’s not a bad job, as long as you can stand the long hours.’
[wrong sentence]:  He couldn’t support the way his father used to shout at him.


R:VERB suppose -> suppose
 [explain]:  be supposed to be/do sth (WITH -d): ‘How many pages are we supposed to write?’ ‘You’re suppose do be there in five minutes.’
[wrong sentence]:  All their products are suppose to be guaranteed for six months.


R:VERB take -> bring
 [explain]:  See Language Note on next page See PART
[wrong sentence]:  He asked if he could come to your party and take a friend with him.


R:VERB told -> say
 [explain]:  tell sb sth (WITH indirect object): ‘Jim told me that you’ve been offered a new job.’ say sth (WITHOUT indirect object): ‘Jim said that you’ve been offered a new job.’ John said that he was trying to get to London. ‘That’s where I’m going,’ told the man. John said that he was trying to get to London. ‘That’ s where I’m going,’ said the man. Say is used with both direct and indirect speech: "I’m not going,’’’ he said.’ ‘He said he wasn’t going.’
[wrong sentence]:  He told that he hadn’t eaten anything for over a week.


R:VERB thinking -> wonder
 [explain]:  To introduce a polite request or invitation , use I was wondering if/whether: ‘I was wondering if you’d like to play tennis on Saturday.’ ‘Sally was wondering whether you could give her some advice.’
[wrong sentence]:  I was thinking if you would like to have lunch before visiting the museum.


R:VERB are thinking -> think
 [explain]:  See Language Note at CONTAIN
[wrong sentence]:  Some foreigners are thinking the Japanese are rich.


R:VERB threat -> threaten
 [explain]:  Threat is a noun: ‘The letter was full of threats and accusations.’ ‘The President said he would stand firm and not give in to threats from terrorists.’ The verb is threaten: ‘Whenever they have an argument, she threatens to leave him.’ ‘He is threatening the magazine with legal action unless they publish a full apology.’
[wrong sentence]:  They used to threat each other with atomic bombs.


R:VERB spend -> pass
 [explain]:  When you have nothing important to do and you spend time doing something in order to avoid being bored, you pass the time : ‘At the airport we bought a pack of playing cards to help pass the time.’
[wrong sentence]:  We went to the cinema to spend the time until the train arrived.


R:VERB use to -> (usually
 [explain]:  When you are talking about a present habit, use the present simple tense (NOT use to do): ‘I (usually) have two cups of coffee at breakfast.’
[wrong sentence]:  The meals we use to eat are very simple.
[wrong sentence]:  When I’m not busy, I use to play the guitar.


R:VERB saw -> admire
 [explain]:  look at/admire/enjoy the view (NOT see/watch): ‘We asked the coach driver to stop so that we could look at the view.’ ; have/get a (good) view from a particular place (NOT see): ‘If you stand where I am, you get a much better view.’
[wrong sentence]:  We just sat there and saw the view.


R:VERB drove -> was driving
 [explain]:  To refer to a ‘background action’, use the past progressive tense (was /were doing): ‘While I was having a bath, the telephone rang.’
[wrong sentence]:  While I drove to the airport, my car broke down.


R:VERB will -> is to
 [explain]:  When you are talking about what must happen in order to make something possible, use if ... am/is/are to do sth (NOT will): ‘If we are to get there by six o’clock, we’ll have to get a taxi.’ See Language Note below
[wrong sentence]:  If a developing country will become a developed country, it has to attract foreign investors.


R:VERB won -> beat
 [explain]:  win a game, match, race, competition, election, etc: ‘United have won four of their last five matches.’ ‘Who won the FA Cup last year?’ beat a person, team or group (in a game, match, race, competition, election etc): ‘They’re a good side but I’m sure we can beat them.’ ‘
[wrong sentence]:  We have never won the American team.


R:VERB wounded -> injured/hurt
 [explain]:  See Note at DAMAGE 1
[wrong sentence]:  Only the driver was wounded in the accident.


R:VERB write -> am writing
 [explain]:  For actions which are happening at the time when they are mentioned, use the present progressive tense (NOT the present simple): ‘I’m writing to tell you that I’ll be coming to London next Thursday.’
[wrong sentence]:  Dear... I write to you to ask for your advice.


U:PREP to -> NONE
 [explain]:  go/live/be abroad (WITHOUT to, at, in etc) The only preposition that is used before abroad is from: ‘She came back from abroad saying how much she had missed her home and family.’
[wrong sentence]:  Since I was small, I’ve always wanted to go to abroad.


 [explain]:  allow sb to do sth (NOT to sb): ‘The principal doesn’t allow them to wear jewellery to school.’
[wrong sentence]:  They also allow to the prisoners to keep birds.

[wrong sentence]:  My job involves cooking, cleaning and answering to the door bell.
[wrong sentence]:  I couldn’t answer to the last two questions.

 [explain]:  believe in sth = (1) accept that something exists: ‘Do you believe in ghosts?’ ‘James still believes in Father Christmas.’ (2) think that something is good: ‘I don’t believe in capital punishment.’ ‘I believe in getting a good night’s sleep before an examination.’ believe sth = accept that something is true or real: ‘I believe every word she said.’ ‘I couldn’t believe what I was seeing.’
[wrong sentence]:  She couldn’t believe in what was happening to her.
[wrong sentence]:  You should not believe in everything you hear.

 [explain]:  keep fit (WITHOUT in): ‘She keeps fit by jogging five miles every day.’ ; Compare: ‘Cycling to work helps me to keep in shape.’
[wrong sentence]:  A little exercise will help you to keep in fit.


 [explain]:  Do not use in before here/there when you mean ‘in this country or region’: ‘I’m having a problem with the food here.’ ‘Groceries here are much cheaper than in Japan.’ You use in here when you mean ‘in this building/room/container etc’: ‘Nobody is allowed to come in here except authorized personnel.’ ‘I leave most of my books in here’.
[wrong sentence]:  The weather in here is very cold.
[wrong sentence]:  The people in here believe in Hinduism.


 [explain]:  See Language Note at TIME
[wrong sentence]:  We intend to visit the north of England in this summer.
[wrong sentence]:  I haven’t seen any good films in this year.
[wrong sentence]:  There was almost a nuclear disaster in last year.


 [explain]:  Expressions of frequency, speed, duration etc do not contain in: ‘The shop is open six days a week.’ ‘He visits his father three times a year.’ ‘Bananas cost fifty pence a pound. the hospital at ninety miles an hour.’
[wrong sentence]:  We have 4 classes in a day.
[wrong sentence]:  They usually work 39 hours in a week.


 [explain]:  join a club, society etc (WITHOUT in/up): ‘I’m thinking of joining the Labour Party.’ ‘He left his job and joined the army.’
[wrong sentence]:  You should join in our tennis club.


 [explain]:  nowadays (WITHOUT in): ‘More women have executive jobs nowadays, especially in publishing.’ ‘Nowadays, computer skills are essential.’
[wrong sentence]:  People live longer in nowadays.


 [explain]:  speak in (English) = use (English) on a particular occasion : ‘My grandparents won’t understand a thing if the priest speaks in French.’ speak (English) = know (English) and use it habitually: ‘Do you speak German?’ ‘We’ll have to find someone who speaks English.’
[wrong sentence]:  Neither of my parents speaks in English.


 [explain]:  trust in = (formal or literary) have faith in: ‘All will be well as long as you trust in God.’ ; trust (WITHOUT in) = believe that someone is honest (and will not hurt or deceive you): ‘He’s just a bit too friendly and I’m afraid I don’t trust him.’ ‘I’m the only person he’ll trust to look after his money.’
[wrong sentence]:  After he was caught stealing, nobody trusted in him.


 [explain]:  When where begins a relative clause, it is not followed by in, at, for, etc: ‘We went to see the house where Shakespeare lived.’ Compare: ‘The box that/which she kept her jewellery in had disappeared.’
[wrong sentence]:  The box where she kept her jewellery in had disappeared.

[wrong sentence]:  I wasn’t even there so you can’t blame on me.

U:PREP of -> NONE
 [explain]:  For general reference, put all/most/some etc immediately in front of the noun: ‘In Japan most people use chopsticks.’ For specific reference, use all/most/some + of the/these/their etc + noun: ‘Most of the Americans I met were very friendly.’
[wrong sentence]:  All of children learn to speak naturally.

[wrong sentence]:  Despite of my qualifications, I couldn’t get a job.

[wrong sentence]:  I came to England a year ago to join with my husband.


 [explain]:  marry sb (WITHOUT With/to): ‘Isn’t he the man who married Elizabeth Taylor?’ get married (to sb): ‘I can still remember the day I got married. United were playing at Wembley.’
[wrong sentence]:  He’s going to ask her to marry with him.


 [explain]:  One thing matches/doesn’t match another thing (WITHOUT to/with): ‘You can’t wear that blue tie. It doesn’t match your shirt.’
[wrong sentence]:  We chose the sunflowers to match with the wallpaper.


 [explain]:  meet with sb (mainly American English) = have a (previously arranged) meeting with someone: ‘We met with their representatives to discuss the problem.’ meet sb (British and American English) = be in the same place as someone by chance or arrangement: ‘Guess who I met on the way to the bank this morning?’ ‘I suggest we meet back here in an hour’s time.’
[wrong sentence]:  Could you meet with me outside the station?
[wrong sentence]:  The next time I met with her was in the supermarket.


U:PREP although -> NONE
 [explain]:  If the first clause begins with although or (even) though, do not begin the second clause with but or yet.
[wrong sentence]:  Although I enjoyed my stay in the USA, but I was still glad to come home.


U:PREP as -> NONE
