 [explain]:  call sb/sth + name (WITHOUT as): ‘People call her the Queen of Rock.’ ‘The big apples are called
[wrong sentence]:  Koreans call this room as ‘anbang’.
[wrong sentence]:  This process is called as nitrogen fixation.


U:PREP by -> NONE
 [explain]:  pay by cheque, pay by credit card BUT pay in cash or just pay cash: ‘If you pay (in) cash, you might get a discount.’
[wrong sentence]:  I prefer to pay by cash.


U:PREP over -> NONE
 [explain]:  control sth (verb) WITHOUT of/over: ‘It’s usually the editor who controls what goes into a newspaper.’ The security forces are no longer able to control the situation.’ control of/over sth (noun): ‘It’s usually the editor who has control over what goes Into a newspaper.’ ‘The security forces have lost control of the situation.’
[wrong sentence]:  These people want to control over our lives.


U:PREP about -> NONE
 [explain]:  discuss sth (WITHOUT about/on): ‘He simply refuses to discuss the matter.’ ‘There is nothing to discuss.’ Compare talk about, a discussion about/on: ‘They want to talk about what to do next.’ ‘They want a discussion about/on what to do next.’
[wrong sentence]:  They’d like to discuss about what to do next.


 [explain]:  Subject is not followed by about. Note the alternative: ‘My thesis is about women in the popular press.’
[wrong sentence]:  The subject of my thesis is about women in the popular press.


 [explain]:  understand sb/sth (WITHOUT about): ‘After twenty years of marriage, we still don’t understand each other.’
[wrong sentence]:  People find it difficult to understand about the Japanese.


U:PREP during -> NONE
 [explain]:  When a phrase saying ‘how long’ begins with all, there is no preposition: ‘It rained all night.’ ‘He’s been in bed all day.’
[wrong sentence]:  My uncle has known me during all my life.
[wrong sentence]:  The baby cried during all night long.


U:PREP into -> NONE
 [explain]:  enter a room, building, country etc (WITHOUT into): ‘Whenever a teacher entered the room, the children had to stand up.’
[wrong sentence]:  The number of foreign workers that are allowed to enter into Japan has increased.


 [explain]:  investigate sth (WITHOUT into): The cause of the accident is still being investigated.’
[wrong sentence]:  The Hong Kong government has already investigated into these problems.


U:PREP against -> NONE
 [explain]:  When you are talking about an event or something that lasts only a short time, use fight sb/sth (WITHOUT against): ‘He said he would fight anyone who tried to stop him.’ ‘The best way to fight a cold is to get plenty of sleep.’ You can use either fight sb/sth or fight against sb/sth when you are talking about a long struggle to overcome things such as poverty, disease, injustice or oppression: ‘The ANC has spent half a century fighting (against) racism.’ ‘The group was founded in 1983 to fight (against) the military regime.’
[wrong sentence]:  Why were the boys fighting against each other?
[wrong sentence]:  They spent the whole night fighting against the fire.


 [explain]:  resist sb/sth (WITHOUT to/against): ‘By resisting the Mafia’s attempts to control the region, Dolci was putting his own life in danger.’ Compare: ‘Much of the resistance to social and political change comes from people who oppose the government.’ (noun + to)
[wrong sentence]:  Without weapons there was no way of resisting against the attack.


U:PREP up -> NONE
 [explain]:  jump = (of an amount, price, level, etc) suddenly increase: ‘House pricesjumped almost 20% in the first quarter.’
[wrong sentence]:  The price of vegetables jumped up overnight.


U:PREP from -> NONE
 [explain]:  leave somewhere (usually WITHOUT from): ‘If I leave the office at six, I’m home by ten to seven.’ Use leave from only when you want to refer to the place where a journey, trip, excursion etc begins: ‘The coach will be leaving from in front of the hotel at six o’clock sharp.’
[wrong sentence]:  I leave from Bilbao on 12th June.
[wrong sentence]:  I left from the shop without buying anything.


 [explain]:  stop doing sth (WITHOUT from) = cease an activity: ‘ It’s actually stopped raining at last.’
[wrong sentence]:  The child couldn’t stop from talking.


 [explain]:  suffer an injury, pain, loss, defeat etc (WITHOUT from): ‘She can walk again, but she still suffers a lot of pain.’ ‘The party suffered yet another humiliating defeat in the recent by-election. suffer from a disease, poverty, starvation etc: ‘A lot of the children we saw were suffering from malnutrition.’ ‘Aunt Linda suffers from arthritis.’
[wrong sentence]:  The other passenger suffered from serious leg injuries.


U:PREP if -> NONE
 [explain]:  Unless (= if … not) is NOT used with if: That’s all for today unless anyone has any questions.’ ‘Don’t say anything unless you have to.’
[wrong sentence]:  We can go in my car unless if you prefer to walk.


U:PREP in these -> these
 [explain]:  these days (WITHOUT in/during etc): ‘He’s very busy these days.’
[wrong sentence]:  In these days many children have their own computer.


U:PREP on every -> every
 [explain]:  See Language Note at TIME
[wrong sentence]:  On every Saturday afternoon I play tennis.


 [explain]:  See Language Note at TIME
[wrong sentence]:  On every Saturday morning we go shopping.


U:PREP at last -> last
 [explain]:  last but not least (WITHOUT at): ‘Last but not least, I’d like to thank our sponsors for making this evening possible.’
[wrong sentence]:  At last but not least, I must mention the climate.


U:PREP on last -> last
 [explain]:  See Language Note at TIME
[wrong sentence]:  On last Monday we went to the Railway Museum.


U:PREP at the -> the
 [explain]:  the moment (WITHOUT at) = as soon as: ‘He fell in love with Samantha the moment he set eyes on her.’
[wrong sentence]:  At the moment I saw him, I knew something was wrong.


U:PREP since it’s -> it’s
 [explain]:  If the first clause begins with if, since, as or because, do NOT begin the second clause with so (or that’s why) . See also BUT
[wrong sentence]:  Since it’s his birthday on Monday, so he’s having a party.


U:PREP during this -> this
 [explain]:  See Language Note at TIME
[wrong sentence]:  During this weekend there is a squash tournament.


R:ADV absolutely -> very
 [explain]:  See Note at VERY 2
[wrong sentence]:  It is absolutely important that you see a doctor immediately.


R:ADV completely -> very
 [explain]:  See Note at VERY 2
[wrong sentence]:  Then the lights went out and we were completely scared.


R:ADV much -> very
 [explain]:  Much is used before an adjective (1) in questions and negative sentences: ‘Is British culture much different from ours?’ ‘No, it isn’t much different.’ (2) before comparative forms: ‘His last novel was much longer and much more interesting.’ (3) before certain past participles acting as adjectives: ‘Her drawings are much admired.’
[wrong sentence]:  British culture is much different from ours.
[wrong sentence]:  We were much afraid that we would miss the flight.


R:ADV afterwards -> later on
 [explain]:  Afterwards suggests that the next thing happens as soon as the last thing has finished: ‘On Saturday morning I went to see Adrian in hospital. Afterwards I drove into town to do some shopping.’ When there is a long interval or delay between two actions or events, use later on: ‘I couldn’t understand why she hadn’t answered my letters. Later on I discovered that she had moved to a new address.’ First you draw a long straight line. Afterwards you draw another line, parallel to the first one. First you draw a long straight line. Then you draw another line, parallel to the first one. To introduce the next action in a process or series of actions, use then: ‘Check that the paper is properly loaded. Then press the start button.’ Compare: ‘We all had lunch together at one o’clock.
[wrong sentence]:  We started going out together just to have fun, as friends. Afterwards we both realized that there was more than just friendship.


R:ADV again -> back
 [explain]:  If you give something to the person who gave it to you, you give it back to them. When you mean ‘to the person who had it before’, ‘to the place where something was before’ etc, use back (NOT again): ‘He took the camera back to the shop and asked for his money back.’ ‘Shall I put these books back on the shelf?’
[wrong sentence]:  He’d like to have his bicycle again if you’ve finished with it.
[wrong sentence]:  It’s time I gave you your photographs again.


 [explain]:  If you telephone someone after they have telephoned you, you call/ring/phone them back: ‘Put down the receiver and I’ll call you back.’
[wrong sentence]:  I’ll phone you again in five minutes.


R:ADV always -> still
 [explain]:  Always means ‘all the time’ or ‘every time’: ‘She has always wanted her own Mercedes.’ ‘I always go to work by train.’
[wrong sentence]:  He died a long time ago, but his ideas are always alive.


R:ADV yet -> still
 [explain]:  Yet means ‘up to the moment of speaking’ and is used mainly in question and negative sentences: ‘Do you feel any better yet?’ ‘The post office isn’t open yet.’ When you want to say that an earlier state or situation has not changed, use still: ‘I’ve taken the medicine but I still feel terrible.’ ‘Does Hilary still go to the same school?’
[wrong sentence]:  He left the house at five in the morning, when the family was yet asleep.


R:ADV anywhere -> everywhere
 [explain]:  When you mean ‘in/to all places’, use everywhere (NOT anywhere).
[wrong sentence]:  Computers will be anywhere we go.


R:ADV approximately -> roughly
 [explain]:  Approximately is used mainly in formal written styles: ‘It is estimated that, during the period in question, approximately 47,000 cars were stolen in the London area alone.’
[wrong sentence]:  The train fare is approximately £20.


R:ADV bad -> badly
 [explain]:  Bad is an adjective: ‘I tried to ignore the child’s bad behaviour.’
[wrong sentence]:  I’m afraid I speak English very bad.


R:ADV barely -> rarely
 [explain]:  barely = almost not; only just: ‘His handwriting is barely legible.’ (= you can only just read it) ‘The fog was so bad that we barely made it to the motel.’ rarely/seldom/hardly ever = almost never: ‘He rarely makes any serious mistakes.’
[wrong sentence]:  Although I am a member of the club, I barely go there.


R:ADV scarcely -> rarely
 [explain]:  When you are talking about frequency, use rarely (= almost never): ‘Since she moved to Glasgow, we rarely see each other.’
[wrong sentence]:  He sends them money but scarcely goes to see them.


R:ADV deeply -> firmly
 [explain]:  Firmly/strongly/fervently/sincerely/truly believe (NOT deeply): ‘I sincerely believe that he had no connection with the crime.’
[wrong sentence]:  I deeply believe that tourists will stop coming here.


R:ADV deeply -> strongly
 [explain]:  Firmly/strongly/fervently/sincerely/truly believe (NOT deeply): ‘I sincerely believe that he had no connection with the crime.’
[wrong sentence]:  My brother deeply believes in freedom of speech.


R:ADV much -> far
 [explain]:  far below (NOT much): ‘The cost of the trip was far below what we had expected.’
[wrong sentence]:  The room rates are much below average for hotels in this area.


 [explain]:  At the beginning of a phrase that ends with a plural (countable) noun, use far (NOT much). Compare: ‘much more traffic’, ‘far more cars’
[wrong sentence]:  I make much too many mistakes.
[wrong sentence]:  Nowadays, there are much more criminals than policemen.


R:ADV certainly -> definitely
 [explain]:  When you mean ‘absolutely certain and without even the slightest doubt’, use definitely. This word gives very strong emphasis and is often used in connection with intentions and future events: ‘He definitely wants to be a vet.’ ‘Do you think that you’ll definitely be able to come?’
[wrong sentence]:  Are you sure that you certainly don’t want to go?
[wrong sentence]:  If they certainly can’t find a job, they should be given further training.


R:ADV continually -> continuously
 [explain]:  continual = happening repeatedly over a long period of time, especially in a way that you find annoying: ‘That telephone has been ringing continually.’ continuous = without any break or interruption: ‘Gas central heating provides a continuous supply of hot water.’
[wrong sentence]:  A line of cars stretched continually down the motorway.


R:ADV continuously -> continually
 [explain]:  See Note at CONTINUAL
[wrong sentence]:  The meeting was continuously interrupted.


R:ADV very -> absolutely
 [explain]:  See Note at VERY 2
[wrong sentence]:  When they knew that they had won, they were very delighted.


 [explain]:  See Note at VERY 2
[wrong sentence]:  The shops in London are very fantastic.


R:ADV extremely -> absolutely
 [explain]:  See Note at VERY 2
[wrong sentence]:  I’m extremely convinced that there will not be another war.


R:ADV effectively -> indeed
 [explain]:  Effectively is used to show that you are describing what a particular situation is really like, especially when this is not obvious: ‘At that time, free elections in Zambia were unknown, and Kaunda had, effectively, made himself life-president.’
[wrong sentence]:  We finally got to see the secretary who confirmed that there was effectively a young man being held in prison.


R:ADV especially -> specially
 [explain]:  See Note at SPECIALLY 1
[wrong sentence]:  The course has been especially designed for students preparing for the First Certificate examination.


R:ADV ever -> always
 [explain]:  ever = at any time: ‘Have you ever been to Paris?’ always = from the beginning until now; every time: ‘He’s always been very generous.’ ‘She always arrives late.’
[wrong sentence]:  He has ever been my hero.


R:ADV finally -> eventually
 [explain]:  See Language Note at END
[wrong sentence]:  It was difficult to understand people for a long time but finally I got used to their accent.


R:ADV gently -> politely
 [explain]:  gently = in a way that will not hurt or damage anything: ‘She held the little bird very gently.’ politely = in a way that shows good manners: ‘He politely refused her offer, saying that he didn’t want to trouble her.’
[wrong sentence]:  The shop assistant asked me gently what I wanted.


R:ADV gently -> kindly
 [explain]:  In polite formal requests, use if you would/could kindly (or if you would be so kind as to): ‘I would be very grateful if you would kindly allow Julian to leave school early on Tuesday.’
[wrong sentence]:  I wonder if you would gently lend us a room for one of the monthly meetings.


R:ADV deeply -> sincerely
 [explain]:  sincerely hope (NOT deeply): ‘I sincerely hope that you will understand why we cannot come after all.’
[wrong sentence]:  I deeply hope that there will never be another war.


R:ADV utterly -> sincerely
 [explain]:  Utterly is usually used with words that have a negative meaning or express strong disapproval such as (adjectives) ridiculous, absurd, irrelevant, useless, wrong, impossible, (adjectival participles) confused, amazed, dejected, ruined, (verbs) reject, detest, destroy: ‘This new tin opener is utterly useless.’ ‘The whole idea is utterly absurd.’ ‘I’m utterly amazed.’ ‘The entire building was utterly destroyed.’
[wrong sentence]:  I utterly hope you won’t be angry with me.


R:ADV badly -> seriously
 [explain]:  seriously ill (NOT badly): ‘So far three people have died and five more are seriously ill.’
[wrong sentence]:  He wanted to be with his son who was badly ill.


R:ADV deeply -> seriously
 [explain]:  think seriously/carefully (NOT deeply) (about sth), especially before making a decision: ‘The job has a lot of attractions and in my opinion you should think seriously about it.’
[wrong sentence]:  He advised me to think deeply about it before making a decision.


R:ADV deeply -> well
 [explain]:  (get to) know sb/sth very well (NOT deeply): ‘I know Alex very well. We used to go to the same school.’
[wrong sentence]:  I got to know the south of London very deeply.


R:ADV well -> exactly/precisely
 [explain]:  know sb/sth well = be fully familiar with: ‘After living in Florence for two years, I know the city very well.’ Compare: ‘I’m completely against the idea, as you well know.’
[wrong sentence]:  He knew well where he wanted to go.


R:ADV lately -> recently
 [explain]:  Both lately and recently refer to a period of time that begins in the past and continues until now (the moment of speaking): ‘Just lately/recently I have been wondering whether to look for a new job.’
[wrong sentence]:  Lately someone told me that the fire was caused by a cigarette.


R:ADV mostly -> almost
 [explain]:  Use almost/nearly with all/every/everyone etc (NOT mostly): ‘Almost everyone in the office has had a cold recently.’
[wrong sentence]:  We see each other mostly every day.


R:ADV mostly -> nearly
 [explain]:  Use almost/nearly with all/every/everyone etc (NOT mostly): ‘Almost everyone in the office has had a cold recently.’
[wrong sentence]:  Mostly every family has a television.


R:ADV nervous -> anxious
 [explain]:  nervous = worried and unable to relax, especially because you lack confidence: There’s no need to be so nervous. It’s only an interview.’ anxious = very worried about something which may happen or may have happened: ‘I knew that there were no sharks around but all the same I couldn’t help feeling anxious.’ What really made me nervous was the
[wrong sentence]:  Thinking she might be hurt, I felt very nervous.


R:ADV never -> ever
 [explain]:  never = at no time: ‘I’ve never met his wife.’ ‘I had never met his wife before yesterday.’ ever = at any time: ‘Have you ever met his wife?’
[wrong sentence]:  She said some of the rudest things I have never heard in all my life.


R:ADV quite -> rather
 [explain]:  Before comparative forms use rather, slightly, somewhat or (especially in informal styles) a bit, a little (NOT quite): ‘My new office is rather smaller than my old one.’ ‘He’s slightly older than me.’ Note however the common exception: ‘Yes, I’m feeling quite better, thank you.’ (= completely recovered)
[wrong sentence]:  This year the work at university is quite harder than last year.


R:ADV rapidly -> quickly
 [explain]:  When used in connection with movement, rapid and rapidly usually refer to something that happens at a very fast rate: ‘She could hear rapid footsteps approaching.’ ‘The patient often develops a fever and breathing becomes rapid.’
[wrong sentence]:  I rapidly took the gun out of my pocket.


R:ADV rather -> quite
 [explain]:  When rather means ‘to a small extent’, it is usually used with words that express negative qualities: ‘The bed was rather uncomfortable.’ ‘The lesson was rather boring.’ ‘He’s always been rather lazy.’
[wrong sentence]:  The bed was rather comfortable.
[wrong sentence]:  The weather is rather good today.


R:ADV really -> actually
 [explain]:  Really and actually are sometimes interchangeable: ‘She sold the piano for a lot more than it was actually/really worth.’ When you mean ‘strange as it may seem’, use actually (NOT really): ‘Instead of running away as he normally does, he actually offered to stay and help.’
[wrong sentence]:  To his great amazement, little Nicola really won the race.


R:ADV deeply -> closely
 [explain]:  closely related (NOT deeply): ‘His thesis is that drugs, boredom and juvenile crime are closely related.’
[wrong sentence]:  The size of a family is deeply related to parental income.


R:ADV sensible -> sensitive
 [explain]:  Use sensible to describe someone who makes good decisions based on reason, and never behaves in a stupid or dangerous way: ‘I’m glad to see that she was sensible enough to bring some warm clothes.’ ‘Be sensible - you can’t wear high heels to a garden party.’ Use sensitive to describe someone who is easily upset or offended: ‘He’s very sensitive about his weight, so try not to mention it.’ ‘Don’t be so sensitive - he was only joking.’
[wrong sentence]:  Children are very sensible; they all need love and attention.


R:ADV shortly -> briefly
 [explain]:  shortly = (1) impatiently; not politely: ‘He answered rather shortly that he was NOT the slightest bit interested .’ (2) very soon: ‘The accident happened shortly after they moved into their new house.’ briefly = for a short time: ‘We talked briefly about the financial side of the agreement and then moved on to other matters.’
[wrong sentence]:  She spoke very shortly about how they had lived during the war.


R:ADV specially -> especially
 [explain]:  Use specially when you mean that something is done or made for a particular purpose: ‘We’ve come all the way from Frankfurt specially to see you.’ ‘The stamps were specially designed to commemorate the fiftieth anniversary of the United Nations.’ For all other meanings, use especially: ‘Paris is always full of tourists, especially during the summer months.’ ‘Middle-aged men, especially those who are overweight, are susceptible to heart-attacks.’
[wrong sentence]:  I also enjoy water sports and I am specially interested in sailing.
[wrong sentence]:  They should not drop bombs on innocent people, specially children.
[wrong sentence]:  Ordering a meal can be very difficult, specially when there is no menu.


R:ADV surely -> naturally
 [explain]:  When you mean ‘as anyone would expect’, use naturally or of course: ‘She’s just lost her job and so naturally she’s feeling a bit depressed.’ ‘Henry is still in France but he’ll be back in time for the wedding of course.’
[wrong sentence]:  He said he felt worried because of all the recent air disasters. Surely I became worried too.


R:ADV therefore -> so
 [explain]:  Therefore is used mainly in formal styles: ‘Crops have been badly affected by the drought and therefore food will be in short supply.’
[wrong sentence]:  The child looked lost and therefore I asked him if I could help.


 [explain]:  Therefore is used mainly in formal styles: ‘Crops have been badly affected by the drought and therefore food will be in short supply.’ In non-formal styles use so: ‘It was quite late so I didn’t stay long.’
[wrong sentence]:  The child looked lost and therefore I asked him if I could help.


R:ADV thus -> so
 [explain]:  Thus is used only in formal styles: ‘All vaccines carry some risk of side effects. Thus emergency equipment and appropriate drugs should be available at all immunization sessions.’ ; In non-formal styles use so: ‘The smell of paint can give you a headache and so it’s a good idea to keep the windows open.’
[wrong sentence]:  My house is near Greenwich Park and thus we’ll be able to visit the famous Observatory.


R:ADV very -> so
 [explain]:  so + adjective/adverb + that clause: ‘The music was so loud that I started to get a headache.’
[wrong sentence]:  He looked very funny that I couldn’t help laughing.


R:ADV utterly -> completely
 [explain]:  Utterly is usually used with words that have a negative meaning or express strong disapproval such as (adjectives) ridiculous, absurd, irrelevant, useless, wrong, impossible, (adjectival participles) confused, amazed, dejected, ruined, (verbs) reject, detest, destroy: ‘This new tin opener is utterly useless.’ ‘The whole idea is utterly absurd.’ ‘I’m utterly amazed.’ ‘The entire building was utterly destroyed.’
[wrong sentence]:  I have to make sure that our customers are utterly satisfied.


R:ADV very -> (absolutely
 [explain]:  Do not use very or extremely with adjectives which already have a strong meaning, e.g. boiling (= very hot), convinced (= very sure), exhausted (= very tired), huge (= very big), terrible (= very bad). If you want to increase the strength of these words, use absolutely or (depending on the particular adjective) completely, totally, utterly or quite: ‘By the time I got home I was completely exhausted.’ With adjectives which do not have a strong meaning, use very or extremely (NOT absolutely, completely etc): ‘By the time I got back home I was very tired.’
[wrong sentence]:  I’m very convinced that he is telling the truth.


R:ADV very -> greatly
 [explain]:  Very may be used with past participles that are like adjectives and refer to a state: ‘very bored/worried/interested/pleased’. Past participles with a strongly passive meaning are usually modified by greatly or (very) much: ‘His work is much admired.’ ‘This courageous woman, who helped so many of us, will be greatly missed.’
[wrong sentence]:  Their services are very appreciated by the hospital management.


R:ADJ important -> essential
 [explain]:  See Note at VERY 2
[wrong sentence]:  It is absolutely important that you see a doctor immediately.


R:ADJ big -> serious
 [explain]:  a bad/dreadful/nasty/serious/fatal accident (NOT big)
[wrong sentence]:  Her car was involved in a big accident.


R:ADJ heavy -> serious
 [explain]:  a serious crime (NOT heavy): ‘The public are alarmed by the increase in serious crimes.’
[wrong sentence]:  All the prisoners had committed heavy crimes.


 [explain]:  a serious accident/crime/illness etc (NOT heavy)
[wrong sentence]:  All the prisoners had committed heavy crimes.
[wrong sentence]:  Her car was involved in a heavy accident.


R:ADJ earnest -> serious
 [explain]:  Earnest is used to describe someone who takes life too seriously, often failing to see things that are humorous: ‘He’s one of those very earnest types that go around looking for problems to solve.’ Serious is used to describe someone who is fully committed to something: ‘I see her at the karate club once a week, but I don’t think she’s serious.’
[wrong sentence]:  The earnest students never missed a class.


R:ADJ accurate -> exact
 [explain]:  Accurate is mainly used (1) to describe something said or written that contains no errors: ‘Her novels are always historically accurate.’ ‘These figures can’t be accurate, surely.’ (2) to describe something that produces no errors: ‘Are you sure the bathroom scales are accurate?’ When you mean ‘correct and as detailed as possible’, use exact: ‘The exact time is three minutes to seven.’ ‘Do you remember his exact words?’ ‘They’ll be arriving next week - on Friday at three, to be exact.
[wrong sentence]:  I cannot give you the accurate date of my arrival yet.


R:ADJ actual -> present
 [explain]:  actual = real (as opposed what is believed, planned or expected): ‘People think he is over fifty but his actual age is forty-eight.’ ‘Although buses are supposed to run every fifteen minutes, the actual waiting time can be up to an hour.’ present/current = happening or existing now: ‘No one can drive that car in its present condition.’ ‘Her current boyfriend works for Shell.’
[wrong sentence]:  My actual job involves a lot of administration.


R:ADJ aged -> elderly
 [explain]:  When it means ‘very old’, aged (pronounced /ˈeɪdʒɪd/ ) is mainly used in formal styles, usually in the phrase the aged: ‘The poor and the aged are entitled to free health care.’ The usual word for this meaning is elderly, which also sounds polite: ‘The photograph was of an elderly gentleman with a white moustache.’ ‘The building has been converted into a retirement home for the elderly.’
[wrong sentence]:  Not all aged parents have children to look after them.


R:ADJ old -> elderly
 [explain]:  To refer to old people in general, use the elderly: ‘The building has been converted into a retirement home for the elderly.’
[wrong sentence]:  The old should not be brushed aside by society.


R:ADJ alive -> lively
 [explain]:  lively = full of energy: ‘The Yorkshire Terrier is a lively breed of dog.’
[wrong sentence]:  Our teacher, Mr Collins, is very alive.


R:ADJ alone -> lonely
 [explain]:  alone = without other people around you: ‘I’ve thought about getting married, but I prefer living alone.’ lonely = sad because you are alone and feel that nobody loves you or cares about you: ‘I didn’t know anyone in Boston and felt very lonely.’ ‘Sarah hated the long lonely days in the empty house.’
[wrong sentence]:  I was very alone at first but then I made some friends.


R:ADJ big -> large
 [explain]:  a large amount (NOT big/high)
[wrong sentence]:  I was lucky and won a big amount of money.


 [explain]:  a large/considerable number (NOT big): ‘A large number of fatal accidents are caused by drunken drivers.’
[wrong sentence]:  In England I met a big number of women in senior positions.


R:ADJ high -> large
 [explain]:  a large amount (NOT big/high)
[wrong sentence]:  Cream cheese contains a high amount of fat.


R:ADJ ample -> spacious
 [explain]:  ample = (more than) enough; plenty (of): ‘The boot contains ample room for two large suitcases.’ ‘Just one spoonful should be ample.’ ‘He was given ample opportunity to express his opinion.’ spacious = large, with a lot of space: ‘These ideal holiday homes are extremely spacious and within walking distance of the sea.’
[wrong sentence]:  The kitchen is very ample and has a window overlooking the garden.


R:ADJ amusing -> enjoyable
 [explain]:  amusing = causing you to laugh or smile: ‘The speaker was clearly embarrassed when the microphone stopped working, but the audience found the situation very amusing.’ ‘I don’t see anything amusing about finding a snake in your tent in the middle of the night’ enjoyable = causing you to feel happy; pleasant: ‘It was one of the most enjoyable holidays we’ve ever had.’ ‘Exercise may be hard work, but it can also be enjoyable.’
[wrong sentence]:  The last three years have been the most amusing years of my life.


R:ADJ amusing -> interest
 [explain]:  Something that holds your attention (and makes you want to know more) is interesting (NOT amusing): ‘The most interesting thing about the dinosaurs is their sudden disappearance.’ ‘His plan for raising more money sounds interesting but I’m not sure it’ll work.’
[wrong sentence]:  Most visitors find the museum extremely amusing.


R:ADJ interested -> interest
 [explain]:  See Note at BORING
[wrong sentence]:  It was a very interested idea.


R:ADJ interesting -> interest
 [explain]:  l am very interested in community work. See Note at BORING
[wrong sentence]:  I was interesting to hear she had got married.


R:ADJ annoyed -> irritate
 [explain]:  Something unpleasant that happens repeatedly or continuously over a long period of time tends to irritate or frustrate people, especially because they know that they can do nothing to stop it: ‘I felt so tense that even the ticking of the clock began to irritate me.’ ‘I am constantly frustrated by all the niggling little jobs I have to do.’
[wrong sentence]:  I feel rather annoyed when I see the same advertisement time and time again.


R:ADJ antique -> old
 [explain]:  Antique is used for objects (NOT buildings): ‘The upper-middle class have become collectors of antique furniture.’
[wrong sentence]:  There is a beautiful antique church in the centre of the town.


R:ADJ anxious -> nervous
 [explain]:  anxious = worried because you fear that something bad may happen or may have happened: ‘Their daughter hadn’t come home from school and they were anxious about her safety.’ ‘I knew it was just a minor operation, but I couldn’t help feeling anxious.’ nervous = worried because you are in or about to enter a stressful situation: ‘I was so nervous about the exam that I couldn’t sleep.’ ‘It was our first television appearance and we were all feeling nervous.’
[wrong sentence]:  I always feel anxious when I have to make a speech.


R:ADJ anxious -> worry
 [explain]:  worried = unhappy because you have (or expect that you are going to have) a problem: ‘The business is losing money and the boss is very worried.’
[wrong sentence]:  I was anxious that somebody would see us through the window.


 [explain]:  not worried/concerned/bothered about sth = feeling that something is not a problem: ‘I’m not bothered about how much it will cost.’
[wrong sentence]:  He obviously isn’t anxious about public opinion.


R:ADJ appropriate -> relevant
 [explain]:  appropriate = suitable in a particular situation: ‘Once we know more about the cause of the problem, we can take appropriate action.’ ‘To offer them more money at this stage would not be appropriate.’ relevant = having a clear connection with what you are doing or talking about: ‘I’ll try to answer any questions, as long as they are relevant.’ ‘You cannot enter the country unless you have obtained all the relevant documents.’
[wrong sentence]:  The third paragraph of the essay is not appropriate.
[wrong sentence]:  Will you please send me all the appropriate information?


R:ADJ appropriate -> suitable
 [explain]:  Appropriate and suitable have similar meanings and are sometimes interchangeable: ‘We’re still waiting for a suitable/an appropriate moment to break the news to them.’ When you mean ‘having the necessary qualities, skills etc, the usual word is suitable: ‘The hotel isn’t suitable for families with children.’
[wrong sentence]:  Some bottles are not appropriate for recycling.
[wrong sentence]:  She is clearly the most appropriate person for the job.


R:ADJ artificial -> man-made
 [explain]:  artificial = not made of natural materials: ‘I still prefer sugar to all those artificial sweeteners.’ ‘Most people look better under artificial light.’ man-made = made or caused by people, although sometimes having a natural appearance: ‘This canal is the longest man-made waterway in the world.’ ‘It’s hard to believe that these enormous caves are actually man-made.’
[wrong sentence]:  There are two artificial lakes - one for swimming and one for fishing.
[wrong sentence]:  The city has suffered both natural and artificial disasters.


R:ADJ artificial -> synthetic
 [explain]:  I never eat synthetic foods/food substitutes. synthetic = not natural but made by a chemical process: ‘Many old herbal remedies have been replaced by synthetic drugs.’
[wrong sentence]:  He managed to lose weight by eating artificial diet foods.
[wrong sentence]:  I am worried about taking artificial products into the body.


R:ADJ best -> full
 [explain]:  full/undivided attention: ‘I assure you that this matter will receive our undivided attention.’
[wrong sentence]:  When driving, you must give your best attention to what you are doing.


R:ADJ big -> great
 [explain]:  See Language Note Below
[wrong sentence]:  The party was big fun and we all enjoyed ourselves.


R:ADJ big -> strong
 [explain]:  See Language Note Below
[wrong sentence]:  There is a big possibility that they may not come.


R:ADJ bored -> bore
 [explain]:  See Note at BORING
[wrong sentence]:  My job at the bank was very bored.


R:ADJ boring -> bore
 [explain]:  lf someone or something is boring/exciting/frightening/interesting (-ING), you feel bored/excited/frightened/interested (-ED): ‘The lecture was terribly boring. Some of us were so bored that we fell asleep.’
[wrong sentence]:  We get very boring with the same food every day.


R:ADJ dull -> bore
 [explain]:  When dull is used to describe a person, it means ‘slow to learn or understand’: ‘He was one of the dullest students I’d ever taught.’ Both dull and boring can mean ‘uninteresting’ but in this sense dull usually describes a lecture, book, film etc: ‘The lecture was so dull that some of the students got up and left.’ bored = tired and uninterested: ‘bored students’ boring = causing someone to be tired and uninterested: ‘a boring lesson’
[wrong sentence]:  It was such a dull job that I decided to leave.
[wrong sentence]:  If I did the same thing every day, I would be dull.


R:ADJ calm -> peaceful
 [explain]:  Calm is usually used to describe situations where there has recently been violence or noisy activity: ‘After yesterday’s fighting, the streets of Jerusalem are reported to be calm again this morning.’
[wrong sentence]:  If you prefer a calm environment, try the countryside.


R:ADJ calm -> easygoing
 [explain]:  Calm is usually used to describe how someone behaves in a difficult situation: ‘The boat was being tossed by the waves but we managed to stay calm.’
[wrong sentence]:  My best friend Nick is very calm and never gets upset.


R:ADJ careless -> carefree
 [explain]:  careless = paying too little attention to something: ‘If you’re careless, you’re bound to make mistakes.’ carefree = happy because you have no worries or responsibilities: ‘Some children never know what it means to be carefree.’
[wrong sentence]:  How wonderful it would be to be young and careless again!


R:ADJ cheap -> low
 [explain]:  When you talk about costs, payments, rents, wages, salaries, incomes, expenses, taxes, fees etc, use low/high (NOT cheap/expensive): ‘During the recession, prices stayed low.’ ‘People on low incomes have been severely hit.’ ‘Rents in Helsinki are very high compared to the rest of Finland.’
[wrong sentence]:  The wages in Taiwan are very cheap.


R:ADJ cheaper -> lower/less
 [explain]:  When you talk about costs, payments, rents, wages, salaries, incomes, expenses, taxes, fees etc, use low/high (NOT cheap/expensive): ‘During the recession, prices stayed low.’ ‘People on low incomes have been severely hit.’ ‘Rents in Helsinki are very high compared to the rest of Finland.’
[wrong sentence]:  The monthly payments were cheaper than I’d expected.


R:ADJ pink-coloured -> pink
 [explain]:  When you describe the colour of something, you usually just say that it is red, blue, green etc (WITHOUT -coloured): ‘Her new dress is pale blue with red buttons down the front.’ Adjectives ending with -coloured are quite rare. They are mainly used when the colour of something is difficult to describe exactly (‘pink coloured’ = not exactly pink) and usually come before the noun: ‘a cream-coloured dressing gown’ See Note at COLOUR 2
[wrong sentence]:  The cardigan is pink-coloured and is made of wool.


R:ADJ continual -> continuous
 [explain]:  continual = happening repeatedly over a long period of time, especially in a way that you find annoying: ‘That telephone has been ringing continually.’ continuous = without any break or interruption: ‘Gas central heating provides a continuous supply of hot water.’
[wrong sentence]:  The canals join to form one continual waterway.


R:ADJ continuous -> continual
 [explain]:  See Note at CONTINUAL
[wrong sentence]:  I grew tired of his continuous moaning.


R:ADJ current -> modern
 [explain]:  current = happening or existing now, but not likely to last for a long time: ‘How long has she been going out with her current boyfriend?’ ‘The current boom in long-haul travel has led to fierce competition among the major airlines.’ modern = used or existing in the period of history that we live in now, and not in an earlier period: ‘What do you think of modem architecture?’ ‘Even by modern standards, the pyramids are a remarkable piece of engineering.’
[wrong sentence]:  We need to know current English, not the language of Shakespeare.
[wrong sentence]:  The current world encourages creativity.


R:ADJ damp -> humid
 [explain]:  damp = slightly wet, especially in a cold and unpleasant way: ‘Our hotel room felt cold and damp.’ ‘On damp days, we have to dry the washing indoors.’ humid = warm and full of water vapour: ‘The air in tropical forests is extremely humid.’ ‘I didn’t expect Singapore to be so humid.’
[wrong sentence]:  The summer in Japan is very hot and damp.


R:ADJ drunken -> drink
 [explain]:  Drunken is rarely used to describe a person. It usually describes an action or event: ‘drunken driving’, ‘drunken laughter’, ‘a drunken brawl’, ‘a drunken orgy’. The exception is ‘drunken drivers’ (usually drunk drivers in AmE). Drunken always comes before a noun. To describe a person, use drunk (NOT drunken): ‘I think he wanted to get us all drunk.’ ‘One of the students was always getting drunk.’ Drunk is not used before a noun. When you mean ‘a person who is drunk’, use a drunk: ‘A couple of drunks were causing a disturbance.’
[wrong sentence]:  The man was obviously drunken.


R:ADJ economic -> inexpensive
 [explain]:  See Language Note below
[wrong sentence]:  Travelling around Spain is easy and economic.


R:ADJ effective -> efficient
 [explain]:  See Note at EFFICIENT
[wrong sentence]:  British farmers receive only a little aid since their farms are relatively effective.


R:ADJ efficient -> effective
 [explain]:  We need more efficient ways of dealing with pollution or the problem will get worse. We need more effective ways of dealing with pollution or the problem will get worse. efficient = working quickly and without waste: ‘The more efficient the engine, the less petrol it uses.’ ‘Since the new software was installed, library services have become much more efficient.’ effective = having the desired effect: ‘There are many effective ways of using computers for training purposes.’ ‘The advertisement was simple, but remarkably effective.’
[wrong sentence]:  The medicine proved very efficient.


R:ADJ elder -> older
 [explain]:  The young man was unhurt but the elder one was taken to hospital in an ambulance. The young man was unhurt but the older one was taken to hospital in an ambulance. Do not use elder unless you are talking about the members of a family: ‘her elder brother’, ‘my elder son’.
[wrong sentence]:  The restaurant seems to be more popular with elder people.


 [explain]:  Someone/something is older (than someone/something else): ‘Their children are older than ours.’ ‘Her husband is five years older.’ Elder is mainly used in front of a noun (‘my elder sister’) and as a pronoun (‘Which of the two sisters is the elder?’)
[wrong sentence]:  My sister is just two years elder than me.


R:ADJ electronic -> electric
 [explain]:  See Language Note at ELECTRIC
[wrong sentence]:  An electronic razor is fine until there is a power cut.


R:ADJ exciting -> excite
 [explain]:  See Note at BORING
[wrong sentence]:  It is wonderful to be in London at last. I feel so exciting.


R:ADJ expensive -> high
 [explain]:  See Note at CHEAP 1
[wrong sentence]:  The disadvantage is that the cost is very expensive.
[wrong sentence]:  Prices in Britain are not as expensive as in Japan.


R:ADJ tall -> high
 [explain]:  See Note at HIGH
[wrong sentence]:  I prefer rooms with tall ceilings.


R:ADJ extreme -> major
 [explain]:  Extreme is mainly used to describe situations, behaviour, opinions and beliefs: ‘extreme poverty’, ‘extreme caution’, ‘extreme views’, ‘extreme disappointment’. When you are talking about a change or development, use words such as great/major/important/considerable/dramatic: ‘Without these major changes, the company would have collapsed.’
[wrong sentence]:  Doctors have to keep up with all the extreme new medical developments.


R:ADJ female -> women’s
 [explain]:  Female is used mainly in scientific or technical styles: ‘The male birds are usually more colourful than the females.’ ‘The female butterfly does not require additional salts.’ Female is sometimes used to refer to a woman or girl, but many people find this use of the word offensive.
[wrong sentence]:  In 1992 she won the female marathon in Athens.


R:ADJ few -> little
 [explain]:  Few is used with plural countable nouns: ‘very few cars’.
[wrong sentence]:  With the few money he had been given, he bought an apple and some cheese.


R:ADJ funny -> good
 [explain]:  have a good time = enjoy yourself: ‘Everyone was dancing and having a good time.’
[wrong sentence]:  The carnival is held once a year and everyone has a funny time.


R:ADJ well -> good
 [explain]:  Well is usually used as an adverb: ‘The team played well on Saturday.’ As an adjective, well usually means ‘in good health’: ‘George can’t come because he’s not very well.’ ‘He always looks well after a holiday.’
[wrong sentence]:  His table manners were not very well.


R:ADJ gentle -> kind
 [explain]:  gentle = (1) used to describe someone who never shows anger or impatience in what they say or do: ‘His brother was always ready for a fight, but Tim was quiet and gentle.’ (2) not loud, forceful or rough: ‘He spoke to the child in a quiet, gentle voice so as not to frighten her.’ ‘She found the doctor’s gentle manner very reassuring.’
[wrong sentence]:  My father was a very gentle man, always helping people.


R:ADJ glad -> grateful
 [explain]:  To make a formal request, use would be grateful if (NOT glad): ‘We would be grateful if you could consider this matter at your earliest convenience.’
[wrong sentence]:  I would be glad if you could send me an application form.


R:ADJ thankful -> grateful
 [explain]:  Thankful = happy and relieved that something (good) has happened: ‘We should all be thankful that nobody was hurt..’ grateful = full of thanks: ‘Dr Cameron has received dozens of letters from grateful patients.’ ‘We’d be grateful if you could let us have your views on this matter.’
[wrong sentence]:  I’m very thankful to you for giving me this opportunity.


R:ADJ glad -> happy
 [explain]:  glad (not used in front of a noun) = pleased and happy about something in particular: ‘I’m so glad your wife is feeling better.’ ‘I’m glad it wasn’t my turn to pay.’ happy = enjoying life: ‘She’s a lot happier now that the examinations are over.’
[wrong sentence]:  We hope that you will like this school and be glad here.
[wrong sentence]:  She was a very glad person, and very intelligent.


R:ADJ highest -> tallest
 [explain]:  Use high to describe something that is a long way above the ground (or whose top is a long way above the ground): ‘The rooms on the ground floor have very high ceilings.’ ‘The top shelf was too high for me to reach.’ ‘The high wall made it impossible for prisoners to escape.’ Use tall (NOT high) to describe people, animals, trees, plants and things which are narrow and above average height: ‘Most of the tall trees had been cut down.’ ‘I’d like to marry someone tall with a sense of humour.’ He’s almost six feet tall.’
[wrong sentence]:  Beauvais is very proud of having the highest cathedral in Europe.


R:ADJ high -> tall
 [explain]:  Use high to describe something that is a long way above the ground (or whose top is a long way above the ground): ‘The rooms on the ground floor have very high ceilings.’ ‘The top shelf was too high for me to reach.’ ‘The high wall made it impossible for prisoners to escape.’ Use tall (NOT high) to describe people, animals, trees, plants and things which are narrow and above average height: ‘Most of the tall trees had been cut down.’ ‘I’d like to marry someone tall with a sense of humour.’ He’s almost six feet tall.’
[wrong sentence]:  Smoke pours out of the high chimneys all day long.


R:ADJ hopeless -> dishearten
 [explain]:  Hopeless is usually used to describe a situation, not a person: ‘The firemen tried to get the blaze under control, but it was hopeless.’ When you say that a person is hopeless, you mean that they are very bad at something: ‘He’s hopeless at remembering names.’ ; To describe someone who feels that they have little or no chance of success, especially because they have already experienced failure or disappointment, use dispirited, dejected, disheartened, discouraged, despondent or demoralized: ‘Having lost ten matches in a row, the team felt completely demoralized.’
[wrong sentence]:  I think the students will feel hopeless because they have already tried their best and failed.


R:ADJ human’s -> human
 [explain]:  Human is mainly used as an adjective: ‘We should accept the fact that human judgment fallible.’
[wrong sentence]:  The human’s brain works better in the morning.


R:ADJ humid -> damp
 [explain]:  See Note at DAMP
[wrong sentence]:  He was living in a cold basement flat which had humid walls and almost no furniture.


R:ADJ moist -> damp
 [explain]:  moist = slightly wet, especially in a pleasant way: ‘The bread was still nice and moist.’ ‘These plants prefer a warm, moist atmosphere.’ damp = slightly wet, especially in a cold and unpleasant way: ‘Our hotel room felt cold and damp.’ ‘On damp days, we have to dry the washing indoors.’
[wrong sentence]:  Basement flats are often dark and moist.


R:ADJ ill -> sick
 [explain]:  ill (= sick) is usually used after a verb: ‘I told the doctor that felt ill.’ ‘His father is seriously ill in hospital.’
[wrong sentence]:  I am being trained to look after ill children.


R:ADJ indoor -> indoors
 [explain]:  Indoor (WITHOUT s) is an adjective: ‘an indoor tennis court’, ‘indoor athletics’.
[wrong sentence]:  As it was raining we decided to stay indoor.


R:ADJ indoors -> indoor
 [explain]:  See Note at INDOOR
[wrong sentence]:  Their new house even has an indoors swimming pool.


R:ADJ industrious -> industrial
 [explain]:  Industrious = hard-working: ‘Neil is indeed a quiet student, but you won’t find anyone more industrious.’ industrial = having or connected with major industries’: We visited some of the country’s main industrial areas.’ ‘The economy has been hit by a series of industrial disputes.’
[wrong sentence]:  The South has fewer industrious areas.


R:ADJ inhabited -> occupy
 [explain]:  inhabit = (usually passive) live in a place or area, especially for a long time or permanently: ‘The island is mainly inhabited by sheep.’ The remoter mountain regions are still inhabited by indigenous tribes.’ ; occupy = use or live in a room, house or building for any length of time: ‘Is that seat occupied?’ ‘The flat below was occupied by a young Sri Lankan couple.’
[wrong sentence]:  The bathroom was already inhabited.


R:ADJ intense -> intensive
 [explain]:  intense = strong or very great; extreme: ‘intense heat’, ‘intense pleasure’, ‘intense pain’, ‘intense competition’ intensive = concentrated: ‘an intensive English course’, ‘a period of intensive training’
[wrong sentence]:  The government has embarked on an intense industrialization programme.


R:ADJ large -> wide
 [explain]:  When you are talking about the distance from one side to the other, use wide or broad: ‘The river is much too wide to swim across.’ ‘How did you get such broad shoulders?’
[wrong sentence]:  The road wasn’t large enough for two cars.


R:ADJ last -> latest
 [explain]:  last = (the one) before this one; (the one) at the end of a list, sequence or series: ‘Our last meeting was in Rome.’ ‘I answered all the questions except the last one.’ Latest = designed/produced/published etc more recently than any other: ‘Have you seen Toyota’s latest models?’ ‘The BBC World Service always has the latest news.’
[wrong sentence]:  As a dress designer, I am very interested in the last fashions.


R:ADJ latest -> last
 [explain]:  See Note at LAST 4
[wrong sentence]:  The latest election was won by the socialists.
[wrong sentence]:  In the latest decades of the twentieth century, the world has seen many changes.


R:ADJ recent -> last
 [explain]:  When you mention a number, use last (NOT recent). Compare: ‘In recent months house prices have fallen.’ ‘Over the last 12 months house prices have fallen.’
[wrong sentence]:  Over the recent 30 years there has been a great deal of industrial development.


R:ADJ stricter -> strict
 [explain]:  The comparative ending (-er) is NEVER used with less or more.
[wrong sentence]:  Teachers today are less stricter than they used to be.


R:ADJ severe -> strict
 [explain]:  When severe refers to punishment, criticism, damage etc, it means ‘harsh’: ‘Driving while drunk could endanger other people’s lives, so penalties are severe.’ ‘This non-intervention policy has attracted severe criticism.’ To describe a rule or law that must always be obeyed, use strict: ‘The deer and other animals in the park are protected by strict laws.’
[wrong sentence]:  There are severe rules as to what you can wear to school.


R:ADJ little -> small
 [explain]:  Little usually expresses an emotional attitude such as affection or dislike: ‘We’ve rented a cosy little cottage in the countryside.’ ‘You shouldn’t let that silly little man upset you.’ When you simply wish to describe the size of someone of something, use small: Their daughter is rather small for her age.’ ‘People are turning to smaller cars because they are cheaper to run.’
[wrong sentence]:  He works in a little office building in Geneva.
[wrong sentence]:  His parents died when he was still a little child.


R:ADJ little -> brief
 [explain]:  See Language Note at KILL
[wrong sentence]:  The police asked for a little description of the car.


R:ADJ little -> minor
 [explain]:  See Language Note at KILL
[wrong sentence]:  He told me that I needed a little operation.


R:ADJ little -> few
 [explain]:  See Note at FEW 1
[wrong sentence]:  Today there are too many people for too little jobs.


R:ADJ many -> much
 [explain]:  See Note at MUCH 1
[wrong sentence]:  It’s important to understand why there is so many violence in our world.


R:ADJ marry -> marry
 [explain]:  marry sb (WITHOUT With/to): ‘Isn’t he the man who married Elizabeth Taylor?’ get married (to sb): ‘I can still remember the day I got married. United were playing at Wembley.’
[wrong sentence]:  After they get marry, they’re going abroad.


R:ADJ matured -> mature
 [explain]:  Use matured/mature when you are talking about wine, cheese etc: ‘The whisky is kept here until it has matured/is mature.’ When you are talking about people, the usual word is mature: ‘He’s very mature for a boy of sixteen.’
[wrong sentence]:  Replacing such a politically matured leader will not be easy.


R:ADJ medium -> average
 [explain]:  medium = neither large nor small, neither tall nor short, etc: ‘The waiter was of medium height and walked with a slight limp.’ average = calculated by adding a set of quantities together and then dividing this total by the number of quantities in the set: ‘The average age of students entering the college this year is 19.’ ‘Since 1991 house prices have been falling at an average annual rate of 8%.’
[wrong sentence]:  The medium score was about 6 out of 10.


R:ADJ mental -> spiritual
 [explain]:  mental = affecting or taking place in the mind: ‘People who have had mental illnesses are often unwilling to talk about them.’ ; spiritual = connected with the part of a person which has very deep thoughts and feelings: ‘African music has a spiritual quality which is often lacking in Western music.’
[wrong sentence]:  Modern society provides us with material comforts but very few mental rewards.


R:ADJ middle -> medium
 [explain]:  When you are talking about someone’s height, weight, etc, use medium (NOT middle): ‘The man that police wish to question is of medium height and has a small black moustache.’
[wrong sentence]:  The girl was of middle height and was wearing a blue suit.


 [explain]:  When you describe the colour of something, use medium (NOT middle): ‘a medium grey suit’ (= neither light grey nor dark grey but somewhere in between).
[wrong sentence]:  The wallet was middle brown and contained sixty dollars.


R:ADJ much -> many
 [explain]:  much + uncountable noun: ‘There isn’t much traffic today.’ ‘There was too much furniture in the room.’ many + plural (countable) noun: ‘There aren’t many cars today.’ ‘There were too many chairs in the room.’
[wrong sentence]:  There are as much disadvantages as advantages.
[wrong sentence]:  There are too much people in Mexico.
[wrong sentence]:  We don’t need to hire that much employees.


R:ADJ near -> nearby
 [explain]:  To say that something is only a short distance from a place, use nearby: ‘I stopped at one of the nearby cottages and asked the way.’ ‘It’s very convenient having a supermarket nearby.’
[wrong sentence]:  I sometimes meet friends in a near restaurant.


R:ADJ big -> loud
 [explain]:  loud noise (NOT big): ‘Habitual exposure to loud noises can damage the ear.’
[wrong sentence]:  I was woken up by a big noise outside the room.


R:ADJ official -> officious
 [explain]:  official = done by or connected with a person or group in authority; formal: ‘an official letter’, ‘an official inquiry’, ‘official approval’ officious (expressing dislike or disapproval) = too eager to give orders or make people keep to rules which are unimportant: ‘An officious little man at the check-in insisted that my luggage was half a kilo overweight
[wrong sentence]:  She is good at organizing people without seeming arrogant or official.


R:ADJ oily -> greasy
 [explain]:  The usual word for describing food that is unpleasant because it is cooked and presented with too much fat or oil is greasy: ‘I ate the tomato and left all the greasy chips.’
[wrong sentence]:  The chips were so oily that I couldn’t eat them.


R:ADJ opposite -> different
 [explain]:  Use opposite and the opposite of only when you mean that two things are as different as it is possible to be: ‘I thought that the medicine would make him sleepy, but it had the opposite effect.’ ‘The opposite of long is short.’ ‘The two men went off in opposite directions.’ (= one went to the left and one to the right) To describe people’s opinions , life styles , ways of thinking etc, the usual word is different: ‘These two schools of thought are completely different.’
[wrong sentence]:  People have opposite opinions about this matter.


R:ADJ original -> traditional
 [explain]:  original =(1) completely new: ‘Nobody expected that the bikini, with its daringly original design. would catch on as it did.’ (2) the one that exists first (usually followed later by other ones): ‘The original edition contained only 170 pages.’ ‘The tennis court to the side of the house was the idea of the original owner.’ Traditional = done or used by a group or society for a very long time: ‘The dancers were wearing traditional African dress.’ ‘Kumar gave the traditional Hindu greeting.’
[wrong sentence]:  Many craftsmen have given up their original skills to work in factories.


R:ADJ peculiar -> unusual
 [explain]:  peculiar = strange, especially in a surprising or unpleasant way: ‘I’m not sure about this cheese.
[wrong sentence]:  She said she liked the jumper because the colour was very peculiar.


R:ADJ rare -> unusual
 [explain]:  If something is different from what is usual, it is unusual: ‘The bread had an unusual flavour.’ ‘Kit.
[wrong sentence]:  In San Francisco I saw some rare architecture.


R:ADJ unique -> unusual
 [explain]:  Unique usually describes something that is the only one of its kind: ‘Lennon will be remembered for his songs and for his unique sense of humour.’
[wrong sentence]:  It’s one of the most unique pieces of architecture in Spain.


R:ADJ promotions -> promotion
 [explain]:  In phrases such as get promotion, apply for promotion and chance/s of promotion, the noun promotion is usually uncountable: ‘Women should have the same chance of promotion as their male counterparts.’
[wrong sentence]:  In some professions it is still difficult for women to get promotions.


R:ADJ stricter -> harsher
 [explain]:  severe/harsh punishment (NOT strict/strong): ‘A prison sentence seems a very severe punishment for tax evasion.’
[wrong sentence]:  The police are in favour of stricter punishments.


R:ADJ pure -> fresh
 [explain]:  Use pure when you mean ‘completely clean’: ‘The water in the lake is so pure that you can drink it.’ The usual word for describing air and water is fresh: ‘Her doctor has prescribed a good holiday and lots of fresh air.’
[wrong sentence]:  We opened the window to get some pure air.


R:ADJ rainy -> wet
 [explain]:  When rainy is used it generally comes in front of words like day, afternoon, Sunday and season (NOT after a verb): ‘The baby was born on a rainy Sunday in June.’ ‘When does the rainy season begin?’ The more usual word is wet: ‘Apart from a couple of wet afternoons, the weather was fine.’ ‘If it’s wet tomorrow, we’ll go on Monday instead.’
[wrong sentence]:  The afternoon was very rainy.
[wrong sentence]:  It’s rainy again today.


R:ADJ rapid -> fast
 [explain]:  When used in connection with movement, rapid and rapidly usually refer to something that happens at a very fast rate: ‘She could hear rapid footsteps approaching.’ ‘The patient often develops a fever and breathing becomes rapid.’
[wrong sentence]:  These new trains are very rapid.


R:ADJ rare -> scarce
 [explain]:  If certain things are rare there are only a few of them in existence: ‘Rare coins are usually worth a lot of money.’ A rare event is one that hardly ever happens: ‘I was lucky enough to witness one of her rare public performances.’ To describe something that is usually common but for some reason is difficult to obtain at a particular time or in a particular place, use scarce: ‘After the war, food and clothing were scarce.’
[wrong sentence]:  Water is very rare in some parts of the country.


 [explain]:  See Note at RARE 1
[wrong sentence]:  Chocolate was very rare during the war.


R:ADJ reasonable -> rational
 [explain]:  reasonable = fair, sensible or acceptable: ‘Dividing up the work equally seems like a very reasonable decision.’ ‘They’ll accept any reasonable offer.’ rational = produced by means of careful, logical thinking: ‘There must be some rational explanation - things can’t just disappear!’
[wrong sentence]:  I was far too upset and emotional to make a reasonable decision.


R:ADJ respectful -> respectable
 [explain]:  respectful = having or showing respect for someone, especially someone older than you or senior to you: ‘The principal has warned him that unless he shows a more respectful attitude towards his teachers, he will have to leave.’ respectable = displaying socially acceptable standards in appearance or behaviour: ‘Surprisingly, many of these young offenders come from respectable families.’ ‘Nobody is going to give you a job unless you make yourself look a bit more respectable.’
[wrong sentence]:  If you wish to make a good impression, you’ll have to look a bit more respectful.


R:ADJ revenged -> avenge
 [explain]:  Revenge is usually used as a noun: ‘Once they know who planted the bomb, they are likely to seek revenge.’ ‘She did it in revenge for all the lies he had told her.’ The verb is avenge: ‘They swore to avenge this humiliating defeat.’ ‘He considers it his duty to avenge his brother’s death.’
[wrong sentence]:  The damage done to his honour would have to be revenged.
[wrong sentence]:  One day these innocent victims must be revenged.


R:ADJ safety -> safe
 [explain]:  Safety is a noun (NOT an adjective/adverb): ‘The boy has been missing for six days, and there are fears for his safety.’ ‘The firefighters rescued the children and carried them to safety.’
[wrong sentence]:  In Japan even the big cities are very safety.


R:ADJ save -> safe
 [explain]:  Save /seɪv/ is a verb: ‘This new drug is likely to save hundreds of lives.’ ‘Thanks to the local fire brigade, all three children were saved.’ The adjective is safe /seɪf/: ‘Since the break-in, I never feel safe in the house.’ ‘You should keep your passport somewhere safe.’
[wrong sentence]:  The police telephoned his parents to tell them that he was save.


R:ADJ satisfied -> satisfactory
 [explain]:  satisfied = (of a person) feeling pleased or content: ‘Despite the team’s convincing 3-0 victory, the manager wasn’t satisfied.’
[wrong sentence]:  Her examination results were not satisfied.


R:ADJ satisfying -> satisfactory
 [explain]:  See Note at SATISFACTORY
[wrong sentence]:  The service in the hotel was not at all satisfying.


R:ADJ scarce -> rare
 [explain]:  See Note at RARE 1
[wrong sentence]:  If you are interested in scarce birds, you should visit the bird garden.


R:ADJ shame -> embarrass
 [explain]:  See ASHAMEO she, s/he See Language Note at HE
[wrong sentence]:  When I speak English I sometimes feel shame.


R:ADJ silent -> quiet
 [explain]:  silent : without any sound at all: ‘Apart from the regular ticking of the clock, the room was completely silent.’ quiet: without unwanted noise or activity; peaceful: ‘After a few quiet days in the countryside, we felt ready to face London again.’
[wrong sentence]:  After a hard day’s work, I like to be silent.


R:ADJ social -> sociable
 [explain]:  A person who enjoys meeting and being with other people is sociable: ‘You’ll like John - he’s very sociable and easy to get on with.’
[wrong sentence]:  Our new neighbours are not very social.


R:ADJ somewhere -> anywhere
 [explain]:  See Language Note at NOT
[wrong sentence]:  The poor bus service makes it difficult to get somewhere.


R:ADJ stolen -> rob
 [explain]:  See Note at ROB
[wrong sentence]:  If you look wealthy, you are likely to be stolen.


R:ADJ suspicious -> sceptical
 [explain]:  suspicious = thinking that someone may be guilty of doing something wrong: ‘I started to get suspicious when he refused to tell me where he had been.’ ; sceptical /ˈskeptɪkəl/ = tending not to believe what people say about something: ‘Many doctors remain highly sceptical about the value of alternative medicine.’
[wrong sentence]:  Before I actually started to use one, I was suspicious about the value of computers.


R:ADJ sympathetic -> likeable
 [explain]:  Sympathetic = feeling or showing sympathy : ‘He expected people to be sympathetic because he was an orphan.’ ; likeable = easy to like: ‘If Philip weren’t so arrogant, he’d be quite likeable.’
[wrong sentence]:  He’s a selfish, greedy little man and not at all sympathetic.


R:ADJ tasteful -> tasty
 [explain]:  tasteful = chosen or produced by someone who is able to judge which kinds of art, music, furniture, etc, are attractive: ‘The design of the room was very tasteful - pale colours, matching fabrics, and soft corner lighting.’ tasty = (of food) having a pleasant taste : ‘These sausages are really tasty - where did you buy them?’
[wrong sentence]:  The food was excellent and very tasteful.


R:ADJ high -> advance
 [explain]:  advanced technology (NOT high): ‘The United States government has agreed to provide both capital and advanced technology.’
[wrong sentence]:  High technology requires massive investment.


R:ADJ tiresome -> tire
 [explain]:  tiresome= annoying or irritating: ‘I find these so-called jokes extremely tiresome. ; ‘tiring = causing tiredness; exhausting: ‘Looking at a computer screen all day can be very tiring.’
[wrong sentence]:  After two tiresome days, we both needed some sleep.
[wrong sentence]:  The flight from Hong Kong to London was very tiresome.


R:ADJ typical -> customary
 [explain]:  When you are talking about what usually happens at a particular time of the year, use usually or (especially in formal styles) customary: ‘It’s customary to kiss the bride.’
[wrong sentence]:  After dinner it is typical to sing carols.


R:ADJ unsatisfactory -> unsatisfying
 [explain]:  See Note at SATISFACTORY
[wrong sentence]:  To tell you the truth, it’s the most unsatisfactory job I’ve ever had.


R:ADJ useless -> unnecessary
 [explain]:  If something is not necessary, it is unnecessary (NOT useless): ‘People who refuse to stop smoking are taking an unnecessary risk.’ ‘Sports that cause animals unnecessary suffering should be banned.’
[wrong sentence]:  Staying in a big hotel would involve useless expense.
[wrong sentence]:  Without all this useless packaging, food would be cheaper.


R:ADJ valuable -> expensive
 [explain]:  valuable = worth a lot of money: ‘Your stamp collection must be quite valuable by now.’ ‘If the painting is genuine, it could be extremely valuable.’ ; expensive = costing a lot of money, especially when compared with other things of the same type: ‘He only buys silk ties, even though they’re more expensive.’ ‘Why are these bananas so expensive?’
[wrong sentence]:  The magazine is printed on very valuable paper.
[wrong sentence]:  They always buy valuable brands such as Polo, Louis Vuitton, and Yves St Laurent.


R:ADJ wide -> broad
 [explain]:  broad view/picture/description/outline (of sth), NOT wide: ‘Can you give me a broad outline of what the speech was about?’
[wrong sentence]:  The magazine gives wide descriptions of market trends.
[wrong sentence]:  My education has given me a wide view of life.


R:ADJ worst -> worse
 [explain]:  See Note at WORSE
[wrong sentence]:  The day finished worst than it began.
[wrong sentence]:  The next time I saw her she looked even worst.


R:ADJ worthwhile -> worth
 [explain]:  it’s worth/worthwhile doing sth: ‘It might be worth having one or two copies made.’ ‘Is-it worthwhile talking about it now, or shall we leave it until our next meeting?’ sth is worth doing (NOT worthwhile): ‘Some of these so-called professional teams aren’t worth watching.’ Compare: ‘Was the long drive up to Scotland really worthwhile?’
[wrong sentence]:  The film reviews are always worthwhile looking at.


R:ADJ worthy -> worth
 [explain]:  The cathedral is certainly worthy to be visited.
[wrong sentence]:  It’s not worthy taking your own car.


R:ADJ wrong -> mistake
 [explain]:  To describe a belief or idea that is wrong although people do not know it is wrong, use mistaken: ‘Some people have the mistaken idea that cats need to drink milk.’ ‘I’m afraid you must be mistaken.’
[wrong sentence]:  The belief that Spanish is easy to learn is wrong.


M:OTHER NONE -> the fact
 [explain]:  take into account + the fact + that-clause: ‘They should take into account the fact that these archaeological treasures are extremely valuable.’
[wrong sentence]:  We also have to take into account that the schools are overcrowded.


M:OTHER NONE -> am in
 [explain]:  If ever you have any kind of doubt, come and see me or one of the other teachers. If ever you are in any doubt about anything, come and see me or one of the other teachers. (be) in doubt about sth (= feel unsure): ‘Is anyone in doubt about what they’re supposed to be doing?’ ‘If you’re in any doubt about your child’s safety, talk to your doctor.’ Note that this meaning is more commonly expressed by be unsure/uncertain (or not be sure/certain): ‘Whenever I’m not sure about the meaning of a word, I look in my dictionary.’
[wrong sentence]:  Whenever I doubt about the meaning of a word, I look in my dictionary.


M:OTHER NONE -> piece of
 [explain]:  Equipment is an uncountable noun: ‘The school is trying to raise funds to buy some new sports equipment.’
[wrong sentence]:  It’s a useful equipment and I intend to have it repaired.


M:OTHER NONE -> on how
 [explain]:  instructions on how to do sth: ‘The crew has a full set of instructions on how to launch the satellite.’
[wrong sentence]:  I’m here to give you some instructions to cook Bin Dae Tuck.


M:OTHER NONE -> way of
 [explain]:  way of life = the way that a society, group or person chooses to live: ‘I found the British way of life very strange at first.’ ‘The computer has transformed our whole way of life.’ lifestyle = way of life, especially that of a particular person: ‘As a photographer, she has a very hectic lifestyle.’
[wrong sentence]:  Television has taught me a lot about the American life.


M:OTHER NONE -> sheet of
 [explain]:  When it refers to the material that you write on, paper is an uncountable noun: ‘The printer has run out of paper.’ ‘On the back of the piece of paper she had written her address.’
[wrong sentence]:  Each of us was given a clean paper to write on.


M:OTHER NONE -> get my
 [explain]:  get/take (your) revenge (on sb): ‘He decided that the easiest way to get his revenge was to disinherit her.’ ‘She has vowed to take revenge on each and every one of them.’
[wrong sentence]:  I’m your twin brother and I’m here to revenge.


R:NOUN acts -> action
 [explain]:  The noun act is usually used when you want to comment on a particular thing that someone has done: ‘It was an act of great courage.’ ‘These cowardly terrorist acts bring death and suffering to the innocent.’ When you are talking about someone’s general behaviour, use actions: ‘You can’t be blamed for your parents’ actions.’ ‘Her words and actions have not gone unnoticed.’ See also ACTION 2
[wrong sentence]:  He refused to accept responsibility for his acts.


R:NOUN actions -> things
 [explain]:  Do and action cannot be used together. Use do + things/something/anything etc: ‘She’s always doing things for charity.’ ‘Don’t do anything that might upset them.’
[wrong sentence]:  The actions that we do everyday are made easier by computers.


R:NOUN advantages -> merits/good points
 [explain]:  advantage = something that puts you in a better position than other people: ‘A healthier lifestyle is just one of the advantages of living in the country.’ ‘The main advantage of using word processors is the amount of time you save.’ merit = a good quality; a strength: ‘The merits of the new health programme are gradually being recognized.’ ‘We should judge each application on its own merits.’
[wrong sentence]:  Although the film has its advantages, it also has a serious flaw.


R:NOUN advantages -> benefit
 [explain]:  benefit = a good result which improves your life or situation: ‘Belonging to a union has a number of important benefits.’ ‘It’s over a month since I got back from holiday, but I still feel the benefit.’
[wrong sentence]:  Television provides many advantages.


R:NOUN advices -> advice
 [explain]:  Advice is an uncountable noun: ‘I could do with some advice.’ ; Compare: ‘She gave me a good piece of advice.’
[wrong sentence]:  It is full of good advices on healthy eating.


R:NOUN advise -> advice
 [explain]:  See Note at ADVICE 1
[wrong sentence]:  I asked my lawyer for her advise.


R:NOUN agent -> agent’s
 [explain]:  To refer to a shop or company, use the ‘s form: a greengrocer’s, a chemist’s, a tailor’s, a butcher’s.
[wrong sentence]:  I applied for a job at a travel agent.


R:NOUN aim -> ambition
 [explain]:  See Note at AMBITION 1
[wrong sentence]:  Her lifelong aim was to learn how to fly.


R:NOUN desire -> ambition
 [explain]:  When you are talking about something very important that you want to achieve, use aim or ambition: ‘Her ambition is to represent her country in the Olympic Games.’ ‘Our ultimate aim is to find a cure for the disease.’
[wrong sentence]:  My desire is to become a successful business manager.


R:NOUN aircrafts -> aircraft
 [explain]:  The plural form of aircraft is aircraft (no change).
[wrong sentence]:  All the aircrafts have to be checked and refuelled.


R:NOUN ambition -> aim
 [explain]:  ambition = something very important that you have wanted to do or achieve for a very long time: ‘Sandro’s one ambition is to play for Italy in the World Cup.’ aim = what you hope to achieve when you do something: ‘The aim of the course is to develop the students’ writing skills.’
[wrong sentence]:  My immediate ambition is to find somewhere to live.


R:NOUN amount -> number
 [explain]:  amount of + uncountable noun number of + plural countable noun
[wrong sentence]:  The amount of accidents is steadily increasing.


R:NOUN amounts -> number
 [explain]:  amount of + uncountable noun number of + plural countable noun
[wrong sentence]:  Only small amounts of students will be admitted.


R:NOUN anger -> temper
 [explain]:  control/keep/lose your temper (NOT anger): ‘The problem with George is that he can’t control his temper.’ (= cannot stop himself from suddenly getting angry)
[wrong sentence]:  He’ll have to learn how to control his anger.


R:NOUN announcements -> advertisements
 [explain]:  When you want to give people some important information, you make an announcement: ‘Following the announcement of their marriage, they were pursued by crowds of journalists.’ An advertisement is an item in a newspaper, on television, etc, that tries to persuade people to buy something, apply for a job, etc: ‘At this time of the year, the papers are full of holiday advertisements.’
[wrong sentence]:  On almost every page there were announcements for cigarettes and tobacco.


R:NOUN question -> question
 [explain]:  When any is used with a countable noun, the noun is usually plural: ‘a question’ BUT ‘any questions’.
[wrong sentence]:  If you have any question, ask your teacher.


R:NOUN questions -> question
 [explain]:  each + singular noun: ‘Each child was given a balloon.’
[wrong sentence]:  We had to answer each questions on a new page.


R:NOUN friend -> friends
 [explain]:  When any is used with a countable noun, the noun is usually plural: ‘a question’ BUT ‘any questions’.
[wrong sentence]:  She doesn’t have any friend.


R:NOUN anyone -> everyone
 [explain]:  To refer to all the people in a group, use everybody/everyone (NOT anybody/anyone) .
[wrong sentence]:  Anyone should speak not just one but several languages.


R:NOUN anybody -> everybody
 [explain]:  To refer to all the people in a group, use everybody/everyone (NOT anybody/anyone) .
[wrong sentence]:  Anybody else in the class speaks Japanese.


R:NOUN areas -> regions
 [explain]:  To refer to one of the very large parts of a country, or to an area which includes more than one country, use region: ‘This region of France is famous for its wine.’ ‘We apologize to viewers in the southern region for the poor picture quality.’ ‘This variety of rice is grown mainly in cool temperate regions.’
[wrong sentence]:  A number of different countries and areas face the same problem.


R:NOUN districts -> regions
 [explain]:  district = (1) one of the official divisions of a city or country: ‘the London postal district’, ‘the South Cambridgeshire District’, ‘the Liverpool 8 district of Merseyside’, ‘Last winter several London health districts faced a cash crisis.’ (2) an area in a city (or sometimes in a country) that has a particular quality or feature: ‘a very wealthy district’, ‘one of the most deprived districts in the inner city’, ‘an old slum district where the gangs operate’. A region is usually a large area of a country and has no official boundaries: ‘The President has declared the region a disaster area.’ ‘Such expansion would most likely occur in the Amazon region.’ ‘The people who live in this region have a strong accent.’ When you are unsure about which word to choose, use area. An area can be large or small and has a very general meaning.
[wrong sentence]:  We will be touring the famous fruit-growing districts.


R:NOUN arguments -> reason
 [explain]:  argument = a reason that you give to support your opinion, especially when you are trying to persuade someone: ‘The writer’s main argument is that a better train service will take cars off the road and lead to a healthier environment.’ ‘The argument against higher taxation is very simple.’ reason = something that explains someone’s actions or feelings, or why a particular situation exists: ‘My main reason for doing the course is to improve my qualifications.’ ‘He’s got to go back to Mexico, for family reasons.’
[wrong sentence]:  They don’t want children, for purely personal arguments.
[wrong sentence]:  There are several good arguments for people preferring to live in the countryside.


R:NOUN cause -> reason
 [explain]:  cause = an action, event, situation etc that makes something happen: The cause of the fire is still being investigated.’ These outbreaks of violence will continue to occur until the causes have been eliminated.’ reason = something that provides an explanation: ‘I’m sure that they must have good reasons for wanting to live abroad.’ ‘The reason why there is only one applicant is that the job wasn’t advertised.’
[wrong sentence]:  The cause why I want to change my job is as follows.


R:NOUN purpose -> reason
 [explain]:  purpose = what you hope to achieve by doing something; aim: ‘The main purpose of the trip is to see Helen’s parents.’ ‘Their purpose in coming here is to promote Australian universities.’ reason = the thing that causes someone to do something: ‘The reason why she left him was probably to do with his drinking problem.’ ‘Did they give you a reason for rejecting your application?’
[wrong sentence]:  The purpose why I have come here is to improve my English.


R:NOUN art -> arts
 [explain]:  Subjects of study are divided into the sciences (e.g. biology, physics, chemistry) and the arts (e.g. history, French, geography): ‘If you don’t like numbers, you should stick to the arts.’ The noun arts (WITH -s) is often used in front of another noun: ‘I’m studying for an arts degree.’
