[wrong sentence]:  I worked as a tutor in the Faculty of Art and Social Sciences.
[wrong sentence]:  The college offers both science and art subjects.


R:NOUN aspect -> respect
 [explain]:  When you wish to control the strength of a statement, use in some/several/certain/many/all respects or in one/this/every respect: ‘In many respects she is the best teacher I’ve ever had.’ ‘In some respects the two cultures are very similar.’
[wrong sentence]:  At one time it seemed as if men were stronger than women in every aspect.


R:NOUN case -> respect
 [explain]:  in this/that case = in these/those circumstances: ‘What shall I do if there are no trains?’ ‘In that case you’ll have to go by bus.’ in this/that respect = with regard to this/that point or detail: ‘The film is full of violence and in this respect is unsuitable for children.’ I advise you to eat something now in case there won’t be any food when we get there.
[wrong sentence]:  Switzerland has very little unemployment and in this case we are very lucky.


R:NOUN asylums -> hospices
 [explain]:  asylum = (old-fashioned) a hospital for the mentally ill, nowadays known as a psychiatric hospital hospice = a special type of hospital for people who are dying Note also the term old people’s home = a place where old people go to live when they need someone to look after them: ‘She’d rather stay in an old people’s home than be a burden to her family.’
[wrong sentence]:  Asylums are typically seen as places where people go to die.


R:NOUN athletic -> athletics
 [explain]:  athletic = physically strong and good at running, jumping, etc: ‘I’ve never been very athletic.’ athletics = sports such as running, jumping, and throwing the javelin: ‘I’ve never been very good at athletics.’ The noun athletics (WITH -s) is often used in front of another noun: ‘He’s been selected for the athletics team.’
[wrong sentence]:  The race was organized by the National Athletic Association.
[wrong sentence]:  A lot of athletic reporters write for the magazine.


R:NOUN attention -> service
 [explain]:  service = the help and attention that you get from the people who work in a shop or restaurant: ‘We complained about the poor service.’
[wrong sentence]:  The attention is a bit slow, but the waiters are always polite.


R:NOUN audience -> congregation
 [explain]:  audience = the people who go to watch/listen to a film, play, concert, public lecture, etc: ‘The group has played to vast audiences all over the world.’ ‘At the end of the talk, members of the audience were invited to ask questions.’ congregation = the people who attend a church service:
[wrong sentence]:  The priest stands in front of the bride and groom, facing the audience.


R:NOUN authority -> authorities
 [explain]:  To refer in a general way to the officials who make all the important decisions in a country, use the authorities: ‘The authorities estimate that nearly 100,000 immigrants have entered the country illegally.’ As a countable noun, authority refers to an official group or organization that controls an area or activity: ‘You can claim housing benefit from your local authority.’
[wrong sentence]:  Those who bend the rules are reported to the authority.
[wrong sentence]:  The authority had refused them even their basic civil rights.


R:NOUN baggages -> baggage
 [explain]:  Baggage is an uncountable noun: ‘Baggage must not be left unattended in the terminal building.’ ‘How many pieces of baggage do you have?’
[wrong sentence]:  All the passengers carried their own baggages.


R:NOUN behaviours -> behaviour
 [explain]:  Behaviour is an uncountable noun.
[wrong sentence]:  We were surprised by their peculiar behaviours.


R:NOUN boss -> manager
 [explain]:  Boss is usually used in informal styles: ‘From the way he acts, you’d think that he was the boss.’
[wrong sentence]:  My father is the boss of a small shipping company.


R:NOUN boundaries -> border
 [explain]:  Use boundary when you are talking about an area of land within a country: ‘The Mississippi River forms a natural boundary between Tennessee and Arkansas.’ ‘Their farm is just inside the boundary of the National Park. ‘The place where two countries meet is the border: ‘We’re about to cross the border between
[wrong sentence]:  The country shares boundaries with Ruanda and Burundi.


R:NOUN british -> britain
 [explain]:  The name of the country is Britain (or Great Britain).
[wrong sentence]:  I would prefer to study in America or British.


R:NOUN cameraman -> photographer
 [explain]:  cameraman = a person who is employed by a television or film company to operate a camera: ‘The cameraman had never shot a car chase before and needed the director’s advice.’ photographer = a person who takes (or whose job is to take) photographs: ‘She is one of the world’s leading fashion photographers.’
[wrong sentence]:  Shotaro Akiyama is a famous Japanese cameraman.


R:NOUN capacities -> skills
 [explain]:  capacity = the power or quality that makes someone able to do, experience, give or receive something: ‘These children display an extraordinary capacity for learning.’ ‘Man’s capacity for love and generosity is unlimited.’ skill = what someone needs to have learned before they can actually do a particular job or activity: ‘Being a good manager requires a number of highly specialized skills.’ ‘This course is designed to develop the student’s reading and writing skills.’
[wrong sentence]:  I wish to improve my speaking and listening capacities.
[wrong sentence]:  This type of job requires special capacities.


R:NOUN career -> profession
 [explain]:  See Language Note at OCCUPATION
[wrong sentence]:  I’d like to be a doctor or something related to that career.


R:NOUN certificate -> voucher
 [explain]:  certificate = an official document that states certain facts about someone: ‘a birth/marriage/death certificate’, ‘a certificate of health’ voucher = a kind of ticket that can be used instead of money: ‘a gift/luncheon/travel voucher’.
[wrong sentence]:  The other day I was given a gift certificate, but it was only worth two thousand yen.


R:NOUN characters -> point
 [explain]:  When you mean ‘a feature of someone’s character’, use characteristic or quality: ‘All great leaders share certain mean characteristics.’ ‘What qualities do you need to be a good parent?’ In contrast with qualities that you do not like, you can also talk about someone’s good points.
[wrong sentence]:  However, the Japanese also have a lot of good characters.


R:NOUN use -> point
 [explain]:  it’s no use ... (NOT there): ‘It’s no use complaining.’ there’s no point ... (NOT it) ‘There’s no point in getting upset.’
[wrong sentence]:  There’s no use in waiting any longer.


R:NOUN checking -> check-in
 [explain]:  check-in = the place at an airport (or hotel) where you check in: ‘There’s bound to be a long queue at the check-in.’ ‘The girl at the check-in desk asked if we had a reservation.’ check in = show your ticket, passport and luggage at a counter in an airport and receive a boarding card: ‘Once you’ve checked in, you have to go through customs.’
[wrong sentence]:  Once inside the airport, I made my way to the checking.


R:NOUN choices -> decisions
 [explain]:  When you pick the person/thing that you want (from a range of possibilities), you make a choice: ‘Her parents are not happy about her choice of husband.’ ‘Oxford was my first choice, but I didn’t get the grades.’ When you make a judgment about something, especially after thinking carefully about it, you make a decision: ‘Although the job offer is attractive, I’d like more time to make a decision.’ ‘My decision to leave school at 15 was the biggest mistake I ever made.’
[wrong sentence]:  In my new job I have to make a lot of important choices.


R:NOUN choice -> decision
 [explain]:  When you pick the person/thing that you want (from a range of possibilities), you make a choice: ‘Her parents are not happy about her choice of husband.’ ‘Oxford was my first choice, but I didn’t get the grades.’ When you make a judgment about something, especially after thinking carefully about it, you make a decision: ‘Although the job offer is attractive, I’d like more time to make a decision.’ ‘My decision to leave school at 15 was the biggest mistake I ever made.’
[wrong sentence]:  Please will you let us know your choice by the end of the month.


R:NOUN civilization -> culture
 [explain]:  civilization = (a society or group of societies having) a way of life that is considered to be advanced in terms of culture and social organization: ‘The remote mountain villages are still untouched by modern civilization.’ ‘The film examines the ancient civilizations of Greece and
[wrong sentence]:  American civilization is very different from that of Japan.
[wrong sentence]:  Each country has its own civilization and ideology.


R:NOUN manner -> culture
 [explain]:  See Language Note below
[wrong sentence]:  American manner is all about freedom and equality.


R:NOUN climate -> weather
 [explain]:  climate = the typical weather conditions that exist in a country or region; the place where these weather conditions exist: ‘Northern Europe has a mild climate and a high rainfall.’ ‘These flowers will not grow in cold climates.’
[wrong sentence]:  While I was driving to the airport, the climate was still wet and foggy.
[wrong sentence]:  Before going off in the boat, you should check the climate conditions.


R:NOUN cloth -> material
 [explain]:  Cloth (uncountable) usually refers to material made of cotton, wool etc that is used for making clothes: ‘The tailor took my measurements and then showed me several rolls of cloth.’
[wrong sentence]:  I bought some cheap cloth to make some curtains.


R:NOUN cloths -> clothe
 [explain]:  A cloth (pronounced /klɒθ/ ) is a piece of material made of cotton, wool, etc, usually used for cleaning or drying something: ‘I’m afraid I’ve spilled some milk. Have you got a cloth?’ ‘I need a new face cloth.’ Clothes (pronounced /kləʊðz/ ) are the things people wear, such as trousers, sweaters, etc: ‘I spend half my salary on clothes.’
[wrong sentence]:  He likes fast cars and expensive cloths.


R:NOUN cloth -> clothe
 [explain]:  A cloth (pronounced /klɒθ/ ) is a piece of material made of cotton, wool, etc, usually used for cleaning or drying something: ‘I’m afraid I’ve spilled some milk. Have you got a cloth?’ ‘I need a new face cloth.’ Clothes (pronounced /kləʊðz/ ) are the things people wear, such as trousers, sweaters, etc: ‘I spend half my salary on clothes.’
[wrong sentence]:  None of the cloth shops had any pink socks.


R:NOUN clothing -> clothe
 [explain]:  Use clothing when you are thinking about clothes in general: The population is in desperate need of foreign aid - especially food, medicine and clothing.’ ‘She works in the clothing industry.’ To refer to the things that you wear, use clothes: ‘You’d better take off those wet clothes or you’ll catch a cold.’
[wrong sentence]:  He had grown so much that his clothing didn’t fit him.
[wrong sentence]:  As soon as I arrived, I unpacked my clothing.


R:NOUN clothings -> clothe
 [explain]:  Clothing is an uncountable noun: The population is in desperate need of foreign aid, especially food, medicine and clothing.’
[wrong sentence]:  Those who work with pesticides are given protective clothings.


R:NOUN clothes -> costume
 [explain]:  costume = (1) (countable) a set of clothes worn during a performance by an actor, clown, etc: ‘She used to work for a theatre company, designing and making costumes.’ (2) (uncountable) a set of clothes that are typical of a particular country or historical period: ‘The castle guides were dressed up in Elizabethan costume.’ ‘A group of Hungarian folk dancers came on stage, all wearing national costume.’
[wrong sentence]:  The dancers were dressed in their national clothes.


R:NOUN uniforms -> costume
 [explain]:  uniform = a set of clothes worn by soldiers, police officers, nurses etc when on duty, and children in some schools: ‘Some children hate having to wear their school uniform.’ ; costume = a set of clothes worn by actors or public performers: ‘She used to work for a theatre company; designing and making costumes.’ ‘The tourist guides at the castle all wear medieval costumes.’
[wrong sentence]:  Every year we make new uniforms for the carnival.


R:NOUN collaboration -> co-operation
 [explain]:  Collaboration comes from the verb collaborate (= work in partnership with someone on the same task, especially one of a scientific, artistic or industrial nature): ‘He was one of the scientists who had collaborated with Oppenheimer to produce the first atomic bomb.’ Co-operation comes from the verb co-operate (= be willing to help someone to achieve something; be helpful): ‘Faced with the threat of a full-scale military invasion, the general had no choice but to co-operate.’
[wrong sentence]:  The police were grateful to the public for their collaboration.


R:NOUN comprehension -> understand
 [explain]:  Comprehension refers to the ability to understand the meaning of something, especially something that is spoken or written: ‘I’d like to develop my vocabulary and improve my listening comprehension.’
[wrong sentence]:  There is not enough comprehension between our two countries.


R:NOUN content -> content
 [explain]:  content (WITHOUT s) = the (interesting) facts, ideas or opinions expressed in a book, film, essay, speech etc: ‘His letters tend to have very little content.’ contents (WITH s) = (1) all the (interesting and uninteresting) things that are inside something: The customs officer opened my suitcase and examined the contents.’ (2) all the things that are written or talked about in a book, report, letter, discussion etc: ‘The contents of the diary are strictly confidential.’, (3) a list at the front of a book, report, thesis, etc, saying what each chapter or section is about: ‘If you want to know which chapters to read, just look at the contents.’
[wrong sentence]:  The cover page tells you about the magazine’s content.


R:NOUN contents -> content
 [explain]:  See Note at CONTENT
[wrong sentence]:  I didn’t find the contents of the novel very interesting.


R:NOUN cooker -> coo/chef
 [explain]:  cooker (AmE stove) = a piece of equipment that you use for cooking food: ‘I’ve never used a gas cooker before.’ cook = a person who cooks: ‘My sister is a superb cook.’ chef = a cook who works in a restaurant: ‘Jean-Paul is training to be a chef.’
[wrong sentence]:  The cooker puts too much salt in the food.


R:NOUN costs -> price

[wrong sentence]:  That shop is always empty because the costs are too high.

 [explain]:  See Language Note at PRICE
[wrong sentence]:  In London the cost of life is very high.


 [explain]:  Phrases connected with the idea of money end with living (NOT life): ‘Salaries have not kept up with increases in the cost of living.’ ‘It’s difficult to earn/make a living as an actress.’ ‘Acting is fun, but I wouldn’t want to do it for a living.’
[wrong sentence]:  The cost of life is very high in London.
[wrong sentence]:  During a recession, some people find it difficult to make a life.

 [explain]:  Criteria is the plural of criterion: The company’s sole criterion of , success is high sales.’ ‘These new criteria make it easier to get a visa.’
[wrong sentence]:  These decisions should not be based on purely financial criterias.


R:NOUN custom -> customs
 [explain]:  You talk about a country’s customs (WITH s): ‘In Hong Kong I learned a lot about Chinese customs. See also Language Note at MANNER
[wrong sentence]:  I went there hoping to learn something about Indian culture and custom.


 [explain]:  customs (WITH s) = the place where your bags are examined when you enter a country: ‘We got held up at the customs while they went through our suitcases.’ customs duty (WITH s) =taxes that you have to pay to bring certain goods into a country: ‘The customs duty on electrical goods is twenty percent of the retail price.’
[wrong sentence]:  Will I have to pay custom duty?

R:NOUN damages -> damage
 [explain]:  In its usual meaning, damage is an uncountable noun: ‘The insurance company will pay for any damage.’ ‘The ceiling had suffered a great deal of damage.’ damages (plural noun) = a sum of money that someone is awarded in a court of law: ‘She was awarded $3000 in damages.’ ‘She claimed damages of £2000 for wrongful dismissal.’

R:NOUN district -> region/area
 [explain]:  district = (1) one of the official divisions of a city or country: ‘the London postal district’, ‘the South Cambridgeshire District’, ‘the Liverpool 8 district of Merseyside’, ‘Last winter several London health districts faced a cash crisis.’ (2) an area in a city (or sometimes in a country) that has a particular quality or feature: ‘a very wealthy district’, ‘one of the most deprived districts in the inner city’, ‘an old slum district where the gangs operate’. A region is usually a large area of a country and has no official boundaries: ‘The President has declared the region a disaster area.’ ‘Such expansion would most likely occur in the Amazon region.’ ‘The people who live in this region have a strong accent.’ When you are unsure about which word to choose, use area. An area can be large or small and has a very general meaning.
[wrong sentence]:  The original inhabitants of the district were probably Chinese.


R:NOUN divorce -> divorce
 [explain]:  be/get divorced (WITH ‘d’): ‘They got divorced in 1993 just twelve months after they were married.’ ‘It seems as if getting divorced has become fashionable nowadays.’
[wrong sentence]:  If we were divorce, who would look after the children?


R:NOUN drama -> play
 [explain]:  drama (uncountable) = a type of literature consisting of plays in general, or a particular group of plays: ‘She has always been interested in music and drama.’ ‘Elizabethan drama is too bloodthirsty for my liking.’ play (countable) = a dramatic performance by actors in a theatre or on the radio/television; a piece of literature that has been written for actors to perform in a theatre etc: ‘The film is a clever adaptation of Alan Ayckbourn’s popular play.’ ‘The critics have generally been surprised by this play, which was written to be performed without props or scenery.’ Note that drama is sometimes used as a countable noun to refer to a specific type of play: ‘We were expecting to see a comedy, not a serious drama.’ When you simply mean ‘a dramatic performance’ (as opposed to a musical/ballet/opera etc), use play.
[wrong sentence]:  After the meal, we went to see a drama at the Cambridge Theatre.


R:NOUN dusts -> dust
 [explain]:  Dust is an uncountable noun: ‘The house hadn’t been lived in for a long time and the furniture was covered in dust.’
[wrong sentence]:  People eventually get ill from breathing in all the dusts and smoke.


R:NOUN earth -> world
 [explain]:  earth/Earth = the ball-shaped object that we live on, usually seen as a planet in space: ‘The earth moves round the sun every 365 days.’ ‘On the journey back to Earth, one of the spaceship’s computers failed.’ world = the ball-shaped object that we live on, usually seen in terms of people, countries, cities etc: ‘Tuberculosis is still common in some parts of the world.’ ‘It’s one of the largest countries in the world.’ ‘You’ll be seeing some of the world’s leading golfers.’ Note the expression all over the world (= everywhere in the world): ‘I have friends all over the world.’ planet = the ball-shaped object that we live on, usually seen in terms of the natural environment: ‘This disaster could affect the whole planet.’ ‘The planet is gradually getting warmer.’
[wrong sentence]:  I have friends in different parts of the earth.


R:NOUN earth -> planet
 [explain]:  earth/Earth = the ball-shaped object that we live on, usually seen as a planet in space: ‘The earth moves round the sun every 365 days.’ ‘On the journey back to Earth, one of the spaceship’s computers failed.’ world = the ball-shaped object that we live on, usually seen in terms of people, countries, cities etc: ‘Tuberculosis is still common in some parts of the world.’ ‘It’s one of the largest countries in the world.’ ‘You’ll be seeing some of the world’s leading golfers.’ Note the expression all over the world (= everywhere in the world): ‘I have friends all over the world.’ planet = the ball-shaped object that we live on, usually seen in terms of the natural environment: ‘This disaster could affect the whole planet.’ ‘The planet is gradually getting warmer.’

[wrong sentence]:  For the last three years I’ve been working for a French editing company.


R:NOUN efforts -> effort
 [explain]:  When you mean ‘the energy or hard work that is needed to do something’, use effort (uncountable): ‘Digging the tunnel demanded a great deal of effort.’
[wrong sentence]:  The authorities have put a lot of efforts into making the streets cleaner.

 [explain]:  When someone tries to do something, especially without being sure of success, they make an attempt to do it: ‘Having failed to reach the summit last October, the climbers are now getting ready to make a second attempt.’ ‘All attempts to control inflation have failed.’
[wrong sentence]:  In my opinion, a holiday is a hopeless effort to relax.

[wrong sentence]:  I work for an electronic company.

R:NOUN english -> englishman
 [explain]:  an Englishman, an Englishwoman (NOT an English): ‘She was the very first Englishwoman to set foot on the moon.’
[wrong sentence]:  She’s going to get married to an English.


R:NOUN entertainments -> entertainment
 [explain]:  Entertainment (= all the things that provide amusement) is an uncountable noun: ‘As far as entertainment is concerned, we usually go to the discotheque.’
[wrong sentence]:  The town offers all sorts of entertainments.


R:NOUN countries -> country
 [explain]:  every + singular count noun: ‘I understood every word.’
[wrong sentence]:  There was a representative from every countries.


R:NOUN nation -> country
 [explain]:  A person comes from, lives in, or feels part of a particular country (NOT nation): ‘Some people in this country think that the leadership is too weak.’ ‘People living in former Soviet bloc countries are undergoing a difficult period of transition.’ Nation is less common than country and is mainly used when a country is considered as a political or economic structure: ‘Japan has become the richest nation in the world.’ ‘Representatives from the world’s leading industrial nations will meet next month in Geneva.’
[wrong sentence]:  It’s hard to tell which nation he comes from.


R:NOUN mornings -> morning
 [explain]:  every + singular count noun: ‘I understood every word.’
[wrong sentence]:  Every mornings I give him his breakfast.


R:NOUN week -> weeks
 [explain]:  ‘every day’, ‘every week’ BUT ‘every two days’, ‘every six weeks’
[wrong sentence]:  I have to visit the hospital every six week.


R:NOUN experiences -> experience
 [explain]:  When you are talking about someone’s life as a whole, experience is uncountable: ‘Salary will depend upon qualifications and experience.’
[wrong sentence]:  Many of us have had experiences of living overseas.


R:NOUN extinguished -> extinct
 [explain]:  extinguish = make something stop burning: ‘Before entering the factory, please make sure that all cigarettes have been properly extinguished.’ extinct = (of a type of animal or plant) completely non-existent, because every single one has died or been killed: ‘If nothing is done to save the whales now, the species will soon become extinct.’
[wrong sentence]:  Many species are becoming extinguished.


R:NOUN faces -> side
 [explain]:  two sides of the same coin (= two ways of looking at the same situation): ‘Unemployment and social unrest are basically two sides of the same coin.’

R:NOUN fantasy -> imagination
 [explain]:  fantasy = a picture in the mind produced by the imagination, especially one which is very different from reality: ‘He lived in a world of fantasy.’ imagination = (the part of the mind with) the ability to produce mental pictures: ‘Unlike adults, young children are usually full of imagination.’ ‘His paintings show great technical skill but a lack of imagination.’
[wrong sentence]:  Were the voices real or just products of his fantasy?


R:NOUN fault -> mistake
 [explain]:  Use fault in connection with machines, electrical equipment, and someone’s character: ‘It sounds like there’s a fault in one of the loudspeakers.’ ‘The secret of a successful relationship is to accept each other’s faults.’ In connection with spellings, essays, calculations, decisions, etc, use mistake: ‘There are always a lot of mistakes in Ivan’s work.’ ‘We all make mistakes.’
[wrong sentence]:  I apologize for our fault and will send you a full refund.


R:NOUN faults -> mistake
 [explain]:  Use fault in connection with machines, electrical equipment, and someone’s character: ‘It sounds like there’s a fault in one of the loudspeakers.’ ‘The secret of a successful relationship is to accept each other’s faults.’ In connection with spellings, essays, calculations, decisions, etc, use mistake: ‘There are always a lot of mistakes in Ivan’s work.’ ‘We all make mistakes.’
[wrong sentence]:  If you have time, could you correct the faults in this letter for me?

R:NOUN terribly -> terrible
 [explain]:  Feel is followed by an adjective (NOT an adverb): ‘Does anyone feel hungry?’ ‘We all felt disappointed.’ Other verbs that are followed by an adjective include be, look, seem, smell, sound, taste: ‘You look cold.’ ‘That piano sounds terrible.’
[wrong sentence]:  I woke up the next day feeling terribly.


R:NOUN fellows -> workmates
 [explain]:  To refer to someone you know because you work at the same place, use workmate or (especially of people in professional jobs) colleague: ‘The police are questioning the missing woman’s relatives and workmates.’ ‘I’d like you to meet a colleague of mine, Jean Armstrong.’
[wrong sentence]:  My boss started to shout at me in front of all my fellows.


R:NOUN females -> women
 [explain]:  Female is used mainly in scientific or technical styles: ‘The male birds are usually more colourful than the females.’ ‘The female butterfly does not require additional salts.’ Female is sometimes used to refer to a woman or girl, but many people find this use of the word offensive.
[wrong sentence]:  Most females want to continue their careers after marriage.


R:NOUN fever -> temperature
 [explain]:  When someone feels rather ill and their body is hotter than usual, they have a (high) temperature: ‘I feel as if I’ve got a temperature.’ ‘She’s in bed with a high temperature.’
[wrong sentence]:  I’d caught a cold and had a high fever.


R:NOUN fishes -> fish
 [explain]:  The plural form of fish is usually fish: ‘Did you catch any fish?’ ‘The fish in the market are always fresh.’
[wrong sentence]:  I had never seen such brightly coloured fishes before.


R:NOUN foods -> food
 [explain]:  Food is nearly always uncountable: ‘We need to go out and buy some food.’ It is countable only when it refers to a particular kind of food: ‘baby foods’, ‘health foods’.
[wrong sentence]:  The child was caught stealing foods.


R:NOUN force -> power
 [explain]:  force = the use of power or strength: ‘The demonstrators were made to leave the building by force.’ power = the ability to control people and events: ‘I think the police have too much power.’
[wrong sentence]:  We must not forget Japan’s economic force.

R:NOUN fruits -> fruit
 [explain]:  Fruit (= fruit in general) is an uncountable noun: ‘We eat lots of fruit in our family.’ ‘Would anyone care for a piece of fruit?’ (= an orange, apple, pear etc)
[wrong sentence]:  I never buy tinned fruits.

R:NOUN furnitures -> furniture
 [explain]:  Furniture is an uncountable noun: ‘We need some new furniture for the lounge.’ ‘Our showrooms up and down the country stock the latest ideas in bedroom furniture.’
[wrong sentence]:  The room was so full of furnitures it was difficult to move.


R:NOUN goal -> destination
 [explain]:  goal = something that you hope to achieve: ‘The company’s goal is to double its share of the personal computer market.’ destination = the place that you are travelling to: ‘The immigration officer wanted to know my destination and how long I was planning to stay.’
[wrong sentence]:  We reached our goal just after three o’clock.


R:NOUN goods -> possessions
 [explain]:  goods = things that are made to be sold: ‘The supermarket has a wide variety of frozen goods.’ ‘The average family’s weekly expenditure on goods and services has risen by 20%.’ possessions = all the things that a person owns: ‘After his mother died, Andrew had the unpleasant task of sorting through her possessions.’
[wrong sentence]:  He had very little money and very few goods.

 [explain]:  Soldiers, sailors, police officers etc have different ranks (NOT grades): ‘She joined the police force in 1990 and quickly rose to the rank of sergeant.’
[wrong sentence]:  By the end of the war he had been promoted to the grade of captain.

 [explain]:  Greatly is usually placed in front of a main verb, especially in passive structures: ‘Your kind offer is greatly appreciated.’ ‘Stories of panic on Wall Street have been greatly exaggerated.’
[wrong sentence]:  Living standards have improved greatly.

[wrong sentence]:  I greatly appreciate all your help and guidances.

 [explain]:  See Language Note at MANNER
[wrong sentence]:  It is a habit in Japan to take off your shoes before entering a house.


R:NOUN hand -> hand
 [explain]:  in the hands of (plural) = controlled by: ‘All the political power is in the hands of the government.’
[wrong sentence]:  The future of millions of people should not be in the hand of one man.


R:NOUN side -> hand
 [explain]:  on the one hand ... on the other hand (NOT side ): ‘On the other hand, the airline has an ‘excellent safety record.’
[wrong sentence]:  On the other side, I can understand why she feels disappointed.


R:NOUN holidays -> holiday
 [explain]:  Speakers of British English use (be/go) on holiday, (return/get back) from holiday (WITHOUT -s): ‘I met her while I was on holiday in Switzerland.’ ‘We’re supposed to be going on holiday with them.’ The plural form holidays is usually used with them/my/your etc: ‘Where are you going for your holidays this year?’ ‘During the long summer holidays some students get a part-time job.’
[wrong sentence]:  She said she was going on holidays to France.
[wrong sentence]:  I’ve just got back from holidays.


R:NOUN home -> house
 [explain]:  Leave/stay/go home BUT leave/stay at/go to sb’s house
[wrong sentence]:  We left my uncle’s home at ten o’clock.


R:NOUN homeworks -> homework
 [explain]:  Homework is an uncountable noun: ‘We’re given a lot of homework at the weekend.’
[wrong sentence]:  Have you done your homeworks?


R:NOUN horizon -> horizons
 [explain]:  your horizons (WITH -s) = the range of things that you are involved or interested in: ‘As a politician, his horizons extend no further than the next election.’
[wrong sentence]:  Further education enables you to broaden your horizon.


R:NOUN household -> housework
 [explain]:  household = all the people living together in a house or flat: ‘Be quiet or you’ll wake the whole household.’ housework = all the jobs that have to be done regularly to keep a house or flat clean and tidy: ‘My husband and I share all the housework between us.’
[wrong sentence]:  At the weekend they have to do all the household.


R:NOUN household work -> housework
 [explain]:  household = all the people living together in a house or flat: ‘Be quiet or you’ll wake the whole household.’ housework = all the jobs that have to be done regularly to keep a house or flat clean and tidy: ‘My husband and I share all the housework between us.’
[wrong sentence]:  Some women get no help with the household work.


R:NOUN houseworks -> housework
 [explain]:  Housework is an uncountable noun: ‘There’s always lots of housework to be done.’
[wrong sentence]:  I do all the houseworks myself.


R:NOUN homour -> mood
 [explain]:  Your mood is the way you feel (about life) at a particular time: ‘Doctors believe that tiredness affects your mood and behaviour.’ ‘George is in a bad mood this morning. Has someone upset him?’ Humour is used with a similar meaning, but only in formal or literary styles: ‘Steven was deep in bad humour.’
[wrong sentence]:  Bill looks angry. Is he in a bad homour?


R:NOUN humour -> mood
 [explain]:  Your mood is the way you feel (about life) at a particular time: ‘Doctors believe that tiredness affects your mood and behaviour.’ ‘George is in a bad mood this morning. Has someone upset him?’ Humour is used with a similar meaning, but only in formal or literary styles: ‘Steven was deep in bad humour.’
[wrong sentence]:  Sometimes when I’m in a good humour, I like to cook something special.


R:NOUN impression -> image
 [explain]:  impression = an opinion or feeling that you have about someone or something, especially after just a short time: ‘My impression is that she would make an excellent teacher.’ ‘He gives the impression of being someone you can trust.’ image = the general picture that you want people to have in their minds when they think of you or your company, organization etc: ‘After all the bad publicity, the company needs to improve its image.’
[wrong sentence]:  I decided to change my impression and had all my hair cut short.


R:NOUN impulse -> inspiration
 [explain]:  impulse = a sudden desire to do something: ‘I had a sudden impulse to walk out of the classroom and never return.’ ‘It’s unwise to act on impulse.’ inspiration = (a source of) creative energy: ‘The inspiration for these early poems came from his relationship with Lucy Potter.’
[wrong sentence]:  I hope your trip will provide impulse for the essay you will have to write when you get back.

[wrong sentence]:  The biggest industries in Jakobsberg are Philips and IBM.

 [explain]:  See Language Note above

R:NOUN infancy -> childhood
 [explain]:  Infancy (= the period when a child is very young) is used mainly in formal and technical styles: ‘In those days, large numbers of children died in infancy.’ ; To refer to the period when someone is a child, use childhood: ‘The old photographs brought back memories of my childhood.’
[wrong sentence]:  Mr Fox had a very difficult infancy.


R:NOUN infants -> children
 [explain]:  Infant (= a baby or very young child) is used mainly in literary and technical styles: ‘The infant, cradled in Mariam’s arms, began to cry.’ ‘There are clear differences in temperament and speed of learning among infants at this early stage.’
[wrong sentence]:  I had to stay at home and look after my infants.


R:NOUN kids -> children
 [explain]:  kid is used only in informal styles: ‘I’ll ring you back once the kids are in bed.’
[wrong sentence]:  Films of this type are not suitable for young kids.


R:NOUN ones -> children
 [explain]:  Ones is usually used to refer to things: ‘Rechargeable batteries are more expensive than ordinary ones.’ ‘The red ones are fine, but I prefer the white ones.’ Ones may also be used to refer to particular people: ‘The older children laughed but the younger ones were scared.’ In general statements about groups of people, ones is usually avoided: ‘French students have to work harder than British students .’
[wrong sentence]:  British children have more opportunities than Tunisian ones.


R:NOUN influence -> effect
 [explain]:  If someone (or something) has an Influence, they help to shape the way that someone or something develops: ‘D.W. Griffith had an enormous influence on the motion picture industry.’ If something has an effect, it causes a change to take place in someone or something: ‘The war had a disastrous effect on the economy.’
[wrong sentence]:  I asked him not to keep disturbing me, but it had no influence.
[wrong sentence]:  These drugs have no influence on the virus.


R:NOUN results -> effect
 [explain]:  result = the situation or action that develops from a previous situation or action: ‘The result of the crisis was that in 1649 the king was beheaded.’ ‘The anti-smoking campaign has produced excellent results.’ effect (on sb/sth) = a change in condition that is caused by something: ‘The drug had no effect on him.’ (= there was no change in his condition) ‘Wine always has an effect on me - it makes me want to sing.’
[wrong sentence]:  Nowadays we hear a lot about pollution and its results on our health.


R:NOUN informations -> information
 [explain]:  See Language Note at SCENERY
[wrong sentence]:  Would you please send me more informations about the course?


R:NOUN inhabitants -> population
 [explain]:  To refer to all the people in the world, or all the people living in a particular country, city, town etc, use population: ‘Half the world’s population doesn’t have enough to eat.’ ‘To make it profitable, about a third of the population of France will have to visit Eurodisney each year.’ ‘The town has a population of 15,000.’
[wrong sentence]:  The rapid growth of the world’s inhabitants has produced serious social and economic problems.


R:NOUN inquiries -> enquiries
 [explain]:  In a simple request for information, the usual word is enquiry: ‘We’ve had a lot of enquiries in response to the advertisement.’ When you mean ‘a full investigation or a long serious study’, the usual word is inquiry: ‘a court of inquiry’, ‘There is to be an official inquiry into the cause of the crash.’
[wrong sentence]:  After making several inquiries I finally discovered his address.


R:NOUN inventions -> advance
 [explain]:  When you are talking about the development of new ideas or techniques that have a good effect, use advance/breakthrough/discovery: ‘This new treatment could be the breakthrough that cancer patients have been waiting for.’
[wrong sentence]:  Recent inventions in medicine have helped to reduce the rate of infant mortality.


R:NOUN jargons -> jargon
 [explain]:  Jargon is an uncountable noun: ‘The document was full of legal jargon.’
[wrong sentence]:  I don’t understand computer jargons.


R:NOUN jewellery -> jeweller’s
 [explain]:  jewellery = rings, bracelets, necklaces, watches, etc: ‘The stolen jewellery has never been recovered.’ jeweller’s = a shop which sells and repairs jewellery: ‘I took the watch to my local jeweller’s but they said it couldn’t be repaired.’
[wrong sentence]:  She had bought the ring at a jewellery.


R:NOUN jewelleries -> jewellery
 [explain]:  Jewellery is an uncountable noun: ‘The thieves took nothing but cash and jewellery.’
[wrong sentence]:  You should keep expensive jewelleries in a bank or safe.


R:NOUN journey -> trip
 [explain]:  See Language Note at TRIP
[wrong sentence]:  I booked a journey to Brighton for two weeks.
[wrong sentence]:  I’ve arranged a journey to Malta from April 15th to May 2nd.


R:NOUN voyage -> trip
 [explain]:  See Language Note at TRIP
[wrong sentence]:  She didn’t say much about her voyage to Germany.


R:NOUN junk -> litter
 [explain]:  junk = (informal) anything that you consider to be worthless or useless: ‘The garden shed was full of old junk that hadn’t been used for years.’ litter = things such as empty packets, cans and bottles that are left in a public place: ‘You can be fined up to £100 for dropping litter.’
[wrong sentence]:  If you are caught dropping junk in the street, you are fined.


R:NOUN litters -> litter
 [explain]:  Litter is an uncountable noun: ‘Wherever you find crowds, you’ll find litter.’
[wrong sentence]:  In the cities the streets are full of litters.


R:NOUN kind -> kinds
 [explain]:  Kind, sort and type are countable nouns. After many, all, these and other plural meanings, use kinds/sorts/types (WITH -s): These kinds of fruit do not grow in cold climates.’
[wrong sentence]:  I enjoy all kind of sport.
[wrong sentence]:  There are many kind of job for people with qualifications.


R:NOUN crimes -> crime
 [explain]:  Imprisonment is not a good way of reducing these kinds of crime/so kind/sort/type of + singular form: ‘this kind of envelope’ kinds/sorts/types of + singular or plural form: ‘these kinds of envelope/s’ Note that after kinds/sorts/types of a plural form can sometimes sound awkward and careful users generally prefer a singular form.
[wrong sentence]:  Imprisonment is not a good way of reducing this kind of crimes.


R:NOUN knowledges -> knowledge
 [explain]:  Knowledge never has a plural ending: ‘With all your knowledge, you should be a teacher.’
[wrong sentence]:  I am attending this course to improve my knowledges of English.


R:NOUN landscape -> scenery
 [explain]:  landscape = (a view, painting or photograph of) a wide area of land, especially in the countryside: ‘Having reached the top of the hill, we sat and admired the landscape that stretched far into the distance.’ ‘Constable is known for his beautiful Suffolk landscapes.’ scenery = the natural features of the countryside (hills, valleys, fields etc) considered in terms of their beauty, especially those seen from a particular place: ‘The train journey takes you through some breathtaking scenery.’ ‘Cycling means that you can get fit and enjoy the scenery at the same time.’
[wrong sentence]:  Lots of kings chose to live here because of the beautiful landscape.
[wrong sentence]:  The landscape surrounding the village is really beautiful.


R:NOUN scene -> scenery
 [explain]:  scenery = the natural features of the countryside, especially when seen from a particular place: ‘The Lake District is famous for its magnificent scenery.’
[wrong sentence]:  The driver stopped now and again so that we could enjoy the scene.


R:NOUN sceneries -> scenery
 [explain]:  Scenery is an uncountable noun: ‘Some of the scenery is quite spectacular.’
[wrong sentence]:  You’ll be very impressed by the beautiful sceneries.


R:NOUN lane -> path
 [explain]:  lane = a narrow road or street: ‘We rode our bikes along the little country lanes.’ path = a narrow strip of ground in a garden, park etc that is made for people to walk along: ‘There used to be a path down to the stream, but it’s all overgrown now.’
[wrong sentence]:  It was so dark in the park that she kept wandering from the lane.


R:NOUN laughters -> laughter
 [explain]:  Laughter is an uncountable noun: ‘There was always a lot of laughter and joking when Henry came to dinner.’
[wrong sentence]:  His laughters filled the whole apartment.


R:NOUN level -> standard
 [explain]:  When you are talking about the quality of something, the usual word is standard: ‘All his work is of a very high standard.’ ‘Our company accepts only the highest standards of workmanship.’
[wrong sentence]:  People in developed countries have a higher level of living.


R:NOUN living -> life
 [explain]:  See Note at LIFE 2
[wrong sentence]:  The local farmers will never change their way of living.


 [explain]:  See Note at LIFE 2
[wrong sentence]:  She loves the farm and refuses to change her way of living.


R:NOUN lots -> lot
 [explain]:  a lot of (WITH a): ‘I bet she’s got a lot of money.’ lots of (WITHOUT a): ‘I bet she’s got lots of money.’
[wrong sentence]:  She makes us do a lots of homework.


R:NOUN luggages -> luggage
 [explain]:  Luggage is an uncountable noun: ‘Do you have any more luggage?’
[wrong sentence]:  There was no room in the car for all our luggages.


R:NOUN luxury things -> luxuries
 [explain]:  luxuries (plural of luxury), luxury goods, luxury items (but NOT luxury things): ‘Tax on luxury goods is bound to be increased.’
[wrong sentence]:  They don’t have any money to spend on luxury things.


R:NOUN machines -> engines
 [explain]:  machine = a piece of equipment which performs useful work, such as a sewing machine, washing machine or computer: ‘To operate this machine, simply select the type of coffee you require and press the green button.’ ‘The latest machines can run both types of software.’ engine = a device which provides the power for a motor vehicle, train, aircraft etc: ‘It was difficult to make yourself heard above the roar of the engines.’ ‘Check the tyre pressures and top up the engine oil.’
[wrong sentence]:  Over eighty per cent of these cars are old, and so are their machines.


R:NOUN mails -> mail
 [explain]:  Mail (= letters and parcels) is an uncountable noun: ‘Has there been any mail today?’
[wrong sentence]:  It takes seventeen trees to produce one ton of junk mails.


R:NOUN mark -> feature
 [explain]:  A mark on someone’s face or body is a small area of skin that is a different colour from the rest: ‘You’ve got two dark red marks on your nose where your glasses have been.’ Things such as the colour of someone’s eyes and hair, and the shape of their nose, chin and mouth are their features: ‘As soon as he began to describe her features, I realized that he was talking about Mandy.’ Before buying a cassette recorder, I asked my friend if he could recommend a good mark. Before buying a cassette recorder, I asked my friend if he could recommend a good make.
[wrong sentence]:  Her only distinguishing mark is her long blond hair.


R:NOUN marks -> brand
 [explain]:  mark (also Mk) = a model, type or version (used mainly in trade names): ‘a Mk II Jaguar’ make = a particular type of car, washing machine, camera, etc: ‘If you want a really good make, go for a Nikon or a Pentax.’ ‘Which make of television do you prefer, Sony or Panasonic?’ brand = a particular type of soap, toothpaste, butter, cigarette, etc: ‘I’m tired of being told which brand of washing powder I should use.’
[wrong sentence]:  As for cigarettes, there are many different marks.


R:NOUN marriage -> wed
 [explain]:  marriage = the ceremony of becoming husband and wife considered from a purely religious or legal point of view: ‘Her parents are against the marriage.’ wedding = the occasion when this ceremony takes place and the celebrations that follow it: ‘I never see most of my relatives apart from at weddings.’
[wrong sentence]:  Why didn’t you invite me to your marriage?
[wrong sentence]:  I have just received your marriage invitation.


R:NOUN me -> myself
 [explain]:  See Language Note at MYSELF
[wrong sentence]:  What can I tell you about me?


R:NOUN media -> medium
 [explain]:  When you are talking about television, radio and newspapers, use medium for singular reference: ‘Children learn as much through the medium of television as they do by going to school.’
[wrong sentence]:  Television is a very powerful media.

 [explain]:  When you are talking about television, radio and newspapers, use medium for singular reference: ‘Children learn as much through the medium of television as they do by going to school.’
[wrong sentence]:  The medias, such as radio and television, tell us what is happening in the world.


R:NOUN medium -> average
 [explain]:  If someone’s level of skill or ability is neither high nor low, it is average: ‘Elizabeth shows above average intelligence for a child of three.’
[wrong sentence]:  My ability in English is about medium.


R:NOUN memory -> souvenir
 [explain]:  memory = something that you remember and see as a picture in your mind, especially an experience that you had a long time ago: ‘The old teddy bear brought back vivid memories of my childhood.’ souvenir = something that you buy or obtain in a particular place (especially while on holiday) and keep to remind you of it: ‘The vase is a souvenir of my week in Venice.’
[wrong sentence]:  Each visitor received a small gift as a memory.


R:NOUN memories -> souvenirs
 [explain]:  memory = something that you remember and see as a picture in your mind, especially an experience that you had a long time ago: ‘The old teddy bear brought back vivid memories of my childhood.’ souvenir = something that you buy or obtain in a particular place (especially while on holiday) and keep to remind you of it: ‘The vase is a souvenir of my week in Venice.’
[wrong sentence]:  I bought six postcards and a few small memories.


R:NOUN memories -> memoirs
 [explain]:  To refer to someone’s written account of (a period in) their life; use memoirs (pronounced /ˈmem.wɑːz/): ‘The former President’s long awaited memoirs are to be published next month.’ You will remain in our memory and we all hope that you’ll visit Greece again next summer.
[wrong sentence]:  Casanova recorded his adventures in his memories.

[wrong sentence]:  My favourite menu is cheese and mushroom omelette.

 [explain]:  See Language Note on next page
[wrong sentence]:  You’ll be able to try some of the local plates.


R:NOUN methods -> ways
 [explain]:  Method (of/for doing sth) is used mainly in technical styles: ‘Farming methods have undergone many changes in recent years.’ ‘The research project aims to develop new methods for trapping solar energy.’ In non-technical styles, use way (of doing/to do sth): ‘There are several ways of answering the question.’ ‘The best way to contact him is by fax.’
[wrong sentence]:  We’ll have to find new methods of amusing ourselves.


R:NOUN age -> age
 [explain]:  middle age = the period in a person’s life between youth and old age: ‘People who live this type of life are lucky if they reach middle age.’ Middle Ages = the period in European history from about 1100 to 1500 AD: ‘Life in the Middle
[wrong sentence]:  The history of the town dates back to the Middle Age.


R:NOUN mist -> fog
 [explain]:  mist = tiny drops of water in the air that make it difficult to see things that are far away: ‘As the day wore on, the early morning mist quickly disappeared.’ fog = very thick mist: ‘Traffic on several stretches of the M1 was slowed to a walking pace this morning as the result of thick fog.’
[wrong sentence]:  Her flight was held up on account of the mist.


R:NOUN mister -> mr
 [explain]:  Mister is always written Mr: ‘Mr Jones called this morning about the central heating.’ See also MR
[wrong sentence]:  Dear Mister Southcroft...


R:NOUN mood -> atmosphere
 [explain]:  The streets were full of people in a holiday mood. mood = the way someone feels at a particular time, such as happy, angry, etc: ‘I’d keep clear of
[wrong sentence]:  The mood of the castle depends on the weather.


R:NOUN motor -> engine
 [explain]:  motor = the part of a machine which turns electrical power into movement: ‘My video camera isn’t working. Either the battery is flat or there’s something wrong with the motor.’ engine = See Note at MACHINE
[wrong sentence]:  It’s an old car and the motor is very noisy.


R:NOUN john -> (john) smith
 [explain]:  Mr/Mrs/Miss/Ms (+ first name) + surname: ‘Mrs Waters’, ‘Mr Clive Upton’
[wrong sentence]:  Our English teacher is called Mr John.


R:NOUN musics -> music
 [explain]:  Music is an uncountable noun: ‘Her hobbies are gardening and music.’
[wrong sentence]:  Nowadays I prefer classical musics.


R:NOUN necessity -> need
 [explain]:  If something is a necessity, you must have it or do it, or it must happen: ‘If you saw the terrible conditions in which these people are living, you would appreciate the necessity to step up foreign aid.’ ‘For anyone who runs a large business, a computer is an absolute necessity.’ Need has a similar meaning to necessity but it is also used in connection with something that you want to do/have/happen (although itis not essential): ‘I began to feel the need for a change of life style.’ ‘Don’t you ever feel the need to do something more creative?’
[wrong sentence]:  It was then that I felt the necessity to improve my English.


R:NOUN noise -> sound
 [explain]:  noise = (1) loud unpleasant sounds heard as a single mass: ‘The noise of the traffic gave me a headache.’ ‘People were making such a lot of noise that I couldn’t hear what she was saying.’ (2) a sound that is unpleasant and/or without meaning: ‘The radio started making a funny noise.’ ‘What was that noise?’ sound = something that is heard or received by the ear, especially something that you recognize or can give a meaning to: ‘I’ve always loved the sound of a classical guitar.’ ‘I could hear the sound of someone crying in the next room.’
[wrong sentence]:  He was woken up by the noise of broken glass.
[wrong sentence]:  I turned on the radio but there was no noise.

 [explain]:  See Language Note at NOT
[wrong sentence]:  I checked the essay for mistakes but I couldn’t find none.

R:NOUN obligations -> duties
 [explain]:  obligation = moral duty or responsibility: ‘Having promised to cut taxes, the government now has an obligation to do so.’ ‘Anyone who rents a property is under an obligation to keep it clean and tidy.’ ; duty = what you have to do because it is a part of your job or because you think it is right: ‘One of the principal’s main duties is to improve the quality of teaching and learning in the school.’
[wrong sentence]:  My obligations include doing the housework and picking up the children from school.


R:NOUN occasion -> opportunity
 [explain]:  occasion = the time when an event happens: ‘I’ve been to Rome on several occasions.’ (= several times) opportunity = a time when it is possible to do something that you want to do: ‘The meeting on Tuesday will be a good opportunity for you to make some new contacts.’ ‘She has considerable ability and should be given more opportunity to use it.’ chance = an informal word for ‘opportunity’: ‘If I had the chance, I’d like to be an airline pilot.’ ‘I’ve been so busy this morning I haven’t had a chance to sit down.’ See also OPPORTUNITY
[wrong sentence]:  The scholarship provided me with my first occasion to travel overseas.
