 [explain]:  See Language Note below
[wrong sentence]:  The important thing is to be happy in your occupation.


R:NOUN source -> source
 [explain]:  The noun/pronoun following one of is always plural: ‘one of my friends’, ‘one of her teachers ‘, ‘one of the biggest islands in the world’ .
[wrong sentence]:  The sea is one of our main source of food.


R:NOUN resource -> source
 [explain]:  resource = (1) something that can be used to make a particular task or activity easier: ‘All the teaching resources - books, cassettes and so on - are kept in a special room.’ (2) something that a country has and uses to create wealth, such as oil or natural gas: ‘Brunei is rich in natural resources.’ source = the place where something comes from or the thing from which we can get it: ‘We collect the information from various sources.’ ‘Beans and lentils are a very good source of protein.’
[wrong sentence]:  Teachers are often regarded as a resource of knowledge.
[wrong sentence]:  Tourism is the main resource of money for these people.


R:NOUN ones -> people
 [explain]:  Ones is usually used to refer to things: ‘Rechargeable batteries are more expensive than ordinary ones.’ ‘The red ones are fine, but I prefer the white ones.’ Ones may also be used to refer to particular people: ‘The older children laughed but the younger ones were scared.’ In general statements about groups of people, ones is usually avoided: ‘French students have to work harder than British students .’
[wrong sentence]:  Young people learn more quickly than older ones.


R:NOUN peoples -> people
 [explain]:  a people (countable) = a race: ‘His dream is that the peoples of the world will one day unite.’ people (plural noun) = men, women and children: ‘He finds it difficult to get along with people.’
[wrong sentence]:  Peoples come from all over the world to visit the city.


R:NOUN ones -> one’s
 [explain]:  The possessive form is one ‘s (WITH ‘s): ‘It is difficult to estimate one’s chances of success.’ See also IT’S
[wrong sentence]:  Getting married for economic reasons is not a good start to ones married life.


R:NOUN overall -> whole
 [explain]:  on the whole = in general: ‘On the whole, the people I’ve been dealing with have been very cooperative.’ Compare: ‘Her overall command of English is excellent.’ ‘On the whole her command of English is excellent.’
[wrong sentence]:  England is okay on the overall, although some places are very dirty.


R:NOUN package -> packet
 [explain]:  See Language Note below
[wrong sentence]:  He smokes about one package a day.
[wrong sentence]:  I bought six eggs and a package of tea.


R:NOUN pair -> couple
 [explain]:  Pair usually refers to things (e.g. ‘a pair of scissors/socks’) or to two people who are seen (doing something) together: ‘It’s about time the pair of you did some work.’ ‘The German pair need just two more points for the match.’ Pair also refers to two animals that stay together and produce young: ‘a pair of swifts with a family to feed’. The usual word for a husband and wife (or two people in a similar relationship) is couple: ‘Married couples should benefit from the new tax legislation.’
[wrong sentence]:  The old pair next door have been married for 65 years.


R:NOUN parking -> car park
 [explain]:  a parking space/place = a place in a street, car park etc where a vehicle can be left: ‘My first attempt to back into the parking space was a disaster.’ a car park (AmE parking lot) = a large open area or building where cars can be left: ‘The supermarket has its own car park.’ parking (uncountable noun) = space(s) where vehicles can be left: ‘Parking is available at
[wrong sentence]:  What the town needs is an underground parking.


R:NOUN part -> place
 [explain]:  When you take part in an activity, you do it together with other people: ‘Altogether there are seventy-three competitors taking part in the race.’ ‘She’s been invited to take part in a TV quiz programme.’ take place = (of a planned event) happen: ‘The next meeting of the Nature Society will take place on Tuesday 3rd March.’
[wrong sentence]:  The election will take part within the next two years.


R:NOUN pattern -> routine
 [explain]:  To refer to the things that you do every day, usually in the same order, use routine: ‘The trip to
[wrong sentence]:  Their daily pattern doesn’t include enough exercise.


R:NOUN payment -> fee
 [explain]:  payment = an amount of money that is paid for something: ‘I had to get rid of the car because I couldn’t keep up the payments.’ fee = an amount of money paid to a doctor, lawyer, or other professional person: ‘The fee for one hour’s private tuition is $60.’
[wrong sentence]:  Or Schneider charges a high payment but he is very good.


R:NOUN personality -> character
 [explain]:  When talking about a person’s moral quality, use character (NOT personality). Compare: ‘For a career in sales, you need a forceful personality.’ ‘People of character and integrity never turn their backs on the truth.’
[wrong sentence]:  He is a man of strong personality who will fight for what is right.


R:NOUN phenomena -> phenomenon
 [explain]:  Phenomena is the plural of phenomenon: ‘The breakdown of family life is a relatively recent phenomenon.’ ‘Mathematics explains a wide variety of natural phenomena.’
[wrong sentence]:  This phenomena is called ‘culture shock’.


R:NOUN pillows -> cushion
 [explain]:  pillow = a bag-like object filled with soft material that you rest your head on when you are in bed: ‘No sooner had his head touched the pillow than he was sound asleep.’ cushion = a bag-like object filled with soft material that you put on a chair, sofa etc to make it more comfortable: ‘Would you like a cushion for your back?’
[wrong sentence]:  The pillows had been removed from the sofa and scattered on the floor.


R:NOUN place -> room
 [explain]:  place = an area or a particular part of an area: ‘The best place to sit is right in front of the stage.’ room (or space) = an empty part of something that can be used or filled: ‘There’s room in the back seat for all three of you.’
[wrong sentence]:  I hope there’s enough place in the wardrobe for all your clothes.


R:NOUN processes -> stag
 [explain]:  process = a series of actions or operations that are performed in order to make or do something: ‘The process begins with the gathering of the coffee beans.’ stage = one of the actions or operations in a process: ‘During the next stage in the process the beans are dried.’
[wrong sentence]:  The production of plastic paper clips has seven processes.


R:NOUN professor -> teacher
 [explain]:  In Britain a professor is the most senior teacher in a university: ‘My professor was the famous historian A.J.P. Taylor.’
[wrong sentence]:  He has applied for a job as a professor in a language school.


R:NOUN proofs -> proof
 [explain]:  Proof (= evidence) is usually an uncountable noun: ‘Investigators now have proof of his involvement in the arms deal.’
[wrong sentence]:  Today we have scientific proofs that tobacco is harmful.


R:NOUN propaganda -> advertise
 [explain]:  Propaganda is used in a political context: ‘Roadside hoardings displayed anti-Western propaganda.’ ‘In times of war the public are bornbarded with propaganda.’ Advertising is used in a commercial context: ‘Car manufacturers spend billions of dollars a year on advertising.’
[wrong sentence]:  The television company receives most of its money from propaganda.


R:NOUN publicity -> advertise
 [explain]:  If something is given publicity, there is an attempt to inform the public about it: ‘Scandals involving prominent politicians always receive widespread publicity.’ Advertising is the activity of trying to persuade people to buy something: ‘The big software companies spend millions each year on advertising.’
[wrong sentence]:  I think governments should ban the publicity of tobacco.


R:NOUN properties -> property
 [explain]:  Property (= things that belong to someone) is an uncountable noun: ‘Personal property should not be left unattended.’
[wrong sentence]:  The police eventually found most of the stolen properties.


R:NOUN qualification -> qualifications
 [explain]:  qualifications (plural) = all the examination passes, skills and experience that you need for a particular job: ‘I’d like to apply for the job but I don’t have the right qualifications.’ qualification = a degree, diploma, certificate etc: ‘Do you have a postgraduate qualification?’
[wrong sentence]:  I am taking another course to improve my qualification.


R:NOUN rainfall -> rain
 [explain]:  rainfall = the amount of rain that usually falls in an area during a certain period: ‘The whole region has a very low rainfall at this time of the year.’
[wrong sentence]:  There was no rainfall for over three months.


R:NOUN raise -> rise
 [explain]:  a raise = (American English) an increase in wages or salary: ‘The State Government simply can’t afford to give all teachers a raise.’ a rise = an increase in quantity, cost, price, salary etc: ‘For those on low incomes, another rise in the cost of living could be devastating.’
[wrong sentence]:  There has been an enormous raise in house prices.


R:NOUN regards -> regard
 [explain]:  with regard to (WITHOUT s): ‘With regard to the minutes of our last meeting, may I first draw your attention to Item 3.’ as regards (WITH s): ‘As regards transport, I would suggest that we hire a mini-bus for the days in question.’ See also REFERENCE, REGARDING
[wrong sentence]:  With regards to accommodation, there are several excellent hotels.


R:NOUN relation -> relationship
 [explain]:  See Language Note below
[wrong sentence]:  I thought that offering to help him might improve our relation.


R:NOUN researches -> research
 [explain]:  Research is usually an uncountable noun: ‘A team of American scientists is carrying out research into the effects of acid rain.’ Researches (plural) is also used, especially in formal styles of British English and usually refers to a series of related studies by a particular person or team: ‘His researches go back to the 1950s.’ ‘Their more recent researches point to a decline in the mortality rate.’ The more usual word for this meaning, especially in American English, is studies.
[wrong sentence]:  The government invests a lot of money in scientific researches.


R:NOUN respond -> response
 [explain]:  Respond is a verb: ‘I’m surprised they responded so quickly.’ Response is a noun: ‘I’m surprised we got such a quick response.’ ‘In response to your last comment, I’m afraid I don’t agree.’
[wrong sentence]:  I don’t care what they say, as long as I get a respond.


R:NOUN richness -> wealth
 [explain]:  If someone has a large amount of money, property etc, they are very wealthy or have considerable wealth (NOT richness): ‘The country’s wealth is in the hands of a small minority.’
[wrong sentence]:  They go to Hollywood in search of richness and fame.


R:NOUN roof -> ceiling
 [explain]:  roof = the covering on top of a building that protects it from the weather: ‘If the rain is coming in, there must be a hole in the roof.’ ‘All the rooves were covered in snow.’ ceiling = the part of a room that is above your head: ‘When he stands on tip-toe, he can almost touch the ceiling.’
[wrong sentence]:  In the bedroom, the roof looked very damp.
[wrong sentence]:  The bedroom roof was one huge mirror.


R:NOUN row -> queue
 [explain]:  row = a line of people or things: ‘Along one side of the river there was a row of cottages.’ ‘When our coach arrived at the hotel, the staff were standing in a row waiting to greet us.’ ‘At school I always used to sit in the front row.’ queue = (British English) a line of people standing one behind the other, waiting for a bus, to buy a ticket, etc: ‘I never go to the bank at lunchtime because of the long queues.’
[wrong sentence]:  There was already a long row of passengers waiting to be checked in.


R:NOUN manufacturers -> manufacturer’s
 [explain]:  When talking about something which is possessed by or connected with someone, use ‘s/s’: ‘Tim’s new car’, ‘one of Sandra’s friends’, ‘her parents’ bedroom’, ‘our children’s education’.
[wrong sentence]:  The shopkeeper said the fault was the manufacturers responsibility.


R:NOUN kitchen’s -> kitchen
 [explain]:  The possessive ‘s/s’ is used mainly with nouns that refer to people and animals. Compare: ‘All her father’s tools were missing.’ (NOT ‘tools of her father’) ‘The handles of the tools were all made of wood’ (NOT tools’ handles’) Note also that some nouns can be used like adjectives (WITHOUT ‘s/s’): ‘the kitchen floor’, ‘a garden shed’, ‘a pencil sharpener’.
[wrong sentence]:  The children were playing marbles on the kitchen’s floor.


R:NOUN chemist -> chemist’s
 [explain]:  The word for the shop ends in ‘s: ‘You can buy them at the greengrocer’s in the high street.’ ‘I’ll stop off at the butcher’s on the way home.’
[wrong sentence]:  These vitamin pills are always available at the chemist.


R:NOUN sack -> bag
 [explain]:  sack = a very large strong bag: ‘He’s hurt his back trying to lift a sack of potatoes.’ ; bag = a container made of cloth, paper, leather etc: ‘Somewhere in this shopping bag there’s a bag of sweets.’
[wrong sentence]:  When I took the two oranges out of the sack, I discovered that one of them was bad.


R:NOUN children -> children’s
 [explain]:  for ... –s’/-s’ sake: ‘For Wendy’s sake, we’ve postponed the wedding until the first week in April.
[wrong sentence]:  I came here for my children sake.


R:NOUN salary -> pay/wage
 [explain]:  A salary is the amount of money that someone earns for a year’s work, usually paid once a month directly into their bank account: ‘I’ll pay you back at the end of the month when I get my salary.’ ‘She’s on a salary of £23,000 a year.’
[wrong sentence]:  The salary is 800 yen an hour.
[wrong sentence]:  The basic salary is £60 per week.


R:NOUN satisfied -> satisfactory
 [explain]:  satisfied = (of a person) feeling pleased or content: ‘Despite the team’s convincing 3-0 victory, the manager wasn’t satisfied.’
[wrong sentence]:  The salary they offered was more than satisfied.


R:NOUN scene -> view
 [explain]:  scene = what you see when you are in a particular place, especially something that is unusual, shocking etc: ‘Some of the scenes inside the concentration camp were too horrific to describe.’ ‘The President arrived by helicopter to witness a scene of total chaos.’ view = the whole area that you can see from somewhere, especially when you can see a long way into the distance: ‘Remember to book a room with a view of the sea.’
[wrong sentence]:  From the window, there was a beautiful scene of the lake.


R:NOUN vision -> view
 [explain]:  vision = a picture in the mind of what the future will be like: ‘This romantic vision of a world ; without war is far removed from reality.’ view = the way you think about something that exists now: ‘This report sets out the views of our members very clearly.’ ‘At that time there was a widely-held view that fascism was not a threat to Europe.’
[wrong sentence]:  We’d like to know your personal vision of the situation.


R:NOUN seaweeds -> seaweed
 [explain]:  seaweed is an uncountable noun: ‘Can you really eat seaweed?’
[wrong sentence]:  The first diver reappeared covered in seaweeds.


R:NOUN selling -> sale
 [explain]:  To refer to the act of selling something, use the sale of: ‘The money that comes from the sale of these drugs is used to buy weapons.’
[wrong sentence]:  They make all their money from the selling of vegetables.


R:NOUN selling -> sales
 [explain]:  sales = the number of things that are sold by a company: ‘They’ve recruited a top advertising agency to help boost sales.’ ‘Last month’s sales figures are very pleasing.’
[wrong sentence]:  The first proposal would reduce the selling figures of the local shops.


R:NOUN sensibility -> sensitivity
 [explain]:  To refer to the ability to understand other people’s feelings and problems, use sensitivity: ‘To teach young children, you need lots of sensitivity and imagination.’
[wrong sentence]:  I felt terribly annoyed by his lack of sensibility.


R:NOUN shade -> shadow
 [explain]:  See Note at SHADOW
[wrong sentence]:  I thought I saw someone’s shade go past the window.


R:NOUN shadow -> shade
 [explain]:  shade = sheltered from the sun: ‘It’s too hot here. Let’s go and find some shade.’ ‘The branches provided plenty of shade.’ shadow = a dark shape that you see on a wall or surface when a light shines behind someone or something: ‘The setting sun cast long shadows down the beach.’
[wrong sentence]:  I sat down in the shadow of a huge eucalyptus tree.


R:NOUN sheeps -> sheep
 [explain]:  The plural form of sheep is the same as the singular form: ‘one sheep’, ‘two sheep’.
[wrong sentence]:  A lot of cows and sheeps died because of the polluted water.


R:NOUN smokes -> smoke
 [explain]:  Smoke is an uncountable noun: ‘The room was full of smoke.’
[wrong sentence]:  People eventually get ill from inhaling the smokes.


R:NOUN someone -> anyone
 [explain]:  See Language Note at NOT
[wrong sentence]:  Somehow he had to get on the train without someone seeing him.


 [explain]:  See Note at SOME 3
[wrong sentence]:  I ran over to the car to see if someone was injured.


R:NOUN something -> anything
 [explain]:  See Language Note at NOT
[wrong sentence]:  By six o’clock I am too tired to do something else.
[wrong sentence]:  At first we couldn’t see something suspicious.


R:NOUN spectators -> audience
 [explain]:  spectator = a person who goes to watch a sporting event such as a football match: ‘The new stadium can hold up to 60,000 spectators.’ ‘The police should stop spectators from running onto the pitch.’ audience = see Note at AUDIENCE
[wrong sentence]:  The spectators laughed at every joke.


R:NOUN sport -> sport
 [explain]:  Use sports in front of a noun (NOT sport): ‘a sports centre’, ‘a sports club’, ‘sports equipment’, ‘sports injuries’.
[wrong sentence]:  She bought a bright red sport car.
[wrong sentence]:  I need some new sport shoes.


R:NOUN squeezed -> squash
 [explain]:  squeeze = (1) press something firmly, especially by closing your hand round it: ‘She squeezed my arm and told me not to worry.’ (2) get liquid, cream, paste etc from something by pressing it firmly: ‘Squeeze the lemons and then pour the juice into a jug.’ (3) force someone or something into a small space: ‘Somehow we managed to squeeze everyone into the car.’ squash (or crush) = press something, usually with great force, so that it becomes flat or broken: ‘Mind you don’t squash the tomatoes.’
[wrong sentence]:  The train was so full that I was frightened of getting squeezed.


R:NOUN staffs -> staff
 [explain]:  The noun staff refers to a whole group of people, (NOT a single person): ‘(The) staff at the Central Hospital have rejected the new pay offer.’ ‘Several senior members of staff have opted for early retirement.’
[wrong sentence]:  The assembly and packing department has 50 staffs.


R:NOUN statistic -> statistics
 [explain]:  statistic (singular) refers to one piece of data: ‘This terrible crime will soon become nothing more than a statistic in police records.’ statistics (plural) refers to a set of data: ‘Statistics show that the population has almost doubled in the last twenty years.’
[wrong sentence]:  This view is supported by statistic published in 1985.


R:NOUN stimulant -> stimulus
 [explain]:  A stimulant is a drug, medicine etc, which makes the mind or body more active: ‘The caffeine in coffee acts as a stimulant.’ When you are not talking about a drug or medicine, use stimulus (= something which causes activity, growth, or greater effort): ‘The new textbook provided a good stimulus for both teachers and students.’
[wrong sentence]:  Political stability acts as a stimulant for foreign investment.


R:NOUN storey -> floor
 [explain]:  Storey is usually used to describe the structure of a building: ‘These office blocks are usually three or four storeys high.’ ‘a multi-storey car park’, ‘a detached two-storey house’. When you are talking about where someone lives/works/goes etc, use floor: ‘My flat is on the seventh floor.’ ‘We took the lift up to the third floor.’
[wrong sentence]:  They live on the second storey.


R:NOUN strangers -> foreigners
 [explain]:  stranger = a person you have never met before: ‘Although he was a total stranger, he started asking me for money!’ foreigner = a person from another country: ‘Foreigners need a visa to enter the country.’
[wrong sentence]:  There are a lot of strangers visiting England.


R:NOUN strongly -> tightly
 [explain]:  Strongly is used with verbs such as suggest, advise, recommend, agree/disagree, believe, feel etc (NOT with verbs that refer to actions): ‘I would strongly advise you to think again.’
[wrong sentence]:  When the plane took off, she held my hand strongly.


R:NOUN study -> study
 [explain]:  To refer to the work that a student does at a college or university, use studies: ‘After the war he resumed his studies at the University of Turin.’ Compare: ‘They are conducting a study of sex education in local secondary schools.’
[wrong sentence]:  Next July I’m going to the USA to continue my study.


R:NOUN living style -> lifestyle
 [explain]:  See Note at LIFE 2
[wrong sentence]:  I find this living style very attractive.


R:NOUN subconscious -> unconscious
 [explain]:  subconscious = (of a thought or desire) existing or occurring in the mind without the person being aware of it: ‘His dream about crossing the ocean single-handed probably arose from a subconscious desire for fame.’ unconscious = (of a person) in a sleep-like state, especially because you are ill or have been hit on the head: ‘The cleaner found him lying unconscious on the bathroom floor.’
[wrong sentence]:  The driver of the car was taken to hospital subconscious.


R:NOUN technique -> technology
 [explain]:  technique = a way of doing something, especially one that requires special training: ‘Thanks to these new surgical techniques, patients spend far shorter periods in hospital.’ ‘The purpose of these seminars is to keep our staff up to date with the latest teaching and testing techniques.’ technology = (the study of) the use of scientific theories and methods for practical purposes: ‘Can you imagine what the world would be like without science and technology?’ ‘Space research has produced major advances in computer technology.’
[wrong sentence]:  The main cause of unemployment is modern technique.


R:NOUN thoughts -> opinions
 [explain]:  thought = something that you (suddenly) think of, remember or realize: ‘Has the thought ever occurred to you that he might be guilty?’ ‘Does anyone have any thoughts about where we should eat?’ ; opinion/view =what you feel about something, especially after thinking about it for a long time: ‘Journalists are supposed to report the facts, not personal opinions.’ ‘If you want my opinion - I think he’s guilty.’
[wrong sentence]:  People’s attitudes and thoughts don’t change overnight.


R:NOUN thoughts -> beliefs
 [explain]:  belief = a strong feeling that something is true or untrue, good or bad etc: ‘In the old days people were persecuted for their religious beliefs.’ ‘The poet’s belief in life after death is not evident in these early poems.’
[wrong sentence]:  They are prepared to kill in order to defend their thoughts.


R:NOUN thunders -> thunder
 [explain]:  Thunder is an uncountable noun: ‘When Mr Cameron got angry , his voice was as loud as thunder.’
[wrong sentence]:  We waited for the thunders and lightning to stop.


R:NOUN timetable -> schedule
 [explain]:  Timetable = a list or plan showing the times when lessons take place or the times when buses, ; trains etc arrive and leave: ‘According to my timetable, history is on Mondays and Thursdays.’ ; schedule = a detailed plan of all the things that have to be done during a certain period: ‘It’s important that we all try and keep to the schedule.’ ‘The project is running 6 months behind schedule .’
[wrong sentence]:  The manager’s timetable next week is very busy.


R:NOUN titles -> headline
 [explain]:  title = the name of a book, play, painting, piece of music etc: ‘I can remember the title of the book but not the author.’ ; headline = the heading above a report in a newspaper, especially at the top of the front page: ‘Have you seen today’s headlines? There’s been another car bomb in London.’
[wrong sentence]:  The newspaper titles were all about the earthquake.


R:NOUN toasts -> toast
 [explain]:  Toast is an uncountable-noun: ‘There’s some toast for you in the kitchen.’ ‘How many pieces/slices of toast would you like?’
[wrong sentence]:  You make some toasts and I’ll set the table.


R:NOUN travel -> journey
 [explain]:  See Language Note on next page
[wrong sentence]:  The travel takes about half an hour.


R:NOUN travels -> journey
 [explain]:  See Language Note on next page
[wrong sentence]:  For long travels we use the train.


R:NOUN voyage -> journey
 [explain]:  See Language Note at TRIP
[wrong sentence]:  On the first day we’ll rest because the voyage will be tiring.


R:NOUN travels -> travel
 [explain]:  See Language Note on next page
[wrong sentence]:  He was exhausted from all the travels.


R:NOUN troubles -> trouble
 [explain]:  Trouble (= difficulties or problems) is an uncountable noun: ‘I hope you didn’t have any trouble getting here.’ ‘His back has been giving him a lot of trouble recently .’
[wrong sentence]:  I’m having some troubles with my supervisor.


R:NOUN trouble -> nuisance
 [explain]:  A person or thing that annoys you or gives you problems is a nuisance: ‘The post office closes early today, which is a bit of a nuisance.’ ‘A/an! Stop being a nuisance and find something to do!’
[wrong sentence]:  Sometimes my little brother is a real trouble.


R:NOUN type -> type
 [explain]:  See Note at KINO OF 1
[wrong sentence]:  The manager of the shop told me that he was not responsible for these type of fault.


R:NOUN vegetable -> vegetables
 [explain]:  Unlike fruit, vegetable is a countable noun: ‘A/an has never been keen on vegetables.’
[wrong sentence]:  I buy a lot of fresh fruit and vegetable.


R:NOUN view -> sight
 [explain]:  View refers to the whole area that you can see from somewhere, especially when you can see a long way into the distance: ‘His studio has a spectacular view over Sydney Harbour Bridge.’ ‘I’ve booked a room with a view of the sea.’
[wrong sentence]:  The view of all the blood on the ground made her feel ill.


R:NOUN vocabularies -> word
 [explain]:  vocabulary = all the words in a language or all the words (in a language) that someone knows: ‘English has a vast vocabulary.’ ‘By the end of the course students should have an active vocabulary of around 2000 words.’
[wrong sentence]:  The article contained a lot of American English vocabularies.


R:NOUN vote -> election
 [explain]:  vote = show which person or which course of action you prefer, especially by marking a piece of paper or raising your hand: ‘Who will you be voting for at the next election?’ ‘Fifty-three per cent of Danes voted in favour of the Maastricht treaty.’ elect = choose a leader or representative by voting: ‘Roman Catholic cardinals are meeting at the
[wrong sentence]:  On the day of the vote, they couldn’t be bothered to go and vote.


R:NOUN wage -> salary
 [explain]:  See Note at SALARY
[wrong sentence]:  He has an annual wage of $40,000.


R:NOUN wages -> salaries
 [explain]:  See Note at SALARY
[wrong sentence]:  The company directors receive very high wages.


R:NOUN wastes -> waste
 [explain]:  See Language Note at INFORMATION
[wrong sentence]:  The report deals with the problem of nuclear wastes.


R:NOUN worth -> value
 [explain]:  Worth is usually used as a preposition: ‘A four-bed roomed house in the middle of town is probably worth about £200,000.’
[wrong sentence]:  The ring has great sentimental worth.
[wrong sentence]:  They do not appreciate the worth of life.


R:NOUN wrapping -> package
 [explain]:  wrapping (also wrappings) = paper or paper-like material that is put round something: ‘I wanted to tear off the wrapping and see what was inside.’ packaging = the container or material that something is placed in by a manufacturer, especially to protect it or make it look attractive: ‘Packaging should be biodegradable and kept to a minimum.’ Note that both wrapping and packaging are used in connection with food: ‘Somewhere on the packaging/wrapping there should be a date stamp.’
[wrong sentence]:  Manufacturers should dispense with all unnecessary wrapping.


R:MORPH actions -> act
 [explain]:  Blackmail is the action of trying to obtain money from someone by threatening to expose them. Blackmail is the act of trying to obtain money from someone by threatening to expose them. an/the act of (doing) sth (NOT action): ‘Such acts of violence will not be tolerated.’ ‘For some people, the very act of talking to a doctor can help them to feel better.’
[wrong sentence]:  It is difficult to forgive those responsible for actions of terrorism.


R:MORPH alternate -> alternative
 [explain]:  In British English alternate and alternative have different meanings. alternate = (1) happening in turn, first one then the other: ‘alternate periods of sun and rain’ (2) every second (day, week, etc): ‘Our local football team plays at home on alternate Saturdays.’ alternative = that may be used instead of the usual one or the one you had planned to use: ‘In view of the roadworks, motorists are advised to use an alternative route.’
[wrong sentence]:  We decided to make alternate arrangements in case the hotel was fully booked.


R:MORPH alternatively -> alternately
 [explain]:  alternatively = another possibility is: ‘I thought we’d stay at home. Alternatively, you might like to go for a walk.’ ; alternately = in turns, first one then the other: ‘The play is alternately sad and happy.’ ; Note the more common alternative: ‘In Sweden many wives and husbands take it in turns to stay at home and look after their children.’
[wrong sentence]:  In Sweden many wives and husbands stay at home alternatively to look after their children.


R:MORPH arm -> arm
 [explain]:  armed = having one or more weapons: ‘Both hijackers are believed to be armed.’ the armed forces = (a country’s) army, navy, and air force
[wrong sentence]:  The plan to reduce the arm forces is strongly opposed.


R:MORPH artistic -> art
 [explain]:  artistic = (1) showing the skill or imagination of an artist: ‘Both my parents were highly imaginative and artistic.’ (2) connected with art or artists: ‘His paintings have little artistic merit.’ art = things produced by artists such as paintings and sculptures. This word is used as a noun and to modify another noun: ‘The portrait is a fine example of early Renaissance art.’ ‘She owns one of the finest art collections in Europe.’
[wrong sentence]:  The country’s artistic treasures should be left where they are.
[wrong sentence]:  Students coming from artistic schools can join the Academy of Arts.


R:MORPH base -> basis
 [explain]:  base = (1) the main part of something: ‘Most of the paints we produce have an oil base.’ (2) the lowest part of something: ‘The base of the column is made of marble.’ basis = the very important thing from which something else develops or is made possible: ‘The basis of a successful marriage is mutual respect.’ ‘Newton’s discoveries provided the basis for future research.’
[wrong sentence]:  Political stability provides the base for economic development.


R:MORPH basis -> basic
 [explain]:  Basis is a noun. See Note at BASE 1
[wrong sentence]:  The course is for students with a basis knowledge of English.


R:MORPH captured -> captivate
 [explain]:  capture = make someone a prisoner: ‘That day they captured twenty enemy soldiers.’ ‘The leader of the resistance group was captured and executed.’ captivate = strongly attract and impress someone: ‘From the day she met him, she was captivated by his charm.’
[wrong sentence]:  Her blue eyes and long blond hair captured him.


R:MORPH character -> characteristic
 [explain]:  When you mean ‘a feature of someone’s character’, use characteristic or quality: ‘All great leaders share certain mean characteristics.’ ‘What qualities do you need to be a good parent?’ In contrast with qualities that you do not like, you can also talk about someone’s good points.
[wrong sentence]:  She has that rare character - the ability to listen to people.


R:MORPH classic -> classical
 [explain]:  classic = being among the best or most typical of its class; serving as a standard or model: ‘The painting is a classic example of sixteenth" century Venetian art.’ classical music = the music of Mozart, Beethoven, etc love the sound of a classic guitar.
[wrong sentence]:  I prefer classic music to pop.


R:MORPH colour -> colour
 [explain]:  colour = showing people and things in their natural colours: ‘a colour television’, ‘a colour photograph’ coloured = having one or more colours (not white or black), especially in order to look attractive: ‘Do you want plain envelopes or coloured ones?’ ‘Each book is full of brightly-coloured full-page illustrations.’
[wrong sentence]:  At Hari Raya we hang colour lights around the house.


R:MORPH coloured -> colour
 [explain]:  See Note at COLOUR 5
[wrong sentence]:  He showed me the coloured photographs he had taken.


R:MORPH defect -> defective
 [explain]:  Defect is a noun: ‘The test flight revealed a number of small defects in the navigation system.’
[wrong sentence]:  Any products that are found to be defect will be replaced.


R:MORPH delightful -> delight
 [explain]:  delightful = (fairly formal) giving great pleasure: ‘Thank you for such a delightful evening. George and I thoroughly enjoyed ourselves.’ delighted = extremely pleased: ‘I’m delighted to hear that you are feeling better.’
[wrong sentence]:  I know that Dad will be delightful if you can come.


R:MORPH desert -> desert
 [explain]:  desert = a large area of land, where there is usually nothing but sand: ‘the Sahara Desert’ deserted = empty and quiet, especially because the people who are usually there have all left: ‘I came back to find the house dark and deserted.’ ‘At night, the city streets are deserted.’
[wrong sentence]:  I was miles from anywhere, stuck on a desert country road.


R:MORPH economical -> economic
 [explain]:  The economical crisis was caused by a sudden increase in the size of the population. The economic crisis was caused by a sudden increase in the size of the population. See Language Note at ECONOMIC
[wrong sentence]:  The undeveloped countries need economical support.


R:MORPH economics -> economic
 [explain]:  See Language Note at ECONOMIC
[wrong sentence]:  My country has serious economics problems.


R:MORPH elderly -> elder
 [explain]:  elderly = old: ‘an elderly man with white hair and a stick’ elder = (of brothers, daughters etc) older: ‘our elder daughter’
[wrong sentence]:  John, her elderly son, is still at university.


R:MORPH electric -> electrical
 [explain]:  See Language Note above
[wrong sentence]:  My father’s company imports electric goods.


R:MORPH emphasis -> emphasize
 [explain]:  Emphasis is a noun: ‘Modern society places emphasis on achievement.’ The verb is emphasize: ‘Modern society emphasizes achievement.’
[wrong sentence]:  Both philosophies emphasis the individual.


R:MORPH exhausting -> exhaustive
 [explain]:  exhausting = causing tiredness: ‘Pushing the car uphill was exhausting.’ exhaustive = so thorough or complete that not even the smallest detail or possibility is missed: ‘Following exhaustive inquiries, the police are at last able to issue a description of the suspects.’
[wrong sentence]:  An exhausting investigation finally revealed the cause of the accident.


R:MORPH fool -> foolish
 [explain]:  Fool is a countable noun and refers to a person: ‘She thinks I’m a fool for lending him the money.’ The adjective is foolish: ‘You were right. It was foolish of me to lend him the money.’ ‘This foolish idea could ruin everything.’
[wrong sentence]:  I was fool to believe him.


R:MORPH foreigner -> foreign
 [explain]:  Foreigner is a noun and refers to a person: ‘As a foreigner who had only just arrived in England, I found the phrase ‘car boot sale’ very confusing.’ The adjective is foreign: ‘I enjoy visiting foreign countries.’ ‘His wife teaches English as a foreign language.’
[wrong sentence]:  In my job I have to speak foreigner languages.
[wrong sentence]:  Some foreigner students feel homesick.


R:MORPH golden -> gold
 [explain]:  Golden is used in idiomatic expressions such as ‘a golden opportunity’, ‘a golden handshake’, ‘a golden wedding anniversary’, and in the phrase ‘golden hair’ (used mainly in literary styles). To describe something that is made of gold or is the colour of gold, use gold: ‘a gold bracelet’, ‘a black dress with red and gold stripes down the front’.
[wrong sentence]:  It’s a navy blue coat with golden buttons.
[wrong sentence]:  The purse contained six golden coins.


R:MORPH hardly -> hard
 [explain]:  hardly = scarcely; almost not: ‘It was hardly raining at all.’ ‘I could hardly believe my eyes.’ ; hard = (1) with a lot of effort: ‘If you work hard, you’re bound to pass.’ (2) heavily or severely: ‘The authorities are coming down hard on tax evasion.’ Before a past participle, use severely for this meaning: ‘If they are caught, they will be severely punished.’
[wrong sentence]:  I tried hardly to remember where I had parked the car.
[wrong sentence]:  It was raining hardly and we all got wet.


R:MORPH historic -> historical
 [explain]:  historic = (1) very important in history: ‘a historic voyage’, ‘a historic decision’ (2) having a long history: ‘a historic tradition’, ‘a historic building’ historical = (1) about or based upon people who actually lived or events that actually happened: ‘Historical records suggest that the prince was fully aware of the murders.’ (2) connected with or found in history: ‘a diary of great historical interest’, ‘a historical document’, ‘for historical reasons’.
[wrong sentence]:  She likes reading historic novels.


R:MORPH historic -> history
 [explain]:  a history book/lesson/museum etc = a book/lesson/museum etc that deals with the subject of history: ‘Our history teacher next term is Mr Young.’
[wrong sentence]:  We spent the afternoon at the historic museum.
[wrong sentence]:  He likes reading historic books.


R:MORPH historical -> historic
 [explain]:  See Note at HISTORIC 1
[wrong sentence]:  We shall be taking you to see several interesting historical places.


R:MORPH imaginary -> imaginative
 [explain]:  imaginary = not existing in real life but only in someone’s mind: ‘This new novel takes the reader on an imaginary journey into space.’ ‘The little boy kept firing an imaginary gun at me.’ imaginative = having or showing a powerful imagination: ‘She is one of the most imaginative designers of her generation.’ ‘His paintings tend to be highly imaginative.’
[wrong sentence]:  People tend to become less imaginary as they grow older.


R:MORPH kindly -> kind
 [explain]:  Kindly is nearly always used as an adverb: They’ve kindly offered to lend me their car while they’re away.’ ‘Would you kindly sit down and stop being a nuisance.’
[wrong sentence]:  She is always very kindly and helpful.
[wrong sentence]:  You are very kindly to spare me so much of your time.


R:MORPH lit -> light
 [explain]:  See Note at LIGHTED
[wrong sentence]:  All I could see in the darkness was the end of his lit cigarette.


R:MORPH luxurious -> luxury
 [explain]:  To refer to a type or class of hotel, apartment, furniture, services etc, use luxury as a modifier: ‘a luxury apartment in the heart of London’, ‘luxury bedroom suites imported direct from Italy’, ‘a luxury liner’. Use luxurious to describe something that is very comfortable and expensive: ‘The car’s soft cream leather interior was extremely luxurious.’
[wrong sentence]:  Consumers are offered more and more luxurious goods.


R:MORPH material -> materialistic
 [explain]:  Material = connected with the physical needs that people have (as opposed to spiritual or intellectual needs): ‘In material terms they are very well off, but spiritually they are deprived.’ materialistic = believing that money and the things that money can buy are more important than anything else: ‘Modern society is becoming increasingly materialistic.’
[wrong sentence]:  In today’s material society, most people think only about money.


R:MORPH matured -> mature
 [explain]:  Use matured/mature when you are talking about wine, cheese etc: ‘The whisky is kept here until it has matured/is mature.’ When you are talking about people, the usual word is mature: ‘He’s very mature for a boy of sixteen.’
[wrong sentence]:  He isn’t matured enough to get married.


R:MORPH measurements -> measure
 [explain]:  measurement = the length, width etc of something: ‘You can’t buy new curtains without knowing the window measurements.’; measure = an action intended to have a particular effect; a law or ruling: ‘New measures are to be introduced in the fight against crime.’
[wrong sentence]:  In my opinion, the authorities have to take even stricter measurements to save our archaeological treasures.


R:MORPH negligent -> negligible
 [explain]:  negligent = failing to take proper care, especially in your job; careless: ‘The court decided that the pilot of the crashed aircraft had been negligent.’ negligible = (of an amount) so small that it has no effect and can be ignored: ‘The cost of maintaining the machine is negligible.’
[wrong sentence]:  There was a negligent amount of liquid in the test tube.


R:MORPH notice -> note
 [explain]:  notice = a short written statement giving information or directions, usually found in a public place: ‘There was a notice on the wall saying "Private property. No parking." note = a short informal letter or written message from one person to another: ‘Just a quick note to say that Helen had a baby boy yesterday.’
[wrong sentence]:  I wrote him a notice saying that the package had arrived.


R:MORPH object -> objective
 [explain]:  object = the purpose of an action or event: ‘The object of the game is to score as many points as possible.’ ‘Nobody knows the real object of their visit. They’re keeping it a secret.’ Objective= the thing that you are working towards and hope to achieve by the end of a course of action: ‘The company’s long-term objective is to increase sales overseas.’ ‘The course description began with a long list of aims and objectives.’
[wrong sentence]:  My object is to improve my English as much as possible.


R:MORPH officer -> official
 [explain]:  official = a person with a position of authority in an organization: ‘WHO officials are monitoring the spread of the disease.’
[wrong sentence]:  A British Airways officer told us that there was a delay.


R:MORPH outdoors -> outdoor
 [explain]:  See Note at OUTDOOR
[wrong sentence]:  There are lots of outdoors activities in and around Coimbra.


R:MORPH pity -> pitiful
 [explain]:  pity (noun) = a feeling that you have when you feel sad and sorry for someone because of their situation: ‘They don’t want out pity. They need our help.’ pitiful (adjective) = causing people to feel sad and sorry: ‘The horses were in a pitiful condition, thin and covered with sores.’
[wrong sentence]:  When I saw these pity children, my eyes filled with tears.


R:MORPH pleasant -> please
 [explain]:  pleasant = (of a person) polite and friendly: ‘I’ve always found Bob very pleasant to work with.’ pleased = happy, satisfied: ‘I was very pleased to hear that you’re feeling better.’ ‘She’s pleased that the exams are over.’
[wrong sentence]:  We would be very pleasant if you could attend.


R:MORPH reasonable -> reasonably
 [explain]:  When you mean ‘fairly/quite’, use reasonably (adverb): ‘The team played reasonably well on
[wrong sentence]:  She usually gets reasonable good marks.


R:MORPH relation -> relationship
 [explain]:  See Language Note below
[wrong sentence]:  The relation between teachers and students is very friendly.


R:MORPH respectful -> respectable
 [explain]:  respectful = having or showing respect for someone, especially someone older than you or senior to you: ‘The principal has warned him that unless he shows a more respectful attitude towards his teachers, he will have to leave.’ respectable = displaying socially acceptable standards in appearance or behaviour: ‘Surprisingly, many of these young offenders come from respectable families.’ ‘Nobody is going to give you a job unless you make yourself look a bit more respectable.’
[wrong sentence]:  Some former prisoners are now respectful citizens.


R:MORPH safety -> safe
 [explain]:  Safety is a noun (NOT an adjective/adverb): ‘The boy has been missing for six days, and there are fears for his safety.’ ‘The firefighters rescued the children and carried them to safety.’
[wrong sentence]:  She doesn’t feel safety when she is on her own.


R:MORPH scientific -> science
 [explain]:  Scientific is mainly used to describe something that is done or produced by scientists: ‘We still don’t have a scientific explanation for these mysterious events.’ ‘Many scientific research projects are funded by the private sector.’ When you mean ‘used for, devoted to, based on or specializing in science’, use science + noun: ‘a new science laboratory’, ‘a science lesson’, ‘science fiction’.
[wrong sentence]:  I’m studying for a scientific degree.
[wrong sentence]:  The Scientific Centre is next to the History Museum.
[wrong sentence]:  He’s good at scientific subjects.


R:MORPH signature -> sign
 [explain]:  signature: your name, written in the way that you usually write it on a cheque, business letter etc: ‘These letters need your signature, Mr Ross.’ ‘You can tell by the signature if the painting is genuine.’
[wrong sentence]:  The signature of the new protocol took place in Rio de Janeiro.


R:MORPH social -> socialist
 [explain]:  social = connected with (life in a) society: ‘Government attempts to tackle two of today’s most serious social problems - crime and unemployment - have had no effect.’ Socialist = connected with socialism (the political system that favours equality of opportunity and public as opposed to private ownership): ‘Why is it that so many of these so-called socialists send their children to fee-paying schools?’
[wrong sentence]:  The next election was won by the Social Party.


R:MORPH suitable -> suit
 [explain]:  suitable (for) = right or appropriate for a particular purpose: ‘The film isn’t really suitable for children.’ ‘We’d like to give her the job but her qualifications aren’t suitable.’ suited to = having the qualifications, experience, personality etc that make you suitable for a particular job or situation: ‘Her interest in poetry makes her better suited to a literature course.’
[wrong sentence]:  I didn’t feel suitable to a career in medicine.


R:MORPH technique -> technical
 [explain]:  technical = (1) involving or requiring detailed knowledge, especially of an industrial or scientific subject: ‘The flight was cancelled because of a serious technical problem.’ (2) requiring special knowledge to be understood: ‘Engineering students need a lot of technical
[wrong sentence]:  The job requires proper technique training.
[wrong sentence]:  He used a lot of technique language that I didn’t understand.


R:MORPH thank -> thank
 [explain]:  thanks to sb/sth (WITH-s) : Thanks to parents’ generosity, the school will soon have its own swimming-pool.’
[wrong sentence]:  Thank to the microwave, we always have a hot breakfast.


R:MORPH touristic -> tourist
 [explain]:  Touristic and touristy are used, especially in informal styles, to describe a place that is unpleasantly full of things for tourists to see and do: ‘He refuses to go there any more - it’s become too touristy.’ When you simply mean ‘visited or preferred by tourists’, use tourist + noun: ‘London is full of tourist attractions.’ ‘During the tourist season the hotels are packed.’
[wrong sentence]:  It is one of the most popular touristic resorts in Spain.


R:MORPH voluntaries -> volunteer
 [explain]:  Voluntary is an adjective (NOT a noun). It describes someone who agrees to work without being paid, or work that is not paid: ‘On Saturdays she does voluntary work at an old people’s home.’
[wrong sentence]:  In my opinion, these young voluntaries should be congratulated.


R:MORPH wood -> wooden
 [explain]:  The adjective meaning ‘made of wood’ is usually wooden (NOT wood): ‘Stir the mixture gently with a wooden spoon.’
[wrong sentence]:  The coat has a leather belt and three brown wood buttons.


R:SPELL adviced -> advise
 [explain]:  Advice is a noun. Advise is a verb.
[wrong sentence]:  I adviced him to tell the police.


M:NOUN NONE -> people
 [explain]:  advise sb to do sth: ‘I advised her to see a lawyer.’ advise doing sth (NOT to do): ‘I would advise leaving very early. Then you’ll miss all the traffic.’
[wrong sentence]:  Nowadays many doctors advise to live in the countryside.


M:NOUN NONE -> number
 [explain]:  an/the average of + NUMBER: ‘The average of 3,4 and 8 is 5.’ ‘The test results produced an average of 65 per cent.’ the average number/amount/level/age of + NOUN: ‘The average age of the children is five years and seven months.’ an/the average + NOUN: ‘The average salary is $2100 a month.’
[wrong sentence]:  The average of hours spent watching television has increased.


M:NOUN NONE -> minutes
 [explain]:  When telling the time, minutes must be used after all numbers except five, ten, twenty, and twenty-five.
[wrong sentence]:  The train arrived at exactly twelve past three.


M:NOUN NONE -> bar
 [explain]:  snack = a light meal or something that you eat between meals: ‘Instead of going out to lunch, I usually have a quick snack in my office.’ snack bar = a cafe or similar place where you can buy a light meal: ‘The snack bar gets very busy at lunchtime.’
[wrong sentence]:  She usually has lunch at a snack in Barceloneta Square.


M:DET NONE -> the
 [explain]:  See Language Note at TIME
[wrong sentence]:  School finishes at five in afternoon.


 [explain]:  the British Council: ‘Professor Sinclair’s visit was organized by the British Council.’
[wrong sentence]:  I am learning English at British Council.


 [explain]:  go to the cinema (WITH the): ‘Before the baby was born, we used to go to the cinema about once a week.’ See also SCHOOL 1

 [explain]:  care for = (1) (formal) like: ‘Would you care for another drink?’ (2) = (usually adjectival or passive) look after: ‘Don’t worry. [wrong sentence]:  They don’t take care about religion.


U:VERB to -> NONE
 [explain]:  control sth (verb) WITHOUT of/over: ‘It’s usually the editor who controls what goes into a newspaper.’ The security forces are no longer able to control the situation.’ control of/over sth (noun): ‘It’s usually the editor who has control over what goes Into a newspaper.’ ‘The security forces have lost control of the situation.’

 [explain]:  daren’t do sth (WITHOUT to): ‘I daren’t tell George what happened or he’ll be furious.’

 [explain]:  do (anything/nothing/what you like etc) + except + bare infinitive: ‘There was nothing I could do except sit and wait.’
[wrong sentence]:  She was willing to do anything except to tell me her name.


 [explain]:  When there is a change of subject after hope, use hope + that clause (NOT hope + to-v): ‘She hopes (that) you’ll come again.’ We hope (that) your parents enjoyed their stay.’ When there is no change of subject, use hope + to -v (or hope + that clause): ‘She hopes to come again.’ ‘she hopes that she will come again).’ (=she hopes that she will come again) ‘They hope to visit the Istana.’ (=they hope (that) they will visit the Istana)
[wrong sentence]:  I hope you to have a good time at the party.


 [explain]:  make sb/sth do sth (WITHOUT to): ‘They made him take the examination again.’
[wrong sentence]:  What made you to decide to work in the theatre?


U:VERB is -> NONE
 [explain]:  Do not confuse depend on/upon and the more formal phrase be dependent on/upon. Compare: ‘The speed of a car depends on the size of the engine.’ ‘The speed of a car is dependent on the size of the engine.’
[wrong sentence]:  The insurance payment is depends on the value of the goods.


 [explain]:  In a subordinate clause, the subject and verb do NOT change places. ; Compare: ‘How much did she pay for it?’ ‘Do you know how much she paid for it?’
[wrong sentence]:  Please let me know how much is the postage.


U:VERB was -> NONE
 [explain]:  The verb end is usually intransitive: ‘The war ended in 1975.’ ‘When does the next programme end?’ ‘Just as the film was ending, the baby woke up.’ When end is transitive, it means ‘finish or stop something’: ‘I couldn’t decide how to end the letter.’ ‘To end the meal we had some coffee and an ice-cream.’
[wrong sentence]:  The film was ended at eight-thirty.

[wrong sentence]:  I’ll wait here until you will return.

 [explain]:  When the object of the sentence is a wh- clause, the subject and the verb in the wh- clause do not change places. Compare: ‘How old are you?’ ‘She asked me how old I was:
[wrong sentence]:  They asked him what would he like to do.


R:WO below report -> report below
 [explain]:  When below refers to the position of something on a page, it comes after the noun: ‘Last month’s sales figures are set out in the table below.’
[wrong sentence]:  The below report describes my recent stay at the Hotel Grove.


R:WO both are -> are both
 [explain]:  Two of the biggest firms are Apple and IBM, which both are in the computer business. Two of the biggest firms are Apple and IBM, which are both in the computer business. See Language Note at ALWAYS
[wrong sentence]:  Anne and John both are scientists.


R:WO certainly i -> i certainly
 [explain]:  Certainly is usually used like a middle position adverb (see Language Note at ALWAYS): ‘She certainly likes you.’ ‘His work has certainly improved this year.’ For extra emphasis, however, certainly may be placed before the first auxiliary verb and before be when this is the main verb: ‘His work certainly has improved this year.’ ‘She certainly is one of the best teachers on the staff.’
[wrong sentence]:  Certainly I think so.


R:WO have each -> each have
 [explain]:  See Language Note at ALWAYS
[wrong sentence]:  They have each their own problems.


R:WO i shall either -> either i shall
 [explain]:  The position of either should be the same as the position of or (i.e. immediately before a subject, immediately before a main verb, immediately after a verb, etc): ‘Either stay or go.’ ‘You should either stay or go.’ ‘You should stay either here or at home.’ ‘You should stay with either me or your uncle.’ Note that this rule applies mainly in formal styles. In everyday conversation, either often goes immediately before the main verb: ‘We can either go by bus or by car.’ In fact, a motorway wouldn’t either disturb the animals because they are used to cars. In fact, a motorway wouldn’t disturb the animals either because they are used to cars. When either is used after no/never etc, it goes at the end of the clause: ‘John isn’t going to the party, and Ray doesn’t want to go either.’
[wrong sentence]:  I shall either go home to Brazil or my family will come to England.


R:WO either can go -> can go either
 [explain]:  The position of either should be the same as the position of or (i.e. immediately before a subject, immediately before a main verb, immediately after a verb, etc): ‘Either stay or go.’ ‘You should either stay or go.’ ‘You should stay either here or at home.’ ‘You should stay with either me or your uncle.’ Note that this rule applies mainly in formal styles. In everyday conversation, either often goes immediately before the main verb: ‘We can either go by bus or by car.’ In fact, a motorway wouldn’t either disturb the animals because they are used to cars. In fact, a motorway wouldn’t disturb the animals either because they are used to cars. When either is used after no/never etc, it goes at the end of the clause: ‘John isn’t going to the party, and Ray doesn’t want to go either.’
[wrong sentence]:  We either can go by bus or by car.


R:WO especially the bavarians -> the bavarians especially
 [explain]:  Especially cannot come at the beginning of a sentence. Put it after the subject (NOT in front of the subject): ‘This year’s examination results are surprisingly good. The girls especially have done better than we expected.’
[wrong sentence]:  In the south of Germany there is a different mentality. Especially the Bavarians are very lively and cheerful.


R:WO especially young children -> young children especially
 [explain]:  Especially cannot come at the beginning of a sentence. Put it after the subject (NOT in front of the subject): ‘This year’s examination results are surprisingly good. The girls especially have done better than we expected.’
[wrong sentence]:  Looking after children can be very tiring. Especially young children need a lot of attention.


R:WO even might -> might even
 [explain]:  See Language Note at ALWAYS
[wrong sentence]:  In the future it even might be possible to work only thirty-five hours a week.


R:WO ever has -> has ever
 [explain]:  See Language Note at ALWAYS
[wrong sentence]:  I’m surprised that nobody ever has thought of it.


R:WO happened something -> something happened
 [explain]:  The subject of the sentence comes in front of happen (NOT after it): ‘Something unexpected has happened.’ ‘Something has happened that I think you ought to know about.’
[wrong sentence]:  Then happened something that made me very angry.


R:WO hardly could -> could hardly
 [explain]:  See Language Note at ALWAYS
[wrong sentence]:  It was so dark that we hardly could see.


R:WO i was pleased -> pleased i was
 [explain]:  how + adjective/adverb + subject + verb: ‘I can’t describe how sad I felt.’ ‘How clumsy you are!’ ‘I was amazed at how fast she was driving.’
[wrong sentence]:  I must tell you how I was pleased to receive a letter from you.


R:WO i am lucky -> lucky i am
 [explain]:  how + adjective/adverb + subject + verb: ‘I can’t describe how sad I felt.’ ‘How clumsy you are!’ ‘I was amazed at how fast she was driving.’
[wrong sentence]:  I keep telling myself how I am lucky to have such wonderful children.


R:WO began immediately -> immediately began
 [explain]:  See Language Note at ALWAYS
[wrong sentence]:  At the airport he began immediately taking photographs.


R:WO just have -> have just
 [explain]:  See Language Note at ALWAYS
[wrong sentence]:  In the evening you should practise what you just have learned during the day.


R:WO mainly these problems -> these problems mainly
 [explain]:  See Language Note at ALWAYS
[wrong sentence]:  Mainly these problems involve the definition of words.


R:WO neither studied -> studied neither
 [explain]:  Neither should be placed immediately before the first of the connected items and nor immediately before the second. Compare: ‘I have neither studied nor experienced the culture.’
[wrong sentence]:  I have neither studied the language nor the culture.


R:WO to never -> never to
 [explain]:  Never and not usually go immediately in front of a to infinitive: ‘He’s promised never to do it again.’
[wrong sentence]:  I asked him to never arrive late.


R:WO never can -> can never
 [explain]:  See Language Note at ALWAYS
[wrong sentence]:  You never can get really good beef in our local supermarket.


R:WO i had -> had i
 [explain]:  See Language Note at NOT
[wrong sentence]:  Never I had seen such an ugly face.


R:WO to not -> not to
 [explain]:  Not and never usually go immediately in front of a to infinitive: Try not to worry about anything.’ ‘It’s hard not to feel sorry for him.’
[wrong sentence]:  He told me to not spend too long in the sun.


R:WO often people -> people often
 [explain]:  See Language Note at ALWAYS
[wrong sentence]:  Often people stop smoking when they are ill.


R:WO often has -> has often
 [explain]:  See Language Note at ALWAYS
[wrong sentence]:  He often has said that he would like to be young again.


R:WO often are -> are often
 [explain]:  See Language Note at ALWAYS
[wrong sentence]:  The trains often are late.


R:WO had once -> once had
 [explain]:  See Language Note at ALWAYS
[wrong sentence]:  You may remember we had once a long talk in the hotel bar.


R:WO only be reduced -> be reduced only
 [explain]:  To avoid confusion in written English, only is usually placed as near as possible to the word or phrase that it modifies. Compare: ‘Alison only posted the letter to Mr Jones.’ (= she didn’t write it) ‘Alison posted only the letter to Mr Jones.’ (= she didn’t post the other letters) In spoken English the position of only is less important because the speaker uses stress to make the meaning clear.
[wrong sentence]:  The level of pollution can only be reduced by the introduction of new laws.


R:WO you only -> only you
 [explain]:  When if only is used to express a wish, the two words usually stay together: ‘If only I could stop smoking.’ Sometimes, only is placed in front of the main verb ‘If I could only stop smoking.’
[wrong sentence]:  If you only would stay longer, your English would improve.


R:WO only would -> would only
 [explain]:  When if only is used to express a wish, the two words usually stay together: ‘If only I could stop smoking.’ Sometimes, only is placed in front of the main verb ‘If I could only stop smoking.’
[wrong sentence]:  If you only would stay longer, your English would improve.


R:WO opposite woman -> woman opposite
 [explain]:  When opposite means ‘facing the speaker or the person/place being talked about’, it comes immediately after the noun: ‘The house opposite is also for sale.’
[wrong sentence]:  The opposite woman was knitting a cardigan.


R:WO a quite -> quite a
 [explain]:  When quite means ‘rather/fairly’, it comes in front of a/an: ‘The survey covered quite a wide range of newspapers.’ ‘On the whole, it was quite a good essay.’
[wrong sentence]:  In the circumstances, it was a quite rude answer.


R:WO really a -> a really
 [explain]:  The position of really (= very) is a matter of emphasis. Very often it comes immediately before the adjective or adverb it modifies: ‘some really good news’, ‘a really old car’. Otherwise, it is usually placed immediately in front of the main verb: ‘You really fooled me.’ ‘We’re really enjoying ourselves.’
[wrong sentence]:  It was only a short trip but we had really a good time.
[wrong sentence]:  That’s really a good idea.


R:WO had really -> really had
 [explain]:  The position of really (= very) is a matter of emphasis. Very often it comes immediately before the adjective or adverb it modifies: ‘some really good news’, ‘a really old car’. [wrong sentence]:  It was only a short trip but we had really a good time.


R:WO feel still -> still feel
 [explain]:  See Language Note at ALWAYS

R:WO still is -> is still
 [explain]:  See Language Note at ALWAYS
[wrong sentence]:  My country still is not very rich, but at least everyone has enough food to eat.


R:WO would you -> you would
 [explain]:  When a wh- clause is part of a sentence (e.g. the subject or the object), the subject and verb in the wh- clause do not change places. Compare: ‘Why did she leave so soon?’ ‘Do you know why she left so soon?’
[wrong sentence]:  Please tell me what would you like for a wedding present.


R:WO is she -> she is
 [explain]:  See Note at WHAT 3
[wrong sentence]:  I don’t know when is she coming.


M:PRON NONE -> it
 [explain]:  Do not use all to replace a pronoun. Use all (or all of) with a pronoun: ‘She took six exams and passed them all.’ ‘She took six exams and passed all of them.’
[wrong sentence]:  I didn’t like the meal and couldn’t eat all.


 [explain]:  appreciate + it (+ if/when clause): ‘I’m sure she’d appreciate it if you could give her a hand.’ See also GRANTED
[wrong sentence]:  I would greatly appreciate if you could send me Ray’s address.


 [explain]:  See IT 1
[wrong sentence]:  Nowadays is very difficult to get a job.


 [explain]:  can’t bear + it (+ if/when clause): ‘I can’t bear it when people start complaining about nothing.’

[wrong sentence]:  I can’t bear any longer,’ he said. ‘I’m ready to resign.’


 [explain]:  blame sb (for sth): ‘Nobody can blame you for telling the truth.’ blame sth on sb/sth: ‘Whenever children behave badly, people blame it on the teachers.’
[wrong sentence]:  I wasn’t even there so you can’t blame on me.


 [explain]:  make it clear + that-clause (WITH it): ‘She made it quite clear (to him) that she wasn’t interested in getting married.’
[wrong sentence]:  I should like to make clear that the accommodation is far from luxurious.


 [explain]:  find + it + easy/difficult/impossible to do sth: ‘I find it hard to concentrate when I’m hungry.’ ‘You might find it interesting to spend a few hours at the museum.’
[wrong sentence]:  Nurses find very difficult to start a family while they are working.


 [explain]:  take it for granted (that ...) WITH it = assume that something is true or will happen: ‘She didn’t ask me if I wanted to go. She just took it for granted.’
[wrong sentence]:  We cannot take for granted that a computer system will never fail.


 [explain]:  like + sb/sth + doing/to do sth (NOT that clause): ‘I don’t like the children staying/to stay out late.’ like + it + when/if clause: ‘I don’t like it when the children stay out late.’
[wrong sentence]:  I don’t like when friends of mine get married.


M:PRON NONE -> you
 [explain]:  assure sb that: ‘I assure you that we are doing everything we can to avoid further delays.’

M:PRON NONE -> them
 [explain]:  tell sb sth (WITH indirect object): ‘Jim told me that you’ve been offered a new job.’ say sth (WITHOUT indirect object): ‘Jim said that you’ve been offered a new job.’ John said that he was trying to get to London. ‘That’s where I’m going,’ told the man. U:CONJ and -> NONE
 [explain]:  Do not use and between two adjectives that come before a noun unless they describe similar qualities, e.g. ‘a red and green umbrella’ (two colours), ‘a gold and silver bracelet’ (two materials), ‘a hunting and fishing knife’ (two functions).
[wrong sentence]:  There is a beautiful and old church in the centre of the town.


 [explain]:  And is not used with etc: ‘On the first floor they sell washing machines, dishwashers, spin driers, etc.’
[wrong sentence]:  They make televisions, radios, and etc.


 [explain]:  many + adjective + noun (WITHOUT and): ‘He is convinced that many serious accidents could be prevented.’
[wrong sentence]:  There are not many and interesting Sunday newspapers.
[wrong sentence]:  I had never seen so many and bright stars in the sky.


 [explain]:  The room where I do my work is nice and quiet. Two-part adjectival phrases with nice such as ‘nice and quiet and ‘nice and clean’ are always placed after the noun they modify: The house is always nice and tidy.’ Do not use and after nice when it comes in front of a noun: ‘Right now I could do with a nice cold drink.’
[wrong sentence]:  There is a nice and quiet room where I do my work.


U:CONJ but -> NONE
 [explain]:  If the first clause begins with although or (even) though, do not begin the second clause with but or yet.
[wrong sentence]:  Although I enjoyed my stay in the USA, but I was still glad to come home.


 [explain]:  See Note at BUT
[wrong sentence]:  Even though I paid her for the room, but I knew she would prefer to live alone.


M:VERB NONE -> be

M:VERB NONE -> drop
 [explain]:  People drop/leave litter (= leave things such as empty packets, cans and bottles in a public place): ‘There is a $100 fine for dropping litter.’ Things litter a floor, street, area etc: ‘Discarded clothing began to litter the dance floor.’

 [explain]:  get/take (your) revenge (on sb): ‘He decided that the easiest way to get his revenge was to disinherit her.’ ‘She has vowed to take revenge on each and every one of them.’
