 [explain]:  When you talk about an action or situation which began in the past and continues into the present, use a present perfect tense: ‘I’ve been waiting here since 9 o’clock.’
[wrong sentence]:  Since that journey, I never sailed again.


U:ADV also -> NONE
 [explain]:  another (NOT also another): ‘Another problem, of course, is finding a job.’ Families fall into two categories. One is the composite family; another is the nuclear family. Families fall into two categories. One is the composite family; the other is the nuclear family. When you are talking about two people or things, use the other (NOT another): ‘One of the twins is called Youki and the other is called Azusa.’
[wrong sentence]:  There is also another point that is worth mentioning.


U:ADV most -> NONE
 [explain]:  An adjective which contains the sense ‘most’ as part of its meaning cannot be used with most.
[wrong sentence]:  My most favourite drink is lemonade.


U:ADV in -> NONE
 [explain]:  Do not confuse lack sth (verb + object, WITHOUT in/of) with be lacking in sth and (a) lack of sth: ‘He lacks the confidence to start his own business.’ ‘At school he was found to be lacking in confidence.’ ‘She certainly doesn’t show any lack of confidence.’ ‘I think I’m suffering from a lack of sleep.’
[wrong sentence]:  At school I used to lack in confidence.


U:ADV rather -> NONE
 [explain]:  Do not use rather and prefer together: ‘If the weather’s fine, we’d rather play tennis.’
[wrong sentence]:  I’d rather prefer just to lie on the beach.


U:ADV again -> NONE
 [explain]:  repeat sth (WITHOUT again): ‘Would you mind repeating the question?’ , "I just can’t believe it," he repeated.’
[wrong sentence]:  I asked her to repeat again what she had said.


U:ADV back -> NONE
 [explain]:  Return contains the meaning ‘back’: ‘He had to return to India to look after his mother.’ ‘He’s left a note asking you to return the book he lent you.’
[wrong sentence]:  She returned back after half an hour.
[wrong sentence]:  When is he going to return back the money he owes you?


U:ADV so -> NONE
 [explain]:  If the first clause begins with if, since, as or because, do NOT begin the second clause with so (or that’s why) . See also BUT
[wrong sentence]:  Since it’s his birthday on Monday, so he’s having a party.


U:ADV more -> NONE
 [explain]:  bad, worse, (the) worst: ‘The medicine just made me feel worse.’ ‘It’s the worst film I’ve ever seen.’
[wrong sentence]:  People’s lack of responsibility makes the problem even more worse.


U:ADV also another -> another
 [explain]:  another (NOT also another): ‘Another problem, of course, is finding a job.’ Families fall into two categories. One is the composite family; another is the nuclear family. Families fall into two categories. One is the composite family; the other is the nuclear family. When you are talking about two people or things, use the other (NOT another): ‘One of the twins is called Youki and the other is called Azusa.’
[wrong sentence]:  Also another way to lose weight is to eat less.


U:ADV very fine -> fine
 [explain]:  When fine means ‘very well’, it is not used with very or extremely.
[wrong sentence]:  Hello, John. How are you? Very fine, thank you.


R:PUNCT appeared -> occur
 [explain]:  appear = (1) become visible or (suddenly) be seen: ‘Small red patches appeared all over the child’s back.’ ‘A minute later the manager appeared and asked what was wrong.’(2) (of something new) become available or be seen for the first time: ‘The first edition appeared in 1987.’ ‘The new model will not appear in the shops until the end of the year.’ ‘When did dinosaurs first appear?’ occur = (1) (of unplanned events) happen: ‘The crash occurred just minutes after take-off.’ ‘These storms usually occur in the late afternoon.’ (2) exist or be found (in a particular place): ‘The
[wrong sentence]:  Unfortunately, another problem has appeared.


R:PUNCT destroyed -> ruin
 [explain]:  When you mean ‘make something less attractive, effective or useful’, use spoil or ruin (= spoil completely): ‘I didn’t join them on their walk because I didn’t want to spoil my new shoes.’ ‘If you open the camera, you’ll ruin the film.’
[wrong sentence]:  The water had been much too hot and most of the clothes were destroyed.


R:PUNCT feeling -> feel
 [explain]:  feelings (plural) = what you think or feel about something: ‘My own feelings are that the marriage won’t last more than a year.’ ‘Why does he always try to hide his feelings?’
[wrong sentence]:  The meeting provided a good opportunity for her to express her feeling.


R:PUNCT hardly -> hard/severely
 [explain]:  hardly = scarcely; almost not: ‘It was hardly raining at all.’ ‘I could hardly believe my eyes.’ ; hard = (1) with a lot of effort: ‘If you work hard, you’re bound to pass.’ (2) heavily or severely: ‘The authorities are coming down hard on tax evasion.’ Before a past participle, use severely for this meaning: ‘If they are caught, they will be severely punished.’
[wrong sentence]:  Society shouldn’t punish these people too hardly.


R:PUNCT with -> by
 [explain]:  judge sb by a particular measure: ‘It’s better to judge people by what they do, not just by what they say.’
[wrong sentence]:  What standards should we judge them with?


R:PUNCT shivering -> shiver
 [explain]:  make sb/sth do sth (WITHOUT to): ‘They made him take the examination again.’
[wrong sentence]:  The cold water made me shivering.


R:PUNCT menaced -> threaten
 [explain]:  Menace is usually used as a noun or adjective: ‘These street gangs are a social menace.’ ‘He was convicted of menacing behaviour.’ Menace is not often used as a verb. It appears mainly in formal styles and means ‘make or try to make someone feel their life or safety is in great danger’: ‘His mighty war machine continues to menace neighbouring countries.’ When you are talking about something which is a danger to peace, survival, safety, health, happiness, etc, use threaten: ‘These poisonous liquids threaten the drinking water supply of two million people.’ ‘AIDS could threaten the economic well-being of Asian countries.’
[wrong sentence]:  Nowadays world peace is menaced.


R:PUNCT perfect -> perfectly
 [explain]:  Perfect is an adjective: ‘My grandmother enjoys perfect health.’
[wrong sentence]:  I am pleased to say that the tape recorder now works perfect.


R:PUNCT rewards -> award
 [explain]:  See AWARD
[wrong sentence]:  Two of the German films were given rewards.


R:PUNCT speaking -> talk
 [explain]:  When just one person does all or most of the talking , either speak or talk may be used: ‘He spoke/talked about his years in the army.’ When two or more people have a conversation , the usual word is talk (NOT speak): ‘We must have been talking for hours.’ ‘If someone brings me a problem, we just sit down and talk about it.’
[wrong sentence]:  We hadn’t seen each other for a year and so we spent the whole night speaking.


R:PUNCT deeply -> properly
 [explain]:  understand sb/sth properly/better; fully/really understand sb/sth (NOT deeply): ‘Make sure that you understand the instructions properly before you begin.’ ‘Nobody fully understands how the drug actually works.’ Note the alternative: ‘Reading helps us to develop our understanding of the world.’
[wrong sentence]:  You have to know our culture in order to understand us deeply.


M:ADV NONE -> there
 [explain]:  See THERE 1
[wrong sentence]:  On Saturdays is usually a party at someone’s house.


M:ADV NONE -> about
 [explain]:  When you mean ‘finally cause something to happen or exist’, use bring about: ‘These new manufacturing methods brought about an increase in production.’ ‘The company’s poor performance was brought about by factors beyond its control.’
[wrong sentence]:  Nuclear power could bring the destruction of our planet.


M:ADV NONE -> when
 [explain]:  To introduce a subordinate clause, use even if, even though or even when (NOT even on its own): ‘You’ll have to accept the invitation, even though you don’t want to.’ ‘Even when my father is angry, he never raises his voice.’
[wrong sentence]:  Even I go to bed early, I’m still tired in the morning.


 [explain]:  Except (a preposition) cannot be followed by a clause that has a finite verb (‘came’). Use unless (a conjunction) or except when, except while, except if, except that: ‘Americans are just like any other people in the world, except that they are more tolerant.’ ‘He tends to ignore me except when he needs something.’
[wrong sentence]:  The old lady never spoke to anyone except someone came to visit her.


M:ADV NONE -> away
 [explain]:  A place that is a long distance from another place is far away: ‘Her children all live far away, but they still manage to visit her.’
[wrong sentence]:  When the bomb exploded, everyone tried to get as far as possible.


M:ADV NONE -> very
 [explain]:  very + adjective + noun + Indeed: ‘I thought it was a very clever answer indeed.’ very + adjective/adverb + indeed: ‘The quality of the recording is very good indeed.’ ‘Some of the students did very well.’
[wrong sentence]:  Secondary students are under great pressure indeed.
[wrong sentence]:  We all had a good time indeed.


M:ADV NONE -> as
 [explain]:  be kind enough to do sth: ‘Would you be kind enough to forward the enclosed documents to Mr Tomkin’s new address?’ be so kind as to do sth: ‘I wonder if you’d be so kind as to inform your colleagues of the new arrangements.’
[wrong sentence]:  I wonder if you would be so kind to send me further details and an application form.


M:ADV NONE -> so
 [explain]:  so + adjective/adverb + that clause: ‘I’m so tired that I can’t keep awake.’ ‘He spoke so quickly that nobody could understand him.’
[wrong sentence]:  I was shocked by the sight that I could hardly speak.


U:ADJ beloved -> NONE
 [explain]:  Beloved is used only in literary and humorous styles: ‘There were many regrets in her life, but her beloved son was not one of them.’ ‘He wants to move closer to his beloved football team, Chester City.’ It is particularly common on gravestones and old-fashioned birthday cards: To the memory of Alice Holt. beloved wife of Thomas Holt.’
[wrong sentence]:  He spends as much time as he can with his beloved children.


U:ADJ more -> NONE
 [explain]:  good, better, best
[wrong sentence]:  German cars are more expensive but they are more better.


 [explain]:  Do not use more with the -er form of an adjective or adverb: ‘Leather bags are more expensive but they tend to last longer.’ more or less
[wrong sentence]:  These machines make farming much more easier.
[wrong sentence]:  In Taiwan the food is more cheaper than in England.


U:ADJ dead -> NONE
 [explain]:  When it is clear from the context that the person referred to is dead, use body (WITHOUT dead): ‘The police found his body floating in the river.’
[wrong sentence]:  His dead body was flown back home for burial.


U:NOUN bit -> NONE
 [explain]:  A bit and a little bit are used mainly in informal styles. In other styles, it is better to use a little, slightly, rather, quite or somewhat: ‘His brother was somewhat older than we had expected.’
[wrong sentence]:  The instructions were a little bit confusing.


 [explain]:  Use a (little) bit before adjectives: ‘She looked a bit tired.’ Before nouns, use a little or (in informal styles) a (little) bit of: ‘He could do with a little help.’ ‘He’s been a bit of a nuisance recently.’
[wrong sentence]:  I watched a little bit television and went to bed.


U:NOUN colour -> NONE
 [explain]:  The noun colour is not usually used with. the name of a colour (red, green, blue etc). Colour is used only when the colour of something is not pure or is difficult to describe exactly: ‘It’s an unusual bluish-grey colour’. See Note at COLOURED 1 . Note however that the name of a colour can be used with in colour: ‘It’s brown in colour with white buttons down the front.’
[wrong sentence]:  I bought a blue colour shirt and a pair of socks.


U:NOUN language -> NONE
 [explain]:  They know Japanese language.
[wrong sentence]:  I speak Arabic language all the time.


U:NOUN alan -> NONE
 [explain]:  Dear is followed by Mr/Mrs/Ms + surname only (NOT first name + surname): ‘Dear Mrs Jackson’
[wrong sentence]:  Dear Mr Alan Jones,


U:NOUN music -> NONE
 [explain]:  (jazz/rock) band or (pop/rock) group (NOT music band): ‘The band’s new album has just been released.’
[wrong sentence]:  After leaving school, he joined a music band.


U:NOUN o’clock -> NONE
 [explain]:  Do not use o’clock for times that include minutes or parts of an hour.
[wrong sentence]:  It was twenty past four o’clock when the train arrived.


 [explain]:  Use EITHER o’clock OR a.m./p.m. (NOT both).
[wrong sentence]:  By seven o’clock p.m. the child had been found.


U:NOUN ones -> NONE
 [explain]:  Avoid ones immediately after these/those, especially in formal styles: ‘Within this group, there are those who are willing to take risks and those who are more cautious.’
[wrong sentence]:  This book will be of interest to all those ones involved in the tourist industry.


M:PART NONE -> up
 [explain]:  break up = stop being together as a couple or group: ‘Did you know that Carol and Richard have broken up?’ ‘Can you remember when the Beatles broke up?’
[wrong sentence]:  The family broke just after he was born.


 [explain]:  clean up = (1) clean a place, especially by taking away all the things which make it look dirty, untidy or unattractive: ‘You can play in your bedroom as long as you promise to clean it up afterwards.’ (2) make an area or organization a better place for people to live or work in, especially by removing criminals, corrupt officials, etc: ‘It’s time someone cleaned up this city; we have one of the highest crime rates in the country.’
[wrong sentence]:  The local residents would like to clean the neighbourhood.


 [explain]:  Then you cut the carrots and put them into the saucepan. Then you cut up the carrots and put them into the saucepan cut up = cut something into small pieces: ‘Two-year-olds can’t eat must unless you cut it up for them.’
[wrong sentence]:  He cut the strip of photographs and gave one to me.


 [explain]:  end = finish or cause (something) to finish: ‘The lessons usually end at five o’clock but some teachers end their lessons early.’ end up = (1) be forced to do something (after everything else has failed): ‘For months she refused to pay us any rent, so we ended up taking her to court.’ (2) eventually find yourself in a particular place or condition: ‘If you don’t stop smoking, you’ll end up in hospital.’
[wrong sentence]:  Since nobody would lend me the money, I ended asking my father for it.
[wrong sentence]:  Nowadays very few criminals end in jail.


 [explain]:  grow = (1) (of children, animals, plants etc) develop or get bigger as part of a natural process: ‘Mary’s little boy grew four centimetres last year.’ ‘Orchids don’t grow in cold climates.’ (2) cause plants, vegetables etc to do this by taking care of them: ‘In the back garden we grow onions, potatoes and carrots.’ grow up = (of children) pass from childhood into maturity: ‘Of course I know him - we grew up together in New York.’ ‘Tom wants to be a scientist when he grows up.’
[wrong sentence]:  Many of these children grow in an atmosphere of violence.


 [explain]:  When you go towards someone and stop in front of them. you go up to, over to or across to them (NOT to or near to): ‘One of the Japanese students walked slowly up to me and, with a huge smile, banded me a rose.’
[wrong sentence]:  He came into the shop and went to the woman behind the counter.


M:PART NONE -> down
 [explain]:  In an emergency situation, policemen, firemen, etc break down the door of a room or building: ‘If the door’s locked, you’ll just have to break it down.’
[wrong sentence]:  The police had to break the door to get in.


 [explain]:  cut down = make something tall (such as a tree) fall down by cutting through it close to the ground: ‘All the elms were diseased and had to be cut down.’
[wrong sentence]:  To build the motorway, they will have to cut a lot of trees.


M:PART NONE -> out
 [explain]:  cut out = remove a part of something by cutting all around it: ‘Each child had to draw a face and then cut it out.’
[wrong sentence]:  I cut the picture and stuck it on a sheet of paper.


 [explain]:  find = discover something by chance or by looking for it: ‘Did you ever find the sunglasses you lost?’ ‘I can’t find my comb. Have you seen it anywhere?’ ; find out = get information about something that you want to know: ‘He’s gone to find out which gate the plane leaves from.’ ‘I think we should find out exactly what’s missing before we start accusing people.’
[wrong sentence]:  I’d like to find why nobody likes me.
[wrong sentence]:  The doctors are trying to find what is wrong with him.


M:PART NONE -> on
 [explain]:  operate on sb: ‘He is too weak at the moment to be operated on.’
[wrong sentence]:  My niece was recently operated for appendicitis.


M:PART NONE -> over
 [explain]:  When you go towards someone and stop in front of them. you go up to, over to or across to them (NOT to or near to): ‘One of the Japanese students walked slowly up to me and, with a huge smile, banded me a rose.’
[wrong sentence]:  She stood up, walked to John, and asked him what he was doing.


U:PRON myself -> NONE
 [explain]:  See Language Note at MYSELF
[wrong sentence]:  I couldn’t concentrate myself as there was someone talking.


 [explain]:  See Language Note at MYSELF
[wrong sentence]:  I usually relax myself by taking a hot bath.


 [explain]:  See Language Note at MYSELF
[wrong sentence]:  I intend to specialize myself in French literature.


U:PRON me -> NONE
 [explain]:  confirm + direct object (WITHOUT me, us, them etc): ‘I am pleased to confirm that your application has been approved.’
[wrong sentence]:  Could you please confirm me whether you have received my order.


U:PRON herself -> NONE
 [explain]:  See Language Note at MYSELF
[wrong sentence]:  She says that she feels herself trapped in the job.


U:PRON you -> NONE
 [explain]:  recommend sth: ‘Can you recommend a good hotel?’ recommend that: ‘My accountant recommends that I should open an offshore account.’ ‘We recommend you choose your wedding ring about three months in advance.’ ‘They recommend that 100 be regarded as a minimum number.’ recommend doing sth: ‘I’d never recommend sending a young child to boarding school.’ British English also uses recommend sb to do sth: ‘I wouldn’t recommend you to let your children watch it.’
[wrong sentence]:  I recommend you a walk along the Seine.


U:PRON him -> NONE
 [explain]:  say that: ‘She said that she might be late.’ tell sb that: ‘She told me that she might be late.’
[wrong sentence]:  When he arrived, they said him that his friend had died.


U:PRON i thank -> thank
 [explain]:  Use thanks (informal), thank you or (especially in formal styles) I would/should like to thank you (NOT I thank you): ‘On behalf of the team, I’d like to thank you for your invaluable advice and support.’
[wrong sentence]:  I thank you very much for your last letter.


noop NONE -> -none
 [explain]:  Congratulations is a plural noun: ‘Many congratulations to you both. I’m sure you’ll be very happy together.’ ‘Give them our congratulations and say that we’ll be getting in touch soon.’
[wrong sentence]:  Congratulations! You must be feeling very proud.


 [explain]:  See Note at EVERYONE 1
[wrong sentence]:  Everyone was tired of listening to her voice.


 [explain]:  everyone = all the people: ‘Hurry up! Everyone is waiting for you.’ everyone = each single one (of a group or number): ‘When we examined the books we found that every one of them had a page missing.’
[wrong sentence]:  This problem affects each and everyone of us.


 [explain]:  (make) a hole in sth (NOT on): ‘The bucket had a hole in the bottom.’
[wrong sentence]:  The yacht hit the side of the ferry and made a hole in it.


 [explain]:  let sb do sth (WITHOUT to): ‘Why wouldn’t Alice let you help her?’
[wrong sentence]:  Do you think your parents will let you go to England?


 [explain]:  Do not use the in front of one of : ‘We stayed at one of the cheaper hotels.’ ‘She is one of the strongest political leaders in the world today.’
[wrong sentence]:  Fluency in English is one of the best qualifications you can have.


R:PART with -> up
 [explain]:  end = finish or cause (something) to finish: ‘The lessons usually end at five o’clock but some teachers end their lessons early.’ end up = (1) be forced to do something (after everything else has failed): ‘For months she refused to pay us any rent, so we ended up taking her to court.’ (2) eventually find yourself in a particular place or condition: ‘If you don’t stop smoking, you’ll end up in hospital.’
[wrong sentence]:  The trouble with smoking is that you end with making a habit of it.


R:PART near -> up
 [explain]:  See Note at TO 2
[wrong sentence]:  I went near to the girl and told her my name.


R:PART off -> out
 [explain]:  go out = (of a coal fire, cigarette, candle etc) stop burning: The problem with cigars is that they keep going out.’
[wrong sentence]:  Eventually, the fire went off and the room got colder.


R:ORTH every thing -> everything
 [explain]:  Every thing (two words) is not common and is used only when things are considered as separate items: ‘She worries about every silly little thing.’
[wrong sentence]:  The shop sells every thing, from toothbrushes to radios.


R:ORTH every day -> everyday
 [explain]:  See Note at EVERYBODY
[wrong sentence]:  This example shows how computers affect our every day life.


R:ORTH every body -> everybody
 [explain]:  everybody (one word) = each person; everyone: ‘One day everybody will be able to travel to the moon.’ every body (two words) = each (dead) body: ‘Every body was collected from the battlefield and given a proper burial.’
[wrong sentence]:  There aren’t enough jobs for every body.


R:ORTH everyday -> every day
 [explain]:  everyday (one word) = not special or unusual in any way: ‘A good photographer can make everyday objects look rare and special.’ every day (two words) = each day: ‘Every day I try to learn ten new words.’
[wrong sentence]:  The sun shines here everyday.


R:ORTH forever -> for ever
 [explain]:  forever = continually; all the time: ‘He is forever asking me for more pocket money.’ ; for ever = for always: ‘He promised that he would love me for ever and a day.’
[wrong sentence]:  Nobody lives forever.


M:ADJ NONE -> new
 [explain]:  When experience means ‘something that happens to you’, it is usually modified: ‘The book is based on her experiences as a reporter in El Salvador.’ ‘Don’t give up just because of one bad experience.’ ‘The trip to Niagara Falls was an unforgettable experience.’
[wrong sentence]:  If I go to England, I’ll have a lot of experiences.


M:ADJ NONE -> local
 [explain]:  The inhabitants of a particular town, country or area are the people who live there, especially considered in terms of quantity or history: ‘Just ten years ago the town had only 12,000 inhabitants.’ ‘The first inhabitants of the island planted coffee beans.’ To refer to the people living in and around a village or town, who have shared interests and activities, use local inhabitant/local residents/local population or (in informal styles) locals: ‘The local residents against the motorway proposal.’
[wrong sentence]:  The inhabitants certainly know how to make money from tourists.


M:ADJ NONE -> italian
 [explain]:  After typical you usually mention the exact group to which someone or something belongs: ‘McGarron looked like a typical American car salesman.’ ‘The old fireplace and the few pieces of typical Basque furniture give the room a homely atmosphere.’
[wrong sentence]:  Angelo took us to a typical restaurant.


M:ADJ NONE -> good
 [explain]:  See Note at VALUABLE 3
[wrong sentence]:  The large size is very value.


R:PRON housekeepers -> housewives
 [explain]:  housekeeper = a person who does the cooking and keeps a house clean, usually paid by the owners of the house: ‘We can’t afford to employ a housekeeper.’ housewife = a married woman who does not have a full-time job outside the house: ‘She doesn’t want to give up her job and become a housewife.’
[wrong sentence]:  Most Korean women are housekeepers.


R:PRON me -> myself
 [explain]:  See Language Note at MYSELF
[wrong sentence]:  I got me a drink and sat down on the sofa.


R:PRON occurred -> happen
 [explain]:  Occur is used mainly in formal styles: ‘These violent incidents frequently occur without any warning.’
[wrong sentence]:  You’d better tell them exactly what occurred.


R:PRON you -> yours
 [explain]:  When the meaning is possessive, use of + mine/yours/his/hers/ours/theirs. Compare: ‘That’s a photograph of him.’ (= showing him), ‘That’s a photograph of his.’ (= belonging to or taken by him)
[wrong sentence]:  A friend of you phoned and wants you to call her.


R:PRON that -> who
 [explain]:  That is used to introduce an identifying relative clause (one which identifies, defines, or restricts the preceding noun): ‘The woman that is sitting behind us is Tom’s music teacher.’ ‘The man that I marry will have lots of money.’
[wrong sentence]:  Sitting next to me was an old lady, that seemed to be sound asleep.


R:PRON trembling -> shiver
 [explain]:  You tremble when you are afraid, nervous, upset or excited: ‘As she opened the envelope, her hands started trembling and she started to cry.’ ‘She trembled with excitement just at the thought of seeing him again.’ When you are cold , you shiver: ‘I stood at the bus stop shivering and wishing that I’d worn my coat.’
[wrong sentence]:  It was so cold that I couldn’t stop trembling.


R:PRON welcomed -> welcome
 [explain]:  welcome (verb) = greet a guest or visitor in a friendly way when they arrive: ‘Her Royal Highness was welcomed at the airport by a party of Cabinet Ministers.’
[wrong sentence]:  You can bring as many friends to the party as you like. Everyone is welcomed.


R:PRON what -> (that
 [explain]:  What is not used as a relative pronoun. After all, everything, anything etc, use that or nothing: ‘You can have anything (that) you like.’ ‘I have everything (that) I need for the time being.’
[wrong sentence]:  I’ll do all what I can to help you.


R:PRON what -> which
 [explain]:  What is not used as a relative pronoun. When you comment on a previous statement, use which: ‘Lizzie ate the whole box of chocolates, which was very greedy.’
[wrong sentence]:  She told him that she didn’t want to marry him, what in my opinion was very silly of her.


R:PRON you’ll -> you
 [explain]:  See Language Note at WILL
[wrong sentence]:  Whenever you’ll hear about this place, you’ll want to come back.


R:PRON whom -> who/that
 [explain]:  When you need a subject relative pronoun, use who/that (NOT Whom): ‘He was the one who/that came to meet me at the station.’
[wrong sentence]:  She is one of the few people whom I think might be good at the job.


R:CONJ or -> nor
 [explain]:  neither ... nor ... (NOT neither ... or ... ): ‘The sales assistant was neither friendly nor helpful.’
[wrong sentence]:  His parents neither shouted at him or smacked him.
[wrong sentence]:  Inside the examination room we could neither smoke or talk.


U:PART up -> NONE
 [explain]:  raise sth (WITHOUT up): ‘If you want to ask a question, just raise your hand.’ ‘The age of retirement should be lowered, not raised.’
[wrong sentence]:  They offered to raise up my salary.


U:PART out -> NONE
 [explain]:  When you mean ‘grow, develop or become increasingly common’ use spread (WITHOUT out) : ‘They couldn’t stop the fire from spreading.’ ‘Dissatisfaction with the present government seems to be spreading.’ Compare: ‘The search party spread out across the field.’ ‘Her clothes were all spread out on the bed, ready to be packed.’
[wrong sentence]:  Rumours spread out very quickly.


 [explain]:  voice your feelings and opinions (WITHOUT out): ‘I’m not just voicing my own opinions - we all feel the same.’ ‘Whenever one of us voices the slightest disapproval, the chairman takes offence.’
[wrong sentence]:  Students should feel able to voice out their opinions.


M:CONJ NONE -> and
 [explain]:  Then is an adverb (NOT a conjunction). It cannot be used to link clauses.
[wrong sentence]:  We took a bus to the city centre then caught a train to London.
